Glitter, Glue, and Signs of Life

by turtlebass

Summary

Agatha couldn’t move. It was her, she was sure of it—the dancer from the club, on the night of the last day of school. A stripper was standing in her classroom, addressing her second-graders. A stripper who, until this very moment, had been filed neatly into the category of things Agatha would never think about again. (Except that one time, when she got too drunk on the Fourth of July. And that other time, when she got too drunk on the Seventh of July, which was just a normal Tuesday.) And yet here she was, in practical jeans and a black tee, looking every inch like a competent professional.

OR

First ten minutes of Afternoon Delight meets...the American public school system? (THEY'RE TEACHERS)

Last Day of School

Chapter Notes

Hi, lovelies. Heads up that I am carving out a long love affair with this piece of writing. I'm taking my sweet time and loving on every paragraph. Bookmark it and wait until I'm done to spend a night on it, or read along with me as it takes shape--whatever feels most like a little treat. I'm grateful to be slowly polishing off the rust from this writing thing after largely ignoring it for years, and especially grateful to be doing it in this space with our two favorite players of all time. Welcome in! Hope you enjoy it. 🖤

 

“Our story began with uncertainty, and with uncertainty, it ended—not with a bang, but a whisper.” -Aura Biru



Being stuck in the parking lot on the last day of school was Agatha Harkness's personal nightmare. In fact, she'd literally had this nightmare before—a hoard of parents closing in on the car windows with lifeless eyes. She slumped her head against the window, and considered it might be time to stop watching The Walking Dead on school nights. A podcast played automatically through her speakers, and the re-telling of a woman's horrific murder faded to background noise. Surely human serial killers were more innocuous than zombies.

She scanned the playground automatically, and her eyes fell to the usual trouble spots: the slide, the corner by the lunch table, the section underneath the play structure. A fourth-grader threw her tiny arms around her best friend, their cheeks pressed together in earnest. Their mothers stood patiently nearby, watching the line of cars grow steadily longer.

Agatha remembered when she used to feel more like a fourth-grader than a mother on the last day of school. She remembered the final bell, and the smell of hot asphalt, the June sunshine peeling the weight off her shoulders. Now, the ache of the year dug deep into her back. Her contacts scratched at her eyes, and the furrow between her brows felt like it was settling deeper into her face with every passing glance in the rearview.

A honk startled her, and she gestured to the car in front of her, which had moved a total of four inches. The man behind her honked again, and Agatha locked eyes with him in the mirror.

“There’s no room, asshole!” she yelled, plastering a smile on her face.

“Agatha!”

She jumped. Her head hit the roof, and she winced, clutching at the sore spot. She twisted around to find Wanda peering through her very open back window.

“Fuck me,” Agatha muttered.

“You know your back windows are open?”

Agatha resisted the urge to point out that Wanda's head was still sticking through said window.

“Katie Buckers just came up to me and asked why Mrs. H. was yelling and listening to a, quote, ‘killing story?’” 

She moved up to the front, and Agatha glanced at the man in the mirror again. He ceremoniously flipped her off.

“What can I do for you, Principal Maximoff?”

“Don't call me that. Where’s Nicky?”

“Sleepover.” 

“With who?” 

“Oh, don’t be jealous. Jacob, from his class.”

“Aren’t the boys sleeping over at our place next week?”

Wanda ignored her and shot her a sympathetic look. “What did you have planned?”

“I thought we could get last-day-of-school ice cream," Agatha said, trying not to sound so pathetic. "But apparently, that's not as enticing as whatever new video game Jacob has, which I literally don’t understand how to pronounce.” 

“Of course you can’t pronounce it. You still call them video games.”

“What the fuck else am I supposed to call them?”

“They’re just ‘games.' Everything is a video now; you don’t have to say it.” 

“Great,” Agatha said. “Now I feel sad and geriatric around these twenty-seven year-old mothers with amazing tits. Thank you for that. I’m going to go home and not come back here for two months, if that’s okay with you, Principal.” 

“Your tits look great and you know it.”

“I’m calling HR.”

Wanda laughed. “Do you want to grab a drink tonight? My treat, for my employee of the year.”

“You’re kidding.”

“About employee of the year, obviously. I know you're the one that enabled screen reader on Sharon's desktop, by the way."

Agatha stifled a laugh. "The woman sits at that computer all day; you can't say you weren't a little curious."

"I could hear extremely detailed romantasy reviews from inside my office, so thanks to you, I'll never be curious about anything ever again. And I'm not kidding about the drink. You need to loosen up a little; I’ve never seen you like this on the last day of school."

Agatha took a deep breath, leaning her head back against the seat. She placed both hands on the wheel, considering the proposition honestly. She hadn’t been able to really hang out with Wanda in months outside of school, and she did miss her friend. 

“I can’t,” she said finally, eyes falling to the dashboard clock. “Nicky’s out, which means Josh and I have to do date night.”

“Ooh, ‘have to’? Really selling it.” 

Maybe she should have felt offended on behalf of her husband, or herself, but she'd said it—it seemed pointless to backtrack. 

Wanda patted Agatha’s arm as a parent beckoned her from the front steps. “Call me sometime. Let’s hang out, without our children, before it’s August all of a sudden.”

“Deal."

“Don’t have too much fun tonight,” Wanda called.

"I will hope and pray for boring," Agatha said, fingers crossed and arms over her chest.

The car in front of her moved, finally, and she glanced in the rearview again. “Honk at me one more time,” she muttered, but he didn’t catch her eye. He was leaning his head in his hand, talking to his kid. She recognized that same blank stare that meant one half of his mind was elsewhere, and the other was responding to whatever sweet, simple nonsense his kid was spouting at him. She ignored the tug in her chest and the emptiness of her own back seat, and pulled out of the lot, towards home.

 

 

Agatha deposited the laundry onto her bed, and deposited herself on top of the pile. She snuggled into the clothes and inhaled. It was a strange habit of hers, spooning the laundry fresh from the dryer. She felt she'd be content to die that way, buried alive under a warm embrace of shirt sleeves and pajama pants.

She closed her eyes, and tried not to think about the night ahead—the familiar stagnancy of conversation in a dimly lit restaurant, looking at the man she'd known so deeply for twenty years, and knowing even more deeply that she'd long run out of things to say. The clothes started to cool, and she hated them for it. She felt that if she really tried, she could cry about it. The clothes, not the husband. Never really the husband.

She sat up, forcing her hands to fold, and considered fast-forwarding her podcast to the grizzly details. Despite all the parenting books on the dangers of desensitization, she'd secretly always thought that unless someone was being chainsawed or stuffed into a freezer, just-plain-murder was a little mundane.

“Laughlin frequented the same strip club in Orange County, California, but he fell outside of the usual mold—Laughlin was married. His wife of nine years, Patricia, often visited the club with him. The club owner at the time described her as an independent, confident woman, often chatting with the dancers on her own, presumably building relationships on behalf of her husband, who would later reap the solemn reward.”

Agatha winced at the over-the-top wording, then froze, wondering if she'd imagined the sound from her front door. She swore that her hearing had changed once she became a teacher, and then again once she became a mother—she had no clue when someone was yelling her name, but she could hear the doorknob turn in slow motion from across the house. Josh looked at it, the light flickering, his mouth open. “I thought you said you–”

“I lied.” She cut him off, gathering her purse more firmly over her shoulder. Irritation flared in her chest, the self-loathing kind, the everyone-and-everything-loathing kind. She stared at the pink woman in spite. “What now? Do we just go home?”

“Oh fuck, dude,” Josh said. “So…I guess you’re not, like, in the mood to…”

He trailed off, and she looked at him, her eyes unfocused. She let out a quiet, defeated sound. He was still staring at her, pupils blown, on the sidewalk outside of a strip club in Boston. And that was it—that was the moment, under the pink neon sign of a naked woman, her mouth tasting sour, that she finally knew she had nothing left to give.

“You need new fucking underwear,” she said. And then she called them a taxi.

TWO AND A HALF MONTHS LATER



Agatha let herself be dragged out of bed by a very excited, very awake Nicky at six in the morning. He was practically vibrating, a reminder that the slow, forgiving mornings of summer were behind them. She startled like she did every morning, and smacked the top of her alarm clock before remembering that the off button was on the side. She hadn’t owned a top-snooze alarm since the nineties, but her brain refused the simple adjustment. The satisfying slap, paired with that distinct mix of thrill and dread, still lived in her muscle memory—the reflexive first motion of a School Day, lodged deep in the bend of her elbow. 

She had bought the alarm clock in a bout of nostalgia last fall in an attempt to wean herself off late-night scrolling, like all the articles said she should. And now, one year later, she started her mornings with a violent assault of a plastic box, and she still took her phone to bed.

She didn’t think about it every morning anymore, or every night—the fact that her bed was empty. It had been almost two months since Josh had moved out, since their last knock-down, drag-out fight about something so impossibly stupid, they could no longer pretend. They were in the middle of screaming about the correct way to load new batteries into the TV remote (“You have to face them different ways, Agatha; that’s how fucking electricity works!” “Batteries aren’t fucking electric, Josh; that’s how fucking science works!”) when Josh had grabbed for the remote. It flew to the hardwood floor, the batteries skittering across the room. 

He gripped his hair, and said: “Why are we doing this? We don’t even fucking love each other anymore.” 

She stood there, her socks slipping on the hardwood, and felt something terribly akin to relief. She watched one of the batteries roll slowly under the couch.

“No,” she said. “I don’t even really like you.”

And then a week later he moved out, and a week after that she cleaned under the couch, and her fingers brushed against something smooth. She pulled out the stray battery, its edges covered in a crust of dust and acid. She sat on her heels in the living room, staring at the corroded metal, and laughed, the sound strange and unrestrained. It was an old, used battery that had been mixed in with the new. Her fingers closed around it, and she laughed and laughed on the floor of her empty house until her stomach started to ache, because of course, it wouldn't have mattered which way she’d been putting them into the remote.

 

 

Agatha curled her toes against the cool tile and took in her reflection. Her hair was its usual unsalvageable mess, but the dark circles under her eyes added a nice, unhinged touch—just the thing parents were hoping to see when they looked at their kid's new teacher. She reached for her glasses, not even considering the idea of rubbing contacts into her red-rimmed eyes.

Through the end of her marriage, through the separating itself, she hadn’t cried. Not when Josh assembled the moving boxes, not when he bubble-wrapped his bedside lamp and broke up the antique set (even though she was the one that had searched for them, and found them in Connecticut, and haggled for a good price with the hunched old man at the store, and since fucking when had Josh cared about that lamp?) 

She didn’t cry until they told Nicky. That night, she'd wracked herself to sleep with sobs, the words snagging and rolling under her ribs. She was selfish. A bad mother. A cold wife. And the most damning of all: responsible.

Since that night, the tears came too easily. Not just when she thought about Josh, or Nicky, or what could’ve been, but when she wasn’t thinking at all. When she folded socks, or put her car in park, or stood at her kitchen sink, scraping hardened spaghetti sauce off a plate. 

Last night had been one of those nights. She’d never been able to sleep before the first day of school—not at five years old, not at forty-two. She was accustomed to the rhythm of it, the tossing and turning until the sun came in to relieve her. But this was the first time she'd cried instead.

She yawned, raking a brush through her hair with one hand and brushing her teeth with the other. She nudged the bathroom door open with her hip, sunlight turning the blue tiles green, and as expected, Nicky was standing there. His grin split into a delighted giggle, and Agatha felt the sharp, specific pride that came from making him laugh. Like most parents, she was an above-average multitasker, so it was hardly a special skill. But Nicky didn’t know that yet. For now, when she brushed her hair and teeth at once, she was special. She made a show of switching hands as he clung to her pant leg, his laughter bubbling louder with every exaggerated motion. 

“Your turn,” she said, spitting into the sink and moving him into position.

“I want to do both, too!”

“Not today,” she said quickly, sidestepping the version of this morning where Nicky showed up to the first day of second grade with toothpaste in his hair.

She pulled a brush through his shoulder-length hair while he talked around the toothbrush in a garbled mess of syllables she pretended to understand. She hummed at the right moments, untangling a particularly stubborn knot near the nape of his neck.

“Still no haircut?” she asked lightly.

Nicky shook his head with the conviction of someone who didn’t have to detangle clumps of grass and dirt daily.

“All right, then,” she said. “Spit, rinse, and meet me in the kitchen, sailor.”

“Aye aye, Captain!” he said, toothpaste dribbling down his chin.

 

 

Agatha was halfway through packing their lunches when her phone rang. She put it on speaker, loading a sandwich into Nicky’s lunchbox.

“What’s wrong?” 

“Why do you assume something’s wrong?” Wanda's voice crackled with the telltale bad service that meant she was already at school.

“Why are you calling me when I’m going to see you in forty minutes?” Agatha countered, shoving celery sticks on top of the sandwich. She closed the lid, knowing full well Nicky was going to pretend the celery wasn't there. 

“I just wanted to check in on you. Big day, rough summer, to say the least.”

“I'll be fine." 

“You know I’ve got your back, right? Nobody blames you for what you said.”

Agatha laughed. “That is a bold-faced lie.”

“Okay, people definitely blame you. But I don’t," Wanda added. "You’re a great teacher, you were going through a hard time, and you have nothing to worry about.”

“I really don’t want to talk about this right now, okay?” Agatha glanced at her watch. “I have to make breakfast. Is there anything else?”

“Yes,” Wanda said. “Stop by my office before class. I need to run something by you.”

Agatha froze, her arm elbow-deep in Nicky’s purple backpack. “You just said I have nothing to worry about.”

“You don’t. I just need a favor.”

Agatha zipped up the bag and paused. “Look, I know you pulled some strings for me. With the whole…summer thing. And to get Nicky in my class. It’ll help to have him close by this year, you know? This is a lot of change for him. And I know it probably wasn’t easy. Thank you, Wanda."

“Damn straight it wasn’t easy,” Wanda said, and Agatha laughed. “But you’re worth it, and so is that kid.”

“He is pretty fucking great,” Agatha said, and added: “See you soon, Boss.” She hung up on Wanda’s groan.

Nicky wandered into the kitchen, fully dressed in neon orange sweatpants and a Lilo and Stitch pajama shirt. Agatha stared at him, eyebrows raised.

“Did you get dressed all by yourself?”

“Yeah,” he said proudly. “I’m in second grade. I can do it myself.”

“You’re not going to get too hot in those pants?”

“Nope.”

“Well, okay, then,” Agatha said. “What do you want for breakfast, baby?”

She looked at him, seated across the kitchen island, his body slouching and and wiggling in that seven-year-old inchworm way. She felt her chest warm at the sight, followed by the recently renewed wave of guilt and fear that she was ripping apart his home over his very head. Yet another familiar piece of their morning routine.

“Pancakes!”

“Ooh, pancakes.” She glanced in the fridge. “Nope, no eggs. Try again.”

“Ummmm… Mac n’ cheese!”

“You want mac ‘n’ cheese for breakfast? How about cereal ‘n’ cheese?”

“What!” he squealed, and Agatha laughed. 

“How about just cereal?” he said, eyes wide, and Agatha nodded seriously. 

“Cereal it is.”

“Wait,” he said. “With milk, too.”

“Wild man,” Agatha muttered, and poured a bowl for each of them.

 

 

Agatha's hand drifted automatically to the lid of her coffee mug before they hit the first speed bump, and she glanced at newly painted mural on the front of the school. Oyster Bend Elementary. The letters, bright and cheerful, floated above a vibrant sea of river otters clutching oysters to their chests. 

“Fucking idiots,” Agatha muttered.

“Mama,” Nicky said from the backseat. “That’s a bad word.”

“And what do we say about bad words?”

“I can say them when I’m old enough to drive a car,” he recited, resigned.

“That’s right. Look at the mural."

Out of the corner of her eye, she caught two mothers standing side by side, glancing furtively at her car. One of them pointed surreptitiously, and the other shifted her PTA tote bag over her shoulder. Once, she would have thrived on this kind of attention, their eyes darting after her glossy heels clicking on the pavement.

In her twenties, when Agatha still subscribed to the allure of new beginnings, she used to primp for the first day of school. She would curl her hair or wear something that had to be ironed first—shirts that brought out the blue in her eyes and pressed blazers, and pencil skirts that showed off her calves. Her body had been toned and tight in all the right places, the way bodies were before they made room for another. When beginnings were still beginnings, she'd worn her heart and her confidence on her sleeve, and now it sat behind her in a booster seat, kicking its orange-clad legs.

Agatha tightened her grip on the steering wheel, looking pointedly away from the whispering women. She ignored the angry heat of embarrassment rising up her neck, and focused on the mural. 

“I like it,” Nicky replied decisively. “The pink one is my favorite.”

“River otters don’t even eat oysters. Sea otters eat oysters, and our mascot is a river otter, because sea otters don’t live in Massachusetts.”

“But they’re not eating them,” Nicky said. “They’re holding them. Maybe they’re just friends.”

Agatha smiled despite herself. She leaned back against the headrest, her eyes catching his in the mirror. His ponytail bobbed as he swung his feet, his sweatpants obnoxiously neon in the morning sun.

“You know where you’re going when the bell rings, right?” she asked.

“Mama’s classroom,” Nicky said, raising his hands like he’d just won a prize. “Because you’re my teacher!”

“Right again, sailor.” 

Agatha watched him jump from the car and join a group of kids who were climbing up the slide and falling like dominoes. Then, reluctantly, her eyes fell on the green double doors at the top of the steps, and she followed.

 

 

Wanda was waiting for her in the classroom when Agatha walked in, straightening a pile of papers on her desk.

“Hey,” Agatha said, hanging Nicky’s backpack on a hook near the door. “What—oh, shit. You told me to meet you in your office.”

“I knew you’d forget,” Wanda said, beckoning her into a hug. She squeezed harder than necessary.

“So,” she said. “How are—”

“Don’t ask how I’m holding up." Agatha stopped her, hand in the air. “Not you.”

“Okay,” Wanda said, stepping back. “Fair enough. I have something I need to talk to you about. Sit.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes at Wanda’s tone and slid into one of the student desks, folding her arms across the too-small surface. They were silent for a moment, faint squeals of laughter drifting from the open window. 

“Like I said on the phone,” Wanda started. “I don’t blame you for what happened this summer.”

Agatha opened her mouth, but Wanda cut her off with a look.

“I don’t blame you,” she repeated firmly. “But there are extra eyes on you now. I’m getting pressure from certain parents who aren’t thrilled about what you said. Or about the fact that Nicky’s in your class, even though it's against our policy.”

“You said it was fine."

“It is, because we all agree these are extenuating circumstances. But I need you to do something for me in return. To ease the pressure from the PTA, the school board, and the parents.”

“The PTA?” Agatha scoffed. “Since when are you in the pocket of those blonde hell-demons?”

“That's not fair,” Wanda said, and her expression softened. For the first time, Agatha noticed the slight shadows under her friend’s eyes. 

“It’ll help if they have something else to focus on,” she continued lightly. “Or someone. I have a student teacher for you.”

“No,” Agatha shook her head. “Absolutely not. I have enough on my plate with my usual routine without babysitting some sweaty grad student who’s afraid of seven-year-olds.”

“Agatha,” Wanda said, sharper now. “I know this isn’t what you want, but here’s the deal: if you don’t take her, and you don’t play nice this semester, the board will vote to revoke your tenure. And then it’s out of my hands.”

Agatha’s mouth dropped open, her ears ringing. “Revoke my—are you serious?”

“I tried to fight them on it, but they don't love it when people humiliate them and get away with it. This is damage control. Show them you’re a team player. Let the student teacher do her thing, you do yours, and by the end of the semester, this whole mess will blow over.”

Agatha stared, open-mouthed. “This is not the definition of nothing to worry about.”

Wanda gave her an apologetic smile, placing a thin file on the desk. “I’ll send her in after recess. Her name is Rio Vidal.”

“Great,” Agatha muttered, and Wanda opened the door.

“I’m sorry,” Wanda added sincerely. “Happy Doomsday.”

The door shut behind her, their affectionate nickname for the first day of school feeling too apt all of a sudden. Agatha let out a low, exasperated groan, and tossed the file on her desk. She busied herself with her real concern of restoring the sanctity of her classroom. The supplies she’d hastily dumped last week were still piled in a crooked tower of cardboard and plastic, appropriately underneath the clock.

She stewed as she worked, stringing up twinkling lights and rehanging the paper leaves that had already fallen from the ceiling since last week. So what if some wet-behind-the-ears twenty-something was about to scrutinize her every move? So what if the PTA thought she was a loose cannon, if the school board thought she was unfit to teach? She paused with her stapler poised above the bulletin board, a paper squirrel caught under her impending strike. The squirrel stared back at her, the paper grin stretching smugly across its face, and she punched the stapler through its eye.

She stepped back to survey the scene. Kids whispered about her classroom in the hallways, their wide eyes peeking through the door whenever they thought she wasn’t looking. Even her old students found flimsy excuses to come back. Some claimed they were saying hi to a sibling or that they’d wandered in by mistake, but a few didn’t bother pretending. They just walked up to her and wrapped their arms around her waist. She liked that the best—when they remembered the world they’d been part of, and wanted to slip back inside for a little while.

The ceiling was a canopy of green, thick with paper leaves that glowed under the string lights. Against the back wall stood her pièce de résistance: a giant paper mache tree, its wide trunk rising from the floor, weighted by sandbags painted to look like mossy rocks. The branches stretched to the ceiling, where felt animals peeked from the leaves—an owl in glasses, a fox curled up with a book, a squirrel carrying a tiny red backpack. Beneath the tree, the rug was a vivid, grassy green, and the mismatched carpet squares were dotted with mushrooms and wildflowers. At the center sat her old armchair, worn and blue.

Every year, on the first day of school, students stepped into her room and froze. Their jaws dropped as they tilted their heads back to take it all in, wide eyes darting from the paper tree to the glowing canopy. Every year, Agatha watched them with a quiet thrill. It was, by far, her favorite part.

For a few hours a day, her students could forget the outside world entirely. In here, they could expand until they were bigger than they thought possible. The classroom was their very own planet, solid under their feet.

Agatha jabbed the stapler at a vine dangling over the edge of the bookshelf. Let the PTA gossip, and the school board hover. Let some overenthusiastic student teacher stare at her with moony eyes. They could serve whatever reprimands paired best with the sticks up their asses, but if they wanted to take her classroom, they'd have to drag her out by the feet first.

 

 

Three hours into the day, Agatha felt her sense of stability returning. She'd spent recess sipping her lukewarm coffee from her travel mug, her lungs expanding steadily, and selectively ignoring some of the more playful roughhousing. She was miserable at meditation, but recess worked. Now, the lights in her classroom dimmed, she felt herself settle, her inner critic closing its eyes and curling up by the light of the projector.

“Does anyone know the name of the town that we live in?”

Various children shouted answers at her. She called on June, quietly raising her hand, her bangs falling into her eyes.

“Massachusetts,” she said with a confidence that made Agatha smile.

“Hmm, good guess. That’s the state. I’ll give you a hint…it’s in the name of our school.”

“Oyster Bend!” June said, her face lighting up. Agatha let herself enjoy the comfortable rhythm in the clean back-and-forth of a lesson.

“Exactly. And who knows what a mascot is?” 

Nicky raised his hand. “It’s an animal.”

Agatha nodded absently, distracted by the sight of him fidgeting in his seat. He tugged at the waistband of his sweatpants, his face flushed. She knew she should have made him change.

“Ms. Harkness?” A boy with messy brown hair was suddenly at her side—Lonzo? No, Oliver. “My brother’s in high school, and his mascot is a Pilgrim. That’s not an animal.”

“That is a great point, Oliver,” Agatha said, eyeing him. “Why are you—Did you need something up here?” 

He ignored her question, taking a piece of tape from her dispenser, and then another, wrapping them around his index finger.

“A mascot can really be anything,” she said to the class, moving the tape out of Oliver’s reach and pointing him towards his desk, “that represents the students at a school.”

Agatha’s eyes landed on two girls behind Oliver. Ellie was braiding Kara’s orange, frizzy hair, Kara completely unaware.

“Hands to ourselves,” Agatha reminded softly, and Ellie dropped her hands, her face pink.

Agatha took a deep breath. It was the faintest start to an unraveling that meant she was losing them. She skipped ahead a couple slides. “Does anyone know what our mascot is?” Agatha asked. “Maybe you saw it on the new mural when you came in this morning?”

“A dragon!”

“Unicorns!”

“Oyster!”

Second-graders weren’t known for their self-restraint, especially when guessing animals, but she could already feel the effect of this summer. She knew the signs: the attention slipping from her grasp one pair of eyes at a time, the too-quick fraying of her patience. It was far, far too early to be losing her patience.

Out of the corner of her eye, Agatha caught Oliver, still playing with the tape. Somehow, he'd managed to pry the entire roll out of the dispenser before she’d thought to push it away. A disastrous oversight, she knew instantly. 

He unraveled a foot-long piece, and pulled too hard. The tape snapped off the roll, his arm flung backward, and the tape jackknifed straight into Kara’s hair. Kara and Ellie screamed. Kara’s hand flew to her head in horror, feeling for the tape and finding it tangled in the amateur braid she hadn’t even realized was there.

“I can help,” Ellie said, her small, clumsy fingers making it undoubtedly worse. Oliver stared at his own hand, bewildered. Agatha was at their desks in seconds, crouching over Kara, her fingers working quickly to pry the tape loose.

“It’s okay, love. I’ve got it. Let me see,” she murmured, her voice calm. “Oliver. Tape on my desk, now.” 

It wasn’t the first time she had pulled something out of a kid’s hair, but Kara’s hair was too frizzy, and clung stubbornly to the now-twisted piece of tape. 

“So,” she continued valiantly. “Who else has a guess for what our mascot is?”

Kara started to cry, her sobs growing louder by the second, and Agatha shushed her soothingly. 

“Mama, I’m HOT!” Nicky said suddenly, and an alarm sounded in Agatha’s head. 

She recognized that voice—the one that meant he was seconds away from taking matters into his own hands. Embarrassingly, a thought struck her for the first time. She had talked to Nicky about what it would mean to have his mother as his teacher, but she had forgotten about the room. Nicky had grown up in this classroom, long before he was a student in it. His stroller had rested beside the desk, the smell of dry-erase settling into his newborn skin. The first things he'd seen, patterned on his brown eyes like rings on a tree trunk, were his parents’ anxious faces and a canopy of paper leaves. This had been his room since the day he was born, as much as it was Agatha’s, and he didn’t know any better than to act like it.

She turned just in time to see him walk himself confidently to the hooks by the door, and reach into his backpack. He pulled out his pair of shorts. 

“Nicky,” she said, her hands still tangled in Kara’s hair. “Not now.”

“Yes now,” he yelled, and decisively, he pulled down his orange sweatpants. 

The room erupted into laughter. Nicky froze, blinking like he’d miscalculated something fundamental. Kara wailed louder. Agatha felt her sanity begin to slip. And then, of course, the door opened. Wanda stood there, taking in the scene before her, her mouth hanging slightly open. 

“Principal Max,” a few students shouted, waving excitedly. They always treated Wanda like some kind of celebrity, and usually, Agatha found it hilarious, but right now, she wanted to throw a stapler at her head for choosing this exact moment to enter.

“Hey, everyone! Hi Nicky,” Wanda said, glancing down at him with an amused smile. 

“I’m hot, Aunt Wanda,” he announced, holding up his shorts.

“Yeah, not a great time, Principal Max,” Agatha said through her teeth, her hands still tangled in Kara’s hair. “Students, can anyone quietly raise their hand, and tell Principal Max our school mascot?”

“An owl!”

“A fairy!”

“A TURTLE!” 

They were genuinely screaming now, and Agatha stood up, abandoning the tape. 

Wanda was unfazed. “I wanted to introduce you to your student teacher—”

“A giraffe!”

“A human!”

Agatha glanced at the kids, counting down from five to no avail, and out of the corner of her eye, saw a figure move into the room behind Wanda. Agatha’s eyes fell on the woman’s face. Her very familiar face. Why did she look so familiar?

“Octopus,” Oliver yelled. “No, a monster!”

Nicky pulled on his shorts, finally, and turned to face the class, hands over his ears.

“It’s a river otter, you fucking idiots!”

The room fell silent at the pitch of his yell. Agatha caught the stifled laugh on Wanda’s face, but she couldn’t focus enough to deal with Nicky. Her eyes were still on the woman standing behind her. 

For five impossibly long seconds, Agatha‘s scanned her face: the dark eyes, the line of her jaw. She knew this woman, she realized faintly. She felt her face and her posture like a word on the tip of her tongue. 

Then, with a deafening, shuddering shift, she remembered. Her tongue. Tucked in the side of her cheek. The press of knees into her thighs. Her own fingernails leaving half moons dented in her palms. Lights, lace, high heels pressed against her—pink, black, green—

The world narrowed, her vision tunneling. Blood roared in her ears, swallowing the crescendo of kid-yells. No. No, no, no. Not her. Please, God, anyone but her. She gripped the edge of the desk behind her to keep herself upright. 

Agatha blinked at Wanda, trying and failing to focus.

“You didn’t forget already, did you?” Wanda muttered, eyeing her strangely, and then turned to the class. “Students, this is…?” She trailed off, looking at the woman expectantly.

“Hi everyone. I’m Ms. V.”

She looked at Agatha, and extended a hand. “Rio.”

Agatha couldn’t move. It was her, she was sure of it—the dancer from the club on the last day of school. A stripper was standing in her classroom, addressing her students. A stripper who, until this moment, had been filed neatly into the category of things Agatha would never think about again. (Except that one time, when she got too drunk on the Fourth of July. And that other time, when she got too drunk on the Seventh of July, which was just a normal Tuesday.) And yet here she was, in practical jeans and a black tee, looking every inch like a competent professional.

Agatha was supposed to teach alongside this woman. A woman who had, not three months ago, given her a lap dance that, according to her brain’s treacherous, seductive little whisper, had been nothing short of fucking incredible.

Agatha shoved that thought aside with brute force, and shook Rio’s hand in a daze, her arm acting separately from her body.

“You’re right,” Wanda whispered to Nicky, taking mercy on Agatha. “River otters. Even though there were some other words in there you probably shouldn’t have said, right?”

“Sorry,” Nicky mumbled, his eyes avoiding Agatha’s with surprising skill as he walked back to his seat.

“How can I help?” Rio asked, her voice startlingly quiet. Her gaze flicked between Agatha and the chaos in the room, and she set her bag down behind Agatha’s desk as if it already belonged there.

“I don’t—” The words caught her throat. She saw Wanda’s raised eyebrows and felt the weight of her silent get it together glare. Agatha cleared her throat. “Take Kara to the nurse and get the tape out of her hair,” she said, her eyes fixed on the back wall.

“Got it.” Rio moved without hesitation. She crouched beside Kara, speaking to her in a low, soothing voice. Somehow, impossibly, the tears stopped. Agatha watched in stunned silence as Kara’s hand slipped into Rio’s, and the two of them walked effortlessly out the door.

Wanda lingered for a moment, her expression inscrutable. Then she closed the door behind her, leaving Agatha alone in a room full of second graders who, miraculously, were silent.

Agatha gripped the edge of her desk again and took a breath. She resisted the urge to run to her car just to hyperventilate in private. The last time she’d felt this close to a full-blown panic at work was seven years ago, sitting in the same green-tiled bathroom that still stood down the hall, staring at two pink lines on a plastic stick. But twenty-seven pairs of eyes were watching her, waiting to hear about river otters. So she exhaled, pushed Rio to the outside the edges of her mind, and clicked to the next slide.

 

 

Rio returned at the tail end of lunch. Agatha watched as Kara, now Jolly-Rancher-equipped and all smiles, gave Rio a hug in the hallway. Agatha braced herself as Rio approached, the two of them finally alone under the paper tree canopy. She had done such an effective job of pushing the thought of Rio out of her mind, it was like she was recognizing her all over again, the features of her face coming into focus. She looked ordinary, her hair tied back and face bare of makeup. But her eyes—they were the same, weren’t they? They felt the same. And despite the simplicity of her look, there was still something striking about her. That thing Agatha didn’t have a name for that made it so difficult to look away.

“Uh, she’s fine,” Rio said, gesturing toward the door where Kara had disappeared. “She took a while to calm down, but the nurse cut the tape out. She’ll never know the difference—that kid has amazing hair.” She smiled, and Agatha felt something in her stomach twist in cold realization: Rio didn’t recognize her.

The relief was immediate and overwhelming. But beneath it, she felt a tiny pinprick of disappointment. She shook the thought away, embarrassed at herself.

“Is everything okay?” Rio asked, her brow furrowing as she stepped closer to the desk. “Look, I know this was a last-minute reassignment, and I know you’re extremely busy. But I’m looking forward to working with you, and I know my way around a school—I promise, I will make this as easy as possible.”

Agatha forced herself to speak, her throat feeling dry. “No,” she said. “I mean, yes. Yes, everything is fine. It’s fine. I’m sure this will be…good.”

“Thank you,” Rio said. She seemed unbothered by Agatha’s incoherence, and shoved her hands in her pockets. “I emailed you my proposed teaching schedule. If we can go over it tomorrow, I would appreciate your input.”

“Oh,” Agatha said, impressed even above the wave of panic. “Yes, sure.”

Rio smiled again, and took her first real look around the classroom. She wandered slowly, fingertips grazing paper vines and skimming book spines. She stopped at the reading corner, her eyes scanning the felt animals. “Did you do all of this yourself?” she asked, her hand resting on the trunk of the paper tree. 

“Yes,” Agatha said again, unable to keep the pride from creeping into the single word.

“Wow, Agatha. This classroom is amazing.”

Rio looked at her with genuine admiration, and Agatha felt startled by the casual ease with which she'd addressed her. Usually the student teachers were younger, more uncertain. Scared of her, even.

“What?” Rio said, arching a brow. “You’re not one of those teachers who makes adults call them by their teacher name, are you? Or am I supposed to call you 'Ms. Harkness,' like a seven-year-old?”

Agatha let out an involuntary laugh, and she smoothed a hand over her hair in an attempt to gather her bearings. Any of her bearings. Where the fuck were her bearings?

“God, no,” Agatha said. “Of course not.”

“I figured.” 

Agatha watched, her head still spinning, as Rio’s hands brushed and inspected and danced over the objects in her classroom. For a minute, Agatha tried to convince herself that maybe this wasn’t the same woman she had met that night. Maybe this was someone else, maybe her mind was playing tricks on her after a long, brutal summer. But the way Rio’s hips swayed, the subtle roll of her shoulders, the confidence threaded through her every motion were unmistakable. This was the dancer: Reaper, Lady Death, River of Life. Rio.

She thought about the signposts that lined the rest of her summer. The grout between the shower tiles, the steady drip of the bathroom faucet, and the rest of the conspicuous cracks in her house that seemed to suddenly emerge once it was empty. She remembered the nagging feeling that she could hide in one of the corners, folding into nothing, and the world would just move on. 

Maybe that’s why she couldn't let it go, couldn’t stop feeling the barbs catch on an old, familiar scar. The answer couldn’t be something as simple as a fallible memory. The apparent truth was much worse: that even when she looked into Josh’s face and felt nothing, even on that sidewalk, bathed in a neon glow, she was the one who'd been forgettable all along. Maybe that’s why, when Rio finally turned to face her, Agatha braced her hand on the desk and said, incredulous: “You don’t remember me?”

The bell rang, punctuating her question, and silence followed. For a split second, Rio’s confusion deepened into something closer to dread, and Agatha felt the faintest, most shameful flicker of satisfaction at seeing her own panic reflected back at her. But there was no spark of recognition. Rio frowned and turned to help the first student with the clasps on her lunchbox, smoothing invisible flyaways at her temples.

Little bodies spilled into the room, laughter ringing loudly. Rio slipped into the background, and Agatha stepped into the center of it all, steadying her hands. Right now, there was only teaching. But as soon as the bell rang, the final toll of her twentieth Doomsday, she was going to find Wanda.

 

 

Agatha gave Nicky one last hug in the parking lot, long enough to draw a deep, grounding breath but short enough not to let her mind wander to Josh. Then she turned to Jen and hugged her, too, knocking her baseball cap askew. Her earrings swung wildly from the force of the attack.

Jen had been Agatha’s student in her very first year of teaching. Even once she went to college, she'd come back to volunteer at every school event, bringing Agatha coffee and Bugles, their shared guilty pleasure. Now twenty-seven, she was the youngest assistant principal in the school’s history. 

Jen had once driven three miles out of her way to return a kid’s single lost mitten, complaining all the while, and she'd babysat Nicky since he was in diapers. If there was anyone Agatha trusted to hand Nicky off to Josh, and spare herself the fresh hell of doing it in front of a crowd, it was Jen.

“Everything good, H?” Jen eyed Agatha. “Haven’t heard from you in a while.” 

Agatha had told Jen years ago to start calling her by her first name, but Jen could never stomach making the switch, even at twenty-seven. The most she could manage was dropping the “Mrs.”

“Yup, all good,” Agatha replied, counting the kids for the fourth time. 

“You sure?” 

Agatha sighed. “She told you, didn’t she?”

“Told me? I was at the meeting.”

The school board meeting. Not her marriage, although Jen presumably knew about that too. She'd always had a frustrating way of knowing everything, even at age seven. 

“Right. Sorry. I was a little…” Agatha trailed off, searching for the right word.

“Crazy-town?” Jen offered. 

“Good to see you, J,” Agatha said instead of answering, and made her brisk escape.

 

 

She reached the front office in record time, and Sharon greeted her with an exaggerated gasp of delight.

“There she is!” She stood from her desk to envelop Agatha in a cloud of rose perfume. “I was wondering when I’d get to see you.”

She leaned back, her smile faltering. “Oh, honey. You look awful.”

Agatha forced herself to smile. “Hi, Sharon. Love the honesty, as always. I really need to talk to her. It’s an emergency.”

Sharon’s expression softened with the practiced concern of someone who handled a daily barrage of ‘emergencies.’ Agatha liked to think hers always took priority, but Sharon was unfortunately great at her job, so she was never sure.

“Oh, my. Go ahead; she’s not in right now, but I’ll let her know you’re here.”

“Thanks,” Agatha muttered, slipping into the cool interior of Wanda’s office. The room was dark, a reprieve from the late-afternoon sun streaming through the rest of the building. Agatha paced its length, listening to her own rhythmic thuds against the carpet. She felt that if she sat down, even for a second, she would sink so deeply into the chair, the night custodian would have to dig her out. She didn’t know how long she’d been pacing when the door creaked open, admitting two streaks of movement, followed by Wanda, a hand to her own head.

“Hey, boys,” Agatha said, fist bumping Billy and Tommy. 

“Hey, Agatha,” they said in unison. Wanda collapsed in her chair as Billy and Tommy tossed a plastic ball at each other.

“How’s fourth grade treating you?” Agatha asked. 

“Too slow.” Tommy whipped the ball across the desk, and Wanda shielded her face, grimacing.

“It’s okay,” Billy added diplomatically, bending to retrieve where it had fallen.

Wanda sighed. “Boys, you can head home. Remember to stay in the bike lane, okay?”

Tommy took off first. Billy, a little slower, gave Agatha a quick wave, and she winked at him. 

“Wear your helmets!” Wanda called after them.

The door clicked shut to silence, and Wanda slumped even further in her chair.

Agatha looked at her. “Hey, why is it that my kid calls you ‘Aunt Wanda,’ and your kids just call me 'Agatha?'”

“Because your son is civilized,” Wanda said. “Why, do you want them to call you ‘Aunt Agatha?’”

“Fuck, no. I’m not one hundred years old.”

“Glad that’s settled.” Wanda put her head in her hand. “Please don’t tell me that I came all the way here just for you to ask me that.”

“Rough day?” Agatha asked with a smirk.

“Doomsday? Never. Today is when parents are famously their most relaxed and gracious." 

Agatha took a deep breath and squared her shoulders. “Listen, I can’t do this student teacher thing.”

Wanda groaned. “It’s been half a day! You’re not even going to try? Your job literally depends on it.”

“I’m sure she’s... skilled,” Agatha said, wincing at her own words. “I mean, I’m sure she’s good. At teaching. And… things. Look, I’m just saying I can’t do this with her.”

Wanda’s arms flung out in exasperation. “If she’s good, then why can’t you do it?”

“Because she’s...” Agatha’s mouth opened and closed like a fish out of water, completely at a loss.

Because she’s a stripper. Which I know because, on the last day of school, I accidentally ended up at a strip club in Boston with my husband—who, by the way, is not my husband anymore. And he paid her to ride me against a wooden bench in a manner that would definitely not be shown on network TV. And by the way, she has a great tattoo of the Tree of Life—not in a biblical way, though—which isn’t important, except I can’t stop thinking about it. Also, the way her hips move, and those goddamn eyes. And I can’t do this because this is my place of work, at least for a little while longer, barring any major catastrophes. And this is one big monumental fucking catastrophe waiting to happen.

“It’s just... fast," she said instead. "I mean, what do we even know about her, really?”

Wanda gave her a long, piercing look. “‘What do we know about her?’ That’s what you’ve got?” Her eyes narrowed, and she tilted her head. “You’ve had five student teachers, two since I’ve been here. Both turned out fine, and, if I recall, you gave them glowing recommendations." 

Agatha shifted her weight from foot to foot, her eyes landing on the cork board above Wanda’s head. A handful of red push pins formed a crude smiley face. She locked onto it stubbornly, the sudden, acute sensation washing over her that she was a fifth-grader, holding court in front of Principal Max.

“Am I missing something here?” Wanda pressed. “Is it because she’s thirty and not twenty-two?”

“God, of course not,” Agatha said quickly. “That’s way better. She knows what she’s doing. She’s confident. She’s not afraid to advocate for herself.”

“Then I’m lost,” Wanda said, scrubbing a hand over her face before leaning both elbows against the desk. She leveled Agatha with a look. “From where I’m sitting, it sounds like she made a good first impression.”

Agatha bit her lip so hard she almost drew blood. The truth was on the tip of her tongue, just shy of tumbling out. She took in the shadows under Wanda's eyes, her sleeves rolled up to her elbows. She thought about the hoops Wanda had jumped through for her—the ones she knew about and the ones she didn’t. For the first time all day, Agatha felt something poke through the thick haze of her own panic. She couldn’t drop this on Wanda, not after this summer. Not on Doomsday. Agatha let out a slow, steady breath.

“Like you said. This summer was a fucking disaster.”

“I don’t think I said ‘disaster.” Wanda's lips curved lightly, her head resting in her hand.

“I read between the lines."

Agatha kept her gaze steady on the red push-pin smiley. It stared back at her, dead in the eyes. She heard the click of her own jaw as she clenched and released.

“One semester?” she said finally, looking back at her friend.

Wanda watched her carefully. “One semester.”

Agatha tapped her foot, then gave a single, sharp nod. “Fine.”

“Oh, thank god,” Wanda groaned, throwing her arms up in triumph. “It’s a miracle!”

“Shut up,” came Agatha’s grumbled reply.

 

 

Agatha stepped outside, and stopped short. The parking lot had mostly emptied, the asphalt shimmering faintly in the late afternoon sun. But there was Nicky, his small hand tucked securely in Jen’s. And on his other side stood Rio. Agatha had been so hellbent on talking to Wanda about her, she'd forgotten to tell her she could leave.

Agatha looked at her watch, a familiar irritation flaring, and she walked towards them with purpose. Josh’s car rolled up just as she reached them, and she didn’t stop until she was leaning into the open window, the air conditioning and the sharp scent of his cologne slamming her in the face. 

“I told you three o’clock,” she said, skipping the formalities. “It’s three-thirty; where the fuck have you been?”

"I know, I know,” Josh said. “I’m sorry I’m late; I got caught up at work, and my boss wanted—”

Agatha leaned farther in, lowering her voice. “I don’t care if your boss wanted you to chop down a tree and fuck him with it. When I say three o’clock, you need to be here to get him at three o’clock.”

“Wow. That’s…descriptive." Josh rubbed at his face. “You really want to do this here? The whole, ‘I’m perfect, you’re an idiot’ routine? It’s getting a little redundant.”

“Not as redundant as blasting the AC with the windows down.” She held his gaze, letting the contempt settle between them until a purple backpack bobbed in her periphery.

She folded her arms and stepped back as Josh waved to Nicky, smiling at him with a gentleness that burned. Whatever Josh was—an infuriating man, a mediocre husband—he’d always been a good father. She never managed the ability to twist that fact, no matter how angry she was, which, of course, only made her angrier.

“Bye, Mama!” Nicky called from the backseat, waving eagerly out the window.

“I love you, baby.” Her smile felt bright and brittle. “I’ll see you tomorrow.”

She blew him a kiss, and fought hard against the lump in her throat, willing the tears not come. She felt the panic rise as Nicky grew smaller in the distance through the sedan window. That deep, inexplicable instinct kicked in, screaming at her to sprint across the parking lot, yank the door open, and clutch him to her chest.

She stood there instead, shallow-breathing with her knees locked. The cars started to blur. Her thumb rubbed hard against her ring finger at the spot her wedding band used to sit. She hadn’t yet lost the habit. She mused that maybe she'd rub the skin on her ring finger and her feet would fuse to the hot asphalt until the car came back in the morning. 

She felt Rio and Jen behind her, silent, and forced herself to take one step, then another. She kept her eyes firmly on the ground as she walked to her car, slamming the door. She curled her fingers above the leather, nails pressing into her closed fist before unclenching slowly. She traced over the faint indents with her fingertips and wondered if anyone else had half-moons on their palms instead of laugh lines.

Usually, she welcomed the quiet, a much needed reprieve after the sheer noise of the school day. But today, it felt empty—her car, and before that, her ring finger, and before even that, her TV remote. Her forehead fell to the steering wheel, her glasses digging into the bridge of her nose. Here she was, forty-two years old and wearing a twelve year-old t-shirt on the first day of school. Disgraced by her marriage, nearly disgraced by her job, and bookending the summer with an empty back seat. She squeezed her eyes shut against the prick of tears.

A latch clicked suddenly in the silence. She heard the the pull of metal, and felt the slight dip of the car. Her shoulders jerked upright, and her hand slammed against the horn automatically.

“Hey!” Rio yelled over the blare, settled comfortably in the back seat.

Agatha yanked her hand off the wheel, twisting around. She stared.

“What the fuck—” 

“All the kids are gone,” Rio said smoothly. She gestured toward the school. “Am I going to be doing that every day? Waiting with the last kid?”

Agatha blinked at her, frustration coiling at Rio’s easy invasion of her space. In one day—or two days, two months apart—Rio had wandered into the wreckage of her marriage and her career. She shook hands, and smiled, and swung her hips as she trampled around like the ruins of Agatha’s life were a tourist attraction. 

“No,” Agatha said emphatically. 

“I mean, I’m more than happy to,” Rio said. “But Jen told me it actually wasn’t my job to wait there at all.” She crossed her arms, and Agatha took in the sheer audacity of this woman, arrogant and indignant in her back seat. 

“Just so you know,” Rio said, “a little suburban hazing isn’t going to throw me. I may be a student teacher, but I’ve been working with kids since I was one, and let’s just say, I’ve seen worse.”

“I wasn’t…” Agatha stopped herself. She wasn’t going to do this. 

“You know what, yeah. I was hazing you. It builds character.”

She faced forward again, staring at the sparse shrubbery in front of her parking spot. She tried not to cave, tracing the bare branches of a dying bush with her eyes, but her gaze drifted inevitably back to the rearview mirror. 

Rio was looking at her. And just like that, she was back in the dark, music pulsing, Rio’s gaze burning through her from across the club. The sudden force of the memory made her fingers curl tighter around the wheel. 

“Was there something else?” she asked expectantly, holding her voice even despite the threading of her pulse. Rio looked at her hand, picking absently at her cuticle. 

“Earlier,” she said slowly, “you asked if I remembered you. Have we met?”

Agatha forced a laugh that she hoped with all of her soul was convincing. 

“Oh, no, I don’t think so,” she said quickly. “You know what it was: I thought I recognized you from the grocery store, last week, but I realized that was a totally different woman I was talking to about the, the new…organic mayonnaise.”

Organic. Fucking. Mayonnaise. Jesus Christ. She clamped her teeth shut, and Rio nodded, lips twitching.

“You talk to a lot of women in grocery stores about condiments?” 

Agatha felt instantly caught somewhere between mortification, and worse, the urge to laugh.

“I try not to make it a habit." She adjusted her glasses, hiding, as she often did, behind her ‘polite suburban mother’ persona. Far from the truth, but convenient at times, like when she ‘accidentally’ double-booked her dentist appointments with PTA meetings. Or when a stripper was sitting in her back seat.

Rio scanned the car, her gaze landing on Nicky’s booster seat beside her. She didn’t mention it, and looked back at Agatha instead. Agatha felt the pit of dread in her stomach creep slowly up her spine. Rio smoothed her fingers over her temple, lingering too long. Agatha noticed it again, the fleeting expression of stress.

“You know me from the club,” Rio said simply.

Agatha felt her lungs deflate. The heat bloomed on her cheeks, the light spotting in front of her eyes. She could still deny it—it would be easy, however unconvincing.

But still, still, an insane little part of her was annoyed. How many people must Rio have seen between then and now for her to have no memory of Agatha at all? How many people each night? How many tragically married, middle-aged women who had come to drunken, hazy revelations underneath her in that curtained pink room?

“Look, I normally wouldn’t say anything,” Rio continued, taking Agatha’s silence as the confirmation it was. “I’m not trying to pull anything crazy, but you’re friends with the principal. So, does she…did you tell her?” Her tone was conversational, but her knee bounced steadily. Agatha watched it, and shook her head once. 

“Are you going to?” Rio asked.

Agatha looked at the open question on her face.

“I haven’t decided yet,” she said finally. “Doesn’t this seem a little…I don’t know…”

“Inappropriate?” Rio finished, looking at her pointedly. “Unbecoming? Shameful? Go on.”

Agatha was silent. 

“I’m not, like, dancing for kids; I’m not a criminal,” Rio said, rolling her eyes. “I need a supplemental source of income. I was a caseworker, and now I’m a grad student, and those two things aren’t exactly known for great benefits and a living wage.”

“Strippers get a lot of benefits, then?” Agatha shot back automatically.

Rio’s lips twitched again. “Only ones that sue the clubs they work at, and who has the time?” She paused, and then added, “And we prefer being called dancers.”

Agatha scoffed. “What’s the difference?”

“Well for one,” Rio smirked, “I start my shift with my clothes already off.”

Agatha blinked, trying with every fiber of her being to keep her face neutral.

“Okay, then why teach at all?” she said. “Why not just be a… dancer full time and make money?”

Rio looked out the window, scrunching her nose, and took in the deserted playground. She glanced back at Agatha almost sheepishly.

“Oh, don’t fucking say it,” Agatha said, holding up a hand.

“I love it,” Rio shrugged. “I’ve always loved it. Kids like me, and they’ve always made more sense to me than adults. I never really get tired of them.”

“Never?” 

“Okay. Maybe not never,” she admitted. “But I kind of thrive off of the chaos.”

Agatha was starting to lose the thread of why she was so hellbent on making Rio sweat. Truthfully, she felt the exact same way about kids—they never seemed to turn on her the way adults did.

Agatha leaned her head back and closed her eyes for a second, taking a deep breath. Maybe this would be fine. If Rio didn’t remember her, then they could just pretend it never happened, and start fresh. Agatha could play nice, for the sake of her career, and as a favor to Wanda. 

“Okay,” she said quietly.

“If you tell Wanda, I'll be reassigned and—wait, what?” Rio looked at her, surprised.

“Okay,” Agatha repeated. “It’s your second job; it’s none of my business. I could use a little help this year from someone who knows what they’re doing. And you…seem to. And anyway, it’s not like…I mean, I just saw you from across the room when I was there with my husband, and that’s why I recognized you. It doesn’t have to be, like, this big thing. People meet in passing all the time.”

Rio smiled to herself, looking down for a second, and then out the window again. She still looked a little surprised.

“I did mean what I said.” She caught Agatha's eye. “I am excited to be working with you. I can see that you’re a really good teacher.”

“You saw me teach for like, one hour.”

“I talked to some older kids at pickup,” Rio smiled. “You know, they were just generally up in my business, wanting to know who the fuck I was. And when I said I was teaching with you—holy shit, it was like a crowd of raving fans. Fans that all had something incredibly personal that they wanted me to tell you.”

Agatha couldn’t stop the pull of a smile. 

“Katie Buckers got a haircut last month,” Rio continued, her face perfectly straight. “She made it very clear to pass that along. And Alejandro said he misses you and he wants to marry you.”

Agatha laughed then, the familiar spike of love and pride loosening the tension in her neck. She narrowed her eyes.

“Did you collect flattering testimony in case this conversation went a different way?”

“In general,” Rio grinned, “I aim to please.”

The heat rose up Agatha’s throat, drying out her tongue and wiping the smile from her face. Fuck, what was she thinking? Of course she couldn’t forget about it, and of course this was just such a bad, terrible, horrible idea. She swallowed.

“Can you get out of my car now?” she said, her voice rough to her own ears. “You can’t just get into a stranger’s back seat like a serial killer.”

Rio stared at her for a second, and then, decisively, she leaned forward. Agatha could feel her breath on the nape of her neck, tickling the wisps that had escaped from her ponytail through the day. She stayed firmly facing forward, her fingers still wrapped tightly around the wheel.

“We’re not strangers,” Rio said smoothly, her voice low and matter-of-fact. “It was a Friday night in June. You didn’t have glasses, and your hair was down. You were wearing a black jacket, a hideous shirt, and brown pants, which means you hate yourself a little, or you don’t know that you’re too hot to be dressing like an actuary from the eighties, or you didn’t know you were coming to a strip club. Or maybe all three. Your husband paid me fifty bucks, I gave you a lap dance, and you liked it.”

She leaned back, holding Agatha's eye for a beat. Then she was gone, the car door slamming Agatha into stillness. And once again, she sat there in Rio’s abrupt absence, this time without the haze of tequila, and felt the afterimage of Lady Death’s breath against her ear.

 

 

 

Chapter End Notes

i just...want to watch this on TV...is that too much to ask... 😩

Back to School Night

Chapter Summary

in which Agatha still can't catch a fucking break

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

 

“We like to think that we are rational beings; humane, conscientious, civilized, thoughtful. But when things fall apart, even just a little, it becomes clear we are not better than animals. We have opposable thumbs, we think, we walk erect, we speak, we dream, but deep down we are still routing around in the primordial ooze; biting, clawing, scratching out an existence in the cold, dark world like the rest of the tree-toads and sloths.”

-Meredith Grey, Grey’s Anatomy

 

 

The tree behind Agatha’s classroom stretched over the picnic table, the surface dotted with the first few fallen maple leaves of September. Mornings had started to chill, dew settling in the grass, her coffee already starting to cool. The plastic lid of her travel mug was cracked, from when she'd dropped it in the parking lot last year. She kept forgetting to buy a new one, so eventually, she'd resigned herself to lukewarm coffee.

She tapped her pen against the stack of worksheets in front of her. It was still early, students likely still asleep in their beds, parents just barely stirring. Agatha loved being here before everyone else, drinking in the quiet, still air before it became unrecognizable with commotion.

A rustle startled her, and she groaned at the sight of Wanda, rocking on her heels five feet away.

“Nice,” Wanda said. “Not really how friends love to be greeted.”

“I know that look. You’re about to ruin my morning with some bullshit.”

Wanda sat on the bench opposite, her own coffee steaming in her hand. “It’s very possible. How are you feeling about tonight?”

“I was trying not to think about it,” Agatha muttered. “And I was doing pretty well, until you came to my secret spot and forced me to think about it.”

“Really?” Wanda eyed her pointedly. “You curled your hair. You’re wearing a dress and a push-up bra.”

“Settle down.” Agatha rolled her eyes, but her cheeks felt warm. It was a specific flavor of humiliation, being caught caring about her self-image.

It was Back to School Night, and truthfully, she'd been feeling a rather-set-herself-on-fire-and-burn-at-the-stake level of dread. 

“You look good; it’s a good thing,” Wanda said knowingly. “How’s Rio?” 

Wanda kicked her feet idly under the table, and Agatha smiled at the motion, clocking it as another automatic movement that adults seemed to remember in their grown-up bodies.

“Fine,” Agatha said. “Good. I’m letting her do some of the presentation tonight, so she’s probably off rehearsing. You know how they get.”

“Does she even know what she’s in for? All the Agatha lore?”

Agatha huffed but didn’t look up from her papers.

“You should really tell her what she’s walking into,” Wanda said, “before she’s blindsided by a room full of foaming-at-the-mouth parents.”

“Is that why you came out here?” Agatha put down her pen. “You want me to tell Rio every humiliating detail of the worst night of my career?”

“As your friend, I think you’re being paranoid. But as your principal, that particular can of worms is none of my business.”

“Great,” Agatha said flatly. “Good chat.”

“You’re giving the closing remarks tonight.”

Agatha’s jaw dropped. “You’re kidding. I'm the last person who should be doing that right now.”

“You’ve done it before,” Wanda said, unfazed. “And Ralph’s coming.”

“Ah. It all comes down to a Bohner.”

“Shut up,” Wanda said with a laugh. “He’s got it out for you, and he’s doing his best to make sure this is your last Back-to-School Night.”

Agatha rolled her eyes. “Doesn’t he have anything better to do than witch-hunt me?”

“You have a real talent for motivating that man.”

Agatha watched as Wanda tilted her head back, taking in the light filtering through the leaves. She closed her eyes for a moment, and Agatha felt an unexpected wave of affection. She remembered what it had been like before Wanda. Paul, the former principal, had left Oyster Bend six years ago for a position at the district office. He'd known Agatha since grad school, where he was a fourth-year PhD student, and her TA, which Agatha found hysterical. Even years ahead and on a PhD track, Paul had never quite been able to keep up with her, a first-year master’s student.

He'd asked her to dinner on three separate occasions, which she was somewhat sure would never be allowed now, but she hadn't minded so much at the time. Paul was funny and had a nice smile, and she'd actually liked him. But she'd liked Josh better, so she'd said no, and he'd backed off.

But friendly as he was, he was a mediocre leader, more concerned with keeping up appearances than keeping the school running. If Ralph wanted something, Paul handed it to him without question. If the PTA batted their lashes, he folded. Agatha had learned early that if anything was going to get done, she would have to do it herself. Those pre-Wanda years felt cold and lonely to think about now.

Wanda sighed. “I don’t think you realize how mad people are.”

Agatha snorted. “Oh, I realize.”

“No. You don’t.” Wanda’s voice was quieter now. “I’m trying to shield you from it as much as I can. And yeah, a lot of them are fucking crazy—don’t quote me on that—but crazy or not, they can still do some damage.”

Agatha scoffed. “Since when does ‘crazy’ not matter?”

“Since you stood up in a public meeting and told half the parents in this town they were bad at raising their kids.”

Agatha’s mouth snapped shut.

“I get why you said it.” Wanda pressed on. “But you know it wasn’t completely fair. Not all the rich parents are assholes—some of them actually use their money to help this school.”

Agatha chewed at her bottom lip.

“If the board decides to push you out, your students lose an incredible teacher. I lose an incredible teacher.” Wanda’s voice wavered slightly. “And there won’t be a single fucking thing I can do about it.”

“But I just—”

“Please.” Wanda shook her head, her eyes shining now. “Please don’t get yourself fired, and leave me here to find your replacement. I can handle a lot, but I can’t handle that.”

Agatha looked at her for a long moment, then sighed. “Okay. Okay. I’m sorry.” She ran a hand through her hair. “I’ll do the remarks. I’ll… try to smooth things over. And I will avoid Ralph.”

Wanda let out a deep breath, nodding. Agatha reached across the table and squeezed her hand. “I’m not getting fired.”

“Yeah, well, you’ll forgive me if I don’t completely believe that.”

Wanda looked at her out of the corner of her eye, and added, “So you’ll tell Rio?”

“Tell me what?”

Agatha choked on her coffee, and Wanda whipped around. Rio’s head poked out of the back classroom door.

"How long have you been there?" Agatha coughed.

"Who's Ralph?"

Agatha heard Wanda’s barely stifled laughter as she walked away.

“Don’t you have a school to run?” Agatha called after her.

Rio took Wanda’s seat, and Agatha turned her focus back to the papers in front of her.

It had been two weeks since their conversation in Agatha’s car, and she was still at a loss. With every look Rio threw her way, every confident entrance in the morning, every easy conversation with a student, Agatha felt a distinct restlessness take root in her limbs. There was something unmooring about her. It lingered, like a layer of tissue paper stuck to her insides.

Worse, it was starting to feel normal, the way Rio broke the morning quiet. At first, Agatha had just stared, incredulous. How could Rio—a dancer, graceful by trade—be so clunky and distracting in a desk? She would throw the door open, black backpack slung over one shoulder, her nose red from the cold. She’d drop into the seat across from Agatha, sniffling lightly. She would pull her hood off her hair, shake it out, and tie it up. Every morning, without meaning to, Agatha would find herself watching. By now, it felt almost rhythmic. Sit. Sniffle. Hood off. Hair up. Laptop. Coffee. 

Coincidentally, Rio drank from the exact same mug as Agatha’s. But Rio’s was infuriatingly spotless, her lid pristine. Her coffee was probably always hot. It set Agatha’s teeth on edge, starting with the matching mug and ending with the way Rio was so at ease around her, slouched with her fingers tapping idly against the wood. Meanwhile, Agatha sat rigid, every muscle stiff in opposition.

She tried to ignore the way Rio’s mannerisms had started to become familiar. The way she pulled her sweatshirt strings between her teeth when she was thinking. The way she picked at her nails with a furrowed brow. The way she pressed her tongue into the inside of her cheek, something between defiance and amusement. The way her hands and eyes came alive when she talked to the kids. Maybe the most confounding of all—it was impossible to draw a smile from her on purpose. It came when Agatha least expected it, softening, shifting Rio’s face into something entirely different. Like one of those hundred-year-old optical illusions in Nicky’s puzzle books where if you stared long enough, the duck wasn’t a duck anymore. Look away for a second, and when you looked back, it was a rabbit. How many people, over the past hundred years had looked at a duck, only to realize the rabbit had been there all along?

"What?"

Agatha blinked. Rio was watching her.

"Nothing." Agatha shook her hair back and stood, gathering the stack of papers. "Look over these and give me your notes by the end of the day."

"Didn't you already go through these?" Rio asked, eyeing the purple pen scrawled across the top page.

"Yes."

"So this is a test?"

"No," Agatha said, pointedly. "This is your job."

 

 

She felt each hour of the day drag against her temples. Five hours left. Then four. Then one, which was somehow still too many. She tapped her fingers against her desk, restless, unsure if she was trying to will time forward or stop the day from turning to night.

A small hand was raised to her right. Luca looked around the classroom, eyes landing on Rio, then back to Agatha. He rested his chin in his hand, brown eyes wide and serious beneath his messy hair.

“I have to talk to Ms. V,” he whispered.

“Ms. V is a little busy right now.” She looked at Rio, who was flipping through worksheets and clearly listening. Agatha turned stubbornly back to Luca. “I promise I can do my best to answer.”

Luca swayed from side to side, considering, and shook his head. Agatha forced a smile over the quick sting. She beckoned Rio with a short nod of her head, and returned to her desk. The next time she looked up, Rio was settled cross-legged on the carpet, listening intently with her head tipped toward Luca. Agatha's stomach flipped at the sight, and she turned her gaze back to the drag of the wall clock.

 

 

Rio leaned against the door with a sigh, fingers curled loosely around the handle. Agatha recognized the familiar end-of-day exhaustion in her eyes, but something else lingered underneath. Rio leaned for another second, then picked up a stack of papers.

“Can we go through these now?”

Agatha narrowed her eyes. Unease needled at her, and turned quickly to annoyance. Rio was keeping something from her.

“Sure,” Agatha said finally. She strode to the back of the room, settling into her armchair, and crossed her legs under her purple dress.

“Go ahead,” she said, gesturing at the empty rug. Rio paused, tongue in cheek. Then, she plucked a carpet square from the stack and dropped it in front of the chair. She looked Agatha right in the eye, and lowered herself onto the floor with deliberate ease. Agatha had meant to get under her skin, but now Rio was sitting on the floor, looking up at her through her lashes, and Agatha felt a prickle of heat at the base of her throat. She blinked and looked away.

“You need to stop acting so weird around me," Rio said finally. "People are going to notice.”

“What?” Agatha spluttered. “I’m not—I’m acting normal. You're the one that’s acting like you live here, interrupting my mornings every fucking day, sitting down right across from me with nothing to say!"

“If I’m not saying anything, how am I interrupting?” 

“Because you’re—” Agatha stopped, then leaned forward. “Stop looking at me like we have this…big secret.”

Rio smirked. “But we do.”

“Rio.”

Rio groaned. “I’m just doing my job. You need to actually look at me to observe my teaching. What's it going to take for you to get over it? It’s not like I’m going to suddenly forget where I am and climb on top of you in your fucking armchair.”

Agatha’s brain short-circuited. Rio, in her lap. Legs bracketing her thighs, hands bracing the chair. Her fingers curling into the blue fabric, into her hair, into—Jesus fucking Christ.

She grabbed her coffee, desperate for a distraction. The second the bitter taste hit her tongue, she choked, coughing. She spit the sip straight back into the cup.

“That’s mine,” Rio said.

“I gathered.”

Agatha fumed, looking down at the mug she'd just spit into, and then handed it to Rio. “Tonight’s a big night,” she said. “I’m thinking about other things besides you, if you can believe it.”

“Besides me?” Rio grinned. She looked at the mug, and then, her eyes back on Agatha, she took a slow, deliberate sip. It was so obscenely wrong—the way Agatha felt her breathing shallow. She blinked, hard. 

“What did Luca want?”

“I can’t tell you.”

Agatha stared. “What do you mean you can’t tell me?”

“If I tell you right now, you’re going to be distracted tonight.”

Agatha stared, and Rio angled her body away from Agatha’s gaze.

“We need to talk about those first,” Rio said, nodding to the small posters lining the bookshelf. “Are you keeping them up tonight?”

“Since when do you care about my taste in classroom decor?”

“Since I noticed that.” Rio pointed at a laminated quote. "‘Somewhere inside all of us is the power to change the world.’ Roald Dahl. You know he was, like, wildly racist and sexist, right?"

Agatha froze. “What? No. Was he?”

“That’s kind of his thing."

Agatha gaped at her, then at the quote. “His thing is James and the Giant Peach, not racism, or everyone would fucking know that, including me.”

Rio shrugged. “He also claimed women were biologically inferior to men, which I personally disagree with, but you know, it’s your classroom—”

Agatha was already at the bookcase, yanking the quote down. She crumpled it and threw it in the trash.

“How long were you going to let me leave that up there?” 

“I just saw it today,” Rio said with a grin. “You've got to do your research.”

“Oh, please. Like you didn’t love his books before you knew he was a raging idiot. By all means, come up with a replacement.”

“How about, ‘I had some help in becoming who I am?’” Rio said theatrically, her arm raised in front of her. A piece of paper fluttered out of the folder in her hand. She reached for it, and Agatha caught the shift in her smile.

“Tell me about Luca,” Agatha said, her curiosity piquing again.

Rio looked at her. She hesitated for a beat, and then held out the yellow note in acquiescence. “He has a crush."

Agatha looked at the shakily drawn heart and laughed, relieved. “That’s why he didn’t want to talk to me; it happens every year. I’m a notorious heart-breaker in the second grade.”

Rio smiled. “Not on you. On Nicky.”

Agatha blinked. “Oh. Wow, okay. I don’t think Nicky's had any crushes yet, because he can barely dress himself. So I don’t think they’re a relationship in the making, but I still don’t see the problem.”

Rio’s shoulders relaxed, and she grinned. “Hey, I had my first crush when I was five. Mrs. Archambeau. I was the only one that could say her name, so she gave me a ladybug sticker, and that sealed the deal. Nice, hot, and definitely the head bitch in charge.”

Agatha snorted. “So that would be your type, then?” 

They both seemed to realize what she said at the same time, and Agatha felt the acute need to sink into the chair. A flicker of surprise flashed across Rio’s face, and Agatha wasn’t sure if she was imagining it, but she could swear she saw a tinge of pink across her cheeks.

“I’m just saying,” Rio continued, conspicuously ignoring the question, “that Nicky could have a crush. He might not, but it’s not really a blanket age thing.”

“Fair enough,” Agatha said, clearing her throat, and then paused as another thought occurred to her. “Wait, you didn’t think I was going to be…I don’t know, some raging homophobe, did you?”

Rio laughed at that, the sound tumbling out of her. She was still sitting cross-legged on the carpet.

“You never know,” she shrugged and then added, “When we met, you didn’t seem very… comfortable.”

“Jesus,” Agatha hissed, glancing automatically toward the door. “Just because I’d never been to a fucking strip club doesn’t mean I’m a bigot. I live in the suburbs, not the middle ages.”

“Okay,” Rio said, arms raised in surrender, “point taken, and for the record, I’m glad.”

She paused, fiddling with the edge of the carpet square. “Luca told me some kids were bothering him about it at lunch today. He wouldn’t tell me their names, but he said he'd tell me if it happened again, so I was going to keep an eye out tomorrow. I wanted to wait and check it out, just in case you…” She stopped herself, and rubbed the back of her neck, confirming what Agatha had suspected. “Just in case.”

Agatha’s heart lurched. There it was, the reason for Rio’s sad smile.

“I know,” Rio said, eyeing her face. “So do we tell his parents tonight, or wait until tomorrow?” 

"Wait."

Rio looked at her curiously. "Why?"

Agatha didn’t answer. She sat back in her chair, fingers fidgeting against the armrests, eyes fixed back on the clock.
