Agatha sent Rio home for a couple hours, and when she came back, Agatha was notably impressed. She'd changed into a navy blue suit, her pants tapered under a crisp blazer. Her white shirt was buttoned up to the collar, and her hair fell in loose waves over her shoulder. Agatha hadn’t seen her wear makeup since the night at the club, and she stared at the bold eyeliner and touch of pink.

“Nice,” she said finally.

“Thanks.” Rio looked at Agatha’s purple dress, the loose top with the long sleeves and pencil skirt that fell just above the knee. “You too.” 

“I’ve been wearing this all day.”

“I know."

Agatha felt the tug of a smile and bit it back, turning to organize the papers on her desk that were already in neat piles. Rio turned away as the first parents arrived, and Agatha forced herself to follow suit. The room filled gradually, some greeting her warmly, others more stiffly. They looked up at her classroom, just like their kids, at the leaves and the tree and the vines. Fewer of their faces lit up. It was much less satisfying to watch.

 

 

The predictability of her presentation was comforting—first-time mothers scribbling notes, repressed fathers shifting at the mention of social-emotional learning, PTA moms exchanging conspiratorial glances.

“This year, I’m thrilled to introduce my student teacher, who will be co-teaching this semester,” Agatha said, gesturing to Rio. “I’m going to let her introduce herself."

Agatha sat down at her desk to the polite smattering of applause, eyes shifting sideways to take in Rio's effortless confidence. No nervous shifting, no filler words. At Agatha's first Back-to-School Night, she’d gripped the sides of the clunky projector cart so tightly her knuckles had turned white, but Rio spoke as if stage fright was a foreign concept. Maybe a classroom presentation wasn’t so nerve-wracking in comparison with dancing naked in front of strangers.

Judging by the silence, Rio's calm assurance was contagious. Agatha exhaled, half-impressed, half-relieved. At least whatever it was—that thing, the way Rio drew people in, made them lean forward—was apparently universal.

“My teaching philosophy,” Rio was saying, and Agatha watched her hands as she spoke, “is that students learn best when they’re in a safe, affirming environment. This means leading by example with communication, active listening, and inclusivity of all identities, family structures, and lived experiences.”

The slide behind Rio was simple: a tiny cartoon of a blended family and a small rainbow flag. Still, Agatha felt it coming, like the wind before a heavy rain. She watched the rustle of unease.

A woman raised her hand from the back row, and Agatha’s stomach turned. She recognized the expression—sweet, rehearsed, and laced with just enough plausible deniability to claim innocence. It was the face of someone who ‘just had a quick question,’ that turned into a polite opinion, that turned into a crusade. Agatha opened her mouth to intervene, but Rio called on her first.

“Isn’t all of this, kind of… a lot for second-graders?” she said, looking Rio up and down. “Why do we have to force this stuff on them so early, you know? What does ‘identity’ have to do with math and reading?”

Rio paused, and Agatha ground her teeth together, bracing her hands on the desk to stand.

“Great question,” Rio said, smiling, and Agatha froze. She recognized that expression, too.

"As I mentioned, I’m a student teacher,” she said. “Which means I’m required to stay up to date on the latest education research. Studies show that when students are given structured spaces and trusting adults that affirm their identities—like race, gender, and sexual orientation—they report higher rates of perceived safety at school. And higher perceived safety leads to better overall academic performance.”

The woman blinked, clearly taken aback.

Rio smiled again, tilting her head. “Or, as you put it—‘math and reading.’”

Agatha felt every creak of a desk like a pinprick against her skin. The entire back row was whispering now, heads ducked close, eyes flicking toward Agatha every so often.

Rio continued with unhurried precision, and Agatha forced herself to focus on the presentation, nodding at the appropriate times. All she wanted was for every single parent to get out of her classroom, and it took thirteen excruciating minutes to get her wish.

Agatha and Rio stood on either side of the door as the parents trickled out, until only two remained.

“Josh couldn’t make it?” Oliver’s mother asked. Kara’s mother nudged her, hard, and Agatha pretended not to notice.

“You know, I think we’ve got it covered, since Nicky’s in my class,” she said sweetly.

“That’s right,” the woman replied, matching her tone. “It must be hard for him, with everything going on at home. I don’t know how you do it. How’s he doing?”

“Great.”

Agatha didn’t offer anything else, and they took the hint, walking out with one final look of pity. Their hushed remarks echoed back into the classroom.

“Her husband leaves her, she gets drugged up and loses her mind at the school board meeting, and her new student teacher is a lesbian?”

“Oh, I know. I don’t know if I should call US Weekly or my pastor.”

Their voices faded down the hall, and Rio snorted, shutting the door behind them.

“Don’t do it,” she said, her voice laced with amusement.

Agatha snapped her head toward her. “Don’t do what?”

“Fight them in the parking lot. Not worth it.”

“Josh didn’t leave me. And I was not ‘drugged up’ at that meeting.”

“Well, at least they got mine right,” Rio said, gathering papers off the desks. She widened her eyes, her voice an exaggerated, haunting whisper: “Lesbian. OoOOooh.”

Agatha didn’t laugh. She picked at an octopus sticker a kid had left on the door, scraping at the cheap laminate. It curled under her nail in uneven patches, the sticky residue fused stubbornly to the wood. Too far gone to peel clean, and definitely too far gone to still resemble an octopus. 

“Were you going to tell me about that slide, by the way?” She'd meant it to sound casual, but knew as soon as the words left her mouth that she'd failed.

Rio straightened slowly, turning to face her.

“What slide?” she asked, too measured. 

Agatha ran her tongue over her teeth. “Never call on parents until the last five minutes. That way they can’t derail the whole presentation.”

“Just to be clear,” Rio said, crossing her arms, “you’re telling me I should have handled that woman’s question by… not letting her ask it in the first place?”

“Yes,” Agatha said without hesitation. “And you should have run all of that by me first. I agree with you, but there are buzzwords that are going to cause a whole thing with parents here, and trust me, you don’t want to a target on your back in this school district.”

Rio set the papers down on the desk next to her, too hard.

“This is Oyster Bend, Massachusetts,” she said, her voice low. “I’ve worked in city shelters and group homes where kids are just trying to make it to school in one piece because they live in an epicenter of gun violence. What you have on your back are white-wine-drinking playground bullies—not a target.”

Agatha’s stomach tightened, and Rio came closer.

“So many of these kids have the luxury to think beyond their immediate needs, but that brings a whole different set of issues, doesn't it? Some of these parents have never lived anywhere else. They throw their money and influence at the things that scare them, which includes people like me. But kids like Luca need teachers like me. I needed teachers like me.”

She took a step closer. “And unlike you, I don’t change my mind about what I believe based on who’s watching.”

The bitterness rose quickly in Agatha’s throat at the humiliation of being lectured in her own classroom.

“You think you’re so enlightened,” she snapped, stepping forward, “because you know which authors are canceled and which identity terms will send the suburban moms into a spiral. That woman who tried to derail you with her little microaggression? That was Luca’s mom, by the way.”

Rio’s eyes flickered in recognition.

“You have to know what you can say in front of whom,” Agatha pressed. “Because it’s always going to come back to the kids. What good are you going to do Luca if his mom decides to transfer him out of this class, or this school?”

Rio didn’t answer.

“You pretend to be so cool with everything, so unapologetic,” Agatha continued. “But you still lie about your job.”

"That's different." Rio was completely still now.

Agatha let out a humorless laugh. “Wanda asked me what your bartending hours were yesterday.” She tilted her head. “ A bartender? Nice. You’re a fucking stripper, Rio. So forgive me if I’m having a hard time standing here, taking judgment from you, of all people.”

Rio’s nostrils flared, her mouth parted in rare, visible anger. 

“God, Agatha, you’re no different than them. Those annoying, ignorant, gossiping parents. You hate them. But all you want is their approval."

She swung her arms. “You just switch between whichever Agatha gets you an ounce of control. The depressed, married lady about to fucking jump me after a fifty-dollar dance—”

Agatha’s stomach dropped.

“—or the teacher who tells me off for being unprofessional, when all I did was tell the truth.”

Agatha could feel her nails digging into her palms, the sharp pulse in her throat. Somehow, she couldn't think of a single thing to say.

Rio’s voice softened, which Agatha felt was even worse than the yelling.

“You don’t even know which Agatha you actually are, do you?”

Agatha turned sharply and yanked the door open. She set off at a reckless pace toward the auditorium, and knew that Rio wouldn’t follow.

 

 

She ran into Ralph outside the bathroom, talking to Jen. She almost cursed, then remembered her promise to Wanda this morning, and moved to walk around them.

“Agatha,” Ralph called, his tweed blazer looking two sizes too big on his wiry frame. “Good to see you.”

Agatha snorted before disguising it as a cough, glancing at Jen.

“President Bohner,” she said, saluting lightly.

“I’ve asked you repeatedly to call me Ralph.”

She smirked. “Yes, but President Bohner seems more official, don’t you think?”

Jen pressed her lips together, clearly trying to keep a straight face. Agatha shook her hair back over her shoulder, standing a little taller. She could still feel the tension twisting in her chest, but this, at least, she knew how to handle.

“I hear you have a student teacher,” Ralph said.

“I hear it was partly your idea.”

“We all thought it would be beneficial.”

“‘We?’” Agatha repeated. “Who, you and Paul? Let me guess, you guys get together every week over Shirley Temples to talk about me?”

“Bourbon,” Ralph said lightly, but his jaw tightened.

“Oh, come on. Bourbon is no good for Paul’s weak stomach. In grad school he used to throw up after three beers, and I recall a district Christmas party where someone,” she pushed a finger into his chest playfully, “ate half the bottle of maraschino cherries.”

Ralph gritted his teeth through his smile, but he fell silent.

“Anyway,” Agatha said, “I am so flattered, Bohner, but just imagine what you could do for the school with all that time you spend coming up with beneficial ideas for me.”

Jen finally cleared her throat, straightening the lapels of her pink blazer. She looked a little like she was trying not to choke, and shot Agatha a look. Agatha pointedly ignored it.

“Well, listen—I know you’ve been having a tough start this year,” Ralph said smoothly. “Hopefully that hasn’t interfered with your collaborative spirit.”

Agatha smiled, but it felt more like baring her teeth.

“We work very well together," she said sharply. "Like Thelma and Louise.”

Rio’s voice echoed in her head. You don’t even know which Agatha you actually are.

“That’s wonderful. Where is she?” Ralph peered behind her. “I’d love to be introduced.”

“Lady trouble.” Agatha gestured vaguely to her abdomen.

Ralph blinked and adjusted his blazer.

“Right… well.” He cleared his throat. “I'm looking forward to your remarks.”

He turned down the hall, and as soon as he was out of earshot, Jen whirled on her.

“Lady trouble?” 

“It’s the only thing that gets him to leave,” Agatha muttered, taking the folder from Jen’s outstretched hand. “He probably thinks ‘lady trouble’ is when we dig for loose change in our vaginas.”

Jen cackled, clutching Agatha’s shoulder, but her amusement faded quickly. She hesitated, then gave her a long look.

“Are you okay?” she asked finally. “I know you’re going through stuff with Josh and everything, but you seem… something. I don’t know. Off. ”

Agatha let out a sharp breath, flipping open the folder, glancing at the remarks inside without really seeing them. She snapped it shut.

“I’m fine, kid.” She forced a smile. 

Jen didn't look convinced. “I’m in my late twenties. You can't call me that anymore. Also, you know Thelma and Louise die at the end, right?”

"Huh." Agatha stared. She'd forgotten. “Whatever. Admit it, J. You think Ralph is a boring, vengeful coward with terrible taste in blazers.”

Jen scoffed. “I am way too on the clock to answer that right now." Then she stepped into the wave of entering parents, and mouthed, Ask me over drinks.

 

 

The old, battered microphone screeched the second Wanda touched it—another sound Agatha had come to associate with School. Of all things, she thought that should’ve been figured out by now. They’d gone from transparencies to smart boards, from computer labs to personal tablets, but the house mic still couldn’t turn on without contributing a violent noise of its own. Which, arguably, was the one thing a microphone was not supposed to do. 

From the wings, Agatha watched as Wanda nodded pointedly in her direction. She hadn’t really been listening, but she he stepped onto the stage, mustering as much composure as she could. No matter how many times she did this, introducing herself always felt like a strange sort of confession, or an introduction at an A.A. meeting.

“Hello, everyone. I’m Agatha Harkness, and I teach second grade.”

The expected murmurs rippled through the crowd. She caught sight of Rio by the refreshment table in the back of the room. The PTA’s god-awful blueberry lemonade sat untouched next to a box of donuts, each one cut into sixteenths. Since when had one-eighth of a donut become too much to handle? Did no one realize how ridiculous they looked, cradling a communion wafer of Krispy Kreme and a cup of blue juice? Her stomach growled in betrayal, but she would never be hungry enough that she'd stand in line at the snack table to receive the body and the blood of the PTA. 

Rio’s face was stony next to the colorful display. Agatha took in the sight of her, and just like that, their argument flared behind her eyes again. It spread hot through her body, like Rio had slipped a hand to the small of her back and struck a match against her spine. 

Arrogant, asshole, know-it-all.

“You know,” Agatha said, the perfect opening presenting itself. “I’ve been really enjoying new types of collaboration in my classroom this year.” She flicked a dry glance at Ralph. “And I think collaboration is an important skill to demonstrate for our students.”

She gestured to Rio. “I have the pleasure of being a mentor this year, which means sharing knowledge I’ve learned in twenty years of teaching.” She paused, letting the weight of the utter bullshit saturate in the stale air.

“Earlier, I shared a quote with my colleague—one I think we can all learn from.” She turned her gaze to the rest of the audience, smiling. Then, deliberately, she repeated Rio’s words back to her. “‘I had some help in becoming who I am.’”

Rio’s eyes widened. 

Good, Agatha thought. Let her feel like a deer in the headlights for one fucking minute and see how she liked it.

“Remind everyone here who said that,” Agatha said, her voice carrying easily through the room.

Heads turned, attention shifting in a slow, rolling wave. Rio blinked, her expression unreadable. The confident, eloquent Rio from the classroom was gone, and Agatha felt the curl of satisfaction in her gut. 

“I…” Rio started, then stopped.

She shifted her weight, fingers tapping once against her elbow. A flicker of something crossed her face, but it passed too quickly to pin down.

“I forgot.”

Agatha arched an eyebrow.

“Oh, come on. I’m sure you remember.” She turned back to the crowd, her voice cloying. “She’s like a steel trap, this one!”

Rio didn’t react right away. Then, slowly, she crossed her arms.

“Nope. Sorry.”

The crowd shifted again, their heads moving in time with the volley, interest sharpening. Rio’s voice was smooth, but there was something definitely off about it now. Agatha had meant to challenge her, and maybe hurt her back. But now, she didn't want to stop at backing her into a corner. She wanted to drive Rio to anger again, and make her say or do something she couldn't take back. She wanted Rio to sound unfit in front of all these people, and for someone else, anyone else, to know what it was like.

“Well, it's alright," Agatha said, the words tipping almost to a singsong. "You're still new, you have a lot to learn. Don't worry about it."

Rio looked up at her with a slow, measured tilt of the head.

“Actually,” she said. "I just remembered."

Her voice was steady, and her eyes held that familiar, dangerous gleam.

Agatha had just enough time to think, Wait— before Rio said loudly:

“Charles Manson.”

The room went silent. The words flew at Agatha like a baseball to the teeth. Her mouth flooded with the sharp taste of metal, and for a second, it felt like she was actually bleeding. There was a beat of silence, and then the microphone screeched again. 

Charles. Manson. No fucking way.

The silence gave way to murmurs, fingers tapping furiously on screens. She realized she’d bitten the inside of her cheek, and the taste of blood in her mouth was real. Her eyes landed on the sharp set of Rio’s jaw, and she knew that she wasn’t joking. Agatha had just, with absolute sincerity, quoted Charles Manson to a room full of parents and the president of the school board.

Her eyes found Wanda’s huge ones. Wanda was already moving, her face pale, her hands reaching.

“Do you have a Charles Manson quote in your classroom?” someone in the crowd demanded. Agatha felt the microphone being yanked from her grip, Wanda’s fingers curling tight around it.

“No, I do not.” She kept her hands on the mic, even as Wanda pulled at it. “It was a joke! Obviously! She was kidding—I was kidding!”

Wanda tore the mic away, stepping in front of her. Agatha was left yelling into the room, her voice suddenly too small.

“I do not support Charles Manson, or his—his work.”

Screens lit up across the room with frantic searches Agatha could imagine with perfect clarity: ‘what did charles manson do,’ and ‘charles manson cult,’ and ‘agatha harkness oyster bend cult murderer.’

Her stomach twisted, heat rising in her face, and Wanda stepped forward. Her voice was smooth and practiced, and Agatha recognized the tone. It was the voice she used to clean up after someone’s graceless mess. Too often lately, that someone had been Agatha. She didn’t want this to feel familiar—Wanda stepping in front of her and doing damage control, like cramming squeezed-out toothpaste back into the tube, or ramming the pin back into a live grenade.

“What she meant was,” Wanda said, herding Agatha off to the side with a firm press of her palm. “You’ve all relied on someone to help you through obstacles and challenges. At Oyster Bend, that’s what our mission is—to be there for your young scholars when they need someone to lean on. No matter who you are.”

A murmur of agreement rippled through the room, and a few parents nodded, and Agatha didn’t hear the rest. She forced herself to glance toward the back of the room, hoping, at least, to find an ounce of guilt on Rio’s face. 

Rio was eating three sixteenths of a donut. Agatha barely held herself onstage until the applause, and the second parents started standing, she made a beeline for the doors.

"Agatha," a voice sing-songed, and suddenly, Kristy, PTA President and vindictive nightmare, was standing in front of her. Kristy had been on her case since last year, when Agatha informed her that her son had used a slur in class.

"Logan would never," Kristy had snapped, launching into a tirade about how Agatha was lying—about something that had happened right in front of her—while poor Logan sat in the corner, biting the edge of his sleeve.

Logan and Agatha both knew he’d said it. He hadn’t even realized it was a hurtful word—kids usually didn’t. They just knew some words were meant for when you were angry. Maybe in his world, it was casually, unapologetically thrown around. But the moment Agatha explained it to him, he’d apologized to her, and to the other kid. 

She’d summarized the situation in that parent-and-kid-friendly tone she could execute in her sleep, ending with, "We had a good chat, and he agreed not to use that word anymore." Logan had nodded seriously, returning Agatha’s thumbs-up. But Kristy had gone on for eleven full minutes before pausing just long enough to say:

"Well, wait. Which slur was it?"

Agatha had blinked. "Is that really important?”

"I just want to know if it’s the one I’m thinking of."

And there it was.

"If one immediately comes to mind," Agatha had said, "then it’s probably that one, isn’t it?"

That had earned her fourteen more minutes, an indisputable feud, and Kristy’s eternal hatred. Including, apparently, right fucking now.

“Please move," Agatha said, trying her hardest not to reach out and push her.

"That was classy," Kristy mused, sipping her blue juice. "Reminded me of your little event this summer.”

Agatha’s breath stilled. Her fingers curled into fists.

"Move." 

Kristy stepped aside slowly. As Agatha sped past, she barely registered the flick of an arm before she felt it. Cold liquid hit her clavicle, and her breath caught in shock. Blueberry lemonade splattered across her chest, soaking into the fabric. She looked down at herself, vaguely reminded of the murder documentary she’d fallen asleep to last night. She felt like the victim in a staged reenactment—a dark blue stab wound blooming over the neckline of her dress.

"Oops." Kristy tilted her head, so deliberate in her timing that Agatha knew exactly how long she’d been imagining this moment. “What a shame. I’d throw that in the wash before the stain sets.”

The weight of the room pressed against Agatha’s skin—the eyes on her, the lemonade drying against her collarbone, the fury and humiliation hot on her neck. Kristy was still standing there, smiling. Agatha’s fingers twitched at her sides. She could take her. Kristy was tall and gangly; she’d topple right over. Just one good shove—

"Agatha?"

She whipped her head around, hands balled into fists, entirely ready for one more person to say her name tonight. Josh was standing there, flyer in hand like a straight-A student. He blinked at her dress, his mouth opening slightly like he'd considered saying something but thought better of it.

She stared at him. “Where’s Nicky?”

"I got off early and got a sitter," he said, still sounding confused. His gaze drifted past her.

Agatha wanted to shake him, even for that. She'd just humiliated herself in front of a crowd, again, she was covered in juice, and whatever was behind her was more interesting?

Her anger sharpened to a fine point. She opened her mouth, ready to lay into him in front of all of these parents, because someone deserved to get their ass handed to them tonight. And it was either going to be Josh, tail between his legs, or Kristy, picking her bloody veneers off the floor.

She turned to Josh, already halfway through the first syllable of whatever was about to rip him apart, until she realized that he was staring toward the back of the room. He was looking at Rio.

Rio, whom Agatha had definitely told him about—as in, I have a new student teacher, Ms. V, so you might see someone new at pickup. But definitely not: Hey, so, Nicky’s other teacher is the stripper you paid to give me lap dance.

Maybe foolishly, she hadn’t worried about that particular thread unraveling. Josh, when drunk, was the perfect combination of unobservant and forgetful. He'd laid eyes on Rio for a grand total of five minutes, in the middle of a blackout. But now, Josh was looking at Rio. Really looking at her. And Rio—mouth slightly open, holding the last sixteenth of a chocolate donut in midair—was staring right back.

"Hey, I keep meaning to ask," Josh said. "The student teacher—she looks, like, super familiar.” His brow furrowed, gaze still locked on her. Agatha stopped breathing. 

“Did she used to work at Olive Garden?"

She let out her breath, steadying herself on the chair next to her.

"Grocery store," she replied faintly. She moved deliberately toward Rio, pouring as much hatred as she could into a single look.

"Lucky fucking break," she gritted out. And then, she snatched the pathetic morsel of donut straight out of Rio’s hand, because apparently, she was that hungry. Rio bit back a smile.

Agatha stalked toward the exit, shaking out her hands at her sides. She shoved the donut piece into her mouth as she passed Kristy.

"Sixteen is too many fucking pieces," she said with her mouth full. She didn’t stop when she reached the door, or when she heard Rio call her name, footsteps echoing behind her. Before she even realized where she was going, she was in the little room off the library.

She unlocked the supply closet door, shoved herself inside, and flipped on the light. The scent of sharpies and fresh printer paper hit her at once, and she sagged against a shelf of pink poster paper, her phone already buzzing. She swiped mechanically through the outrage in her inbox until a subject line caught her eye:

IS THIS THE WOMAN YOU WANT TEACHING YOUR CHILDREN?

She tapped the link against her better judgement, expecting to find her own juice-covered likeness. The video buffered, a grainy, pixelated freeze-frame of her face, mid-sentence. Instead of the harsh lighting of the auditorium, she registered the dull gray of the school board meeting chambers. Then, she recognized her own pale, drawn appearance. 

She muted the video, but still, she watched. Shadows clung to her face on the screen, and she noticed the way she wavered slightly. Maybe she only saw it because she remembered how she hadn’t slept in days, or how Josh had moved out that same morning.

Her eyes stung as she watched herself on the screen. She was going to get fired because she couldn’t keep it together. Eventually, Rio’s secret would come out, and she’d be gone, too. Wanda was magic when it came to cleaning up messes, but she wasn’t God. And Josh—Josh was unobservant, not stupid. He’d put it together, sooner or later. How would she explain? What would she even be explaining? Would he keep Nicky away from her, and decide she was too unstable to be a mother? Would the whole town rally behind him?

The blue glow of her phone flickered in the dim closet. The walls pressed in. She heard three soft knocks.

“Let me in.”

Rio’s voice cut through the quiet, just above Agatha’s shallow breathing. She didn’t respond. The panic in her chest coiled tighter, sharpening into something hotter, something more manageable. This fucking woman. This was all her fault.

“I know you’re in there,” Rio said. “I can hear you having your little panic attack. It’s really not that bad, okay? Just let me in.”

Agatha’s fist closed around the first thing it could find. She hurled it at the door. A handful of colorful pom-poms bounced harmlessly off the wood, scattering across the linoleum. She glared at them, and kicked the nearest ones. 

“Not to point out the irony of where you’re hiding,” Rio said through the door, “but I do know a thing or two about closets, if you—”

Agatha yanked the door open before she could finish. Rio just looked amused, the start of a smirk across her lips.

“Excuse me?” Agatha snapped, crossing her arms.

“Kidding,” Rio said lightly, and gestured behind her. “May I?” 

Agatha didn’t move, glaring at her as she brushed past. Parent voices echoed down the hallway, and Agatha shut the door quickly. Rio glanced around at the supplies.

“I’ve only been in here once,” she said finally.

Agatha glared at the shelf of highlighters, willing them to walk themselves out of their boxes and right into Rio’s mouth, effectively shutting her up.

Rio continued, unbothered by Agatha’s death stare.

“Remember last week, when Lonzo’s mom said she was filing a complaint with the board because Where the Wild Things Are promotes ‘sinful behavior? Which is fucking nuts, by the way—if anything, she should be mad about the colonialist tropes, you know? Like, a white adventurer taming the wild.”

Agatha stared, her blood boiling, wondering why she had opened the door at all.

“Anyway,” Rio went on, “remember how you got so mad, you stapled a piece of paper, like, fifty times?”

She looked around again, assessing the stock. “This is where I came to get more staples.”

Agatha took a step forward, her patience finally snapping.

“Can you stop acting like this is all so fucking hilarious?”

Rio just raised an eyebrow. Agatha kept going, advancing on Rio in the small space.

“First of all, you and Lonzo’s mom are the only two people on the planet who don’t like that book, which is just my luck. Second of all—and much more importantly—you have no idea what you just did to me in there. Charles Manson? Are you fucking kidding? You crucified me, Rio.”

Rio crossed her arms, mirroring Agatha.

“What I did?” Her voice had a definitive edge now. “I was obviously being sarcastic when I said that quote. And by the way, you reprimanded me for not communicating with you, then ten minutes later, you lied to an entire room to make yourself look good. Just because you were mad at me.”

She took a step closer. “And then I actually tried to give you an out, and you chose not to take it.”

Agatha’s felt her words land. Her chest rose and fell, the weight closing in on her in this stupid, suffocating closet. Rio watched her carefully, eyes flicking over her stiff posture, and then to the phone at her side.

Her gaze sharpened.

“What is that?”

“Nothing.” She clicked it off.

“Is this why you’re freaking out? Let me see.”

Rio reached for the phone.

Agatha yanked it back, twisting away.

“Stop—”

Rio was already lunging for it, faster than Agatha expected. Rio caught her wrist, then the phone, fingers latching onto the case. Agatha stumbled against the shelf behind her. Her spine bit into the wood, a box of ballpoint pens toppling over her shoulder as she tried to wrench her arm away. But Rio was stronger, forcing Agatha’s hand back against the shelf.

“Let go,” Rio said, pulling.

The phone slipped from Agatha’s fingers for a fraction of a second, and then she put her weight into it, and Rio lost her footing. She let out a startled noise as she staggered, and Agatha took the opening. For a second, they were a tangled mess of scrabbling hands and shifting weight, and Agatha could hear Rio’s muffled, breathless laughter.

Agatha wasn’t laughing in the slightest. She swiped for the phone, missed, and her fingers tangled in Rio’s hair. Rio hissed through her teeth, her head jerking to the side. She twisted Agatha’s wrist in retaliation, and in response, Agatha dug her nails into Rio’s forearm.

“Jesus,” Rio breathed, wincing, pulling her arm and her hair free. “You’re not trying to ‘Charles Manson’ me, are you?”

Agatha all but growled at her then, pushing her back into the shelf, the weight of her body pinning Rio in place. A container of paperclips tipped over above them, emptying over their heads. Frozen in blind rage, with metal raining around them, she felt Rio's breath hitch. Suddenly, Agatha noticed the way Rio's throat pushed against her collar with every inhale. 

Agatha found her eyes. They were dark, almost curious, and her gaze flickered to Agatha’s mouth. Agatha blinked. Rio was looking at her mouth. That same strange, feeling from the club crept up her spine—every sensation heightened, everything too much. Agatha wasn’t drunk at a strip club, and yet, here was Rio, at her place of work, being paid to pay attention to Agatha. Now, it was neon pink poster paper glowing above her heads instead of a naked woman’s flickering silhouette. This was decidedly more terrifying.

She looked at Rio's mouth, and heat curled at the base of her neck, her fingertips starting to tighten around Rio’s arms.

Agatha took an automatic step back.

For a moment, neither of them moved. Then Rio relaxed, dislodging herself from the shelf. Agatha took in the sight of her crooked blazer, her fingers removing a paper clip from her disheveled hair. She looked at Agatha, still.

Slowly, she held out her other hand, and opened her fingers, offering the phone back. Agatha reached for it carefully, like Rio might make a sudden movement, but she didn’t. 

“Let me see,” Rio said again. But her voice was softer this time. She was asking.

Agatha bit the inside of her cheek. She felt the sticky fabric of her dress against her chest, the droplets of blueberry lemonade in the tips of her hair, flattening her curls that she hadn’t cared about anyway, but that maybe, she'd cared about a little.

Her thumb hovered over the screen, and her eyes flitted to Rio’s. She placed it slowly back into her palm. She turned around then, planting her eyes on the wall. She stared at the mess on the floor, at a single red pom-pom, smushed on one side.

And then, she heard Ralph’s voice.

 

 

“I say we move to approve the new mural for this coming school year, commissioned by a former student.” 

The shaky audio filled the supply closet: the feedback from the cheap microphone, the rustling of papers, someone coughing faintly.

“I’m sorry, Agatha, did you have a concern to voice? I see that you’re shaking your head.” 

Then, the warped, strange sound of her own voice. 

“Do I have a concern, Ralph? Yeah, I have a concern.”

The scrape of a chair across the floor. Her voice was somehow still too sharp, even through her waterlogged speakers. 

“My concern was voiced fifteen years ago, actually, when you and the old board and a hairbrained PTA decided that Oyster Bend’s mascot was a river otter, even though river otters have nothing to do with oysters. Believe it or not, sea otters still don’t live in Massachusetts, and sea otters and river otters are still not the same animal. But fine, let’s throw it on a mural to commemorate the utter lack of attention to detail that goes into running this school district.”

“Okay, let’s just take a second to calm-–”

“No. You asked me if I had a concern, Ralph, so you’re going to listen to my fucking concern.”

Agatha heard the beat of stunned silence. Then, she kept going.

 

 

“You people make it impossible to be a teacher.

And being a mom and a teacher? I have to teach your kids to have some fucking perspective. And then I have to go home to my own kid, and figure out how to explain it all. 

There will never be enough hours in the school day for me to undo what you’re doing to your kids. I'm trying, but I go back to school, and go back home, and realize that I’ll just be doing that for the rest of my life until I fucking die, because I’ll probably still be in that classroom when I take my last breath.

You know how when you’re younger, you can’t imagine that anything will ever feel like the end? And you think you’re so smart—that you’re making these little deals with yourself. One year, they want to add a couple kids to your class. You think—sure, what’s a couple more? I'll pull in some extra desks. The next year, they want to make your lunch five minutes shorter. You think—no big deal, I can leave five minutes earlier. It’s a fair exchange, like in a stupid board game, right? It’s what we teach the kids: ‘Monopoly only works if everyone agrees to play by the same rules.’ 

So you tell yourself—it’s fine. It’s just one more thing. But then, all of a sudden, you realize you’ve been passing ‘Go’ for twenty fucking years. And now, you’re required to respond to every parent email, even if it takes all night. Even the ones from Kristy, asking if her son can re-do his worksheet because he only wrote two sentences. He’s seven, Kristy. Two full sentences is a miracle. I asked him where he wants to go on vacation, not for a personal statement. And by the way—he said he wants to go to his ‘friend Riley’s house,’ so you’re wasting your fucking money on those trips to San Tropez.

So yes, Ralph, I am concerned. Because you and the PTA—the sweaty back acne of the public school system—are asking me to be here even though I just spent all morning helping my husband move his boxes out of our fucking house. To ask for my opinion on fifth grade biology, which you will ignore, again.

And yes, I came anyway, because that’s what teachers do.

I consider it concerning that one day, you just wake up, and it’s been twenty years since you started this job. You look in the mirror, and it feels like you’re wearing one of those scary fucking Halloween masks that look like real people. And it’s like—of course you know it has to be you, but how can that face possibly be yours?

But maybe, as it turns out, that’s just another fair exchange.

And the worst part, Ralph, is that there’s always a little part of you that knows that it wasn’t always like this. You know that when you got here, you had such big, beautiful things. A brand new house, grad school accolades, a promising career. A love so big and easy you were absolutely sure was the love of your life. But for twenty years, you made so many little deals with yourself, gave away so many little green houses, that now—you can’t even remember what you started with.

And you’re standing there in front of these incredible kids, telling them it’s so wonderful to grow and change, when it’s all a fucking lie. Because growing and changing means getting ripped apart by people like all of you, who have no idea what it’s like to love something so much, you will give yourself away to keep it.

This summer, today, it’s my husband and a fucked-up mural.

Tomorrow, and the day after, it’ll be something else.

Just one more fucking thing.”

 

 

Agatha heard the click of her phone, and still, Rio said nothing.

The closet was too bright, the overhead light drilling into her skull. Her mouth opened before she could stop it.

“Kristy tried to stop me from getting in my car after that.” 

A pause—then, Rio’s voice, quiet. “Sounds like Kristy has a death wish. What'd you do?”

Agatha rubbed a hand over the bridge of her nose. “I ‘spilled’ my coffee into her vintage Coach purse.”

There was a soft snort from behind her, and Agatha turned, finally, still looking at the floor.

“That would explain the…” Rio gestured to the stain on Agatha’s chest, and she tilted her head in acknowledgement. 

“Agatha,” Rio said after a beat, and there was something soft in her voice. Agatha looked up, and Rio was looking at her with something like understanding where Agatha had expected to find pity. And there was something else, too, pooling deep and warm in her eyes. The word affection came to mind, although that didn’t make sense.

Rio put the phone on the shelf beside her, eyes still on Agatha, and reached for the lapels of her own blazer. She slid it off her shoulders and draped it over a stack of chairs. For one, ridiculous second, Agatha wondered if Rio was disrobing for a fight.

Then, Rio lifted her hands to the top button of her collared shirt.

“What are you…” Agatha’s voice failed her, eyes glued to Rio’s hands moving deliberately down the line of buttons.

Rio didn’t answer, shrugging off her shirt, revealing a fitted black tank. She took a step closer, and Agatha swallowed the fluorescent shine against her collarbone.

“Turn around,” Rio said quietly. 

Agatha stared at her, frozen. She wanted to ask what the fuck Rio was doing. But her mouth stayed shut, her pulse hammering in her throat. And then she turned, slowly. She felt a light tug on her hair, then Rio’s hands. She was twisting, Agatha realized, securing it into a bun at the nape of her neck, hiding the damp, flattened ends. 

“I’ve been a dancer since I could walk,” Rio said quietly, her hands still working. “All kinds, anything you could name. I know how to handle a wardrobe emergency.”

Agatha felt Rio’s fingertips dance over the back of her neck, and she shivered, the dress giving as the zipper slid down. Rio peeled the wet fabric down her torso, pausing at her waist, and Agatha pulled her hands from the sleeves as if she were sleepwalking. She felt the words rising against her tongue, quick and defensive, to protect herself from Rio’s gaze on her skin, but before she could force them out, she felt something cover her shoulders. Rio’s shirt. Agatha breathed, and pulled her arms through, her fingers fumbling over the buttons. 

Rio pulled at the long sleeves of her dress, now dangling at her waist, and cinched them behind Agatha’s back. She could feel Rio’s hands at her sides, pressing the shirt into place under the makeshift hem, and it should have felt odd, being dressed like a child that had spilled juice on herself at a party, but it didn’t feel like that at all. Agatha tried to steady her breathing, Rio’s hands warm as they circled around her from behind, dipping the shirt into the waistband at her stomach. Rio draped the blazer over her last.

Agatha could feel Rio’s breath against her neck, and when she spoke, Agatha could feel the words curl into her ear.

“‘He’s just a boy, pretending to be a wolf, pretending to be a king.' I never said I didn’t like Wild Things.”

Agatha felt Rio brush past her, catching a glimpse of her bare arms as she slipped out of the closet, looking somehow still poised and professional in a tank top and slacks. The door clicked shut behind her.

Agatha curled her fist in the bucket of pom-poms again, her knuckles white. She exhaled, unsteady, and finally looked down at herself. The cuffs of Rio’s white shirt brushed her wrists. The scent still clung to the fabric, warmth and spiced cologne, cinnamon and vanilla. It felt like fall, and the maple leaves that fell over her picnic table, and the first day of school. 

She took a deep breath and looked down at the gaudy mess: pom-poms, pens, colored paper. She brushed her hands against her hair and felt the bun Rio had twisted for her. Her fingers snagged against a paper clip by her ear—her tiny, crooked crown. She pulled the blazer tighter around her shoulders, and standing under the fluorescent lights of her kingdom, she let herself cry, a graveyard of wild things at her feet.

 

 

 

Chapter End Notes

pour one out for the body and the blood of the PTA 🗣️

Halloween Harvest

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

 

 

"I am too young to die and too old to eat off the kids' menu! What a stupid age I am!"

—Jason Mendoza, The Good Place



 

For once, the teachers’ lounge was full, the last of the volunteers crammed almost shoulder to shoulder in the tiny space. The room was starting to feel hot, the October afternoon sun streaming through the windows. Agatha gathered her hair off of her neck and fanned herself with her clipboard. Twenty years, and the temperature control in this school was still presumably operated by the devil himself. 

She looked at her phone again. Today marked the third time Rio had been late in the last week, which she was keeping track of because it was her job, and not for any other reason.

A thin, orange fiber clung to the side of her screen. Last night, she and Nicky had sat at the kitchen table, their heads bowed toward each other over two enormous pumpkins. Nicky’s sounds of delight were still fresh in her mind, his squeals at the feel of the cold, slimy seeds and guts slipping through his fingers onto newspaper. She even giggled a couple times herself, hands squelching into the pulp. Her own laughter lingered, too, like it might slip out again this morning if she weren’t careful.

She'd let herself enjoy it, long after she put Nicky to bed—the sound of the steady scrape, the feel of the knife moving through the flesh and fiber. One hand on the handle, the other caressing the cool skin, holding it steady. She carved out a terrible, dangerous smile, wild eyes and sharp teeth. And still, she sat, plucking seeds out of the innards one by one. Her fingers wove steadily into the night, caught up in the rare ease of repetition.

Now, the room was hot and loud, and the dried pumpkin caught under her thumb. She picked at it, her nail tapping against the screen. Still no word from Rio. Agatha looked at the array of teachers and parents perched on the worn furniture, exchanging small talk at various levels of exhaustion. Jen leaned against the armrest of the green sofa, next to a poorly strung paper ghost. 

Teachers spent too much time transforming every other inch of the school, including the bathrooms, paper bats and pumpkins stuck to the mirrors. By the time the spiderweb bags ran empty and their exhausted feet made it down the hallway, all they wanted to do was collapse into the green couch. The only evidence of Halloween in the teachers' lounge was one crooked paper ghost and empty packaging in the trash can. 

Her phone buzzed, and she felt the familiar crest and fall. Not only had Rio still not arrived, but she'd now received three messages from her accountant. For the past month, she’d been trapped in the world’s worst game of telephone between her accountant and her divorce lawyer, and she'd taken to compulsively ignoring them both in favor of even the most undesirable tasks. Keeping with the tradition, she turned her phone face down on the desk and cleared her throat.

"Happy Friday, everyone."

The room quieted instantly. It wasn’t really the volume that did it; it was just that teacher thing where she could reach a hand into the air and twist the energy in a room. She’d always enjoyed having that particular skill, even if her recent execution had been shaky at best.

"Thank you for giving us your Friday afternoon," she started, scanning the faces in front of her. A few teachers lingered by the doorway—teachers who, notably, had never stuck around for event prep. Kristy tended to the sparkling water and homemade rice krispie squares, this time cut to a reasonable size. Since last month, she'd taken to watching Agatha like if she even closed her eyes for a second, Agatha might suddenly strip naked and climb the flagpole. 

Agatha was still plagued by choppy, nightmarish flashes of Back to School Night at the most inconvenient times, most often when she pulled into the parking lot. But one image had stayed oddly clear: the slow ripple of nods through the crowd, right before Rio revealed the source of the quote. Agatha had issued an apology, her shoulders stiff and face burning as she wrote. Wanda had peered over her shoulder and assured her that it would all blow over. Agatha had brushed it off as well-meaning best-friend-type bullshit, but miraculously, the leaves turned brown over the grass, and the engine of outrage sputtered back to a crawl.

At first, Agatha figured they felt sorry for her, dripping in lemonade and emerging from a supply closet in a new outfit. But late one night, mid-shower and mid-reach for the conditioner, something more plausible had struck: they hadn’t recognized the quote either. They didn't want to admit they’d missed something as glaring as the accolades of an infamous cult leader. She'd played the fool publicly, but the spectators in their plastic seats knew that the only thing more humiliating than playing a fool was believing in one.

By late October, she felt the difference in the hallways: soft smiles, nods on the playground, quick waves in the parking lot. Teachers who'd once kept their distance now looked at her like they wanted to say something. A fifth-grade teacher, Peggy, had clapped her on the back just yesterday and muttered, "That board meeting video? Well said." As Peggy left her the last half-muffin in the pastry box, Agatha let herself believe that maybe, possibly, things were not as catastrophic as she’d originally thought.

"We are twenty-four hours away from Halloween Harvest!" Agatha announced, and the small room echoed with weak, smattered applause. Jen whooped from her place on the sofa, and Agatha glanced at her phone again. Logic dictated that she should delegate the haunted house—the one thing she had reluctantly placed into Rio’s hands after making her swear to take it seriously. 

She looked back at the ordered chaos on her clipboard, the twice-rewritten lists and notes-to-self in the margins. She knew every inch of this event inside and out. The guts were cleaned and disposed, seeds separated. 

"New volunteers. I still need costume contest judges, a cotton candy operator, a playlist for the haunted house, and a teacher on electrical. Sign up if you can help. After that, check the list outside for what we still need to finish. Find me if you need anything."

She left the clipboard on the table as parents lined up, and heard a quiet "Hey." She turned to her left, and Alice, the PE teacher, was leaning against the wall next to her.

Agatha paused. "Hey." 

Alice had been the PE teacher for three years, but this was definitely the first time she'd ever spoken to Agatha. Her hair was streaked with orange, and Agatha briefly wondered if it had always been that way, and she'd never noticed until now.

"I can do electrical," Alice said.

"Oh. Great." Agatha tried not to sound surprised. "You know where the ladders are, and the utility closet with the panel? And all the outlets?"

"Yup," Alice said, still leaning against the wall. "Also, last month? That was crazy—tough break."

Agatha braced for the punchline, but Alice said it offhand. "That video? Where you made that kid’s mural, like, super political? Solid."

Agatha let out a laugh, and studied Alice. "Why did you like it?"

"It was dark," Alice said. "Most teachers I know are a little dark. Rio said you guys were just messing around at back to school night, too, so I’m sure you’re sick of hearing about all of it. Just wanted to say, I’m a fan."

Agatha blinked. Of course. 

In the days following the event, which Wanda had categorized "A.M.," for "After Manson," Rio had been strangely subdued. She'd even bought Agatha a new purple coffee mug with a working lid as a peace offering. At first, Rio’s sheepishness had been satisfying, because Agatha really had been furious. Furious enough to open Rio's mid-semester evaluation one night, drunk off of too many margaritas after one particularly scathing local article. She sat on the floor of her living room, leaning against the couch, and let her fingers type freely with unfiltered rage. Then Wanda had texted her and snapped her out of her tequila-fueled fury. So she'd shoved the laptop under her bed, and saved the task for a more lucid day.

Now, Rio was forming alliances for Agatha behind her back, and Agatha wasn’t sure whether to feel irritated or grateful. Maybe she would have a better idea if Rio were actually here.

"Right," she said to Alice, unsure how else to respond. "Thanks. For the electrical."

Alice nodded and pushed off the wall, her keys swinging in her hand.

 

 

Agatha's phone rang as soon as she stepped outside.

"Where are you?" she snapped, and silence rang out on the other end.

"I’m… in my office," a voice said. A man’s voice. Agatha looked at the screen.

"Fuck me." She mouthed it emphatically in place of shouting, but it didn't feel quite the same. She put her phone back to her ear.

"I wasn’t talking to you. I’m busy, Paul. What do you want?" She made her way toward the auditorium, and felt the breeze sting against her cheeks. She made a mental note to tell someone to tie down the scarecrows so they didn't have another situation like last year.

"I was just calling to check in," Paul said.

"Please. You only call when you need something."

"Ouch, not true."

Agatha could hear the creak of his office chair that meant he was leaning back. "Not even three weeks ago, I called to tell you that one of our old classmates got arrested for child porn. Which, by the way, was a huge blow to my PhD's credibility."

Agatha stopped walking in the middle of the basketball court, pressing a hand to her forehead before curling it into a fist.

"Paul." She took a slow, deep breath. "What do you want?" 

"I really am calling to check in. It seems like you’ve been getting your fair share of bullshit recently, which is a little more on brand for me, not so much you."

Agatha blinked, still standing in the middle of the blacktop. 

"I also wanted to give you a heads up," Paul continued. "Ralph is planning on dropping by tomorrow, and invited a local journalist to come with. I don’t think it’s personal. Just, you know, optics. Or maybe it’s a little personal. Either way, keep in mind that if you go apeshit on him again, it’ll be on the record."

"Jesus, I’ll be fine." She shifted her weight, racking her brain for ulterior motives, then, finding none, asked point blank: "Why are you telling me this?"

"Because I’m a nice guy," Paul said, and Agatha rolled her eyes. 

"And because Ralph has been on my ass, and it’s getting annoying. Just promise you’ll act surprised. Maybe even a little shell-shocked."

Agatha paused again. Of all the times Paul had rolled out the 'Trust me, I’m on your side'  schtick, this might have been the first time in twenty years that she actually believed him. She rolled her neck to one side before tilting it back. The sky was a dizzying, cloudless blue, all wrong for this time of year. She took a deep breath, the phone warm against her ear, and squeezed her eyes shut in hasty prayer.

God damn Paul and his motherfucking goddamn phone calls, and please let this not be a trap, and please Jesus let Ralph and his god damn fucking tweed blazers get lost on the way to this event. Amen.

She opened her eyes and kicked a pebble by her foot, watching it tumble into a clump of weeds. Her big toe stung through her sneakers. 

"Fine," she said finally. "Thanks for the heads up."

"No problem. And you should answer your phone a little more politely."

"Fuck you."

 

 

 

 

Agatha was still thinking about the phone call as she ducked under a curtain of loose, black plastic, flapping in the entrance of the auditorium. Ralph bringing a journalist didn’t bother her—Halloween was the one day a year she knew she was unflappable. But Paul giving her a heads-up was uncharacteristic, at least in the last decade. Calling her twice in one month just to check in was certainly more unsettling than any journalist.

Agatha frowned, shaking off the thought. The decorations were partly underway—a pile of fake bones in the corner, cardboard headstones stacked and waiting against the wall. She stepped over an extension cord and adjusted a skeleton, two fingers under its chin. She scanned the wooden frames, searching for safety hazards or poor artistic choices, but curiously, she found none.

Volunteers were stringing up black trash bags as makeshift walls that shifted in the cross-breeze. For once, no one needed her direction. Agatha watched, fiddling uselessly with an orange streamer. For a second, she basked in the thrill of watching something she'd created come to life, like it had every year for almost two decades.

Sunlight spilled through the back doors, and a flicker of yellow caught her eye. She followed it, stepping into the cool autumn air. The back lot stretched in front of her, the sounds of parent chatter fading as she took in the beginnings of a pumpkin patch. She closed her eyes and inhaled the distinctly comforting combination of glue, hay, and paint.

Her eyes landed on a red folding chair pushed against the wall. Some kid's abandoned ice pack sat on the seat, left to melt, condensation dripping down the sides of the plastic bag. 

Beyond the chair, a whiteboard leaned against the building, covered in a familiar, uneven scrawl. Rio might as well have written her name. Agatha moved forward, drawn to the shape of the words, and her thumb swiped absently at a smudge in the margin. The board was covered in tasks. Agatha’s list had been comprehensive, but this list was different. This one had changes, and additional notes, and initials by each item. This one was color-coded. 

She was halfway through scrutinizing the line items when she heard a muffled grunt, followed by the hollow slap of hands on cardboard. Rio appeared suddenly around the corner, approaching a pile of unopened boxes in the back of someone’s pickup. She was carrying the largest one, and shifted it to one side, yanking off the marker cap with her teeth.

Agatha cleared her throat, the sound too loud in the quiet lot. Rio turned her head, the cap sticking out of her mouth. "Hey," she said around the plastic. Her tongue flicked absently against it.

Agatha forgot her opening line, her eyes catching on the mindless motion, the gentle bite of Rio's teeth and the light press of her tongue. Rio set the box down at her feet, and capped the pen.

"Sorry I’m late. Anything specific you need?"

Agatha crossed her arms, walking closer. She eyed the boxes unnecessarily. "I need you to be on time."

"Yeah, I know. Won’t happen again."

The afternoon light was harsh over her features, hitting her brow, turning her eyes to dark hollows as she continued her inventory. Agatha studied her tousled hair and rumpled sweatshirt. 

"That’s it?" Agatha said. "No excuses? No lecture about how time is a social construct?"

"Nope," Rio said simply, lowering herself to the ground. She swayed, her eyes widening, and Agatha’s hands shot out to steady her. Rio blew out a slow breath as she settled stiffly on the pavement. Rio glanced at Agatha’s nails still pressing into her bicep.

Agatha straightened quickly and took a casual step back. And then another.

"What’s wrong with you?" she asked finally, noticing the way Rio’s leg stretched out to the side. 

Rio surveyed the contents of the box, rifling through tangled LED strips and remnants of masking tape. She pulled one strip out, and the entire pile came with it, clinging tightly together. She dropped it and rubbed a hand over her face.

"I pulled a muscle," she said finally. "Jogging."

"You jog?" Agatha said dryly, fidgeting with the corner of the cardboard lid.

"You don’t think I can run?"

It had all the words and cadence of Rio's deadpan jokes, but it fell too cold and flat.

"I think you’re more of an American Ninja Warrior type," Agatha said, watching her carefully.

Rio smiled a little at that, and Agatha felt a surprisingly strong wave of relief. She watched Rio work for a minute, separating out the loops of tape from the lights, setting them aside one by one, her fingers plucking and pulling. It reminded her of sitting at her kitchen table, picking out the seeds from the guts, one by one. She had the strange, certain feeling that she could stand here for another hour, and Rio would keep working in steady silence.

Agatha wanted to know what happened with a ferocity that side-swept her. The longer she stood there watching, the more her fingers twitched. She wanted to tear the lights from Rio's hands, grip her by the shoulders, and make her say something. But there she sat, working silently, and Agatha didn’t know how to push her when she was already sprawled out on the ground.

She'd never been able to stand this particular feeling—the guessing and the missing. When she was nine, her twelve year-old neighbor, Val, had taught her how to spit off the roof of their apartment building. "You have to guess where it’s going to land," she'd said, "and really go for it."

They'd take turns until Agatha’s stomach hurt from laughing and the street lights turned off. Val’s mother would poke her head out of the roof door, and say, "Come on, Valkyrie, time for dinner." And "Agatha, I’m sure your mother wants you back inside, too." And Agatha would say, "Yes, I heard her calling me," and Val and Val’s mother and Agatha would all three know she was lying. They'd let her walk downstairs with them anyway.

Val had a seemingly magical ability to know where her spit was going to land—on that car, on the sidewalk across the street, on the man’s bald head as he walked by. Agatha could barely even see it hit the ground. She chalked it up to the superior age and wisdom of twelve, and just yelled and cheered whenever Val did, her chest ballooning with pride when it seemed like she'd gotten one right.

After Val moved away, Agatha kept going to the roof, but she could never call it right again. She tried every night, until the night before her mother moved them out of that apartment and into a smaller, older one with water-damaged walls and bars on the windows. Finally twelve years old, she'd given herself one last chance. She picked a spot, planted her feet in the gravel and given it her all, but she'd still lost it in the air.

She spent her last night in that apartment crying, face hot with failure and disappointment. At twelve years old, standing on a roof, Agatha decided that there must be something wrong with her that she couldn’t get it right.

 

 

Rio sat on the ground, silent and impossible, and Agatha might as well have been back on the roof, spitting fervently into the wind. The same frustration rubbed at her skin, the need to know how it would land, and the gnawing fear that she never would. That this woman in front of her would be the one thing she would never be able to understand.

Instead of trying, she turned to the chair by the auditorium door, eyeing the plastic bag full of water. She pulled her radio from her waistband, and clicked it on to the familiar hum of static.

"Happy."

"This is Happy; copy."

"Hey, it’s Agatha. Can you run me an ice pack to the back of the auditorium?"

"Copy. Coming right up."

She swung the bag of water into the trash can, and when she looked back at Rio, her hands had stilled.

"It melted," Agatha said, and she saw something soften slightly in Rio’s expression. Her fingers started up again, buried in the tangled pile. Pluck, separate, pull. 

"I hurt myself… bartending," she said carefully. "Last night."

"What happened?"

Agatha spotted Happy zooming toward them in a golf cart in the distance, wavering with the speed of his turns. Rio followed her gaze.

"It was nothing. I was late because I was icing it in my car, and I fell asleep. Sorry." 

Agatha was hit with the image of Rio passed out in her car, ice melting in the plastic bag against her leg. Her chest tightened, frustration dissipating in the face of Rio’s plain exhaustion. The question still lingered in her mouth, but Rio had already turned back to the lights, fingers working through the mess.

"It’s fine," Agatha mumbled, as Happy pulled up next to them. He dismounted from the cart with the biggest bag of ice Agatha had ever seen, and she blinked.

"Happy, I asked for an ice pack."

"Yeah, here you go, ma’am."

"This is enough ice for a frat party. I need an ice pack, like, for an injury."

"You injured?" Happy said, inspecting her. "Let’s go, we're going to the nurse. Hop on."

"No," Agatha said, setting the bag down next to Rio. "It’s for her, and she’s fine."

Agatha turned to Rio, who was smiling. "You are fine, right?" she added.

"Yeah. I’m good. Hey, Happy. Thanks for the ice."

"Hey, Ms. V. Anytime, okay?"

Agatha held a hand to her head as he drove away, and the two of them stared at the party bag of ice on the ground. Finally, Rio laughed, the half-untangled mess of lights falling back into the box, and Agatha broke, too, the tension melting from her shoulders at the sight of Rio at ease.

"Excuse me," she heard, and found a parent standing behind her. "Which ghost should we put at the entrance? The animatronic one or the hanging one?"

"Hanging," Rio and Agatha answered at the same time, and the parent looked between them, their faces still flushed from laughter. Agatha opened her mouth, and then closed it, gesturing to Rio.

"It’ll be easier to block the view of what’s past the entrance," Rio explained.

The parent nodded, and left Agatha looking at Rio, suitably impressed. 

"Okay," Agatha said after a beat. "You’ve obviously got things covered. I'll leave you to it."

She turned toward the pumpkin patch, and then hesitated. She turned back, and Rio looked at her, standing there uncertainly, six paces away and rocking on her heels.

"Also…" Agatha paused, shifting her weight. The breeze tugged at the edge of her collar. There it was: the wind-up, feet rooted in the gravel, wind on her face.

"It's never looked this good by Friday afternoon. I usually have to tear down a bunch of stuff and make them start over. And the parents actually seem to know what they’re working on, for once. So whatever you’re doing, just…keep doing it."

The look of surprise on Rio’s face was almost comical, and Agatha almost rolled her eyes and took it all back. But then, Rio smiled at her—a real smile, spreading slow across her lips. Agatha’s pulse somersaulted in her throat before she turned decisively towards the pumpkins.

 

 

When her mother died, thirteen Octobers ago, she decided to go back to that childhood apartment. She went straight to the roof. She looked down, remembering the evenings she'd spent in competition with Val, and then, even longer, with herself. And standing there at twenty-nine years old in the afternoon sun, she wound back, and spit with her full, grown-up might. With her expensive, high-powered contact lenses, and wisdom of age and fresh tenure, she still didn’t see it hit the ground. 

For the first time, she'd thought to consider Val a child, instead of a sage, and suddenly understood that Val had never been able to see it, either. She'd been making it up the whole time, and Agatha had celebrated all the same. 

 



Agatha sat heavily on Nicky’s bed, bouncing once before flopping over.

"Mama, you can’t sleep!" he giggled, pulling her by the arm. "We have to go!"

"But I’m sleepy," she mumbled into his pillow. She breathed in, snuggling into the blanket, and it smelled like him. The toll of teaching and event-planning weighed her down in the small bed. Maybe she could just close her eyes for a second. 

"Mama." He was using his serious voice now, and Agatha peeled her eyes open, fighting the urge to laugh. He was looking at her, arms crossed. No question where he got that look.

"Okay, I’m up." She leaned on her elbows, and nodded towards the mirror. "Did you check it out? How’d I do?"

Nicky walked back to the glass, his gaze falling over his black and white striped dress, and he touched the two braids over his shoulders. His face was painted white, his eyelids dark. He made his best scary face at himself in the mirror, lost in the carefree self-admiration of seven. He leaned in and stuck out his tongue, making a wet mark on the glass.

"Don’t touch your face for a few minutes, okay?"

Agatha pulled her bare feet out of the blanket and watched him, unable to stop. He was swaying side to side to see how the dress swished around his high socks. She smiled, and finally, reluctantly, turned to the more difficult task at hand.

When Nicky had assigned her the role, she hadn’t told him this would be her third time dressing up as Morticia. Granted, the first two times were in her twenties. The only remnants of her twenties in her closet were an Oyster Bend t-shirt, her wedding dress, and Morticia Addams black velvet. She felt a strange mix of deep excitement and existential dread as she pulled the velvet dress up by the sides. It was a little bit of a constructed nightmare, forcing her hips and sides into an outfit from twenty years ago, the dress just as snug and unforgiving, the body and face underneath warped and changed.

The ankle-length velvet swept around her as she moved, her sturdy, platform boots adding three inches that felt like a foot. She shifted her weight, making sure she could still pivot and step easily so she wouldn't trip and fall on her face in front of Ralph and his pet journalist.

The neckline swept under her collarbone in a wide scoop, and her sleeves widened at the wrist. She'd straightened her hair, and it fell smoothly behind her shoulders. Once she'd finished her makeup—dark lipstick, eyeshadow, and white powder dusting her face and chest—she stared at her reflection in surprise. This was the most effort she'd put into an outfit all year. 

Nicky pushed her door open and stopped in his tracks, wide-eyed. "Mama, you look beautiful."

Agatha laughed, squeezing his arm. "So do you, my little Wednesday."

Nicky looked down at his own dress, playing with the hem, and Agatha watched the flitting of his eyes, replacing the raw confidence from just a moment ago.

"What’s up?" Agatha said, eyeing him.

"Jeremy said it’s weird to wear a dress," he said, picking at a loose thread. 

Agatha resisted the urge to curse Jeremy out in front of Nicky.

"And what do you say?" she said instead, resting a hand on his head.

"I like being Wednesday," he replied, looking up at her. "Can I still wear it?"

Agatha looked at him, and made sure he was looking at her. "You wear whatever you want, whenever you want, baby," she said. Then, unable to resist, added, "Jeremy has dressed as the blue power ranger for the past three years in a row, so he doesn’t really get to contribute an opinion here. We’re operating way above his level."

"Yeah," Nicky said, nodding. "Way above his level."

"Plus," Agatha said, waggling her eyebrows. "Wednesday and Morticia are weird. That’s what makes them so cool."

"We look so cool," Nicky agreed, looking at them side by side in Agatha’s mirror, and Agatha felt that strange, motherly glow of invincibility. She was unstoppable. She looked weird, and cool. Morticia Addams was mysterious, and confident, and sexy.

She stuffed her laptop in her bag, and caught a sticky note that fluttered to the floor. It read: rio eval. She groaned, deflating a little. The evaluation. The one she had told herself she would finish today, that she'd been actively not finishing for the past two weeks.

She stuffed the sticky note in with the laptop, half hoping it would crumple itself into something unrecognizable in the bottom of the bag.

"Let’s go, Wednesday," she said. "We've got a costume contest to win." 

She followed Nicky to the car, a container of homemade cookies in hand, and felt the thrill rising in her chest. It was Halloween, and maybe it would be the only night of the year where she’d feel this good. Maybe tomorrow, the villagers would go right back to chasing her through the woods with their pitchforks and torches. But that was a problem for tomorrow. For Agatha, the tired divorcee, or Ms. Harkness, the second-grade teacher. Tonight was for Morticia, queen of the trash-bagged castle, Atropa belladonna of the ball—beautiful woman and deadly nightshade, all wrapped up in one. 

 

 

The sun dipped behind the school, kids and their parents starting to arrive in the usual array of unoriginal costumes. Of course, no one had tied down the scarecrows, so she had to do it herself, the hay sticking to her velvet dress as Nicky ran off with the twins.

She walked to the haunted house as fast as she could, brushing hay off of her lap. She waved to the parent at the entrance, and ducked under the tarp. She heard volunteers calling to each other, checking their positions and the various flashing lights, and she wandered slowly through the passageways, looking for Rio.

Rio had never seen her dressed this way, and for some reason, the thought wouldn’t leave her alone. In fact, she hadn’t stopped thinking about it since she put on this dress. She caught her reflection in a mirror as she walked, scrutinizing her own makeup. 

She stopped in her tracks under the archway to the center room. What had previously been a haunted library, Rio had transformed into a dining room. Looming cardboard figures in black cloaks sat at the table, their plates filled with horrors: snakes, eyeballs, a skull. Cobwebs strung across the corners, the fake candles on the table flickering, and Agatha stared. It looked incredible. 

Across the room, Rio was buttoning a black cloak around a volunteer, helping obscure her into the corner of the room where she could jump out at unsuspecting children. Rio was dressed in a purple suit, her hair spray-painted green. Agatha smiled to herself. 

She opened her mouth, and then closed it, still watching. Rio was talking to this red-haired woman, a volunteer Agatha had seen a couple times but never met—Natasha, maybe? Rio's hands secured the fabric around the woman’s neck and chest. She was laughing, and the woman, this unrealistically attractive, model-like woman, was leaning in close, her hand brushing Rio’s arm.

Something odd swept through Agatha’s stomach, heat crawling up her throat, thick and suffocating. It was too fucking hot in here. Someone needed to cut holes in the plastic for some goddamn ventilation, and fuck, she had definitely forgotten to put that on her list. The velvet stuck to her skin, and her legs suddenly needed to move, to leave this plastic-bagged dining room from hell. She moved backwards, tripping over a chair leg in her haste, and she cursed.

Rio and Natasha looked in her direction.

"Agatha?" Rio said in surprise, and her eyes fell to the dress. Her expression was impossible to read underneath the eerie makeup, but her eyes swept over Agatha in a dark, slow roll. 

"Looks great in here," Agatha managed, the dress clinging to her skin. "And you–" she gestured vaguely to the woman, who was watching them both, "...look great, too, both of you. The Joker, that's a good one. Good pick."

Agatha was suddenly, acutely aware of the stretch of her neckline and the weight of the dress pulling her down. Her fingers twitched on the chair. She felt a little faint. Rio’s lips were still parted, but no words came. She was just looking, uncharacteristically speechless. 

Agatha turned abruptly, almost tearing the plastic door in her effort to escape. She ducked blindly under curtains, ripping them wherever she could to let the air through, until her hands found metal. She slammed into the back door and burst free, gasping in the sudden open air.

"Ma’am?" she heard behind her, and she startled at both the wind and the voice. She realized suddenly that it was dark.

"Happy," she said, hand pressed to her chest. "Are you supposed to be scaring people?"

"No, ma’am; I’m here to comfort any of the kids that get too freaked out, so that their parents don’t sue us."

"You might want to ease up on the element of surprise, Jesus." Agatha held a hand on the top of her head, still reeling. "And don’t call me ma’am, I’m not that old." 

She almost wiped the hand over her face, and then remembered her painstaking makeup. What the fuck had just happened to her? Was it a hot flash? Maybe she was that old, and she was finally falling into the looming clutches of menopause.

"Okay… Agatha," Happy said, putting a hand to her elbow. "Are you doing alright?"

Agatha blinked and looked at him. "I changed my mind. That was too weird; did that feel weird to you?"

"Yes, Ma’am, very weird."

"Great." Agatha brushed her hair back. "I’m fine. Thank you for working tonight."

"You kidding? I wouldn’t miss it; best night of the year."

Agatha patted him on the shoulder affectionately, and pushed Rio firmly out of her head, along with the mysterious, beautiful woman who was maybe named Natasha but hopefully something more boring. She blinked away Rio’s hands on her shoulder, and the shape of her smile under the wide, crooked lipstick.

 

 

 

 

It was almost eight before Agatha finally sat down, the crowd starting to dwindle. She perched on one of the picnic benches, rolling her aching ankles inside her boots, and Wanda handed her half a churro and a cup of cider. The soft glow of string lights flickered against Wanda’s red gloves, the satin of her hood pulled snug over her head.

"Don’t tell Ralph," she said, "but there’s a little surprise in there for you."

Agatha raised her eyebrows and took a sip, the faint taste of whiskey hitting the back of her tongue underneath the sweet spice. 

"You’re the greatest gift to this earth," Agatha said, clinking her paper cup against Wanda’s. "Where’s that tall, dapper husband of yours?"

"He took Tommy home after he ate, like, ten chicken fingers, ran twenty laps around the field, and then almost threw up."

"That kid’s like a mini Wade Bogs."

"I don’t even want to talk about it," Wanda laughed. "How do you feel?"

Agatha felt the heat of the whiskey unfurl in her chest, and watched a group of kids playing with one of the scarecrows, their costumes askew and faces flushed. Not too far away, Billy was pushing Nicky on the swings.

"Really fucking good, actually," Agatha whispered. 

The Halloween Harvest had been a decided success. From the sheer size of the crowd, the number of people who'd come up to chat with her, it was clear the entire town and then some had shown up. Ralph and his journalist friend—a twenty-something blonde dressed as Catwoman—had kept their distance. Ralph seemed far more preoccupied with the quality of the stitching on her costume, judging by his line of sight, and Agatha had been more than happy to stay out of their way. If Ralph wanted a quote, Catwoman was going to have to chase her down, which felt unlikely given the height of her heels.

The only incident had been a kindergartner named Charlie eating too much candy and throwing up behind the playground before his parents had a chance to intervene. But Happy had sped to the scene and cornered it off with  authority. Even Kristy had failed to find a single thing to complain about, inspecting the decorations before giving Agatha a reluctant grimace that almost resembled a smile.

"The haunted house looked amazing, by the way," Wanda said. "Best I’ve ever seen it."

"It was all Rio," Agatha admitted without thinking.

She’d done an airtight job of keeping Rio out of her head, but now, she'd cracked her own seal. The unease crept back in, and she felt the slow roll of Rio's gaze against her ribs. She sipped at her cider, willing back the high from a second ago, wanting to feel it stretch over her chest in that fuzzy way. 

"Nice work, you two." 

Agatha looked up and rolled her eyes. Paul approached them slow, hands in his pockets of his black suit, sunglasses dangling from his hand.

"Men in Black?" Wanda said. "You can do better than that."

Agatha hid her laugh behind her cup, and then cleared her throat. "You didn’t tell me you were coming." 

"What are you talking about? I called you yesterday."

"You told me Ralph was coming," she corrected, "not you."

"I thought that was implied."

Wanda sat back, eyeing them with interest. Agatha refused to look at her.

"You look great, by the way," Paul said, gesturing to Agatha’s dress. "Morticia again, right?"

Of course he fucking remembered. 

Wanda cleared her throat, slipping out from the bench. "I’m going to go make sure the boys are good," she said, and looked pointedly at Agatha before walking away.

Paul took her seat, and Agatha felt the discomfort settle. It was unfair, she knew, but there nonetheless.

"Listen," Paul said. "I know you and I haven’t always seen eye to eye, in regards to the job." 

Agatha snorted, eyes fixed on a spot in the distance.
