"But I’ve always respected you. And I know things have been tough recently, so I just wanted to, I don’t know, say that I’m here, for you, you know? If you ever want to grab a drink, and talk."

Agatha stared at him. "Paul," she said finally. "You call me maybe once every three to four months, when you need something from me. I know we used to be friends, but we’re in our forties now, and you barely know me. Cut the bullshit. What is this about?"

Paul sighed, running a hand through his hair, and looked at her, a little exasperated.

"I’m trying to ask you out, Agatha."

A sudden gust of wind blew past them, rustling the remains of discarded paper cups. She thought of a rooftop in Boston, and the pavement underneath a pink neon sign, and the double doors of the auditorium opening to the cold. She thought about Rio’s melting ice pack, and that scrawled list of to-do’s to be crossed off by today. She remembered the loops and scrawls under smudges of black dust, gathering first at the edges of the board, and then under the slow scrape of her fingernail. Some part of her had known since yesterday that she would have to cross this off, too. What a waste of black velvet.

"I was getting there," Paul added, and she heard the unmistakable note of deflation.

Agatha kept her eyes trained on the kids playing across the yard. She exhaled through her nose, her knee bouncing. This wasn’t catastrophic. Paul wasn’t important enough to be catastrophic. He wasn’t edgy enough to qualify as a mistake, either. In fact, he wasn’t anything enough that she could even call one dinner date a bad idea. This was, technically, a normal thing that normal people did. 

She'd been separated for four months, which wasn’t that long, but maybe long enough. Maybe it was time for Normal again. Maybe it was time to let someone take her to dinner while they pulled out her chair and asked, "How was your day?" And she could pluck an olive off the toothpick in her martini and say something seductive, like: "I don’t mix work and play," like people did in old movies before they had sex, or pulled off a diamond heist, or got shot sixteen times in the chest with a machine gun. 

Or, maybe instead of trying something new, or even something normal, she just needed to try something. Maybe it was time to get under someone’s skin, and then up against it. 

Paul wasn’t so bad, anyway, right? He used to show up at her first apartment with ice cream and beer, and they would watch reruns of Survivor over education theory flash cards. He was even funny, sometimes, in a goofy, inept kind of way. 

She looked down at the cider in her hands. She swirled the last drops at the bottom of the cup, but the words refused to come. The silence stretched across the picnic table, her fingers tightening around the paper. Maybe it would be time tomorrow.

She stood abruptly, crumpling the cup in her fist, and untangled her legs from the picnic table.

"I have to go," she said, smoothing her dress. "Teacher’s lounge, to check the… supplies. I’ll—let me get back to you."

"Agatha, wait–" she heard him call, but she didn’t turn, her toes pinching in her boots as she sped across asphalt.

 

 

The teachers’ lounge smelled of mixed leftovers, the remains of the potluck dinner for the staff and volunteers. She picked up a cold chicken drumstick from a foil tray and tore into it with her teeth, not bothering with a plate. The end of her sleeve dangled dangerously into a casserole dish, and she rolled them both up, switching hands, drumstick in her mouth. She was getting oil and grease on her dress, and she didn’t care. She wanted to rip it off entirely and hide under the table like a little kid at a picnic who didn’t want to be found.

"Hey."

Agatha snapped her eyes to Rio, standing in the doorway. How was this woman somehow nowhere to be found when Agatha needed her, and always fucking standing in a doorway when she didn’t?

Agatha dropped the bone in the trash can by her feet, finally reaching for a napkin, and took in Rio’s costume in the light.

The purple polyester was brighter under the fluorescence, her hair still stiff with green. Her gaudy makeup was fading, collecting at the lines around her mouth where she would have been grinning and yelling. The white and red smeared across her face, and Agatha noted regretfully that the effect added to her look. 

Rio was similarly taking her in, her eyes dragging down the length of the dress, again. Not saying anything, again. After a long moment of conspicuous silence, she grabbed a bottle of water and dropped heavily onto the couch. Agatha inexplicably wanted to ask where that woman was, but she kept her mouth shut. 

"Did you make these?" Rio asked, pointing to the last cookie remaining in Agatha’s tupperware. "I didn’t know you could bake."

Agatha glared at her. "Of course I can bake. They’re my famous chocolate chip cookies. The parents go rabid for them, even when they’re coming for my throat."

"Rabid?" Rio said, quirking an eyebrow. "What’s the secret ingredient? Blood of your enemies?"

"I’m not telling you." Agatha crossed her arms. It felt petulant, even as she said it.

"Okay," Rio drawled, eyes flaring in mock offense, and then settled back against the cushion. She stretched out her leg in front of her. 

"I saw Nicky’s costume, by the way. He looked awesome." She dug a thumb into the side of her knee, and Agatha watched. "I told him Wednesday was my favorite."

"I feel like I should be offended," Agatha muttered, leaning a hip against the table. Something was still needling at her, something like irritation, but hotter and more bitter. 

Rio eyed her, and her hand stilled on her knee. 

"I said she was my favorite," she said, eyes trailing over Agatha’s neckline. "Past tense."

Agatha felt that instant prickle of heat on the back of her neck under her straight, heavy hair. She swallowed, focusing on Rio’s leg.

"How’s the mysterious injury?" 

"Fine."

Agatha narrowed her eyes, hands smoothing over her dress. 

She thought of the way Rio had looked at her dress in the haunted house, mouth open. Maybe if this dress was all-powerful, she could at least use it to her advantage before she returned it to her closet for another decade. 

Agatha took three slow, purposeful steps closer, and Rio watched her. Agatha's shins bumped against the couch, and she was standing over her now, Rio’s injured leg stretched just to the side of her own. her other knee brushing against Agatha’s. 

"Tell me what happened," she said firmly. 

Rio looked up at her, her eyes dark. Agatha tried not to blink under the marble shine of her stare under the white makeup.

"No," Rio said simply, but her voice was low, her lips quirked with interest. 

Agatha wondered briefly if she looked like Nicky, standing there wearing her serious face, and if Rio was trying not to laugh at her. 

"Why not?" Agatha pressed, nudging Rio's knee lightly with her own. Rio's breath hitched, flinching at the contact. "What, did someone do something to you?"

Rio scoffed. "I was wondering when this was going to come up."

"What?"

"I was wondering how long it would take for you to assume that I’m in some terrible situation that I need to be saved from. I pulled a muscle. I don’t owe you all the dirty details."

Agatha gritted her teeth, and she could feel her forehead vein pulsing. She opened her mouth, and was interrupted by a knock at the open door. Paul was standing there, looking between them, and Agatha took an automatic step back. Her molten irritation cooled to mild dread and sank low in her stomach.

"Hey," he said, stepping in. "Sorry to interrupt, I just wanted to— can we talk for a second?"

Agatha resisted the urge to growl out loud, and she looked at Rio.

"Don’t mind me," Rio said lightly, crossing her arms. "I’m just going to eat this apparently god-sent cookie and mind my own business."

Agatha glared at her and beckoned Paul towards the door.

"I didn’t mean to pressure you," he started. "I know things just ended with you and Josh, but I figured it was a ‘now or never’ situation? And…yeah. To be clear: I would like to have dinner with you sometime. If you would like that."

Agatha was at a loss. She still couldn’t think of a good reason to say no. But was that enough of a reason to say yes?

"Paul, I…" she trailed off, her eyes fixing on the paper ghost on the wall. It hung askew, looking tired. Just a few more hours, she thought, looking at its torn bottom corner. She didn’t know whether she was talking to herself or the ghost, and then she thought that maybe it was a cry for help, that she was commiserating with a piece of paper.

"Hey, Agatha?" Rio called from across the room. 

‘Minding my own business,’ my ass.

"What," she snapped, secretly relieved at the interruption.

"Tell me the secret ingredient."

"I’m kind of in the middle of something here," Agatha said, gesturing in the general direction of Paul’s face. "I will email you the recipe—"

"No," Rio repeated, her voice raspier this time. She rubbed at her mouth, then cleared her throat, her brow furrowing slightly. "I really think you should tell me."

Agatha frowned, and turned to face her fully. 

"Why—" she started. And then she saw the way Rio's breath was coming in shorter bursts, the red creeping up her throat. The dread slammed fully into place, and Agatha’s stomach dropped.

"Oh, fuck me."

She stared in horror. After twenty years in a classroom, she knew the signs by heart: allergic reaction. Her legs were already moving.

"Coriander seeds," she said frantically, hopping over someone’s discarded bag to reach the couch. "No one is allergic to coriander, are you kidding me?"

"I’m allergic to coriander," Rio coughed, her eyes wide. 

"How bad?"

"Bad," Rio yelled, looking around wildly, a hand at her throat.

Agatha didn’t think, forcing Rio back onto the couch, her voice leveling automatically. "Where’s your epipen? Do you have one?" 

"Backpack, I think," she said, still coughing, and Agatha spotted it next to the armrest. She dropped to her knees, rifling through the pockets. "I don’t see it. Paul, Get the first aid kit. It’s in the corner cabinet; there should be one in there." She kept rifling through the backpack, her hands grasping at loose papers and granola bar wrappers.

"Your backpack is insane. What are you, twelve?" She winced at the familiar sting of a paper cut.

"I can’t believe you fucking poisoned me," Rio wheezed. 

"How would I know you were allergic to fucking coriander?" She checked the last pocket for the third time. "Paul!"

"Fuck, I’m looking, I’m looking!" Paul shouted, rifling through the cabinet.

"You made me fill out that stupid form," Rio said, still talking despite her heaving breaths, "on the first week of school. Emergency stuff, medical, allergies! You didn’t even read it?"

"Of course I didn’t fucking read it!" Agatha yelled, finally flinging the backpack to the side in one second of panic. The bag hit the empty box of cookies, papers spilling out over the floor.

"I got it, I got it!" Paul yelled, pulling the epipen free from the plastic bin and plucking off the safety release. Thank god he knows what he’s doing, Agatha thought briefly.

"Excuse me?" 

A muffled voice came from the doorway. A cloaked figure poked their head into the room, just behind Paul’s shoulder. 

Paul turned, and came face-to-face with a grotesque, lifelike Halloween mask, crisscrossed with muscled flesh and blood. 

He let out a high-pitched scream. His arm shot out and sank the epipen directly into the masked figure’s thigh. 

Agatha heard a gasp, and the loud, definite click of the pen. Then, for a split second, there was absolute, deafening silence. Paul took a step back, and the empty plastic clattered on the floor.

"Did you—" the masked figure ripped off the rubber, and of course, it was Natasha, clutching at her leg. "Oh my god, did you just stab me?"

"Paul, for fuck’s sake!" Agatha exploded, and now Natasha was yelling too. 

"Was that an epipen? What does that do? What’s going to happen to me? Oh my god."

Agatha heard Rio’s ragged breathing behind her, and her legs kicked into action again. She pushed past Paul and Natasha at a full sprint, grabbing the doorframe as she rounded the corner. She stumbled in the hallway just long enough to kick off her boots, one hitting the wall with a thud, the other landing somewhere behind her. 

She reached the door of her classroom, and grabbed the first aid kit whole without even looking. She spotted Jen and Wanda in the hallway, staring, and she bellowed, breathless: "WITH ME."

She didn’t stop, unzipping the red bag as she ran. Bandages and gauze fell over the hallway, and she grasped the epipen, the bag falling away entirely as she nearly skidded back into the lounge. 

She made it right to Rio, half collapsed on the couch, coughing, reaching for the pen in Agatha’s hand. Her red-rimmed eyes met Agatha’s.

"I got it," Agatha said quickly, batting her hand away. "One, two, three." She jabbed the pen, and heard the second sharp click. Rio exhaled through her nose, her eyes widening. Agatha held the pen in for three more seconds, and then tossed it aside, watching Rio’s face with laser focus.

She felt eerily calm. It was always the first way she knew there was a crisis—the way the world went quiet around the rushing of blood in her ears, her fingers steady, everything too bright and sharp. 

Her hand stayed firmly on Rio’s thigh, massaging the site. She counted twelve slow seconds before Rio took a deep, clear breath. She counted ten more after that before she took one of her own. 

She sat back on her heels, watching as Rio inhaled and exhaled. Agatha should have lifted her hand by now. It wasn’t needed anymore, but still, her fingers pressed into the muscle. 

The noise of the room filtered back in—Jen gripping Natasha’s arm, holding the empty epipen between them. Wanda’s finger was pointed so close to Paul's throat it was almost touching him, in the middle of delivering an earful that looked like a terrifying combination of mother’s and principal’s wrath.

Rio’s leg shifted under Agatha’s palm, the thick cord of muscle flexing. Rio tipped her head back lightly against the couch cushion, her eyes slipping closed. Her chest rose and fell, still too fast, and her fingers brushed against Agatha’s, and stayed there.

A thought flickered through Agatha’s mind, absurd and fleeting, but insistent. She was kneeling in front of Rio, her hand on her thigh, like some strange reverse of the night they'd met, and Agatha remembered way Rio had looked at her.

Rio had wrapped around her in the most impossible ways, like Solanum dulcamara —‘Bittersweet’—the gentler, slow-release cousin of Deadly Nightshade. Not mind-bending like the belladonna; Bittersweet poured straight into the body instead. It worked its way in through the muscle, through the lungs, through the soft skin on the back of her hand. 

Her grip slid from Rio’s thigh to her elbow.

"Let’s go," she murmured, hauling her upright. Rio’s feverish warmth radiated through her suit. "Hospital’s ten minutes away. I’m driving."

She caught Jen’s arm as they reached the door, voice low. "Take Natasha in. If she threatens to sue anyone, make sure it’s Paul, not the school, or me, for some fucking reason."

Jen almost laughed, a crazed look of panic on her face, and Agatha clapped her on the shoulder.

"Welcome to the grown-up table, J."

 

 

The car ride was short but harrowing, and Agatha swerved around every corner even though the roads were practically empty. Everyone in town really had been at her event. She took a moment to self-congratulate, and then looked at Rio.

She had her forehead pressed to the window, her breath fogging the glass in sharp bursts. Her free hand clutched at her chest. "I swear to god, Agatha, if you don’t fucking slow down, I’m going to throw up in your car."

"You’re just feeling the epi," Agatha said, speeding through the fifth consecutive stop sign. "You’re going to be fine."

Rio groaned, bracing a hand on the dashboard, the other clutched to her sternum. "God, I feel like a fucking lab rat on cocaine."

"You’re acting like it, too," Agatha muttered, honking at a cyclist who was three inches from losing an arm.

Rio whipped her head toward her, pupils blown. "And whose fucking fault is that?"

Agatha opened her mouth, but before she could form a retort, Rio lunged for the glove compartment, wrenching it open with shaky hands. Mostly loose receipts and an old pack of gum. She grabbed the first handful of trash and hurled it at Agatha.

"Rio," Agatha said, her voice rising. "It was an accident."

"FUCK YOU," Rio shouted. Crumpled parking lot stubs and stale gum wrappers pelted Agatha’s shoulder and flew across her field of vision.

By some miracle, they made it to the emergency room in one piece, albeit at full volume. Somehow, a nurse understood what they were saying through all the yelling, and brought over a wheelchair for Rio’s shaky, unsteady legs.

Rio was still fully dressed as the joker, purple suit and green hair, makeup smeared. She didn’t even stand out, the waiting room full of people in various costumes and states of distress—a Batman with his arm wrapped in bloody gauze, Beetlejuice with a hand clasped to her eye.

"What happened?" a nurse was asking.

"She fucking poisoned me," Rio yelled, pointing at Agatha. "Is this because of the Charles Manson thing? I said I was fucking sorry, I bought you a mug," she continued, her voice hoarse now, and Agatha’s mouth fell open. 

The nurses swiveled their heads to stare at Agatha as Rio continued. The pitch of her accusations bounced off of every wall as a nurse forcibly lowered her into the wheelchair.

"Who the fuck puts coriander in chocolate chip cookies?"

"It tastes like citrus," Agatha yelled back. "What kind of grown-ass adult with a life-threatening allergy eats a cookie with a secret ingredient, and doesn’t have an epipen?"

"What kind of grown-ass adult has to bake cookies just so that the PTA will fucking jerk off to you like they used to?" Rio was still yelling, twisted around in the wheelchair even as the nurses wheeled her away, her arms waving wildly. "Oh my god, they’re all right, Agatha! you are fucking crazy!"

"You’re fucking crazy!" Agatha stormed after them, shoving a rolling chair out of her way. It clattered against the wall, the noise echoing down the corridor.

"Okay, ma’am," a nurse said, stepping into her path with a firm palm up. "I'm going to have to ask you to wait here."

Agatha stopped short, fists clenched tightly.

"Are you serious? She’s kidding, I obviously didn’t POISON HER!" She yelled the last words, making sure Rio could still hear her as she wheeled farther down the hallway. She sucked in a breath, chest rising and falling. She might as well have injected herself.

"If I did poison her," she muttered, still staring after Rio, "she’d be so fucking dead."

Rio was long out of sight, and the nurse—Darcy , according to her badge—was lazily chewing her gum. She tapped the clipboard with her pen.

"So, legally, I have to ask: have you ever considered poisoning that woman, yourself, or anyone else?"

Agatha whipped her head around to stare.

"No." 

Darcy clicked her pen and jotted something down. "Okay, but you did just say you wanted her dead, so—"

"I did not say I wanted her dead." Agatha grabbed the clipboard out of her hands and slammed it on the desk next to them. "Where did they find you?"

Darcy crossed her arms. "Wow. That was rude. What if I went to Cornell?"

"Did you?" 

"No. But I did take a Masterclass. Twenty-first century, bitch." Darcy struck a pose.

Agatha gritted her teeth. "I changed my mind. I have thought about poisoning someone."

"Don’t worry, babe," Darcy said. "We’ll take good care of your girlfriend and I’ll let you know when you can go see her." 

"She’s—" Agatha started, but the words died on her tongue. Darcy was already returning to her station behind the desk. At a loss, she collapsed into the nearest plastic chair and, in a sudden burst of frustration, kicked the metal trash can next to her. It toppled over, tissues spilling across the floor.

Four people in the waiting room turned to stare at her. Agatha slumped deeper into the chair, head in her hands, and the exhaustion rolled over her in the wake of adrenaline. 

A loud, wet noise broke the silence, and Agatha looked up. A boy dressed as Frankenstein stared at her as he blew his nose. He glanced at the overturned trash can, then at the tissue in his hand. Then back at the trash can again. Then back at the tissue. Agatha leaned forward, plucked the tissue from his hand, and tossed it onto the floor with the rest of the trash.

"I have mono," the boy said. He turned and walked back to the chair next to his mother.

"Of course you fucking do," Agatha muttered, and leaned her head back against the wall.

 

 

It was almost eleven when they let Agatha in to see Rio. They'd put her in the pediatric wing—limited space on Halloween night—and Agatha tried to keep her flashbacks at bay as they walked down the colorful hallways. Hospitals always tried throwing up patterned wallpaper to cover the fear and grief that lived under the plaster. As if a string of cartoon monkeys holding hands could erase the anxiety that ripped through her chest at the sight of the ICU door.

She thought of Nicky, his first precarious taste of the world, three months too soon, with the umbilical cord wrapped around his neck, face turning blue. He was so tiny, his entire body could fit cradled in Josh's two hands, skin like tissue paper, tube taped to his nose and mouth. She shuddered. Of all her ability to stay calm in crisis, nothing scraped at her nerves quite like the pediatric wing.

She'd called Josh and Wanda, said goodnight to Nicky, and checked in with Jen, who'd spent an hour at the hospital with Natasha before she was discharged. Jen had been conspicuously certain that Natasha wouldn’t be pursuing legal action. Agatha didn’t know exactly how Jen had pulled that off, but she could make a solid guess, and she made a mental note to add that to the list of discussion topics at their next happy hour.

Agatha pulled the door open quietly, but Rio was awake. 

She was clean of makeup, her hair brown again, damp and curling at the ends. A few stray strands clung to the curve of her neck. The only evidence of The Joker was the folded pile of purple by the bathroom door, and the towels in the trash can, streaked with green hairspray. Something about her like this—hair still wet, eyelids heavy with sleep—pulled at Agatha’s chest. 

"Hey," she said, lingering in the doorway, unsure how much of Rio’s anger had faded.

Rio half-smiled, her voice groggy. "Why are you still here?"

Agatha laughed on an exhale and stepped inside. "I’m sorry for accidentally poisoning you. Hospital bill’s on me."

"Seriously?" 

"Already taken care of."

"That's... thank you," Rio said, finally. She yawned, the rest of her words coming slow. "Sorry I accused you of attempted murder in public."

"Let’s call it even." Agatha waved her hand. "It was the epi." 

"It was the epi," Rio repeated, scrunching her nose.

Agatha pointed to the chair in the corner beside the bed, raising her eyebrows in question, and Rio nodded as she settled comfortably in. 

"Nice room. Sophisticated." Agatha eyed a poster of a giraffe above Rio’s head, and Rio rolled her eyes. At least, she tried, but it was really more of a long, slow blink. 

The door opened again, a woman’s head and shoulder poking through. The woman glanced at Agatha, then at Rio.

"Yeah, it’s fine," Rio muttered, shifting in bed.

Agatha raised an eyebrow at her as the woman stepped inside.

"Dr. Danvers," she said, shaking Agatha’s hand.

"Agatha Harkness," she responded, clearing her throat. She wasn’t sure what else she was supposed to say. She hoped the doctor wouldn’t ask her anything else.

"Like we discussed," Dr. Danvers said, referencing her clipboard. "Everything looks okay for now, and we can let you go in a couple hours. I’ll prescribe you some steroids for the next few days to make sure we don’t see you back here."

"You sure you don’t want to prescribe me anything else?" Rio asked with a smirk, raising her eyebrows. The doctor smiled.

"Yeah, I wasn’t finished," she said. "The leg is likely a mild knee sprain—sounds like you know the drill with that one, so just keep icing and rest a little next week, and you should be fine. Call if it gets worse. I also wrote you a new prescription for your inhaler, so that’ll be ready before you leave. And then, I noticed you don’t have an emergency contact listed; did you want to put someone down?" She glanced at Agatha.

Rio’s face flushed. Agatha stared. She'd never seen her do that, not once.

"Uh, no," Rio said. "It’s fine. My roommate doesn’t have a phone."

"You have a roommate?" Agatha said, forgetting to stay quiet, then processed the rest of her sentence. "Who… doesn’t have a phone?"

Rio grinned. "She’s meant for bigger things than modern technology. Or so she says."

"Okay then," the doctor said. "Any other questions for me?"

"Nope. Thanks, Dr. D."

Agatha exhaled, leaning her head against the wall once the doctor left.

"You have asthma?" The words slipped out. Agatha felt the looming unease of the truth. She might know that Rio was ambidextrous, and drank her coffee black, and knew Charles Manson quotes. She might even know what the tattoo on her ribcage looked like in painstaking detail. But she didn’t know her, not really. 

"Yup." Rio scrubbed a hand over her face.

"Didn’t know this hospital bill was covering all of your preexisting conditions," Agatha muttered. "Why don’t you check some moles out, while you’re at it?"

"I didn’t ask you to pay."

"You have student insurance. That would be cruel and unusual."

Rio smiled, and then winced, her hand falling to her knee. 

"Rio." Agatha turned her head just enough to watch her. "Why won’t you just tell me what happened?"

Rio looked sullen, laying her head back on the bed, and brushed her damp hair out of her eyes. "Because it’s embarrassing," she said finally, tapping her knee. 

"You’re kidding," Agatha said, incredulous. "You’ve watched me make a fool of myself on no less than three separate occasions in the last two months, and you’re holding out on me because you’re embarrassed?"

Agatha was relieved despite her indignation. Until now, she hadn’t been sure Rio had ever felt embarrassed a day in her life. Embarrassing, she could handle.

"Fine," Rio said, tucking an elbow behind her head in her hospital gown. Her eyes trailed over a Family Circus comic taped to the ceiling. 

"A couple nights ago, a group of frat guys were in. Standard twenty-one year-olds. We get them all the time."

Agatha wrinkled her nose in mild disgust, and Rio caught the look with a dry smile. "They're usually the most harmless, because they’re secretly terrified. It’s even a little fun, sometimes."

Agatha swallowed, drawing an unwelcome parallel.

"This one guy was super drunk, and he was the one spending all the money. Rich parents, probably. He said he’d pay me six hundred bucks if I did ten reverse crunches off of his shoulders."

Agatha felt her jaw fall open. "But why… I mean, what does that even… do for him?"

Rio shrugged. "He didn’t really know what he was doing; he just wanted to be obnoxious in front of his friends. But I’ve done much more for six hundred. Reverse crunches are no problem." She picked at a small scratch on her hand. "So he lifted me up and I got my legs up around his face."

Agatha blinked, trying to keep her expression even slightly neutral. 

"And I went for it. It’s mostly all legs at that angle, anyway. My thighs were definitely squeezing his carotid artery, so I was kind of hoping he’d pass out, but he didn’t."

Agatha tried and failed not to imagine it, Rio’s thighs squeezing slowly, the man falling to his knees.

"And then what?" she asked. She couldn’t tell if she was more horrified or awed.

"I had already tweaked my knee at school, trying to keep Gemma from jumping off of the slide, because she was putting all her fucking weight on me."

She was staring at the ceiling comic again.

"So I had my legs around this guy and I could feel my knee pulling every time I flexed it, but I had already done three, so I just kept going."

Agatha stared at her. 

"That’s it?" she said finally. "You knew your leg was already hurt, and you hung upside down after a fourteen hour day? That’s unbelievably stupid."

Rio glared at her. "I’m sorry, would you have preferred that some beefy dude threw me into a wall and broke my fucking leg?"

"Jesus, of course not. Just… why didn’t you want to tell me that? It doesn’t really feel like that big a deal."

Rio’s fingers traced her knee, and leaned her head back again. She closed her eyes for a second, considering, and then rolled her head towards Agatha. 

"You know how I told you I used to be a dancer? Like, a real one?"

Agatha nodded. 

"I was good," Rio said, and her eyes had taken on a wistful look. Agatha felt that same twinge in her chest.

"I was really, really fucking good."

Rio smiled, her eyes unfocused, and then her face shifted into something else. "One day, something didn’t feel right, and I performed anyway. It was a big competition; I couldn’t just go home. But in the middle of the piece, my knee twisted, and I landed wrong. And that was… it. I somehow managed to do more damage to my knee with a single fall than... well, probably getting thrown into a wall."

Agatha swallowed. Rio’s voice was detached, clinical, but she could feel the weight of her words landing.

"I was nineteen, and I hadn’t gone to college because I was making a living winning. I was supporting myself, and then it was all just… gone."

Agatha’s hands tightened around the armrest. "What about your parents?" 

Rio shook her head, looking at the wall.

"It was a stupid reason," she said after a beat. "It was a stupid reason to get injured. No crazy accident, not even a mental breakdown leading to my downfall. Just one sore morning, and one wrong step."

"You know that’s not what I meant," Agatha said, her voice low. 

"It happened, and I got my shit together, and I’m here now. I like teaching. And I’m making money dancing, technically."

The guarded look was back in Rio's eyes, the one that meant Agatha should definitely stop talking.

"You’re asthmatic and you have a bad knee," she said anyway. "And you're dancing in stilettos in a smoky club after working a full day at school. That can’t be good for you—you’re showing up late and passing out in your car."

"Back off," Rio snapped, quick and loud. Her voice was edged with exhaustion, and her hands fisted into the blanket. "I’m thirty fucking years old, Agatha; I’m not a child. " 

Agatha pressed her lips together, eyes flitting away from Rio’s hard stare and landing on the side table between them. A container of red jello sat on top of a coloring placemat and a packet of crayons. Rio followed her gaze, and her nostrils flared.

"They have those in all of the rooms," she said.

Agatha squinted, a smile pulling at her lips.

"You colored the elephant."

"I was fucking bored," Rio grumbled, and turned to face the ceiling again. 

Agatha glanced toward the window, catching Dr. Danvers walk by, her blond ponytail swinging. Agatha found herself wondering suddenly if Rio found her attractive. The doctor was definitely—what had Rio called it? A head bitch in charge. Agatha looked at her and remembered what it felt like, having her hands around a clipboard, anchoring her to the command of the room. 

She cleared her throat. "So," she said, watching Rio carefully. She had a fully formed question in her head, but what came out was: "Natasha."

"Wow," Rio shot back, eyeing her. "You want to talk about that? She's some kid's aunt from out-of-town, and she was flirting with me. I was totally above board."

"I'm sure," Agatha said, brushing her hair back. "She seems… nice. Jen said she’s not pressing charges on Paul."

"Jen?" Rio narrowed her eyes. "Jen stole my date, didn’t she?"

"Probably."

She groaned, tilting her head back, and then waggled her eyebrows. "At least I’ve got Dr. Danvers." 

Agatha snorted, and felt the knot in her chest loosen a little. Asked and answered. "Look who’s finding the silver lining," she said dryly, inspecting the label on the jello cup. "The incidentally very tall, hot, silver lining."

Agatha was still wearing her dress, and her emergency sandals from her car, and she felt suddenly uncomfortable in the cold hospital room. She looked at Rio, pulling the blankets tighter around her shoulders.

It was that moment, Agatha knew. She had already exhausted the flimsy list of reasons she should still be there. She should say something boring and expected, like, "Well, I should be heading out," and then stand up and actually leave. She thought about the messy auditorium, waiting for her direction, and her empty house waiting for her after that.

"If you have to go," Rio said lightly, "don’t worry about it. I’m sure Nicky wants you back."

Agatha looked at her, lying there with circles under her eyes and no emergency contacts. They were three hours past crisis, and now Rio was just tired, and Agatha wondered if she had no one to call for non-emergencies, either.

Agatha looked at the red jello on the side table.

"You going to eat that?" she asked, already reaching for it.

Rio shook her head, her lips curving upwards. 

"You like jello?" she said, disgusted as Agatha slurped. "That is surprising and terrible."

"Oh, sure, let’s talk about terrible surprises," Agatha said, her mouth full. She pointed to Rio with the spoon, and a small glob of jello landed on the floor. "Any other critical medical conditions I need to know about?"

"Enough. We’re watching TV."

Rio reached to the other side for the remote, and winced, the table just out of reach. Agatha stood wordlessly, circling to retrieve it. She set down her jello, her mouth sufficiently sweet, and turned the TV on. She perched on the side of her bed, flipping through the channels.

"What are you into?" she said, scrolling. "Reality? Documentary?"

"Wait, stop," Rio said, grabbing her arm. "Look."

Agatha snorted and set down the remote, taking in Morticia Addams on the screen with Wednesday. She felt Rio scoot over, and it was that tiny shift that made her realize she was sitting on Rio’s bed. She turned slightly, glancing at her. Rio was watching her.

"Are you going to sit like that the whole time?" Rio said. 

"Oh. No, sorry." Agatha stood, smoothing down her dress.

"No, I meant—" Rio stifled a laugh. "Sit like a normal person. You look like Elf on a Shelf."

Agatha snorted, and eyed the empty spot. She hesitated. She was somewhat aware that it was odd, hesitating now, given how they'd met. But that was different—that was before. 

She felt a yawn positively slam into her, eyes watering, and ran a hand over her face. Fuck it, she thought. Sitting uncomfortably was for people under forty. She leaned back, swinging her legs up on top of the blanket. She didn’t think about the soap smell from Rio’s face and hair, the faint chemical scent of hairspray, the heat radiating from her. She didn’t think about the way the thin mattress dipped under her weight, tilting Agatha just slightly towards her, their shoulders brushing.

Wednesday Addams was buying girl scout cookies on the TV, and when she said it, perfectly deadpan: "Are they made from real Girl Scouts?" They both burst out laughing, the bed shaking precariously and sending them further into peals of laughter.

Agatha didn’t think about the way Rio clutched at her leg as she laughed, fingers digging lightly into her thigh. The movement was familiar. Agatha’s hand, steady on purple nylon in the midst of chaos, and now, Rio’s grip fluttering over black velvet in reply. The symmetry was comforting. Agatha didn’t let herself wonder when Rio would let go.

 

 

Thirty minutes into the movie, Rio's head was starting to bob against her shoulder, and Agatha’s phone rang. She pulled herself out of the bed with some effort, showing the screen to Rio in explanation, and stepped out of the room, her chest still buoyant from the feeling of watching a good movie with good company.

"Hey."

She leaned against the wall opposite the small window in the door. She could see Rio, her eyes still heavy-lidded, focused on the movie. 

"How’s she doing?" Wanda said through a yawn.

"Good," Agatha said. "Annoying. But good."

"Well, you did almost kill her."

"Thank you for the reminder." Agatha leaned her head back against the wall. The waiting room felt too far. "You still at school?" 

"Yeah. Happy and I led the clean-up crew, and we’re just about done."

"Fuck, I’m sorry," Agatha said. "I should have come back to help."

"You have an acceptable excuse," Wanda said. "You’re a good friend. I just didn’t know Rio had made it to the friend circle."

Agatha felt her brow twitch, and bit at her peeling lower lip. She had been biting it too much recently. 

"I wasn’t going to just leave her to find the hospital on her own," Agatha said finally. "She doesn’t live here."

"I’m sure she appreciates it," Wanda said, and if Agatha didn’t know her so well, she would have thought she was hinting at something. 

"I owe you one." Agatha knew the majority of work would  come tomorrow, the heavy lifting and sweeping and mopping and disposing of dirty paper and fake blood. But she also knew how much weight she pulled, and the resulting weight of her absence.

"Hey," she added. "You know Alice? The PE teacher? What’s her deal?"

"She’s great," Wanda said. "One of those people that are just effortlessly cool. The kids are obsessed with her. We’re lucky we have her, honestly."

"Wow," Agatha said, a little surprised at the heartfelt endorsement. "Why have I never talked to her before?"

"Because you never talk to anyone," Wanda said. "But glad to hear your little time in the spotlight has an upside."

Agatha laughed, a hand to her forehead. She trailed off, leaning one of her feet back against the wall. She cleared her throat, and then went silent. 

"What?" Wanda said.

"Nothing."

"Agatha."

She sighed. "Paul… asked me out."

"Well, about fucking time," Wanda said. "The man practically drools when you walk into a room. What did you say?"

"I didn’t. There were kind of more pressing issues at the time."

"What are you going to say?"

Agatha looked at Rio in the hospital bed, her collarbone peeking out of the gown. She'd turned on her side, curled up a little, her eyes glued to the screen. She looked strangely small under the blanket. 

Dr. Danvers passed Agatha in the hallway, offering her a polite smile. Agatha turned, watching her straight posture.

"Dr. Danvers," she called. "Wanda—I have to go. I’ll call you tomorrow."

"Go out with him," Wanda said.

"What?" Agatha hissed into the phone, and looked at the doctor apologetically.

"Go out with Paul. You need to put yourself back out there. You haven’t had sex in like, what, a year? You really need to take the low-hanging fruit before—"

Agatha jabbed at the red button.

"Sorry," she said to the doctor, waiting patiently nearby.

"No problem. How can I help, Morticia?"

"Oh," Agatha said with a laugh, looking down at her dress. She'd forgotten she was still wearing it. "It’s Agatha."

"I know." Dr. Danvers smiled. 

Agatha blinked, feeling suddenly flustered. "I just wanted to let you know—my…" she gestured to the room. "Rio’s emergency contact. You can just put me down."

"Okay, sure. I’ll have Darcy get that info from you on your way out."

"Thanks." Agatha looked up at the taller woman, her rolled up sleeves and hands in the pocket of her lab coat. She thought of Wanda’s words: effortlessly cool. Did everyone have that except her?

"How much longer do you need to keep her here?" Agatha asked. 

"Probably not more than another hour, because of the asthma. Just want to make sure she’s in the clear."

"Got it. And just for my…additional knowledge," Agatha said, crossing her arms, "let’s say someone had asthma."

Dr. Danvers looked at her curiously.

"Not Rio, obviously," Agatha added, "But some other, random person with asthma. If that person worked at an enclosed location that was filled with cigarette smoke, anywhere from twenty to thirty hours a week, would that be considered safe?"

"I thought she was a teacher," Dr. Danvers said, narrowing her eyes. She was still eyeing Agatha with that look of intrigue.

"Right. She is," Agatha said. "Totally hypothetical. Just interested in expanding my medical knowledge."

"Right," the doctor repeated, smiling. "Well, in my professional opinion, hypothetically, no. That would definitely not be advised, and could certainly worsen symptoms."

"Great," Agatha said. "Thank you. I’ve learned a lot."

"Anytime. Feel free to go grab something from the cafeteria, by the way—they’re about to close for the night."

Agatha nodded, brushing her hair behind her ear, and peeked back into the room. Rio was still curled up, her eyes blinking long and slow. She yawned, the blanket slipping to the side, and Agatha felt the pull to adjust it. She clenched her hand at her side, and turned on her heel.

 

 

Agatha walked back down the hallway in her sandals, two cups of herbal tea in her hand. She wanted coffee, late as it was, but they'd run out, so she'd settled for peppermint. 

She reached the door and was about to push it open with her shoulder when she caught a glimpse of Rio through the window. Agatha followed her gaze to Dr. Danvers, her ponytail swinging as her shoulders shook. Rio smirked, her tongue in the side of her cheek. 

"Fuck," Agatha muttered, the cups starting to burn. She considered putting them on an empty gurney down the hall, then looked at Rio again, and set them down right where she stood. She paced then, a familiar, unwelcome feeling in her stomach.

She pulled her bag over her shoulder firmly, turning toward the waiting room. She'd only made it four steps before Dr. Danvers exited Rio’s room and called her name. Her real name, this time.

Agatha turned. "Dr. Danvers."

"Carol."

"Carol," Agatha repeated slowly.

"Here." Carol reached into her pocket and pulled out two colorful bandaids. She held them out in front of Agatha. "Do you have a preference?"

"I... don’t think I need one," Agatha said, "but thank you, anyway."

"Your finger is bleeding," Carol said. "Looks like a paper cut. We don’t really love open wounds around here."

Agatha glanced down in surprise, and her finger was, in fact, bleeding. She picked the bandaid with dinosaurs on it, because Nicky would like it. She put it on silently, slightly embarrassed to be receiving elementary first aid from, she was sure now, a pediatrician.

"I hope you don’t mind me asking," Carol said, "But what’s your deal? You and Rio?"

Agatha felt her stomach do that twisty thing. "We work together. There’s nothing…you know, no worries there. If you wanted to…" She trailed off, gesturing uselessly.

Carol eyed her again, amused. "I don’t date my patients," she said.

"Oh. Right, of course," Agatha nodded.

Carol shifted her weight, tilting her head. "But you’re not my patient."

Agatha opened her mouth. Nothing came out. She coughed, trying to force words back into her throat.

"I—me?" she finally managed.

Carol raised a brow, amused. "You make a compelling Morticia. And you seem…" she tilted her head. "Interesting."

Agatha’s pulse roared in her ears.

"I’m…flattered," she said, and she could feel the heat in her cheeks. "But I've never…I mean, I don’t….." She cleared her throat. "I don’t date women. I’m sorry."

"Do you want to?" Carol asked without missing a beat, and Agatha blinked. Before she could stop herself, her eyes flicked toward Rio’s door. Just for a second.

Just one second.

But when she glanced back, Carol’s eyes held a suspicious look of understanding, even though, no, stop, there was nothing here to understand.

"Ah," Carol said with a smile. "No worries. Hope it works out."

"Hope what…" Agatha felt weak, her pulse threadbare. Carol just smiled, gave her a polite nod, and brushed past.

Agatha all but gripped the wall as she walked to the waiting room. She sat heavily in a chair, the same chair she'd spent two hours in earlier that night. She felt feverish, her throat tight. This was all too much: Rio in the hospital, Agatha in her bed, being asked out twice in one day, once by a woman. 

Before she could spiral any further, she pulled out her phone. 

"Hey." Paul’s voice came over the phone, sleepy.

"Yes," Agatha said. 

"What?" 

"Yes, I will go to dinner with you. Next week. How’s Thursday?"

There was a beat of silence, and Agatha put her head in her hand for a second. What the fuck was she doing?

"Wow, okay. Yes. Thursday." Paul sounded utterly surprised, and Agatha tried not to feel bad for him.

"One more thing. Shop talk," Agatha said, massaging her temple. She tried to ignore the thought of Rio, alone in the hospital room, curled on her side. 

"Shoot."

"I need an extension on my student teacher eval," Agatha sighed. "I know I’m already late, I just– I need more time. When is the absolute latest I can turn it in?"

"Well I just sent them out this morning. So, yesterday. But for you, end of the week."

"I promise I'll get it to you by Friday."

"Hold on," Paul interrupted. "I could have sworn I saw yours. Vidal, right?"

Agatha snorted. "Well that would be great, but no, I haven’t—" she stopped mid sentence. 

Her stomach plummeted. 

No. She had. She had filled out Rio’s eval, exactly once, a month ago. After three margaritas. 

She'd closed her computer. She knew she had. Hadn’t she?

"Paul," she said, suddenly nauseous. "I need you to check, right fucking now."

"Okay. Give me a second." 

Agatha waited, her knuckles turning white around the phone. The room was spinning.

"Yup, here it is. You submitted it in September. Wow; have you ever turned anything in that early?"

"Please tell me you forgot to send that one," she said, light-headed.

"Nope, got ‘em all," Paul replied,  cheerful and oblivious to the end of the world as Agatha knew it.

"Did you read it before you fucking sent it?" Agatha almost yelped.

"Of course, I read all of them!"

"Cut the bullshit!" Agatha yelled, frantic.

"Okay, okay," Paul said. "I may not have read all of them. Or any of them. It doesn’t really matter anyway, It’s not like I ever do anything with those evals except send them out for the student teachers to review. They barely have any implications unless someone is an actual disaster. I'm sure whatever you wrote was good enough."

"I have to go," Agatha said faintly. It was the second time she'd told him that today.

She needed to get out of here. She needed to throw her phone into the river. And then her laptop. And then her car, with her still in it.

She stood up, pacing. What exactly had she even written? Maybe it wasn’t that bad, right? She wracked her brain, willing the words back.

"Come on, come on," she muttered, tapping her phone in her palm, squeezing her eyes shut. "Remember." 

She stopped short. Her eyes flew open. A sentence popped into her head. She sank to the carpeted floor of the waiting room, head in her hands, her legs giving out. She was absolutely, truly, deeply fucked.



 

Chapter End Notes

well that was like 3x longer than i thought it would be.... but hope you're enjoying the ride as much as i am 🎢

Moonlighting

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

 

“All of us invent ourselves. Some of us just have more imagination than others.” ― Cher

 

The rain hadn’t let up since it started, exactly two minutes after Agatha had pulled out of the school parking lot this afternoon. She’d left her jacket and umbrella in Wanda’s office, somewhere between Wanda saying, Are you sure you’re okay? and Agatha snapping, Why do you keep asking me that? Her tone had been so sharp that Wanda had taken a half-step back in surprise. Then she laid a gentle hand on Agatha’s shoulder, and told her to skip the PTA meeting, just this once, and go home.

Now, she was sitting in her idling car in the pouring rain, one hour away from said home. The rain hammered her windshield, and she turned off her engine, cutting off Cher mid-verse. “After all that’s said and done,” then sudden silence. The water collected on the windows, obscuring the brownstone across the street into blurry red brick.

It had been five days at school without Rio. She'd been reporting them as sick days, but Agatha knew the truth, stomach knotting tighter with each passing morning.

The students had gone decidedly feral as a result of the disruption in their routine—kids were like little barometers that way. Especially children of divorce, or trauma, or separation, or all the other flavors of abandonment, who were all a little extra restless. 

That was Nicky, now, too, Agatha realized with a start. He refused to listen to her instructions in class. He would take his shoes and socks off whenever he damn well felt like it, despite Agatha’s hundreds of attempts to have conversations with him at school, in the car, or at home.

Today, she’d finally cracked. Ellie and Luca were placing another card atop Rio’s shrine in the corner of the classroom, (you would think she fucking died), and Kara had asked her for the seventh time that day when Ms. V was coming back. And Agatha had said, in a voice that was not quite a yell but definitely louder than she meant it to be: “I don’t know, and asking me more times isn’t going to make her magically show up.”

Kara had sat sullenly in the corner for the rest of the day, and Agatha had apologized and tried her hardest to make it through the rest of the day without yelling at anyone else.

Wanda had only asked once if Agatha knew what Rio was sick with. Agatha had said, “Are you sure she said she was sick? Like, did she specifically say the word ‘sick?’” 

Wanda had crossed her arms, looked at her shrewdly, and said: “Why would she say anything else?” 

Agatha hadn’t even planned on coming here. One minute, she was in the school parking lot, staring down the barrel of the weekend. The next, she was stalled outside Rio’s apartment like a stalker. She would settle for seeing Rio’s face, and checking just how deep the anger ran in the lines around her mouth to see if any of this was salvageable.

She looked at the pathetic, grease-stained pastry bag on the passenger seat, and the coffee in the cupholder. Maybe she should have brought soup instead of gas station donuts, in the event that Rio really was bedridden.

She squinted out of the blurred window, then down at her phone for the tenth time. Rio’s onboarding paperwork was still open—more specifically, her address, zoomed in so tight that ‘APARTMENT #3’ filled her entire screen. Because, of course, she'd also forgotten to wear her contacts. She thought of Cher, and what she didn’t say, and pushed the door open. 

 

 

By the third press of the buzzer, Agatha's clothes were soaked through, and she'd come to conclusion that the button was broken.

The door swung open, and Agatha caught it with her shoulder as a man brushed past her. “It’s broken,” he shot in her direction. 

It had been a while since she’d heard a Southie accent. Oyster Bend felt impossibly farther from Boston than one hour. His voice reminded her of her mother's wide vowels and sharp tone. She felt suddenly nostalgic, getting yelled at with lazy r’s as she ducked behind secondhand furniture.

“Tell her she needs to walk down the fuckin’ stairs to get her clients,” the man continued. “I don’t care if she’s in the middle of a fuckin’ seance, or summoning Bloody Mary, or whatevah.”

“Right, I’ll tell her.” Agatha almost smiled, and ducked inside.

 

 

Within a second of laying eyes on the third-floor apartment, Agatha realized exactly who the man had been referring to. A large, crudely painted sign hung on the door:

PSYCHIC READINGS, PALM READINGS, TAROT.

The smell of sage was thick, even in the hallway. Agatha hesitated, knuckles raised, fist still gripping the wet donut bag.

Before she could knock, the door opened. Agatha lowered her fist.

A woman stood there, draped in two colorful, clashing shawls, and the sage hit Agatha at full force. She opened her mouth to introduce herself, and sneezed three times.

"You must be the teacher," the woman said, eyeing Agatha with disdain.

"Wow, nice." 

"Your shirt," she said vaguely, then turned, leaving the door open behind her. Agatha cursed, pulled her flannel tighter around her Oyster Bend t-shirt, and stepped inside.

“You must be Rio’s roommate,” Agatha called, taking in the camped living room through the haze of smoke. Beaded curtains hung in front of a door off the hallway, and Agatha heard a clattering—the kitchen, she assumed. Candles rested on every available surface, lit and unlit, some perched precariously next to newspapers and old magazines.

“Lilia,” the woman said, emerging with two cups of loose leaf tea that she couldn’t possibly have had time to brew in the thirty seconds since Agatha had come in. She set them down on the coffee table and sat expectantly on the velvet sofa. 

"Agatha," she said, taking a seat opposite and extending a hand. "Nice to meet you."

"Can’t say the same," Lilia replied, sipping her tea. She glanced at Agatha’s outstretched palm.

“Right,” Agatha said after a beat. 

“I can assume she’s not here, then?”

“Why should I tell you where she is, or isn’t?” Lilia said. “From what I hear, you tried to toss her to the wolves.”

“I didn’t—” Agatha gritted her teeth. “You know what—I don’t have to explain myself to you. I don’t even know you.”

“I’m finding that I feel similarly about you.” Lilia sat back. “I’ve heard the highlights, of course, but Rio left out some… intriguing details.”

Her gaze swept from Agatha’s wet hair to the coffee cup, then to the crumpled paper bag on the table. Agatha felt heat creep up her face at the scrutiny. She restrained herself from asking for immediate clarification, and took a sip of her tea instead, gagging at the taste as she swallowed. 

“God, what is this?” She wiped at her mouth, wishing she could reach in and scrape the bitter taste off of her tongue.

“Moringa tea. An ancient remedy, known to cure hundreds of ailments.”

“It tastes ancient. No offense to its… magical powers.”

“The Tree of Life doesn’t care if you believe in it or not,” Lilia replied smoothly, sipping from her own floral teacup.

Tree of life. Black ink flickered into Agatha's mind, the branches and leaves that curled along Rio’s ribcage.

Lilia’s eyes narrowed. “Yes,” she said.

Agatha shifted in her seat.

“Moringa. Native to India. Known as the Miracle Tree, or the Tree of Life,” Lilia continued. “It flowers year-round and thrives in almost any condition.”

Without warning, she reached into the paper bag and pulled out the single donut. She inspected it for half a second, then took a bite. A glob of jelly landed on her finger. 

Agatha scoffed. “That was for—”

Lilia held up a hand, chewing.

“It’s wet,” she said. “What was the plan here? That you’d run through the rain, looking like a stray dog, all miserable and forlorn, and hand Rio a soggy donut? And then what? All would be forgiven?”

“No, of course not,” Agatha grumbled, and watched her take another bite. “And I’m not forlorn,” she added under her breath. She was still trying to figure out how she ended up in this smoky, messy apartment being scolded by a stranger. 

Lilia finished chewing and licked the jelly off her finger. “She mentioned you drive her crazy,” she mused. “But I didn’t get the impression you were stupid.”

“Okay,” Agatha snapped, bracing her hands on her thighs. “This has been a delightful waste of time, but if you’re not going to tell me where she is, I’m just going to keep leaving her voicemails until she picks up, and I'll tell her it was your idea.”

Lilia snorted, her face somewhere between amused and impressed.

“Why don’t you stay a while?” she said, pulling a deck of cards from her sleeve. She paused, looking Agatha in the eye. “Or, I’ll call Rio. And I’ll tell her that you showed up here, all wet from the rain like that man from that movie she showed me once. And that you brought donuts and coffee. On a school day. ”

“Ryan Gosling,” Agatha snapped, arms tightly crossed. “And you don’t have a phone.” 

“Ah, so you’ve seen it?” Lilia exclaimed. “Fantastic movie. And I see that you do have a phone, which I’m not above borrowing. By force.”

Agatha scoffed, trying to discern whether an old woman who believed in magic tea and thought The Notebook was an obscure indie film was going to muscle her to the ground.

Lilia tilted her head, considering her. “You don’t want to find out.”

Agatha stared, suddenly plagued by the uneasy feeling that she'd just spoken her own thoughts out loud without realizing it. At the very least, Lilia seemed crazy enough to actually go for it. Then Agatha would have to explain how she ended up in a physical altercation with an old woman. And, worse, why she was here in the first place.

She sat reluctantly, throwing her hair back over her shoulder with as much poise as she could manage. It wasn’t all that much, given that she was still wet.

“Three-card draw,” Lilia said, showing her the deck.

“I don’t believe in—”

“Three-card draw,” Lilia repeated. “The past, present, and future.”

She swept a pile of magazines and unlit candles to the side, laying out the first card in the center. Agatha looked at it, the image of the cartoon basking in the sun. The Fool.

“Great. Thank you.”

“It’s not an insult. The Fool represents new beginnings. You’ve recently felt that new beginnings are behind you.”

Agatha crossed her arms, and Lilia drew the second card.

“Two of Swords. You are facing an incredibly important decision, one that splits the very essence of who you are.”

She flipped the third card. “Strength.”

“Oh, perfect. At least we can end on a positive note.”

“Strength,” Lilia repeated, more slowly this time. “You are on a journey to discover your strength, your inner courage, and…your power?” She paused, inspecting the cards.

“Is that a question?” Agatha said, and Lilia was silent. “Isn’t that kind of...cliche?”

Lilia was still looking at the spread in front of her. “Yes, it is,” she muttered, frowning.

Agatha gathered her things to stand, and something fluttered from the pile of clutter to the floor.

“Here,” Agatha said, tossing the stray card down on the table. “You dropped one.”

Why did you put it like that?” Lilia asked, pointing to it.

“Like what?”

“It’s sideways.”

“So?” Agatha huffed. She looked at it properly.

The Moon.

“Huh,” Lilia said, staring at the spread. “It was never a three-card draw. It’s four.”

“What difference does it make?” Agatha snapped, her frustration boiling over. She was thinking about the long drive home in rainy, rush-hour traffic, no closer to fixing things with Rio. She was thinking about Wanda’s face this afternoon, sharp and pointed, when she'd said, “Why would she say anything else?”  She was thinking of the stupid, terrible words she wrote two months ago that she'd never even believed.

She stood. “Thank you for the magic tea, and the fortune-telling. Keep the coffee, too. My treat. And don’t mention that I stopped by.”

“Sit,” Lilia said sharply.

“Excuse me?”

“Sit,” she repeated, softer this time. “I’ll tell you where she is.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes. “Why?”

Lilia glanced at the cards, and Agatha followed her gaze.

“Oh, you’ve got to be fucking kidding. The cards told you to tell me where Rio is?”

“I did a tarot reading for Rio when she first moved in with me,” Lilia said evenly. “It was right around this time of year. She was seventeen.”

Agatha felt her chest tighten. Seventeen. She should have recognized it sooner—that look behind the eyes, the quiet embarrassment of being alone in the hospital, the guarded posture before Rio scanned a room. These were things Agatha knew. These were the things that came from being on your own before you were ready. 

Lilia was watching her carefully.

“The first card I pulled for her was Death.”

“Dark,” Agatha said casually, but she didn’t move any closer to the door.

Lilia waved her hand. “Common misconception. It doesn’t always mean literal death. It’s a symbol of deep, transformative change.”

Agatha sat back down slowly, her palms pressing into the couch cushions. She frowned, doing the math. “But that was before her injury, wasn’t it?” 

Lilia’s gaze had taken on a faraway look. “Yes.”

“Something about her parents?” Agatha said, remembering Rio’s quiet shake of her head in the hospital room.

“Not my story to tell,” Lilia said, but the way her mouth tightened said enough. “The important part is that I pulled Death for her for ten years. That kind of change isn’t easy, or fast.”

Lilia’s voice was heavy now, and Agatha saw the shine in her eyes. 

“Some people never make it past that chasm,” Lilia said. “They never figure out a way to get to the other side, and they get stuck, or they fall right in. But some people build the bridge themselves, brick by excruciating brick.” Lilia leaned forward. “The last time I did a reading for her, I finally pulled her something new.”

She laid her ring-adorned index finger softly on the first card. 

“Four cards. The first card is no longer the past—it’s now the protagonist of your draw.” 

Agatha followed her finger, her heart thundering all of a sudden.

"The Fool,” Lilia said, affectionately, looking at the image of the person, irreverent and mid-skip. “The Fool is the first card in the tarot deck. Number zero. She is the beginning of the journey through the Major Arcana, characterized by divine madness and limitless potential.”

Lilia’s finger traced the edge of the card, almost lovingly, and met Agatha’s eye. “The Fool is reckless because, in a way, she is brand new. She has nothing more to lose. But that recklessness is fragile. She’s incredibly thick-skinned, but underneath, she is deceptively soft-hearted.”

“She,” Agatha murmured, her voice feeling hoarse all of a sudden. Lilia’s eyes softened as she continued.

“The second card is your body in the physical world.” 

Agatha looked at the two swords piercing into a heart. A split decision.

“We can skip this one. I remember.” 

“I’m sure you do,” Lilia said, not unkindly. “The third card, then, is the metaphysical. You have strength of mind, Agatha. Your courage and will are still with you. Which brings us to the fourth card—the future.”

“The Moon.” Agatha looked at Lilia, finding herself suddenly transfixed with the whole ordeal.

“The way a card is drawn is just as important as the card itself,” Lilia said. “Right side up means one thing, and reversed means another.”

“It’s sideways."

“The moon represents the subconscious—intuition, imagination, illusion. It’s characterized by dualities.” Lilia traced the drawing on the card, and Agatha leaned closer. “Here, you see a path. On one side, a dog, on the other, a wolf. The sun and moon, the two towers, rain and fair skies. The lobster rising from the sea.”

“A lobster,” Agatha repeated. “Okay. You lost me.”

“The path of the lobster,” Lilia said, pointing to the tiny red figure. There was an urgency in her voice that put Agatha on edge. “As it emerges from the ocean and forges down the path, the dualities present themselves. What it chooses is up to you—to whether you are willing to trust your intuition. Whether you can hear it when it shows itself to you.”

Agatha stood abruptly, jostling the coffee table with her knee. The remaining tea in her cup sloshed dangerously against the sides. 

“Great,” she said shortly. “Now that we’re done with that—where's Rio?” 

Lilia straightened, her gaze sharp. “I think you know the answer to that.”

Agatha’s heart sank. She'd been hoping she was wrong.

“You really shouldn’t go there,” Lilia said. And then added, “Again.”

Agatha felt her face heat in anger and humiliation, and she walked wordlessly to the door. 

“I meant wearing that." Lilia gestured vaguely to the wet t-shirt and flannel. “Unless you want to advertise your place of work.”

Agatha cursed under her breath.

“Come with me." Lilia started down the dark, narrow hallway, and Agatha followed helplessly. She was going to end up in some hideous pink shawl, she knew it. They stopped in front of a room at the end of the hall, the door covered in collected signs. One read “Keep out.” Another: “Beware of dog.”

“This is Rio’s room, isn’t it?” Agatha bit back a laugh. 

“Regrettably,” Lilia said dryly, "she has ignored my suggestions to redecorate since the age of seventeen. Wait here.”

Lilia slammed the door in her face, and emerged a few seconds later, flinging her a t-shirt.

“I’m not going to show up wearing Rio’s shirt,” Agatha scoffed. “I’ll just button up my flannel.”

“Oh, It’s just a white shirt, she'll never know. I know for a fact they keep that club over seventy-five degrees.” 

“Are you a regular?” Agatha scoffed, swapping her shirt for Rio's. 

“Of course,” Lilia shot back. “I give the women free palm readings, and Manny makes a mean margarita.” 

Agatha crumpled the discarded shirts into a ball. Lilia was still standing in the hazy hallway, scrutinizing her. 

“You shouldn’t be burning sage when Rio has asthma,” Agatha said finally, crossing her arms.

Lilia looked at her in surprise. “Impressive. Took me two years to get that one out of her. I open all the windows before she gets back.”

Agatha said nothing. Lilia’s quiet assurance grated at her.

“Good luck,” Lilia said suddenly, and Agatha turned toward the front door. This woman was jarring, but at least she sounded sincere.

“I don’t need luck,” she called over her shoulder. “And it’s the twenty-first century—get a damn cell phone.”

 

 

She needed more than luck. To walk into a strip club while it was still light out? She needed divine intervention. She needed a lamb, or a locust, or whatever the thing was supposed to be. She needed God Himself to descend from the heavens on a floating chariot and kick her in the ass.

God was nowhere to be found, so she settled for the rain. It was cold enough make her run, plastering her hair to her neck in the short distance. She caught her reflection in the dark windows, and it looked a little like she’d been washed ashore. She thought of the lobster under the moon, half in the water, claws raised toward land. 

Maybe Lilia was full of shit. Maybe the lobster had never meant to walk the road at all. Maybe, in desperation, he'd decided to beach himself and die instead of choosing anything else at all.

Agatha felt the bass in her throat as she walked down the dark hallway. She waited for someone to look at her, to tell her she clearly didn’t belong here, and then to kick her out. Nobody did, so she sank into a booth, unsure if the roll in her gut was disappointment or relief. 

“Can I get you anything?” The waitress snapped her gum and stifled a yawn. Agatha wondered how many hours she would have to spend here to feel anything close to boredom. She shook her head, and the woman looked at her.

“You gotta order something, honey.”

“Diet coke,” Agatha muttered, and then, before she could stop herself, added, “Actually, I’m looking for Lady Death.”

“Ooh, a fan.” The waitress winked. “She’s up next. Want me to tell her you’re waiting?”

“No,” Agatha said quickly. “It’s fine, I’ll wait.”

Agatha wondered how exactly she was supposed to pass the time at a strip club, alone, with no tequila and no interest in spandex-clad strangers. Except for one, who wasn’t a stranger, and also wasn’t here.

She assessed the walls to give herself something to look at. The room felt laid bare, water damage in dark rings across the ceiling, a strip of silver duct tape patching a tear in a floor mat. She registered the shift in music to something slow and pulsing as her eyes wandered. One second, she was staring at a crack in the wall, tracing it across the room. The next, she was looking right at Rio. Agatha knew, instantly, that no amount of preparation would have made a difference. Rio wore green lace this time instead of black.

Lilia was right. What had she been thinking? Confronting Rio was one thing, but coming face to face with Lady Death was another thing entirely.

Rio stepped onto the small stage near the center of the room, and Agatha’s fingers tightened around her glass, her wet hair starting to warm at the back of her neck.

Rio glowed warmer under the green, her shoulders brushed with gold glitter instead of silver. The tattoo stretched across her midsection, black ink across every rib and muscle. Agatha couldn’t help but stare at the way it stretched and caught the light. Miracle Tree, she thought. Thriving in almost any condition.

At first, Rio's movements were expected—the sway of hips, the half-lidded look of intrigue. Then, her face slipped, and her eyes fluttered shut. Agatha saw her movement shift. She felt the way it changed into something more fluid, more instinctive. She wasn’t performing anymore, at least not for anyone in the room. This wasn’t Lady Death—this was Rio. 

She arched backward, one leg wrapped high around the pole. Her back bowed so far Agatha thought she might snap. Her hair spilled toward the floor, her chest rising and falling in slow, steady breaths as the lights flashed in colors over her skin.

Agatha couldn't look away. This wasn’t the Rio from that private room under the pink lights, hips moving against Agatha’s. This was nothing like the up-close, the teasing tip of her tongue in a practiced accidental swipe. This was something real. This was older. This was a thing that had lived in her body as it grew, freely given, and now lived inside a dark room for the eyes of the unqualified.

Rio’s pushed up slow from the floor until she was standing again. She bent one knee around metal, absently mouthing the words to the song. Her head tipped back, lips parted and face to the light, hair falling over her shoulders.

She was magnificent. Agatha’s throat constricted, guilt and awe and something deeper tangling tight and hot. She shifted in her seat as Rio opened her eyes, head tilting upright again. Her gaze landed immediately, directly, on Agatha.

The air left Agatha's lungs. The glass was warm in her hand as she watched Rio’s face turn from surprise to something searing, and then to cold, calculated detachment.

Agatha pushed herself to her feet, her legs carrying her with a mind of their own as Rio stepped off the stage and turned her back.

“Wait,” Agatha called, her voice sounding weak to even herself. Rio didn’t turn. Agatha kept going, weaving through tables and the growing crowd, bumping past a couple curious waiters until she was close enough to reach out and touch her.

“Rio,” she said again, firmly this time, Rio turned abruptly. Agatha stumbled.

“What are you doing here?” Rio said flatly.

"I want to talk to you."
