The Four Days of Almost-Christmas

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

 

“There are no grades anymore, Greer. Sometimes I think you forget that. There are never going to be grades for the rest of your life, so you just have to do what you want to do. Forget about how it looks. Think about what it is.”

― Meg Wolitzer, The Female Persuasion

 

 

On the twenty-first day of December, Agatha received a ceremonial certificate in the mail for the fifth time. First came her degree, then came marriage. Then the death of her mother, and the birth of her son after that. And now, the official dissolution of the second. 

The pieces of paper that made up her life were stashed in a shoebox at the bottom of her closet, next to the passports and her mother’s stolen diamond earrings she'd never gotten around to selling. She'd left them there, until one year turned into three, and three into ten, and the certificates stuck together with old age.

She would have preferred an email. Or a notary with a penchant for theatrics, showing up at her door to collect her marriage and pack it neatly back into his briefcase. It was unnecessary—sending permanent proof of her mistake instead of just scratching it from the record. She would pay double if the Notary Collector wanted to rip it in half or burn it, right there on her doorstep.

But this was the fifth certificate, not the first. There was no email and no briefcased man on her stoop, and it was time to brush the tangled hair of a stubborn, sleepy boy. The end of her marriage was just another envelope in the mail, between the superstore coupons and the phone plan offerings addressed to the couple next door.

On the twenty-first day of December, which was a Thursday, Mrs. Agatha Harkness legally became a Ms. again. Although, she lost the ‘r’ the same day she lost her husband, five months ago in the nauseating heat of July. 

It was December now, and she wasn't thinking about July. She was thinking about a certain kind of unexpected laugh and a plain white tee. Thinking about these things felt like the ground steaming under the first rain, or a warm leg pressed against hers on the sofa on a Sunday night. It felt like cracking the windows for the storm so the chimes could sing, the smell of petrichor and popcorn on the wind. 

It was familiar and foreign all at once, invasive like the creeping thistle under her front steps waiting patiently for the first sign of thaw. But it was December, not July, and thistle didn’t bloom in the snow. 

Agatha came back inside with the morning mail, her fingers red and numb. She tossed it on the counter, looked at it, and yawned. Then she turned around, and made herself some coffee.

 

 

By two-fifty-four that afternoon, Agatha had her hand forearm-deep in a toilet, and she had all but forgotten about the mail. There were plenty of rewarding, life-changing things about working in education, but this was not one of them.

Ten minutes earlier, at two-fourteen, she'd stepped into the hallway and straight into the familiar, disorienting rhythm of holiday chaos. She'd glanced through the narrow window of her classroom door, where a collection of inappropriately expensive parent Christmas gifts sat on her desk. This year’s winner was a four-ounce bottle of hand-lotion, which she could only assume was brewed with the blood of an endangered species, since—according to her research—it rang up at seventy-two dollars and forty cents. She wanted desperately to sit at her desk with the door closed and rub expensive blood-lotion on her hands.

Winter Break was one day away, and the teachers looked appropriately manic, hanging out of doorways and yelling after students. The fourth grade teacher wore two pairs of glasses—one on his face, one on his head—as he waved a forgotten worksheet at a boy who was long gone.

Nicky had escaped her in the gaggle of her class, and she looked for him with her back against the wall to avoid the muddy spots. The hallway in December smelled like wet jacket and stale candy canes. She liked it, a little. It reminded her of almost-Christmas, the same way the smell of sun on pavement and juice boxes reminded her of almost-summer.

She ducked under a swinging backpack, catching it just before it hit the girl behind her.

“Back-pack,” she said to the kid, “not Air-pack. Put it on.”

She looked through the mass of moving bodies. Oliver was in the middle of a group of boys, shouting, “HO, HO, HO.” He'd been on a Santa kick today. Or, more likely, Agatha suspected, he'd discovered for the first time the grandiose satisfaction of a catchphrase.

She bent to pick up a muddy flyer under her foot that read: Law & Order: North Pole. She crumpled it into a soggy ball.

“Sorry, Brynn,” she muttered. 

Brynn, this year’s winter playwright, was a bright-eyed fifth grader and one of Agatha’s old students. She had an ambitious streak and a specific taste for procedural crime. To Agatha's delight, The PTA arts committee had largely overlooked the plot of the play, and she'd wondered why until she overheard Kristy in the bathroom one afternoon, hissing to a friend: “What if she wins an Oscar one day? We want to be thanked!”

When she’d first heard the title of the play, Agatha herself had wondered if Brynn really should be that familiar with SVU at age ten. Then, she realized she had watched the original seasons of Law & Order when she was the same age.

She remembered Thursday nights in her cramped living room, her mother asleep too early, or gone entirely. Agatha would sit on the floor in front of the flickering TV her mother had rigged to their neighbor’s cable. Their neighbor, Norma, was eighty-five years old with thick, double-paned glasses. 

“She can’t even see her goddamn TV,” Evanora had said. “What does she need cable for?”

So Agatha had absolved herself of guilt and watched the men on the screen. She rarely understood what they were talking about, but her body learned the rhythm of it. Her shoulders would soften, eyelids heavy. Her heartbeats would slow. The familiar “dun-dun” and horrific descriptions of violence would lull her into almost-sleep, until finally, she’d crawl onto the sofa and let her eyes shut.

Grown-up Agatha quickly found her way to empathy. If Olivia Benson had entered her life all the way back then with the borrowed cable on Thursday nights, Agatha probably would have written a play of her own. 

She hadn’t actually seen the play yet. She knew Nicky was cast as a reindeer, but everything else he'd tried to explain to her had sounded like utter nonsense.

Her walkie-talkie crackled, and she closed her eyes, hoping fervently the call was for the other side of the school.

“Is anyone by the East Bathroom?” Jen’s staticky voice came through. “Happy?”

“Nope. Dealing with a snowball fight situation. But I think I saw Ms. Harkness over there.”

Damn it, Happy. 

She scanned the crowd for Nicky one last time, and yanked the walkie from her belt loop. Maybe it would shatter if she squeezed it hard enough.

“Copy,” she sighed. “I got it.”

 

 

She'd gotten to the bathroom to find a first-grader jiggling the metal handle.

“It’s a flush issue,” she said into her walkie. Her boots stuck to the floor, and she hoped it was soap, but knew it probably was not. “Plumbing isn't my area of expertise.”

“Code yellow or brown?” Happy said. She held the walkie to her forehead.

“Yellow,” she enunciated, and the kid giggled behind her.

“Try flushing again. Sometimes they don’t press hard enough.”

“Your suggestion is ‘try again ?’” She slammed the handle. 

To her surprise and annoyance, the toilet flushed perfectly. She stared at it, then raised her walkie again. 

“We’re good here.”

A sudden scream made her jump. She usually prided herself on being hard to startle, but she'd almost forgotten the kid was standing there. The walkie fell from her grasp, and landed, of course, straight in the toilet. 

“F—" She caught herself and clamped her lips, staring at the bowl. She'd accidentally run over her last walkie-talkie in the parking lot, so Wanda had promised this one to her with the specific condition that she not destroy it. 

She turned to the kid. “What happened?”

“Spider,” he said, pointing to the smallest living object Agatha had ever laid eyes on. Her brain thumbed automatically through the shelf of canned responses: ‘It’s not going to hurt you,’ or ‘He’s more scared of you than you are of him.’ 

She took a breath and rolled up her sleeves. “You can go,” she said lightly, “unless you want to stay in here with me and the spider. I think it's a black widow.”

She tied up her hair, and the door slammed shut behind the boy. 

Without hesitating—because hesitating just made her think about what she was about to do—she plunged her arm into the toilet, wiggling the plastic free from the hole it had somehow gotten stuck in. She pulled at it again, and couldn’t believe she'd done this enough times that she knew the best way to stick her arm down a toilet.

Only for you, Principal Max, she thought dryly.

She freed it, finally, a few drops splattering onto her shirt. She held it over the bowl and watched the water drip. What was she supposed to do now—put it in rice? Take the batteries out? She decided on wrapping it in brown paper towels instead, then washed her hands, and thought about her mother. 

When she was ten, it had been: What does she need cable for? And then, eight years later: What do you need a degree for?

Agatha huffed out a laugh, and it echoed against the green-tiled walls. If her mother could see her now.

The wad of paper towels leaked through her fist. Maybe it wasn’t worth all the trouble, but she’d done it anyway. She took a deep breath, the sad brown package bundled in her fist, and pushed the door open to the noise.

 

 

She finally spotted Nicky by the water fountain, wandering aimlessly.

“Hey, you,” she called.

He ran to her and jumped, landing at her feet.

“You know Daddy’s picking you up today, yeah?”

She rested a hand on his head. He nodded.

“And when will I see you next?” 

“Tomorrow!” Nicky said. “At school and at the play.”

“And after that?”

She needed to make sure he knew. She hoped he did, because she wasn’t sure she could say it.

“Christmas,” he said, picking at a sticker on his sleeve.

“That’s right. Daddy’s going to bring you over on Christmas.”

“And we can still do Christmas movie night?

Agatha swallowed. Divorce may have been a simple matter of paperwork this morning, but this was something else entirely.

“You’re with Daddy on Christmas Eve,” she reminded him gently. “You can watch it with him. And maybe the next day we can do our own movie night, just you and me.”

It felt stupid even as she said it—a secondhand movie night, after the presents had already been opened and the mantle cleared, the chimney swept and china stacked away.

“Okay,” Nicky said, and that was it. He looked around for his friends, his attention lent to her and taken back far too quickly. Sometimes, when he lost interest in her grown-up ramblings and got bored of the information and the logic, she felt that she was just borrowing him. That she was always going to have to give him back—first to the hallway, then to Josh, and eventually to the unruly world.

He didn’t ask why, or cry. Just “Okay.” And Agatha knew, somehow, that just-okay was the worst of the three.

 

 

The hallway was still hot and unbearably loud. Agatha was halfway through separating two second-graders who'd braided their hair together when she saw Josh. He was scanning the crowd for Nicky, but his eyes caught hers instead.

She'd spent the last month dodging his calls and every effort to pull her aside, limiting their encounters to logistics. He'd dropped Nicky off for Thanksgiving and she'd practically slammed the door in his face. Now she was trapped, her hands tangled in little-kid hair, and Josh was walking towards her. 

“You said we would talk about it before break,” he said. “It’s almost break. And I’m running out of ways to justify—”

“Okay,” Agatha stopped him. “Not… here.” She nodded to the girls in between them, who were waiting patiently. “I'll meet you at the picnic table.” 

“Don’t ditch me this time."

She watched him head toward her classroom. She looked down at the hair still knotted around her fingers, and tugged too hard.

 

 

Snow from last week’s storm still blanketed the ground. It was quiet, suddenly. Not all the way, because it was never all-the-way quiet at school, but her ears still rang with it. Josh was waiting under the maple tree by the picnic table as instructed. The tree was bare now, snow piled thick on its branches, and Agatha resolved to come out here more often. It was still her favorite place. She almost wished she’d told Josh to wait somewhere else.

“I’m here." She crossed her arms tightly over her chest.

“That’s your opening line?” Josh shook his head. “You hide this… insane thing from me for months, refuse to talk about it, and that’s all you’ve got?”

“What was I supposed to say?” she snapped. “Hey, by the way—if I don’t let our—” she dropped her voice—“ summer stripper co-teach my class, the school board might fire me. And yeah, anyway, let me know when you get the divorce papers?’”

Josh blinked. “They’re going to fire you?” 

She hadn’t meant to say that part.

“Not if I play nice with Rio.”

“Right. Rio.” His mouth pressed into a straight line, and he grasped the lapels of his coat, knuckles white. “I’m not trying to be an asshole, okay? I’m sure she’s a good teacher, or whatever, but I just—I don’t get how you’re okay with this. It’s insane.”

“You said that already.”

“Because it’s true!”

His voice cut through the quiet, and Agatha held up a hand in warning, her eyes flicking toward the school.

“I get it. What now?”

Josh raked a hand through his hair. “You know what? If the positions were reversed, you’d be losing your fucking mind. You can’t deny that.”

She couldn’t. She hated that she couldn’t. That even now—still—he could look at the structural integrity of her arguments and point directly at the weak spots.

“What is it going to take for—”

She stopped abruptly, hearing the echo of Rio’s words from months ago, under a different kind of tree, paper instead of wood. 

What is it going to take for you to get over it?

“If you forget about this,” she said instead, ‘I’ll owe you one. Please.”

Josh's eyes widened in surprise. She never promised him favors. And she didn’t say please.

“Nicky's birthday trip,” he said. "I want to come."

She hadn’t expected him to have an ask ready. Her first instinct was blackmail—before remembering she’d offered the favor in the first place. 

“If I let you come,” Agatha said slowly, “you’ll drop this whole thing?” 

Josh shrugged. “Yeah, fine. As long as she’s not fucking dropping her clothes at the front of the classroom.”

“Jesus, Josh.” She scoffed, looking away, and tried not to think about the fact that she'd practically said the same thing to Rio’s face on the first day of school.

The sweat was starting to freeze around her collar. Even with the volume and demands that waited behind the door, it was an easy answer, that she would rather be standing in there. Her body had known the heat of chaos since birth. Really, it was the cold quiet that set her teeth on edge.

“And I’m sorry. About Christmas,” Josh said, quieter now. “I know it’s not ideal. But you had him for Thanksgiving, and my family’s in town, and you know if—”

“Let’s just not.” She rubbed her arms and shook out her feet—one then the other. Her toes were numb, like her fingers had been this morning, and she thought of the envelope on her kitchen counter. 

Josh reached for the door handle, then paused. 

“This Rio thing,” he said. “You’re not, like…” He squinted, trailing off.

She stared at him, eyes starting to water in the wind, but she couldn't blink now. Of all the things she’d expected him to say, this hadn’t made the list. For fuck’s sake, just a few months ago the man had asked her if Rio worked at Olive Garden. 

“I’m not what?” she said, even though she knew exactly what he meant.

“I don’t know.” He hesitated. “Is there something going on there?”

She felt a flicker of surprise that he’d actually asked. And then, immediately, something ached. 

It wasn’t just what he said; it was how. It was the way they used to talk about people they saw at potlucks and weddings: "Did you see Greg and Lola?” and “Do you think there was something going on?” And there always was.

Josh had known her for half her life. She'd liked him, and loved him—though sometimes she could barely remember what that felt like. It was such a familiar, offhand delivery of such an astounding accusation. She missed that. Not so much him—as in Josh, the forty-three year-old with twenty year-old holey underwear and a knack for forgetting their anniversary—but the amount of space someone else had taken up in her for so long. 

She missed the ease of having someone in front of her that could ask something big and terrifying, like they were simply asking for the time. Someone that could look under the porch steps, part the thistle thorns out of the way, and see the purple in its soft and simple glory.

And then it passed. She saw the space between them, the color gone gray, and the life reduced to a single piece of paper, then two. A once-overpowering certainty, turned to failure, and then to a warning that something good could only become something bad. That she would make this mistake all over again. How dare he see that, when no one else could? It was unnecessary, she thought. To stand at her back doorstep and present her with proof.

Her hands balled into fists. A stale, sour taste rose in her throat.

“Grow the fuck up,” she said, and walked past him through the door.

 

 

“Where are you going?” she heard Wanda call. 

Agatha didn’t slow down. She'd spent enough time in this goddamn hallway today.

“To get a new shirt."

Wanda caught up, eyeing her. Her ponytail was coming loose, sleeves rolled up, and Agatha figured she probably looked just as disheveled herself.

Wanda handed Agatha a candy cane from her pocket. “I need Rio’s schedule for next semester. And I thought we could invite her to come to drinks tomorrow. You know where she is?”

“No,” Agatha said, pocketing the candy. She was sick of candy canes and the sticky red dye that stained kids’ mouths and left red fingerprints all over her classroom. 

Wanda fell into step beside her as they wove past a cluster of kids and started down the basement stairs. The air was colder, and Agatha rubbed her nose at a sudden swirl of dust under her feet.

“What do you mean no?” Wanda said. "You always know where she is.”

“No, I don’t. Go ask Brynn.”

“Okay, whoa." Wanda grabbed her arm, stopping her at the bottom of the stairs . “What’s up?”

Agatha paused, rubbing her hands over her face.

“Josh is coming to Disney.”

“You invited him?”

“No,” Agatha said, pulling at her own hair a little. “I mean, kind of. Yes.”

Wanda narrowed her eyes. “I thought you said you would never invite him in—quote—‘a million fucking years.’”

“I know. It’s fine. It’ll be fine.”

“If you say so.”

Wanda didn’t let go of her arm. Agatha glanced down at her grip.

“I’ve been meaning to ask you,” Wanda said, glancing around the empty passageway. 

Agatha felt suddenly uneasy.

“Have you seen Paul again?”

Agatha tried not to flinch at the question, but her forearm flexed automatically. 

“Nope." She met Wanda’s eye. 

Wanda hummed. “Okay, so…do you know why Paul rejected Rio’s extension, then changed his mind—oh, I don’t know—one day after you guys hooked up?”

Her voice was light, almost playful, but Agatha heard the edge underneath. 

“It’s probably better if you don’t know the answer.”

Wanda’s eyes widened. She let go of Agatha’s arm. 

“Did you blackmail him? With sex?” 

“No, of course not,” Agatha hissed. Then she squinted at a spot on the wall, considering. “I mean… I guess a little bit. But not in, like, a premeditated way.”

“I thought you barely even liked Rio; now you’re trading sexual favors to keep her? What is happening with you?”

“I…there was an eval, and…Paul…Rio...” The guilt tugged at her as she took in Wanda’s incredulous expression. She should have told her sooner. She could tell her the whole thing now. She was already halfway there.

“It’s messy,” she said instead, and even that felt like a confession. 

“I’ll tell you everything when I can, okay? I promise.”

She left Wanda there, open-mouthed and staring, and ducked into the auditorium. 

 

 

Agatha shut the door to the costume room, wondering how long she'd have to stay in here before Wanda went home for the day. The tiny room smelled like old fabric and perfume, and Agatha barely registered the music playing before she turned. 

Rio was hunched at the sewing machine, barefoot in socks, one leg curled beneath her. She'd stripped down to a black tank, her sweater draped on the back of her chair and her ponytail over her shoulder. 

The soft whir of the machine filled the room, and Rio’s arm moved with the rhythm of it, precise and hypnotic. For a second, Agatha just watched. 

Music floated from Rio’s phone speakers, classical guitar with a beat. Rio’s hands moved quickly, adjusting levers and notches on the machine whose functions Agatha had never seen nor considered. Her fingers pulled and shifted, pressed and curled.

Agatha’s sweater felt suddenly too hot again. It was this stifling room, she thought, as she watched Rio’s hands.

Agatha cleared her throat. Rio looked up suddenly, blinking at her.

“Did I forget something?”

Her voice was rough with disuse, and Agatha wondered if she had been working here, hunched over a table, since she'd last seen her. 

Agatha ducked through a rack, brushing aside a feather boa. “Wanda wanted to know if you could come to drinks tomorrow night. We usually go to Paolo’s at the end of the semester. But I’m here for a shirt.”

Rio raised an eyebrow. “Sounds fun. But I can’t—I have plans.” She paused. “What happened to your shirt?”

“I had to fucking fist a toilet,” Agatha muttered, already rifling through capes and animal onesies. “Don’t ask.”

Rio hummed. “Sounds even more fun.”

“One more fucking day.” 

Agatha yanked out the first thing that wasn’t kid-sized—a large white button-up—and mulled over Rio’s plans. She didn’t usually saywhen she meant work. Maybe it was a Friday night dentist appointment. Or another blockbuster rom-com with Lilia, passed off as an art movie. She ignored the obvious third option.

Rio looked up. “Not that one. That’s Brynn’s costume.”

“Of course it fucking is. Liv Benson wannabe.”

“Can you blame her?” Rio murmured, distracted again. Agatha hid her smile as she kept searching.

She pulled out the only other thing that would fit—a gaudy purple vest with giant, sequined flowers, two of them carefully placed.

Agatha glared at it. “Who bought a vest and thought, 'Sure, let me donate flower tits to the elementary school?'”

Rio smirked without looking up, and Agatha studied her. She was quiet now, in that way was sometimes, eyes red-rimmed.

Agatha turned around, pulled off her sweater in one fluid motion, and tugged the itchy vest over her head. When she turned back, Rio was still facing the machine, but her hands had gone still. Agatha smoothed the vest against her torso, trying to quell the flip of her stomach.

“What are you working on?” she asked, bracing her hands on the table next to Rio.

“I need to finish two more before tomorrow, or the snowman and the elephant go pantsless.”

“The elephant?” Agatha blinked. “What the fuck is this play about?”

“I’ve been told not to spoil it,” Rio said through a yawn. 

She pushed back her chair and stretched her legs out. Agatha tried not to look at her arms laced behind her head, or the way her tank top rode up her waist, exposing a tiny sliver of skin. 

“Wanda wants your spring schedule, by the way,” Agatha said, studying a loose thread on the table.

“I know,” Rio said. “I’m still waiting for my advisor to approve it.”

Agatha nodded, still looking at the thread.

“And when I do get that,” Rio added, “do I send it to Wanda? Or to Paul?”

Agatha looked up at the sudden edge in her tone. Rio was leaning back, arms crossed, biting the inside of her cheek.

Agatha straightened. She kept her face steady with excruciating effort. 

“You send it to me,” she said evenly.

Rio just looked at her. “Must have been one hell of a conversation.”

“What was it you told me once?” Agatha said, her face feeling hot. “Was it ‘Let’s be professional,’ or ‘I don’t owe you all the dirty details?’”

Rio’s jaw ticked, and she turned slowly back around, hands falling on the sewing machine. They didn’t move.

“Sorry,” she said finally. She straightened the pant leg under the needle. “You’re right. It’s none of my business. I’m just really fucking exhausted.”

Agatha breathed out, resisting the urge to put her hands on Rio’s shoulders and dig her thumbs in until the tension melted under her palms.

“Maybe you should take a break,” she said instead. “With all your preexisting conditions? Your insurance can’t handle carpal tunnel, too.”

It got the soft snort she was hoping for, and Rio turned her head to look at Agatha, her chin still resting in her hand.

“How do you do this?” Rio asked, gesturing around the room. “It’s like, the best thing ever, until you’re doing seven things at once and your brain is about to fall out of your fucking eyes.”

Agatha smiled. She forgot sometimes that Rio really was new to this. 

“You take breaks,” Agatha said. “You do things that make you forget where you are, even just for ten minutes.”

“What’s your go-to?” Rio said, yawning again.

“Bugles and Youtube,” Agatha said. 

“What do you watch?”

Agatha tucked a loose hair behind her ear, avoiding Rio’s eye.

“That bad?” Rio said, eyebrows raised. 

“It's not bad," Agatha started. “I watch videos of the world’s tallest roller coasters. Like, as if you’re going down them. There’s something about it.”

Rio was laughing, but Agatha didn’t mind so much.

“What about you?” Agatha said. “I’ve never actually seen you take a break.”

Rio looked at her, smile fading a little. She pushed her chair back, and stood. For a second, Agatha thought she was leaving. Then, she stepped closer.

“Do you know Bachata?” 

Agatha shook her head. She didn’t know what was happening, but she knew that look. Rio held her palm up, and Agatha stared at it.

“No way. Rio.”

“You asked what I did to forget where I am,” she said quietly. “This is it.”

Slowly, Agatha relented, placing her hand in Rio’s.

Her hand was calloused and warm, and Agatha felt her throat tighten. They hadn’t even moved yet, but there was something so painfully intimate about silence with her hand in someone else’s. She missed that, too, right alongside the gossip and the rightful accusations. 

She suddenly wished she'd made time to put on the expensive, illegal lotion that sat on her desk, just down the hall. Rio stepped forward, and she forgot about the lotion, and her desk, and the hallway altogether. Rio held her hand under the yellow light. The music was soft and the sewing machine silent. 

Agatha’s pulse was racing, her hands suddenly clammy. She had spent the last several weeks avoiding a situation exactly like this, and here she was, hand in Rio’s. It hadn’t been lost on her that in the last month, Rio’s proximity had gone from something mildly distracting to something that made her lose her fine motor control and ability to string together any two thoughts.

She'd kept her solidly at arm’s length, but now, Rio was right up close, and Agatha realized that maybe she hadn’t been keeping her at arms length at all, and that maybe Rio had just been standing there.

“Bachata,” Rio said, “originated in the Dominican Republic, in the countryside and brothels.”

She took another step closer, and Agatha refused to step back, their toes almost touching.

“The people who lived in places that were forgotten about or forbidden had to create their own sense of physical freedom.” 

Agatha swallowed, drawn into her words. She imagined couples on the hillside and inside the dark walls of the brothels, dancing through the night under the stars, or in the moonlight that shone through the cracks in the walls.

“It’s a simple four-step. One-two-three, tap. Like this,” Rio said, and stepped forward again. Agatha stepped forward at the same time, and Rio paused.

“Are you leading?”

Agatha glared. “Men never know how to dance. I learned to lead so I wouldn’t get left standing on the fucking floor all night.”

Agatha caught a flash of amusement in Rio’s eyes, and then the edge in her gaze as it flitted down to her lips. Agatha swallowed, her mouth suddenly dry.

“Lucky for you,” Rio said, “I’m not a man.”

She spread her palm and wrapped her free hand around Agatha’s waist.

Agatha’s breath caught in her throat, as if Rio’s hand had pulled it straight from her, thumb brushing her ribcage. She’d been imagining Rio like this for weeks—her hands on Agatha, voice low in the dim light.

Since that night with Paul, her skillful imagination had finally come alive in the dark quiet of her own empty house. She'd become familiar with the new, hungry want of her body, and the resulting cries, half in ecstasy and half in anguish that it was all at her own hands instead of Rio’s. And then she'd come to school every day, a mix of quiet thrill and guilt she was sure could show on her face, as Rio kept to her quiet, infuriating promise of professionalism. The irony was wet-hot and filthy.

“Start with this side,” Rio murmured, close enough that Agatha felt the warmth of her breath on her cheek. “One, two, three—then a tap.”

Agatha focused on the feel of Rio’s hand at her back, guiding her, the gentle press of fingertips just above her waistband. 

“Good,” Rio said, quiet. “Again.” 

One, two, three—tap.

The steps were small, confined to a small square, just enough to shift their weight, to feel the push and pull of their hips. Agatha kept her eyes down, watching the pattern of Rio’s socks against the linoleum. 

“Stop looking at your feet,” Rio said, her hand falling to Agatha’s hip. “Just feel the rhythm.”

Agatha exhaled and she closed her eyes. She let go of the tension in her shoulders and followed the pull of Rio’s arms. She felt the palm pressing into the small of her back, and her hips pressed forward in response. 

“You’re doing great,” Rio murmured. Agatha felt her voice vibrate against her jaw, and heat crept up her neck at the compliment. She scoffed quietly, clutching the remnants of her pride while trying to stay upright. 

“This isn’t that hard,” she managed.

“Good,” Rio said. And then she smiled, small and wicked, and rolled her hips in one deliberate motion. She slotted her knee between Agatha’s with devastating precision, and her head tilted back before rolling back up to the beat.

Agatha’s hands flew to Rio’s hips on instinct, using every ounce of willpower not to let the noise escape her throat where it was waiting. Suddenly, they weren’t in this dim, yellow, stuffy room. They were somewhere else—a supply closet with paper clips in her hair, or a hospital bed with the TV on, or a rug under a paper mache tree.

Rio’s thumb brushed under the hem of Agatha’s borrowed vest. It was getting harder to breathe with every brush of Rio’s hips against hers. She looked at Rio’s mouth. It would be too easy, she thought, to just lean forward an inch. To set her teeth to the side of her bottom lip. 

She moved her hand back up, resting in the bend where Rio’s neck met her shoulder, and brushed a thumb against her throat. 

One, two, three–

She felt Rio swallow beneath it, and felt the flutter of her pulse. 

No tap.

“You missed a step,” Agatha murmured, pressing her thumb down, just for a second.

Rio’s breath hitched. Her hand tightened around Agatha’s hip.

Agatha had meant it to be cheeky—and fine, maybe a little flirty—but Rio glanced down at their feet, and something in her face shifted. 

Agatha spent almost eight hours a day with her. She knew her face well enough by now, even the expressions that formed the slightest cracks in her usual cool. It was to be expected, she knew, with someone who took up so much space in her day. Even if it was just her day, not her life. Although, maybe those were the same thing.

Rio wasn’t amused, or annoyed. She was surprised.

She went rigid beneath Agatha’s hands.

“I didn’t mean…” Agatha said, her voice softening. “It’s okay. I was kidding.”

A loud click echoed through the room.

The door.

They reacted at the same time. Agatha stepped back blindly, her back hitting the costume rack. It clattered, rolling to the side of the room. Rio turned, both hands braced on the table, on either side of the sewing machine.

Jen was standing in the now open doorway. Agatha busied herself with the sleeve of a costume within arm’s reach. Her heart was still thundering, sequins scratching at her collarbone. She tried to steady her breath. 

“Hey,” Jen said slowly.

Agatha didn’t look at her, still inspecting the sleeve.

“Hey."

The guitar still strummed softly in the background.

“Sorry." Jen crossed her arms. She didn’t sound sorry in the slightest. “Did I interrupt something?” 

“No–” Agatha and Rio spoke at the same time, then stopped. Rio ran a hand through her hair.

“No,” Agatha said again, gesturing at her vest. “I had to get a new shirt because of the toilet thing earlier—you remember.”

“Uh huh,” Jen said, glancing down at the vest. She looked at Rio.

“Working on costumes,” Rio said. “For tomorrow.”

Jen looked back at Agatha.

“Custodial will be here in twenty,” she said finally. “They haven’t been able to get this room all week and it’s starting to feel a little stuffy.”

Rio didn’t hesitate. She grabbed her bag by the chair, flinging her shirt over her elbow, and yanked the pairs of costume pants from the table.

“I’ll finish these at home." She didn’t look at Agatha, and brushed past Jen. 

Agatha grabbed her sweater from the floor and moved to follow, but Jen stepped into her path. She reached behind her and shut the door.

“Really?” Agatha said. “Are you going all ‘serious boss’ on me for stealing a shirt?”

“I am your boss. And that’s obviously not what I’m concerned about.”

“Concerned,” Agatha lilted. “You know I love that word.”

“Okay—what's another word for what I just saw?” 

Agatha took a step back. “I don’t know, J—what exactly do you think you saw? Nothing happened.” 

“You’ve known me since I was seven. Have you ever known me to misread a room?”

Agatha felt the guilt rise in her throat. She was silent. 

“How long has… that been going on?” Jen hissed. “And when were you going to fucking tell me and Wanda? Does she know?”

“Nothing is going on!” Agatha said, her arms flailing. She took a deep breath, stepping forward. “You know me. You know I would never put your job, or Wanda’s, or Rio’s—or anyone’s at risk.”

“Except your own,” Jen said, exasperated. “It’s like you don’t give a fuck what happens to you! Do you have any idea what it’s like to do damage control for Agatha Harkness? Have you thought about what a tabloid scandal like this would do to you?”

“Of course I fucking have!” 

The words burst out of her, breaking lightly on the end. 

Jen’s eyes widened, and Agatha realized what she had unwillingly said. 

She turned her back to Jen, putting some distance between them, and pressed her palms against her cheeks to cool her face.

When she turned around, Jen’s gaze was softer, and sadder, and something closer to disappointment.

No, Agatha wanted to say. You’re wrong. 

But she wasn’t. Not really. 

“I can’t believe I have to say this to you." Jen shook her head. “But in case no one has—she’s your student teacher.”

Agatha scratched at her eyebrow, the humiliation spreading in her chest. She was being scolded by someone she still looked at and saw with pigtails. A little girl she had once walked to the bathroom by the hand because she was too scared to go by herself.  Sometimes she forgot that she'd given Jen back to the world, too. It was an embarrassing, reckless thing to forget.

Agatha stepped around her, and paused with her hand on the knob. Jen’s eyes were still wide with worry. Agatha wanted to hold her hand, and walk her to the car. She pulled the door open, and walked herself instead.

 

 

On the twenty-second day of December, Agatha stood at the back of a dark auditorium, biting the cuticle around her fingernail. Intermission was over, the plot was incomprehensible, and it was impossible to see into the wings from this far back unless someone knew the exact four-inch spot to look. It was one sliver of view between the back right wing and the last half-curtain, where the architect had miscalculated the line of sight from just inside the doorway.

Agatha had spent more time in these loud, muddy halls than in the award-winning interior design of her own home, curated twenty years ago by yet another career man on her doorstep. He’d carried a messenger bag, which was just another kind of briefcase. He’d plucked the check from Josh’s hands, pointed out the eyesores in her empty living room, and said something stupid and meaningless like, "A beautiful house is only beautiful if people know where to look." And Agatha, twenty-two and late for school, had handed over the keys to her brand new castle.

But here, five certificates later, in the auditorium that always smelled faintly of nervous sweat and concession popcorn, Agatha had always known where to look. It was the last day of the first half of the year, six months to the day after she first laid eyes, but not hands, on Lady Death. And here she was, still, looking at Rio across a dark room. 

On stage, Agatha counted: four snowmen, a tree, three lawyers and/or detectives—including Brynn—an elephant, and a judge dressed as Santa. Nicky had explained three separate times when his big entrance was going to be, but Agatha couldn’t understand the flow of dialogue enough to get an idea of when that was. She’d meant to look at the script yesterday, but after storming out on Jen, speeding to the store in a panicked haze, and forgetting half her list, she’d barely remembered to get Nicky’s costume. 

Her two remaining options had been to ask Brynn, or Rio. Brynn had shown up to school with vanilla milk in a coffee cup, looking like she'd stayed up all night, and Rio had managed to be busy with something every time Agatha tried to catch her eye. 

She was squinting at a singing snowman, trying to remember his name. She had just remembered that he was a nose-picker, and finally landed on the first letter D, when the door cracked open behind her. Alice’s head poked through, and she beckoned Agatha into the hallway.

Agatha glared, and followed.

“What? My kid’s almost on,” she said, and then added, “I think.”

Alice shrugged. “Rio told me to get you.”

Agatha blinked. If Rio hadn’t even had time to text her, something might actually be wrong.

“Okay, just—” Agatha fished her phone out of her pocket and handed it to Alice. “If Nicky comes on, start recording, and don’t stop until the end.”

Alice looked at the phone in her head, and then nodded. “Okay.”

“Thank you,” Agatha said genuinely, feeling that same affection for Alice and her straightforward demeanor. 

She walked quickly around the side of the building, entering the hallway backstage. Children wandered in animal costumes, volunteers shushing kids that got too loud.

“Where’s Ms. V?” she asked to the general crowd, and one girl dressed as the devil pointed down the hallway. 

Agatha did a double-take at the pink pitchfork, and then reminded herself that there was neither time nor good reason to find plot holes in a play written by a fifth grader.

She found the music room empty except for Rio and Nicky. 

“What’s wrong?” she said immediately, her eyes scanning Nicky as she sped to them.

“He's fine,” Rio said around the safety pin in her mouth. She was holding the elastic on Nicky’s pants, folding it in the right places. 

“Hi, baby,” Agatha said to Nicky, worry giving way to sweet excitement. She could see the thrill in his eyes and his fidgeting fingers. He got that from her.

"Hi Mama." He waved at her, mouth a wide grin.

Agatha eyed the pants. “Oh. They don’t fit?”

“Where did you get them?” Rio muttered. “The elastic is like a piece of spaghetti.”

“They were five bucks,” Agatha said, indignant. “How many more times is he going to dress up like a reindeer before he shoots up three inches?”

“It’s fine,” Rio said, still working the elastic around the waist. “That’s not what I needed help with.”

She pinned one side in place, and placed a hand on Nicky’s shoulder. “Give us a second, okay?”

Nicky nodded, and Rio pulled Agatha to the far corner of the room. 

“I’m sure you’ve heard about Nicky’s reindeer scene with Luca?”

“Vaguely,” Agatha said. “Do I need to know the details?”

“No,” Rio said. “Just that they have this cute little dance number we’ve been working on, and it’s in, like, six minutes, and Luca has locked himself in the instrument closet and won’t come out.”

Agatha pointed to a closed door on the other side of the room, and Rio nodded. 

“Do we know why?” Agatha whispered.

“I haven’t had time to talk to him,” Rio said. “I tried, but I have to—” she gestured to the hallway, and in Nicky’s general direction.

Agatha recognized the pressure of a product. She watched the way Rio pushed the hair out of her face, a hand lingering on her forehead. It was a kind of stress Agatha hadn’t seen in Rio yet, not even when she was dancing on a stage by herself. Agatha supposed this auditorium had been her own stage much longer than it had been Rio's. She put a hand on her arm. 

“Six minutes is enough. You take care of the pants, I’ll get Luca.”

Rio nodded, relief evident on her face, and returned to Nicky.

Agatha pressed her ear against the closet door. She heard faint shuffling and quiet sniffles. 

“Luca?” she said softly. “This is Ms. Harkness. You doing okay in there?”

Luca didn’t answer, and Agatha heard more sniffling.

“You know, if you were a little nervous about the dance,” Agatha said, “that would make sense.”

More silence.

“But Ms. V said that you and Nicky have been working on it for a whole week. I’m excited to see it.”

“They were laughing at me.”

She heard Luca’s watery voice through the door. She locked eyes with Rio.

Who, she wanted to ask, but her eyes flitted to the clock. Five minutes.

“I’m sorry,” she said instead. “Can you come out so we can talk about it?”

“No. I’m staying in here,” Luca said, louder.

“Okay,” Agatha said. She pressed her ear to the door again. “Okay. Tell me what happened.”

“Jeremy was making fun of me when I was practicing my dance,” Luca said, still sniffing, “and then he said that his mom said he wasn’t allowed to play with me.”

Agatha leaned her forehead against the wood for a second. When she turned her head, Rio had paused, looking at her.

“Luca,” she said. She was surprised at how even her voice was, despite the steady hum of anger starting to spread. “That was not a kind thing for him—or his mother—to say.”

Agatha knew the unspoken rule never to trash talk a parent to a child. But she had four minutes left, and Nicky was listening, and fuck if she had any patience left for subtlety.

“He said it was because I like a boy,” Luca said.

Agatha closed her eyes. Her chest felt tight, and she couldn’t keep looking at the clock. She couldn’t make someone see themselves in three minutes when it had taken her forty-two years. She looked at Rio, her hands clutching Nicky’s shoulders too tightly. Nicky was biting his fingernail, and Agatha felt a sudden wave of guilt that he had gotten that from her, too.

Agatha lowered herself to the floor and leaned against the door.

“Maybe it is,” she said softly, pressing her hand against the wood, palm flat. It was a useless motion, but she felt the need to do it anyway.“That doesn’t mean it was okay, or right."

She looked at Rio, and her eyes lingered on the curve of her cheekbones, the softness of her gaze, the eyelashes that had fluttered against her cheek just yesterday afternoon. She felt the sharp poke of fear in her chest, like thistle thorns under her porch.

Yesterday, she’d stood outside and looked at the man she used to love, and hated him for seeing her. And then she’d missed him for the same reason. Maybe that’s where she'd gone wrong. Maybe she'd waited, all those years as the thistle grew thicker, for someone to figure out how to move it out of the way without scratches and blood. She’d waited for a man to show up on her porch with hedge clippers, and start the tedious, painful task of clearing the weeds, when really, they'd always been hers to part and pull.

“When you like someone,” she said softly, her fingers pressing tighter against the wood. “That’s a good thing. That’s a beautiful, secret, magical thing—that you get to see all the best parts of someone else. And I know that it can be so scary to let other people see that most magical version of you.”

She looked at Rio. Rio was looking at a spot on the wall.

“Remember yesterday when Ms. V read Stellaluna ?” Agatha said, and Rio finally looked at her.

Luca’s voice was small through the door.

“Yeah.”

“For so long,” Agatha said, “she thought she was a bird. And she tried so hard to be a good one. But then she learned she was a bat. And even though things were a little scary for a little while, she saw a world in a way no one else did."

Agatha looked back at the door, speaking quickly now. “People might laugh at you, because they don’t understand, and that might feel really crappy. The best way to tell them they’re wrong is to do your reindeer dance. That’s what we want to see, Luca. Just you, the way you are right now.”

Agatha held her breath. She didn’t hear sniffling anymore.

Rio was glancing at the clock. One minute.

Agatha stood, rolled up her sleeves, and took Nicky’s hand.

“I’m going to go out there with Nicky, until you’re ready to join us. You can stay in there if that’s what you want.”

"Bye, Luca," Nicky called, and Agatha's heart clenched. How she loved this boy.

She winked at Nicky, and called back to the door, “Luca, just so you know—I’m a really, really terrible dancer. I might accidentally do the whole dance backwards.” 

Nicky giggled, and gripped her hand tighter. Agatha grabbed the spare reindeer headband Rio held out. She didn’t look at her face. She hadn’t laid eyes on a single line of script, and she was about to fake choreography to a song she’d never even heard, dressed like a reindeer. To make a fool out of herself in front of a crowd, for the third time this year.

But this time was different. It was the coffee after the mail hit the counter, or the quick plunge of a fist to save a walkie. It didn’t need a second thought, or even a first.

 

 

The kids had spent five minutes giggling at the sight of Agatha trying—and failing—to copy Nicky’s dance moves. Mic-less and improvising wildly, she made faces at the kids each time she turned around. Even Brynn was laughing in between feeding Agatha her lines, ever the consummate professional.

For a few minutes, in the chaos of high-pitched laughter and hole-punched snow, she let herself forget. She forgot all of the rules, the people watching, the door to the costume room slamming shut behind her yesterday. 

She spotted Rio in the wings. Luca stood beside her, grinning in his costume. Agatha high-fived him as she passed, and then walked right by Rio to thunderous applause. 

 

 

It was almost nine by the time the parking lot had emptied, and the auditorium lights turned off. Agatha had presented Nicky with flowers after the show, and watched him walk to the car with Josh, their hands clasped and swinging. She'd turned right back around and gone back inside.

The stage had been abandoned in post-show disarray, and Agatha stood among the mess. Sweatshirts and crumpled papers littered the wings. She plucked a candy wrapper off the bottom of her shoe, and toed both of them off to the side of the stage. It felt like taking off her shoes at the end of any other long day, everyone packed up and headed home so that she could be alone in hers. 

She wandered the wings, collecting discarded items for the lost and found. This wasn’t her job, but she was already here. She tried not to think of Jen and Wanda waiting for her at the restaurant down the street with a pitcher of sangria.

She paused at center stage and looked out into the dark house. She'd never done theater or dance as a kid, but she liked the feeling of it, not being able to see anything except her own hands in front of her face. Maybe she could learn, like Stellaluna, to love the dark. To understand which way was up, not because she could see the sky, but because she was simply meant to know.

She spotted Nicky’s pencil case on the ground, and shook herself out of her reverie, bending to retrieve it. It lay on top of a well-worn script. Finally, she thought—answers.

The front read: 

PROPERTY OF BRYNN. DO NOT TOUCH!

She smiled and flipped through the packet. She recognized Brynn’s handwriting, which surprised her. It was the kind of thing she never thought about, the way someone’s words on paper were a leave-behind of the shape of their hand and the angle of their fingers that turned into a kind of sweet affection. The lines had gotten better, she mused, still stencil-straight and bafflingly even. 

A sticky note fluttered out of the pages of the script, and she picked it up. Of course Brynn used sticky notes appropriately. She held it at a distance, squinting to read the scrawl. 

 

Brynn–

I am so proud of you. You’ve worked so hard, and you are a superstar. Can’t wait to see you! 

Love, Mom.

 

Agatha held the note for a minute. She walked to the edge of the stage and sat, legs dangling over the edge. The words tumbled in her head. For the third time that day—a record in the last decade—she thought about her mother.

Agatha had hated her mother, but her mother had hated her first. It had taken Agatha years to understand that it wasn’t supposed to be like that. She'd watched mothers pick up their children from her classroom and kiss their cheeks and squeeze them tight, and it had all seemed foreign and strange. 

And then, she met Jen. Jen was bright-eyed and feisty and shy, all at the same time, with no mother to pick her up at the end of the day. Only a grandmother who rode the bus, and reliably ran late. So Agatha, twenty-two and unsure, would sit with Jen for an hour, sometimes two. 

Jen would ask her every question under the sun until Grandma Kale came to get her with harried apologies and scattered thoughts. Jen would remind her of the way home, and give Agatha a hug goodbye so tightly, some days she had to pry her hands loose.

Sometimes, Agatha thought of the number of times she'd ridden the bus by herself by the time she was seven. She could barely read the names of the longer stops—she just memorized the shapes and colors of the signs. She hadn’t realized until Jen, how backwards she had it. The thought of letting Jen wait at a bus top by herself, scared and alone, was unimaginable. 

She tried to call her mother that year, to somehow reach across the phone and share this thing she now knew, to demand answers, to understand. But she’d never picked up. Agatha realized she would never really know her mother, because Evanora Harkness would never let herself be known. But now—after standing side by side with Nicky in reindeer antlers, in front of a crowd—Agatha felt the injustice of it. Not only that her mother had never done this, but that she’d never seen Agatha do it, either. 

She hadn't seen the way Agatha had learned to lead a room with one raised eyebrow. The way she kept a dozen kids afloat while keeping herself upright. But mostly, the way she'd stayed, when she could have quit, or left, or let herself waste away in her empty house. She'd built a life and a person. She'd become something remarkable. And Evanora Harkness had missed all of it, because she went and fucking died. 

She hadn’t even had time to change her mind. To write Agatha a note in green felt tip pen that said, “I’m so proud of you,” and “can’t wait to see you.” To call her a superstar, even if all she did was a silly little dance.

Agatha blinked hard, but the tears came anyway, hot and quiet.

She clutched the sticky note in her fist. It wasn’t fair, or just. And she knew that because she’d learned about fairness and justice thirty years ago, on her mother’s flickering TV. 

The auditorium doors creaked open. Agatha wiped her face quickly, brushing her hair back. She glanced behind her, pretending to inspect the sets.

“Sorry, Ma’am, I didn’t know anyone was still here.”

It was Happy, brandishing his walkie talkie.

“It’s fine, I’m just cleaning up a little,” she said, still looking behind her. “I didn’t know you were even here tonight.”

Happy peered at her a little closer at her bare feet. 

“I'm always here. You okay?”

Agatha huffed.

“Why are you always asking me that?”

“Because I never really see you looking all that happy when you’re by yourself.” He paused in front of her, crossing his arms. “And you’re by yourself a lot.”

Agatha stared at him, at a loss, and then laughed a little in disbelief.

“Do you ever…” She paused, looking down at the note crumpled in her hand. “It’s stupid. My mom died, like, a really long time ago. And she was an evil bitch.”

“Sorry for your loss, Ma’am.”

“Don’t be. One time I threatened to run away, and she said she knew I would come back, because no one else would want me.”

Happy crossed his arms. “Sounds like a real piece of work.” 

“Pretty much.” Agatha played with the edge of the paper. “Do you ever get mad knowing you’ll never be able to prove someone wrong?”

“It sounds like your mom wouldn’t know something good if it hit her in her fuckin’ face.”

Agatha’s mouth dropped open, and Happy held his hands up. “Just my humble opinion.”

Agatha smiled. “That actually helps, a little.”

The doors creaked open again, and Agatha glimpsed a slip of movement, and a black hoodie. Happy turned to the noise, and then back to Agatha.

“So the first person you knew got it wrong,” he said. “That’s just bad luck. There are plenty of people here that know you’re made of the good stuff. You should probably pay more attention to them, instead of a dead evil bitch."

Agatha was so caught off guard, she laughed again. Happy grinned as he turned to leave, and Rio passed him, looking at them curiously.

“Lock up,” Happy called over his shoulder. “I don’t want to hear that this place was open all break for the high schoolers to vandalize.”

“Yeah, yeah,” Agatha called.

Rio looked like she’d just run laps around the school—cheeks flushed, hoodie sleeves rolled up, hair falling loose over her shoulders. She had a candy cane sticking out of her mouth.

Agatha swiped a palm across her cheek, hoping her tears had dried. Rio didn’t say anything. Instead, she held out the reindeer headband Agatha had worn earlier. Agatha smiled, and put it on with a flourish.

“I thought you were going to drinks,” Rio said, settling across from her in the front row.

“I am." Agatha set Brynn’s note beside her and picked up Nicky’s pencil case instead. “I thought you had plans.” 

“I do."

Agatha reached for the pencil case and unzipped it, not with any real curiosity, but because her hands needed something to hold. Her heels tapped against the edge of the stage.

“I told Josh not to get him these sparkly erasers; the pieces fall apart and it gets fucking everywhere.” She shook out her hand, the glitter already stuck stubbornly to her palm.

"I wanted to say thank you,” Rio said. “For earlier.”

Agatha glanced at her. “Oh. It was nothing. You know, all in a day’s work, or whatever.”

“It wasn’t nothing. It was…” She hesitated. “It was kind of fucking extraordinary.”

Agatha laughed. "That's a little dramatic."

“Well, so are you,” Rio said, smiling, and then picked at her nail, her smile fading. “Also, about yesterday. Is everything cool with Jen? She seemed… I don’t know. Upset.”

Agatha’s throat tightened. She looked at Rio, at the flicker of worry tucked neatly underneath her practiced indifference.

“Yeah, it's fine," she said her voice tight. She looked at the pencil case, and turned the sparkly eraser over in her hand. “But maybe we should just do what you said. Keep it professional, stop hanging out in closets and…” She looked around. “Creepy, dark auditoriums. I think people are starting to get the wrong idea, you know, and I don’t want—”

She paused, gesturing between them.

“I just wouldn’t want that to affect our jobs. We make a good team.”

Rio raised her eyebrows. “Which people are getting the wrong idea?”

Agatha hesitated. “Jen,” she admitted. “And… Josh.”

Rio’s expression shifted, her lips curving into something sharper, and she leaned back in her seat. “Ah, the husband. I take it he finally remembered?”

“Ex-husband. And he’ll keep it to himself. We worked it out. ”

Rio tilted her head, her eyes still glinting. “How exactly? Same mysterious way you ‘worked it out’ with Paul?”

Agatha stared stubbornly, and Rio leaned forward, her voice still light. “And just so I know, what exactly is this wrong idea they’re getting?

“You know what I mean.”

“No,” Rio said. “Enlighten me.”

Agatha forced a shrug. “That there’s something... going on. Between us.” 

“And you told them there wasn’t.”

“Obviously.” The incredulity in Agatha’s voice echoed across the stage.

Rio nodded, still looking at her. When she spoke, her voice was quieter.

“I have a date tonight, Agatha.” Rio said. “Those are my plans.”

Agatha felt the heat rise in her cheeks—at what, she wasn’t sure. Embarrassment, maybe? At the genuineness in Rio’s tone, like she had felt she needed to let Agatha know. To spare her the heartbreak. 

Agatha turned her head, and picked at a piece of blue masking tape.

“That’s great,” she said, and adjusted the reindeer headband. It caught in her hair. “That's impressive—a date at nine-thirty p.m. on the last day of the semester. I hope you have a great time. Don’t fall asleep.”

Rio’s lips twitched, and she watched her struggle with the plastic for a second. Then, she stood. She stepped forward into the space between Agatha’s knees. 

“Here—” she reached up, gently working the plastic from where it had snagged. 

Agatha went still. Rio’s hands in her hair and her hips nudging Agatha’s knees sent a tiny, treacherous shiver down her spine. Her fingers tightened around the pencil case.

Rio was too close, her arms reaching up to the sides of Agatha’s head, and for fuck’s sake, this is what Josh meant, and this is what Jen meant, and this is what couldn’t happen—with the door unlocked, with no four-inch hidden nook necessary to see their stupidity laid bare. 

And before she could stop herself, she said it. She meant it as something definitive, to make Rio step back, but it came out like an unwilling admission of guilt as she sat before a backdrop of a poorly-painted courtroom.

“I fucked Paul.”

Rio froze. Her fingers, still tangled in Agatha’s hair, stopped moving. Her eyes widened.

“You… is that why he—”

“Don’t ask me that.”

They stood there. The headband was half-free. Agatha could feel her heart hammering against her ribs, every inch where Rio’s fingers touched her scalp, threaded in her hair, brushed her ears. 

“You’re a good teacher,” Agatha said, quietly, evenly.

Rio nodded, slow, and looked up at her. “And that’s why you… fucked Paul.”

Agatha let the eraser fall back into the pencil case.

“Sort of,” she said. “But…don’t tell anyone. Because they might get—”

“—the wrong idea,” Rio finished for her. Her voice was almost gentle now, softer.

She pulled the headband out of Agatha’s hair, finally, and held it out to her. Agatha grasped one of the antlers, but Rio didn’t let go of the other. They stood there, each holding half of a Christmas headband, looking at each other.

Agatha swallowed. Her eyes fell to Rio’s lips.

“Yeah,” she said.

Rio let go first. She turned, running a hand through her hair, and stood there, taking in the dark house like Agatha had done not ten minutes ago. Agatha stared down at the plastic headband, and the pencil case in her glittering hands. She thought of pink poster paper Rio’s hands tucking in a borrowed shirt at Agatha’s waist. 

She thought that maybe nothing ever really changed at all—that they were just living different versions of the same life, every month, every year, until she gave everyone and everything back, and had nothing but herself, standing in another empty room.

She was forty-two now. She knew how this went. Rio would leave to meet someone for drinks at nine-thirty on a Friday, which was code for not-really-drinks. And Agatha would go to her classroom and sit there instead of joining her friends for really-drinks, because she couldn't pretend to enjoy her chips and salsa under Jen’s newfound scrutiny and Wanda’s fraying patience.

Maybe she would just stay here, and live her life at her desk in ten minute breaks until she grew old with bugles and rollercoasters, because a simple four-step was never simple to begin with. 

She wanted it to be yesterday again. After Rio took her hand, and before Jen sent them flying apart. If she closed her eyes, she could still hear the guitar. She could still feel the sway of her own body, like a buoy with the tide.

One, two, three—

She heard the rustle of fabric, and a backpack hitting the floor.

She blinked her eyes open. Rio was in front of her again, and there was a buzzing in her ears. She felt calloused hands on either side of her jaw, pulling her head down, hard.

The simple four-step had never been simple to begin with, because Rio had never actually said four. She'd said tap. And that was different. Four was final, certain, certifiable proof of the end.

A tap was a hesitation. A breath before the fifth. A heel never quite touching the ground.

Agatha's feet weren’t touching the ground at all. They were dangling off the edge of a stage. And Rio was on her tiptoes, kissing her.

 

Agatha opened her mouth in a gasp of surprise. She heard the clatter of pencils and pens as the pouch emptied over her lap, her hands grasping at something to hold onto. The sound was distant, muffled in her ears. Rio kissed her relentlessly, lips pulling intently at Agatha’s. Her head tilted to reach her, hands falling to Agatha’s thighs, pulling her closer. And finally, Agatha caught up. She threaded her hands in Rio’s hair, thumbs stroking across her jaw, holding on for dear life.

It was fast and messy and too much at once, an uncoordinated, desperate collision. Rio’s tongue slid against hers, pulling unfamiliar, helpless sounds from her throat. She could feel Rio's thumbs digging into her thighs, and pencils poking at her, as Rio nipped at her bottom lip.

Their breaths were too loud. The sound of their mouths was too loud, echoing in the dark, bouncing across the stage, because the building architect may have overlooked the angle of the rows, but he sure as shit triple-checked the acoustics.

Rio hummed into Agatha’s mouth, a low catch of sound, and Agatha’s head felt, inexplicably, all-the-way quiet. Rio smelled like rain—like wet earth, and candy canes.

Her hand slid into the back of Agatha’s hair, pulling, tipping her head as if on instinct, breaking contact with her lips. Agatha bared her throat automatically.

Of all the faces of Rio she'd come to know, she hadn’t yet seen this—her mouth swollen, eyes impossibly dark, hair wild. Glitter sparkled along her jaw, left by Agatha’s grasping hand. 

Rio’s eyes widened, as if she’d suddenly come to and found herself standing between Agatha’s legs, holding her by the hair. She let go suddenly, and stepped back.

There it was again, Agatha thought. The one, two, three.

She curled her hands around the edges of the stage, trying to breathe, but her lungs refused the relief. Her lips felt bruised, and she realized her mouth was still open. She closed it.

Rio wiped the back of her hand across her mouth, and then kept it there.

“I—” she said, the sound muffled.

Agatha smoothed her hair back, looking at the pencils on the stage floor. She didn’t want to hear what came next.

She twisted around and picked them up, one by one, as if Rio hadn’t just kissed her within an inch of her life. As if her tongue hadn’t been in Rio’s mouth five seconds ago, tasting the sound of her quiet hum. 

Up from the floor, into the case. One pencil, then two, and so on, and so. Like the first warm morning each year when she plucked the thistle from her porch. The soft, purple flowers from family Asteraceae, same as the sunflowers in the backyard. One standing tall with its face to the light, and the other a prickly weed, demanding sweat and leather gloves, and penance, on her knees.

“Have a good date,” Agatha said, voice smooth. She reached for a pencil that was stuck to Rio’s half-eaten candy cane. Her hand trembled lightly.

“Yeah, right. You too,” Rio said. “No, I mean—thanks. Yeah.”

It was silent then, and Agatha heard the quiet roaring in her ears, sick from the taste of peppermint on her tongue for an entirely new reason. And when she looked up, the pencils collected and penance paid, Rio was gone.

 

 

On the twenty-fifth day of December, Agatha finally opened her four-day-old mail. She stood in her kitchen, her house too quiet. The front yard was bare of her usual Christmas army—three half-made snowmen, two fallen angels, and one shivering, ecstatic little boy. 

She wanted the clean, satisfying rip of her finger through the paper seam. The envelope came apart softly in her hands instead. She held certificate number five by its bottom edge.

 

COMMONWEALTH OF MASSACHUSETTS

CERTIFICATE OF DIVORCE ABSOLUTE

 

And then, underneath the proceedings, in fine print: 

 

This judgment restores the parties to the status of single, unmarried persons.

 

So, on the twenty-fifth day of December, between her second and third cup of coffee, a piece of parchment between her forefinger and thumb—Agatha’s heels pressed solidly into the cold kitchen tile. 

And if her heels were touching the ground, then this was beat four—the certifiable end of the measure. Which meant that, really, the fifth beat was the same thing as the first.

The fifth was the window cracked open to the storm, and fingers cricked tight against her jaw, and glitter under stage lights in June, or December.

Agatha didn’t rip the certificate in half, or burn it on her doorstep. It would have been too much trouble. The fifth certificate was just another envelope in the mail, stuck between the superstore coupons and the phone plan offerings for the couple next door. 

She tucked it into the shoebox, next to the passports and her mother’s diamond earrings that she would maybe wear one day. And on the twenty-fifth day of December, Agatha let herself believe that maybe the fifth was just the beginning.

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

 

 

“All freedom is relative—you know too well—and sometimes it’s no freedom at all, but simply the cage widening far away from you, the bars abstracted with distance but still there, as when they “free” wild animals into nature preserves only to contain them yet again by larger borders. But I took it anyway, that widening. Because sometimes not seeing the bars is enough.”

- Ocean Vuong, On Earth We’re Briefly Gorgeous 




As a second grade teacher with twenty years of experience and two degrees under her belt, Agatha was more or less an expert on children between the ages of seven and eight. Before she was an expert, when she was just trying to pick a career that didn’t make her want to walk into traffic, she’d come across a book at a garage sale: Your Seven Year-old: Life in a Minor Key. An entire book dedicated to the nuances of seven—the unique tinge of anxiety and worry, and a fascination with the macabre. Agatha, intrigued, had tucked the book under her jacket when the woman wasn’t looking.

Agatha still wasn’t sure when it was or wasn’t okay to steal something if you really wanted it. She'd grown up watching Evanora pocket lottery tickets and expensive jewelry, and, if Agatha was lucky, an extra Twinkie. She was her mother’s daughter, after all—still watching Law & Order on the cable her mother had ‘borrowed’ from next-door Norma, even though Norma was long dead and her son was only keeping the apartment for something called “market value.” 

Evanora and Agatha had only moved away for one year before returning to the water-damaged walls of that shitty apartment, and Evanora’s mental faculties were growing worse by the day. Agatha wasn’t sure whether it was some sort of medical condition that made her lose her mind and her tongue, or a kind of drug Agatha didn’t know about, or if it was just the kind of person she was. Agatha knew she was smart enough to figure it out, but ultimately decided that reading about death-obsessed children was a better use of her time.

She read the book on her walk to school her senior year, after she'd decided to stop skipping class. She'd come to this decision over the summer, when Brandon Murphy had invited her to his house to watch Good Will Hunting on his new DVD player. She couldn’t have cared less about big-mouth Brandon and his weird, tall hair, but she couldn’t say no to a free movie. 

They'd been halfway in before she’d realized that she was Will Hunting, stuck in Southie with a mother who'd never wanted her and made sure she knew it. The lights in Brandon’s basement were too bright, and her heartbeat picked up, and she felt that something important was happening.

That something important was definitely not Brandon sticking his tongue down her throat, and she finally shoved him off and snapped, “I’m trying to watch the fuckin’ movie,” with the Boston accent that would later fade, and the defiance that would not.

There was her life before that day, and her life after. Walking out of Brandon’s house as he waved pathetically from the doorstep, she understood what all the teachers had been going on about this whole time: people who went to college got to move away from their mothers. 

So she decided on teaching. She would sidestep the usual potholes on her route, nose buried in the book, oblivious to the irony of possibly being hit by a car while reading about healthy child development. She was eighteen and hooked, and although she’d missed the mark on the whole not-walking-into-traffic aspect of the job, the book had steered her faithfully to seven.

On her second day of student teaching, when she was twenty-one, she’d been admonished by her mentor teacher, Mrs. Finegar—a rigid, ancient woman who called glasses spectacles, and carried a ruler in her hand. She never used it, of course, but Agatha often wondered if she should’ve even been allowed to carry it around like that, slapping her own palm like she was nostalgic for simpler times. Agatha quickly turned Mrs. Finegar to Vinegar in her head, both for the obvious reason and for her distinctly acidic qualities. 

Vinegar had come in on the second day of school to find children crowded around Agatha, hushed and positively enraptured. Shocked that Agatha could quiet a crowd on just her second day, Vinegar came closer and saw what was captivating their attention—Agatha’s detailed drawing and description of the roadkill she’d seen on her way to school that morning. A skunk, as evidenced by the smell, portrayed by three wavy lines.

Agatha was explaining the different types of scavengers that survived off of decomposing flesh, and had just started to clarify that seagulls did, in fact, eat carrion in addition to the french fries from the school trash, when Vinegar slapped a hand down on the drawing. The children scattered to their seats, and she looked at Agatha through her spectacles settled low on the bridge of her nose. But Agatha, by nature, had never been wet behind the ears, and by nurture, she’d grown tired of righteous old women. She stared right back.

“This is incredibly inappropriate,” Vinegar hissed. “What were you thinking?”

“They wanted to learn,” Agatha replied with a shrug. “Sometimes, animals die.”

Vinegar reported her to her advisor and the district supervisor, and so began the reputation she did not choose. But that same day, a small girl with ringlets named Lucille came up to her desk. She asked loudly why a seagull would want to eat a dead skunk instead of a live one, when her pet gecko wouldn’t eat the crickets she was feeding him from the freezer. 

“Frank only eats the ones that jump,” she’d said, her eyes wide and curious. 

And so, two hours later, began the reputation Agatha did choose. Vinegar left her alone after that, to her simple truths and the macabre—to conduct the room in her beloved minor key. At twenty-one, six years after a movie convinced her to try, Agatha had something better than Will Hunting’s chalkboard equation: her very first class of seven year-olds, won over in earnest by a drawing of a dead skunk.

 

 

Today, Agatha had half a mind to contact the authors of Your Seven Year-Old: Life in a Minor Key and sue them for false advertising. They’d claimed seven was less wild than six or eight, and now the book sat smugly on a shelf in her living room, presiding over a pile of spaghetti Nicky had ceremoniously dumped on the hardwood floor.

Nicky, by all accounts, was an angelic child, and Agatha felt uniquely qualified to make the assessment. He was even-tempered, empathetic, sweet, and fun. But tonight, on this evening in January of a brand new year, her angelic child was standing over his upside-down bowl of spaghetti, staring her dead in the eyes.

“Nicky,” she said slowly, her forehead vein pulsing. “Pick it up.”

“No."

His voice was calm enough for Agatha to break out of her fraying patience for a second and wonder—not for the first time—if maybe her kid was so angelic because he was actually a sociopath. 

A blob of tomato sauce dotted his ankle, and she spotted bits of parmesan cheese in his tangled hair. She felt the restrained rage in her chest scratching desperately for release. He hadn’t even dropped it by accident—he'd lost all semblance of control, and thrown it off the table in a fit of fury, all because she'd said “no ice cream.”

This is so cliché, she wanted to tell him. Losing your shit over no dessert.

But there were two problems with that. The first, and the most obvious, was that children were cliché. They had basic needs and basic reactions and it was all so expected and unavoidable. She knew that too well, twenty times over by the year. But no one—authors, teachers, or otherwise—had informed her how much harder it would be when she had to bring the kid home at the end of the day.

The second, and newer problem, was that she was alone. She lost her power as a seasoned educator the moment Nicky stepped through the front door each afternoon. Everything she said was useless, and everything he said seemed deliberately designed to drive her up the wall. Now it was just her, alone in her living room, with him.

She couldn’t do what she knew she should—stand here with him and make him clean it up, or de-escalate, like she could any other time of the day or day of the week. It was three minutes past his bedtime, and there was a marinara crime scene all over him and the hardwood floor, and now he needed a shower, which meant she’d be getting one, too. The last time they had worked on Nicky showering by himself, he’d gotten distracted by a soap bubble for twenty minutes and forgotten all of the instructions. Agatha had decided maybe they would try again in a year, and not a day sooner.

“School starts tomorrow,” Agatha said. “Don’t you want to get in your comfy-cozy bed and have a nice, big sleep, and wake up excited and ready to go?”

She almost heard laughing in her head as she said it, recognizing the absurdity of thinking that line had ever worked on a stubborn, wound-up child.

“NO!” Nicky shouted, and ran to the bathroom.

Agatha heard his feet pattering on the hardwood, and watched his trail of tomato sauce footprints. She closed her eyes, and took a deep breath. And then another. She took off her glasses, tied her hair back, and tore off a few paper towels. Maybe he was washing his hands, she thought hopefully. And his hair, and his clothes. 

She scooped the spaghetti up by hand, dumped it in the trash, and knelt to tackle the sauce. She didn’t have it in her—a standoff with the only person alive who could win. Or maybe one of two people, she amended, her knees aching from the floor. 

Her chest still burned at the thought of Rio, two weeks later. She’d spent every spare day to herself trying to distract the singular pull of her own mind—lying in bed for hours in the middle of the day, scrolling and snacking and reverting to a version of herself she hadn’t known in years. She thought some people called this depression, and others called it rest. 

Sometimes, out of nowhere, she would feel it the sudden hush in the auditorium, and Rio’s voice turned soft, and her hands in Agatha’s hair. Her lips, her teeth, her tongue. And then the look on her face, like she didn’t know what to do, even though she was the one who had done in the first place.

Since November, she'd grown used to imagining the feel of Rio’s lips on hers and her breath in her ear. She already knew the feel of her skin, the scratch of lace against her chest in the dark, and now that she’d given herself permission to remember, it had almost become a habit. Her breath rasping, thighs shaking, hand pressing hard between them. 

But she read somewhere that it took sixty-six days to make a habit, and she’d only made it to fifty-nine before Rio kissed her on the stage in the dark. She hadn’t touched herself since, so the hobby died just seven days short.

The problem was that if she thought about the kiss, she thought about what came after. She didn’t want to keep seeing it in slow motion, Rio stepping back with a hand to her mouth in panic and regret. But the real, truer problem was much worse than that: she wanted, desperately, for it to happen again. She was aching for it, and knew she couldn’t have it. Another humiliating cliché. 

The paper towel ripped under her hand, and she realized she’d been scrubbing the saturated sauce back into the floor. She sat up, wiping her forehead with the back of her arm, and dropped the wad of paper towels next to her. Sometimes, being a full person and raising another was altogether too hard to be expected of her.

She heard a quiet pattering and looked up. Nicky had returned, and was looking at her curiously. He'd changed his shirt, which was backwards, and not the piece of clothing that had sauce on it in the first place.

“Are you okay, Mama?” He put his arms around her neck and leaned his dead weight on her, falling into her lap. 

“No,” she sighed, looking down at him. “I’m upset that you threw your dinner on the floor.”

Nicky looked at her, his eyes wide and genuine, no trace of the stubbornness from five minutes ago.

“I’m sorry,” he said seriously. “I was just mad because I really want ice cream and you said no.”

She rubbed his back as he patiently explained this to her in his halting little speech. She wished that her seven year old understood the basic logic and necessity of not eating dessert for every meal, and knew it was an impossibility due to the size of his tiny brain. 

“I know, baby,” she said finally, squeezing him. “We just can’t eat it every day anymore, okay?”

“Okay,” he said sullenly, and she smiled a little.

“Thanks for changing your shirt.”
