He grinned then, swinging from her neck in her lap, and she grimaced. He was getting too big to use her like a jungle gym, but she wasn’t ready to tell him to stop. Maybe one more year for that, too.

“Let’s play hot-lava-tag,” he squealed in her ear.

There was quite literally nothing in the world she wanted to do less right now, and the thought weighed on her. It was the unique shape and pull of Mother’s Guilt, that she should be used to this kind of thing. That she was uniquely qualified for it.

Her phone rang, and she breathed in relief, moving Nicky off of her lap with a kiss. He bounded to the living room anyway, apparently set on playing with or without her. She watched the phone vibrate over a stack of bills, and inexplicably, she wondered if it was Rio. Two weeks was the longest she hadn’t heard from her. Maybe she was calling to quit. Or maybe she wanted to talk, which was somehow worse. 

If she waited any longer to grab her phone, it would vibrate itself straight off the counter. She waited for two more seconds before flipping it over. A string of numbers filled the screen, and she breathed in relief, again.

“Hello?” She leaned against the counter to watch Nicky, who was jumping between the couch cushions on the floor.

“Hi, this is OB Pharmacy calling to let you know that your prescription is ready for pickup.”

Agatha frowned. “I picked it up last week. Birth control, right?”

“No, I have an inhaler refill for Rio Vidal.”

Agatha froze.

“No,” she stuttered. “That’s not— I’m not— Who put down this number?”

“We received it from the office of your primary care provider.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes. “No—who, specifically?”

She heard the rustle of papers. “A nurse named…Darcy Lewis?”

Agatha pinched the bridge of her nose. That fucking idiot of a nurse. She’d said emergency contact, not pharmacy.

Agatha hung up and called Rio three times with no answer. Her worry turned acutely from What the fuck am I going to say, to what if she’s lying in a ditch somewhere and can’t breathe?

She looked at Nicky.

“It’s your lucky day, kid.” Nicky paused from jumping on a sofa cushion to look at her. “Get your coat. We’re going on a field trip.”

“Yay!” Nicky yelled, and threw his hands in the air.

 

 

Agatha sat in her car with the paper bag in the passenger seat and Nicky in the back, bobbing along to the music. Not for the first time, she regretted her moment of weakness in the hospital on Halloween. So what? Rio had no emergency contact. Plenty of people had no emergency contact. She could have just kept her mouth shut like a normal, casual work acquaintance. But she hadn't, and now, there were admittedly no good options.

She called a fourth time, this time out of desperation. The line connected on the third ring, and she heard Lilia’s distinctive ‘Hello?” 

“Is Rio okay?” she blurted, gut twisting with equal parts worry and guilt. What if Rio had suffocated to death, all because Agatha had put her name down? This would be her second accidental murder attempt in three months.

“I’m sure she is,” Lilia said. “Your calls are getting a little disruptive. I was in the middle of a session and I could hear the damn phone vibrating from the next room.”

Agatha suppressed her immediate response, which included some choice language she was at least sixty percent sure Nicky hadn't heard before. 

“Where is she," she said instead. "I need to talk to her.” 

“Why is it I only hear from you when you don’t know where she is?”

“Because you’re her roommate! And I called Rio.” 

“Well, I thought I would have heard from you by now about how your tarot draw is faring,” Lilia said. “I may be clairvoyant, but that doesn’t mean I don’t appreciate a little feedback.”

“How could I give you feedback? You don’t have a phone." Agatha ignored the flush crawling up her face. “Listen—I have Rio’s inhaler refill. Does she need it tonight, or can I bring it to school tomorrow?”

“Intriguing,” Lilia hummed. “I wouldn’t know.”

“Thought you were clairvoyant,” Agatha grumbled, and Lilia chuckled at that.

“I think you know that Rio requires more of us than a simple convening of the spirits. But she doesn’t usually need a new inhaler so quickly. And she is doing manual labor today, so best if you drop it off with her just in case.”

Agatha grimaced. “Manual labor? Is that what we’re calling it now?”

“Not at the club, dear. Different kind of labor.”

“How about I drop it off with you?”

“Absolutely not. I have another client tonight and I will not have your sodden aura in my living room to dampen the spirits.”

Agatha tilted her head back against the headrest, and closed her eyes. Nicky kicked the back of her seat.

“Can you at least tell me if this place is G-rated?” Agatha said finally. “My kid is with me.”

“Johnny’s Christmas Tree farm, right outside Salem. You can’t miss it.”

“It’s January."

“You asked, I answered. I thought you would be thrilled—no traffic, twenty-minute drive, and not even raining. This time, you can show up without looking like a drowned cat. Now, is there any other critical, free information I can provide for you, or may I go back to honing my craft and making a living?”

Agatha almost smiled at the satisfaction of pulling an ounce of frustration from her.

“That will be all, dear,” she sneered, and Lilia promptly hung up.

She caught Nicky’s eye in the rearview. “How’s the super special nighttime field trip?”

“Fun,” Nicky said, wide-eyed, and Agatha smiled.

“Good. I think you’re going to like our next stop even better.”

 

 

The sign to the farm was massive, but unlit—Agatha almost missed it. She turned at the last second, crunching into the gravel beside another car. Rio’s car.

“Mama, where are we?” Nicky asked, craning his neck.

“That,” Agatha said, twisting around, “is an excellent question. Ms. V forgot something, and I need to give it to her.”

“What is it?”

“Medicine.”

“Is she sick?”

“No, she’s fine.”

Nicky looked out the window again. “Can I say hi to her?”

Agatha smiled. “Of course, baby. But don’t tell any of the kids tomorrow that you got to see her before school started, okay? It’ll be our little secret.”

Nicky nodded, holding a finger to his lips, and unbuckled with great effort, pushing both hands into the red button. Agatha still liked watching him do that—grasp independence with both hands, through this small thing they’d taught him. One day he would just need one hand, and then just a thumb. And then he wouldn’t be sitting in the backseat at all, but she didn’t like to think about that part.

The gravel crunched under their feet, and the air was sharper here. She'd grown up in the city with little ability to leave it, and was still baffled that twenty minutes could take them from suburbs to farmland and forest.

By the time she was done zipping up Nicky’s layers, he was restless, hopping from one foot to the other. His sneakers lit up with each jump, colors flashing across the light dusting of snow.

“I see her,” he said, pointing and tugging on Agatha’s hand. 

Agatha whipped her head around in the direction of his finger, and squinted. Down a dirt and gravel path, at the bottom of a gentle hill, lights poked through the darkness. She could make out farmhouse windows, and a figure moving faintly in front of them.

“Can you really see that far?” Agatha said, adjusting her glasses, and Nicky nodded at her. Either he was full of shit, or her vision was getting worse.

“Okay, let’s go,” she said, holding his hand. “And this is someone else’s property. So best behavior, okay?”

“Is this Ms. V’s house?”

“No.”

“Who lives here?”

“I don’t—” Agatha caught the response just before it snapped, the barbs catching in her throat. She took a breath, and they loosened. “I don’t know, baby. Let’s find out.”

“Okay,” Nicky said emphatically, pulling on her hand. 

 

 

Agatha could see her breath as she walked, and she steeled herself. Either Rio was slowly dying and would be very glad to see her, or she wasn’t, which would make this all much harder to explain.

As they wove down the path, the moon shone brightly above them. To her right, Christmas trees stood deep green and stiff, pine needles glinting silver. To her left, they lay on the ground in heaps, branches twisted and snapped in places. She spotted a dented red ornament on a broken branch.

As the lights and the farmhouse grew nearer, Agatha could see that the figure in front was, in fact, Rio. She stood by the light of the porch in a black hoodie. Her arms were raised above her head, holding—an axe? Agatha watched as she brought it down in one precise, brutal motion, slamming it into the dead tree trunk in front of her. The sharp crack split the silence, and Agatha stopped in her tracks.

Nicky glanced back at her, frustrated at the interruption in pace, and Agatha stared at Rio. She looked a little terrifying, hooded and backlit, the axe clutched tight at her side. The moon gave her face a ghostly glow where it escaped the shadows of her hood, eyes gleaming and sweat glistening on her nose. She stood perfectly still in the silence, and the only thing moving was her chest, rising and falling, the air releasing into clouds in front of her lips. Alive, Agatha thought. Rio was so alive.

She felt her pulse spike, her breath coming faster. Rio was oblivious in the pitch dark, the axe raised above her head again. Her face was strangely, eerily calm. And Agatha was suddenly furious.

She had been crawling out of her own skin for two weeks, enduring days of exhaustion and half-dreams where she could still feel hands in her hair and gripping her jaw, with no idea why Rio had lost her fucking mind for thirty seconds, then apparently forgotten about it. Because here she was, splitting logs under the moonlight like some ridiculous, stoic lesbian lumberjack.  

Before she could decide whether to announce herself or just throw the inhaler at Rio’s head, Nicky beat her to it.

He yelled “Hi,” innocently into the night. The word hit Rio like someone had poured ice water down her sweatshirt. Her head jerked up, eyes wide, hood falling back. The axe dropped to her side.

She froze, eyes on Nicky, and then a surprised laugh burst out of her as he threw his arms around her legs in a thoughtless hug. She hugged him back with her free hand, and her eyes found Agatha’s, her mouth still open in disbelief.

“What…” she started, and Agatha could already feel the heat in her own cheeks at the humiliation of driving to Salem to save Rio’s life, only to find her perfectly alive, athleticism and strength on full display. Agatha stepped closer and thrust the paper bag toward Rio.

“Lilia told me where you were. I didn’t know if you—” Her voice caught, the briskness in her tone undercut by the wet click. She cleared her throat, and tried again. “I thought maybe you needed this.”

Rio took the bag slowly, peering inside.

“This is mine?”

“There was a phone number mix-up with your pharmacy,” Agatha said flatly. “And a nurse who should be fired. Or shot into the fucking sun.”

“A mix-up,” Rio repeated, watching her.

Agatha shifted. Her chest was tight and the cool, fresh air wasn’t helping. Maybe she needed the inhaler.

“I put myself down as your emergency contact,” she said finally, the words squeezing out of her. “On Halloween.”

Rio blinked, and her face shifted. She looked stunned, her mouth slightly open.

“Nicky,” Agatha snapped, watching him reach for the axe out of the corner of her eye. “Don’t.”

He looked away innocently, and wandered to a pile of branches out of earshot, jumping over it.

“If you’re inches from death, I’m going to need to know about it,” Agatha said, waving a hand dismissively. “Logistically speaking. But you can take me off whenever you want.”

Rio was still staring at her, the start of a smile on her lips, and Agatha bristled. 

“Like, if Lilia gets a phone,” she continued. “Or if you and your… mysterious hot date get serious.”

Rio raised an eyebrow.

“She’s not mysterious. She’s pretty uncomplicated, actually.”

Agatha felt the pointed edge under her casualness, and looked away.

“And let me get this straight,” Rio continued. “You drove to Salem on a Sunday night to ask me about my date from two weeks ago?”

Agatha’s jaw clenched. “Of course not,” she said, too fast. “I drove to Salem because you left your fucking phone at home, and I didn’t know if you were—” She stopped abruptly.

Rio’s expression softened. Her eyes shifted to the ground, and then back to Agatha’s.

“I’m fine.”

“Yeah, I can see that.” 

They stood there for a few seconds in silence, Agatha staring at the pieces of tree trunk at their feet. Rio’s axe rested against the ground like a sinister sort of cane—blade in the dirt, twirling idly between her fingers. It fit, somehow. She fit here.

Agatha wondered who else had ever been invited here besides her, and then remembered that she wasn’t actually invited at all. She had just shown up without warning, expecting… what, exactly?

She felt the trees on either side of her, watching them, her feet planted in the gravel—one foot on the side of the living, the other with the dead. Her hands felt empty without the crinkle of the paper bag, or Nicky’s tugging grasp.

“Right. Well.” She gestured to Nicky. “Sorry for the interruption. We’ll see you tomorrow.”

“You said we were having a field trip,” Nicky whined.

“We did, and it’s over now,” Agatha said sharply, and Nicky’s face crumpled into a scowl. Agatha turned to face him fully, and opened her mouth.

“You guys can stay and help, if you want,” Rio offered.

Nicky looked at Agatha hopefully, and she ground her teeth together. She was outnumbered. 

“What is it you’re doing, exactly?” she said instead of answering. “Also, where are we?”

Rio grinned, wide and easy, and Agatha stared at her. Her smile was easier, bigger, open. Maybe it was just the two weeks of rest, or the fact that she was having stupid hot sex with an uncomplicated stranger, or maybe it was something about this dark, cold air. 

“Lilia grew up here."

“Really?” Agatha replied despite herself, kicking idly at the stray wood chips. “I always figured she grew up, in, like, a cult. Or a commune.”

Rio laughed. “She lived here with her four sisters for a long time, so I guess that’s not far off.”

“Who’s Johnny?” Agatha pointed to the sign, and Rio smiled.

“They inherited this place in the seventies and they wanted to name it after Joni Mitchell. Shockingly, suburban men from Massachusetts in the seventies did not want to drive their families to a farm called Joni’s, run by five women.”

Agatha hummed, impressed despite herself. “One point for Lilia.”

“She and the two sisters that are left fight like crazy, so I usually leave Lilia at home. But the other two are getting too old for this kind of work, and I don’t mind it.”

“What are you doing?” Nicky asked, back from his stick-hopping quest.

“I’m taking the old trees that people return after Christmas,” Rio said, pointing to the piles. “And then I’m chopping them up into smaller pieces, so that we can toss them in the wood chipper, and turn it into mulch.”

“Christmas trees turn into dirt?” Nicky asked, nose scrunched.

Rio smiled. “Yup, and then they use the dirt to plant the next round of trees, and vegetables and flowers in the spring.”

“Cool."

“Very,” Rio agreed. “Do you think you can find all the smaller sticks and branches lying on the ground, and come bring them to this pile?”

Nicky nodded enthusiastically and ran off without question.

“Maybe this will finally tire him out,” Agatha muttered. “The kid is fucking nocturnal.”

“I was being serious,” Rio said, eyeing her. “If you’re going to stay, you need to help.”

“Jesus fucking Christ, okay.” Agatha was still a little incredulous at the whole thing, and still a little furious. She glared, and held up her hands. “Tell me what to do.”

Rio smirked, her eyes catching the moonlight. Agatha swallowed, and regretted her choice of words. 

“Drag over the medium and large ones. Here.” Rio tossed Agatha a pair of leather gloves from the ground. “Think you can handle that?”

Agatha scoffed, and didn’t bother with an answer. She got to work, her breath fogging in the dark, trying not to think about how she had ended up on a farm, watching Rio chop wood like an image straight from some low-quality lesbian porn. Agatha had once watched one that started exactly like this—a woman with an axe, the tree stump, and a voluptuous caretaker pulling up in a pickup. Then a jarring cut to the two of them fucking against said tree stump, both of them unrealistically fresh-faced, not a hair out of place. She’d turned it off once she started thinking about splinters. Neither of the women looked like they were enjoying it, anyway, and Agatha had decided she wasn’t particularly interested in the type altogether.

Now, though, with Rio’s hands gripped tight over the handle of the axe, her breath coming in sharp bursts against the night air, and the muscles Agatha knew were rippling at her back underneath the sweatshirt—Agatha was suddenly having second thoughts. First about the type, and then about every last decision she’d made in the last hour.

They worked for almost an hour in near silence. Agatha had forgotten how good it felt to have her hands in the earth, even through leather gloves. The last time she'd tried, besides the necessary weeding under her porch, was five years ago, when she attempted to grow a garden outside her classroom. In a matter of weeks, the squirrels had eaten the sunflowers and the tomatoes, and the wooden base had fallen apart, and then stayed that way. But now, she could just work. The life cycle of Christmas trees wasn’t hers to own, or to tend. She was just visiting. 

Agatha straightened, her arms starting to ache, and wiped her forehead with the sleeve of her coat.

“Okay, what is all of this?” she said finally, gesturing vaguely at Rio. Rio paused, eyebrows raised expectantly.

“Why are you acting so…” Agatha trailed off. “Happy?”

Rio snorted, and Agatha lowered her voice. “Just be honest. A farm named after Joni Mitchell, and four other versions of Lilia? The weed in that house has got to be fucking incredible.”

“You think I’m high right now?” Rio laughed, and Agatha crossed her arms. She ignored the twist of jealousy at the thought that this sudden lightness might be about a couple good dates, and a person she knew nothing about. 

“I just like it here,” Rio said. “It’s—”

“Uncomplicated,” Agatha offered, with a little more bite than she intended.

Rio’s eyes flashed, just briefly.

“Yeah." 

Agatha dropped her leather gloves on the stump, running a hand through her hair. She felt the banter turning brittle, as it always seemed to. She spotted Nicky in the dirt pile, drawing pictures with his finger. He yawned, wide and slow, and Agatha knew it was past time to put him to bed. She remembered suddenly that she’d left the towels of tomato sauce on her hardwood floor.

Rio followed her gaze and put down her axe, nodding in question to the farmhouse. Agatha hesitated. Maybe a few more minutes would be harmless. It would be pointless to spend all this time doing the work without the prize of seeing the inside of Lilia’s old house, so she nodded her head once in reply.

“I could really use some hot chocolate,” Rio said loudly.

Nicky’s head shot up, and Agatha tried not to laugh as he scrambled to his feet.

“I want hot chocolate,” he said. “Can I have some too?”

“Let’s go see if there’s enough.”

“I really hope there is,” he said, on her heels. “Or maybe I can make more. I know how to make it.”

Agatha snorted at that. “Hope it’s comfy down in that grave you just dug,” she muttered, and Rio turned to smirk at her, tongue in cheek, as the warmth enveloped them.

 

 

Agatha didn’t know what to expect from a farm dedicated to Joni Mitchell in the seventies by five opinionated sisters. She wouldn’t have guessed what it looked like, and yet, everything she looked at seemed perfectly placed.

Her mouth hung open at the sheer volume of things covering the walls and stuffed in the corners. It was less of a collection, and more just accumulation. There was no clear style, surfaces covered with seashells and ceramics shaped like farm animals. It seemed like each sister had preferred a specific style—disco, beach, boho, cottage, modern farmhouse—and no one had wanted to relinquish their own in favor of any sort of cohesion.

She paused to look at the wall of photos, eyes landing on a large portrait in the middle: five women, standing in a kickline.

“Holy shit,” she murmured. “Lilia was hot.”

“What do you mean was?” Rio laughed from the kitchen. “They’re all timeless bombshells. Except maybe the dead ones.”

“Are they here? The live ones?” Agatha peered around the room like they might emerge from the corners with tchotchkes held like pitchforks to the strangers in their kitchen.

“Poker night across town,” Rio said, distracted. She was pouring Nicky a mug of hot cocoa from a thermos, and Agatha felt a flicker of recognition at the color of the cup.

“Not to be boring and uptight,” Agatha said. “But is that the Fiestaware with or without the uranium in it?”

“I replaced the vintage,” Rio said with a smile. “All Fiestaware is post-nineties.”

“What’s uranium?” Nicky said, taking a big sip.

“Something you are not allowed to eat.”

“I want to try it,” Nicky said, and Agatha rolled her eyes. 

“Hey, can you go drink that at the table?” 

“Can I drink it on the couch?” 

Agatha glared at him, and Rio cleared her throat. 

“It’s in the next room," she said. “And there’s a basket of toys and games and stuff in there, too, if you want to look through it.” 

Nicky held the cup carefully, walking slowly to the living room.

“Is that how you know Lilia?” Agatha looked at the photo again. “She was a dancer?”

“Big time. But then she kind of lost her shit a little. Or, the way she puts it, she had a different calling.”

“Seriously? She quit dancing to be a psychic?”

Rio shrugged. “She loves what she does. Can’t really argue with that.”

Agatha looked at her carefully. “Do you believe in that stuff? That she can really see the future, or whatever?”

Rio blew air out of the side of her mouth, pulling two beers out of the fridge.

“When I was seventeen, I tried to steal some gas station sushi for dinner, and I got caught. I was sitting in handcuffs outside, and Lilia Calderu, a former Radio City Rockette, appeared out of nowhere, telling the cop she was my mother.”

Agatha stared.

“My parents had only kicked me out that morning,” Rio continued. “The only way I knew Lilia was from an essay I wrote on her in school. If you can explain that to me, then maybe I’ll be more skeptical. But some things defy logic, and I’ve come to accept that Lilia,” she tilted her bottle towards Agatha, “is one of them.”

She took a long swig, and Agatha took the second bottle, picturing Lilia sweeping her shawl over teenage Rio in handcuffs.

“Did you ever ask her about it?”

“I tried, at first." Rio smiled. “But you know how she is. For all I know, the spirits told her I needed a place to stay. Or maybe they told her I was about to fucking die from eating two-dollar gas station sushi. Either way, she showed up. I don’t really care why.”

Rio leaned forward on the counter, brushing her hair back, her sweatshirt thrown on the kitchen chair. She wore a Goonies t-shirt, and Agatha smiled. She wondered how old Rio had been when she watched The Goonies for the first time, and then her smile faded, thinking of her parents.

“I’m sorry your parents kicked you out,” Agatha said, rubbing the label of the bottle. It felt like an insane thing to say, but she didn’t know how to edge past it gracefully.

Rio smirked. “You don’t even know why they kicked me out. What if I was a teenage menace to society?”

“Were you?”

Rio laughed, and then chewed her bottom lip.

“Worse,” she said finally. “I fell in love with a girl.”

Agatha’s fingers tightened around the bottle.

“What happened?” she asked softly. “If you want to– I mean, you don’t have to, if you–”

“She was a cheerleader,” Rio grinned. “We were studying for a math test in my room one day, and she started touching my face and stuff, so I kissed her. She kissed me back, tongue and all, like a fucking maniac. You know how it is when you’re young and everything just feels so… sparky and desperate?”

Agatha thought of the first time she kissed a boy playing seven minutes in heaven in sixth grade, feeling unsure where to put her hands. Then she thought of the high school dates in the park on summer nights, that inevitably ended with half-hearted hand jobs so she could go home in time to put Benadryl cream on her mosquito bites and watch Xena. So, maybe she didn’t really know. Maybe she'd never known.

“So…yeah,” Rio said. “I let her finger me all over my algebra textbook, and it was probably so terrible, but at the time, I was obviously like, ‘Fuck yeah, this is the meaning of life.’”

“Are you telling this story to brag that you fucked a cheerleader in high school?” Agatha said, and Rio laughed. Agatha's eyes caught on the exposed column of her throat, and the stray wood chips nestled in her hair. The confidence in her posture, smug and unashamed. 

Agatha flicked her bottle cap across the counter and hit her in the arm.

“Ow. Don’t be jealous.”

“What next, Velma?” Agatha said innocently, settling on a barstool with her chin in hand. “Did you go down on Daphne with a calculator and a magnifying glass?”  

“No time," Rio shot back. "She got up off me, walked right into the kitchen crying, and told my parents I was the devil. She didn’t even wash her hands.”

Agatha stared.

“It’s fine,” Rio said, swallowing a sip and letting out a burp. "She’s in prison now.” 

Agatha choked on her beer. “Really?” 

“No. She's a preacher’s wife. But kind of the same thing, right?”

Agatha felt the laugh burst out of her. 

“I’m really sorry that didn’t work out for you,” she said, catching her breath. Then after a beat, she added, “And I am sorry that your parents kicked you out.”

Rio’s fingers played absently with the pepper shaker between them, shaped like Road Runner. There was a chip in the base, and Rio balanced him on his edge, catching him as he fell. Balance, tip, fall, catch. 

“I don’t need pity,” Rio said finally. Road Runner teetered on one leg for almost a full second this time. Agatha wondered if he would stay there, somehow perfectly balanced on a single rounded leg, but then the second passed, and he fell into Rio’s waiting hand. 

“What I need,” she said, “is some goddamn generational wealth.”

Agatha snorted and reached for the salt shaker. She held the ceramic Wile E. Coyote, and inspected his gleaming yellow eyes and sharpened teeth. He was holding a stick of red dynamite. 

“I don’t pity you,” she said, tracing the shape of his black nose, and then the fuse. “Some sorries are still worth saying.”

She set Wile E. down, pointing him towards Rio’s restless hands, and wandered around the kitchen. She knew there was only so long she could rummage through junk and turn over undisturbed treasures before Rio’s patience wore thin. She hid her smile as she pilfered through drawers.

She picked up farm-animal utensils, and “oohed ” at a display of seven different-sized rolling pins. She inspected the uranium-free dishware, and read the label of three different bottles in the wine rack, letting out two sounds of disgust and one appreciative hum. A small, square photo on the fridge caught her attention, and she paused her theatrics, moving closer.

It was Rio, her eyes somehow even bigger in her younger face. She looked maybe eighteen or nineteen, in a full split on the front porch, hands in the air. She had bangs, and there was a blur of a black, curly dog next to her. It was strange, this section of a full, real life Agatha knew nothing about. 

Then, she noticed a picture to the side. Rio, around the same age, but she was sitting in the porch rocking chair instead. Her leg was propped up on a stool, knee in bandages, a pair of crutches leaning beside her. The sunset cast her face in orange, and she stared into the distance, her eyes glowing hazel and lost in thought. Her hand clutched the crutch too tight, her healthy knee bent protectively to her chest.

Before Agatha could comment, Rio plucked the second photo from the fridge and tucked it in her pocket. Agatha raised her eyebrows.

“I don’t like that one,” Rio said quietly.

“Hmm. Picky,” Agatha replied. “I’m sure they’d prefer a photo of you dancing on some sweaty frat boy with a boner. You know, if you had one on hand.”

“If Antonia was still alive,” Rio said, tipping her bottle to the portrait of the sisters again. “She totally would. She was a freak.” She laughed, as if remembering something Antonia had done, and Agatha shook her head slightly, still unable to wrap her mind around Rio's distinct air of lightness.

“Can I ask you something?” she said finally. 

Rio hummed, lips around the bottle.

“Why do you really do it? Dance at a strip club?” She held up a hand at Rio’s rolled eyes, and added, “I mean it, this time. I’m curious, and don’t say it’s for the money, because I know you better now, and I don’t believe that anymore.”

Rio’s mouth twitched upwards, and she leaned back on her elbows. Her hair brushed the top of the Goonies logo.

“I did start for the money. And there was no room to be serious about it. That was good for me.”

“And now?” Agatha said. The salt and pepper shakers faced each other on the counter, forgotten—Wile E. and Road Runner in their tiny, age-old standoff.

Rio looked down again, her fingers tapping the counter. Agatha watched her shoulder muscles twitch and flex.

“I don’t know; it’s hard to explain,” she said. “There’s… a moment.”

“A moment.”

“Yeah. It’s like…when someone’s looking up at me like I’m not even real.” She shifted her weight. Her fingers toyed with the bottle. “Especially women, because they look at me like they didn’t know they were allowed. Their eyes get kind of big, and their pupils dilate, and it’s like—it feels like for a second, maybe I actually am the fucking devil, or something, because it’s like I’m possessing them. I can feel it, even if I’m just standing there. I know I could destroy them, with just a look, or a touch.”

She paused, the wet label rubbing off in her hands.

“It’s a lot of power,” she said, finally looking at Agatha. Her eyes were dark, her voice low. “Knowing I can show someone the entire universe and take it away, in twenty minutes.”

Agatha swallowed. She felt every word of it pour down her spine, hot and slow. She wet her suddenly dry lips, and Rio’s eyes tracked the movement. 

“What about the men?” Agatha said, her voice feeling strangely steady. She hadn’t meant to make a point, but now she was hurtling towards one. Strange, how that always seemed to happen. 

"What about them?"

“When you destroy the men. They usually take that well?”

“They can’t all be gentlemen,” Rio said, her voice cold. “Nothing I can’t handle.”

Agatha’s gut twisted with a familiar combination of worry and anger.

“Rio,” she said, and she watched her face shutter closed. “You know how that sounds, right? Why are you acting like you can’t see it?”

“I have full control,” Rio said, crossing her arms. “I even so much as look at the door, and security is on me. You should know. That’s better than any regulated factory job."

She gestured vaguely, and Agatha got the distinct impression she'd made this argument before. Agatha caught the mistake that many had made before, but never yet Rio. Going head to head with Agatha required a little more than defaulting to autopilot. She felt it wash over her, the certainty of knowing something. Maybe it was the wannabe-Liv-Benson in her that had never truly died, in search of the injustice and hypocrisy. 

Or maybe the feeling wasn’t anything so righteous or evolved. Because really, she was her mother’s daughter—a wild woman’s young turned wild woman. It wasn’t the methodical picking apart of a cross-examination. It was a flood of dangerous, deathly calm, the sound in her ears narrowing to her own pulse. It was the flash of headlights before the skunk lay motionless on its side, or the slow stalk to the edge of a cliff, and the lighting of a faulty fuse. 

Rio watched her approach, arms still crossed, bottle clutched in her hand. Agatha reached past her, fingers curling around the edge of a cutting board, and then metal.

“That’s not control,” Agatha said, voice low, eyes scanning Rio’s face. Rio watched, unblinking.

“For example,” Agatha said. “This is your house, sort of. You're in charge, here.”

“Is that a question?” Rio licked her lips, smirking.

Agatha pulled her hand back, butter knife clutched in her fingers.

Rio’s eyes followed the steel. Agatha touched the flat tip lightly against Rio’s throat. She felt her swallow under it, and caught the hitch of her breath.

“That doesn’t make you safe, does it?” Agatha said. “What happens when you leave the club, and security is gone? What if someone’s waiting in the parking lot? Or outside your house? Or in your kitchen?”

“Is that someone you?” Rio’s eyes gleamed, shoulders squared. “Because you followed me to all three of those places, in case you forgot. And I can take you.”

Agatha tapped the blunt steel against her throat, twice. 

“I wouldn’t be so sure,” she said, and felt the unwieldy edge in her voice pushing through. “You were a little rough around the edges after seventeen. The first time I got mugged, I was ten, and I didn’t lose a fucking dollar.”

Rio raised an eyebrow at that, her interest sharpening. 

“Agency without knowing the risks,” Agatha said, “without understanding what could go wrong, and when, and how—that’s arrogance.” 

She reached for the beer bottle in Rio’s hand, grabbed the neck of it, and tugged lightly. Rio let go, fingers uncurling, and Agatha set it on the counter behind her.

“You let go because I wanted you to,” Agatha said.

She took Rio’s hand, fingers still cold from the bottle, and placed the knife in her palm. She pressed her fingers shut around it. Agatha breathed in, her nose almost close enough to brush Rio’s temple, the smell of sweat and pine filling her lungs. 

She brushed a wood chip from Rio’s hair, smoothing it behind her ear. Rio shivered lightly under her touch. 

“Real control,” Agatha murmured, “is borne from safety. Not stilettos.” 

She stepped back, and Rio’s fingers twitched around the handle of the butter knife. Her throat moved, her eyes dark. Agatha couldn’t stop watching the looped Goonies script rise and fall with her breath, the cotton clinging to her chest, then lifting, again.

The wave of heat hit her suddenly, Rio’s throat still red where Agatha had tapped the flatware, her lips parted. Agatha felt the pulse of it everywhere, her chest, her face, her thighs, and the suddenly sharp ache between them. She was mesmerized by this version of Rio—silenced, speechless. Affected. 

She should have made it to sixty-six days, Agatha thought, because this was definitely not the point she’d intended to make. Maybe if she'd made it to sixty-six, she’d be calmly laying out the pros and cons of the sex work industry, instead of being thirty seconds away from creative use of a tree stump, all because Rio was sweaty and disheveled and looking at her like that, at her mercy in a stranger’s kitchen. But Agatha had gone and named the problem, and now she had to swallow it in earnest. 

Agatha had always understood the risks. When she was young, she would calculate the price of the furniture in her house, in case her mother never came home, to know what to sell first. Then, she learned the quick assessment of what every student would do in their seat at the turn of a phrase. When she was older, she sat in her doctor’s office next to a scared, quiet Josh, and asked for the exact success rate of a geriatric pregnancy in writing, down to the decimal. 

And now, the risk was higher than any one of those things. Now, it was her sweet, tortured seven-year-olds—the ones in their seats, and the one pressing two thumbs into the buckle in her back seat.

She turned abruptly to look out the window, and took a deep breath, her pulse almost hurting where it hammered in her neck. She stood there in silence for a long minute, and finally, she felt the life of the kitchen start up behind her: the tear of a paper towel, the spray of cleaning solution, the smell of artificial lemon. 

Rio was wiping down the counter, the Looney Tunes shakers no doubt collected and returned to their rightful place, and Agatha didn’t even get to see who won—broken-legged Road Runner, or Wile E. with his faulty stick of dynamite.

 

 

For the second time that day, Agatha was staring down her kid in a living room, although this time the living room wasn’t her own. Nicky was on the sofa, legs and arms crossed.

“Why don’t you want to go home?” she said, aiming for patience, but landing somewhere near desperation. Weren’t seven year-olds supposed to want their beds at night? Hadn’t Nicky ever bothered to read Your Seven Year-Old: Life in a Minor Key? 

“You said we were going to Disneyland,” he said, his tone just shy of the whiny pitch that made Agatha’s blood boil.

“What?” She stared at him. “That’s next month, baby. For your birthday, remember?”

“You said we were going today,” he said, his wail pulling tears into his eyes.

“No, I–” Agatha stopped herself, and sat down next to him. She took a breath, trying valiantly to calm her pulse. Kids were inconvenient, she reminded herself. Kids had tiny brains. Kids were cliché.

“You said we were going on a field trip,” he said.

Agatha closed her eyes, finally understanding. “We came on a little field trip here, to see Ms. V, and now it’s time to go home and go to bed.”

“No,” Nicky shouted, flinging a vintage-looking toy truck to the floor, still looking at her.

Agatha was hit with the sudden, overwhelming urge to cry. And not the kind where a few tears rolled placidly down her face, either. She wanted to wail, like Nicky was allowed to. To scream and thrash and curl into a ball on this beige carpet, and sob until her neck was wet with tears.

She wanted to know if she had been like this at seven. If it was her fault that Nicky switched indiscriminately between angelic and wild child. Her mother used to call her that—wild child—but hers had no trace of affection, laced instead with fury and disgust. The truth was that Agatha could barely remember seven, just flashes here and there: the couch in her apartment, pulling the peeling wallpaper by her bed when strangers were making loud and frightening noises in her living room and she couldn’t fall asleep. 

But even with the bits and pieces she still remembered, she knew her mother had been right. She remembered screaming and scratching—even biting, sometimes. She would say what she meant, and raise hell right back to her mother. Until one day, when she just… didn’t anymore. When she decided at all costs that she wouldn’t become like her, that wild woman. 

Agatha leaned forward and put her head in her hands, trying to force the feeling back down her throat. She rocked a little bit. She needed to get Nicky in the car and take him home, however the fuck she was supposed to do that with an unwilling body at nine o’clock in the pitch dark up a winding incline.

She felt a hand on her shoulder, and looked up. Rio was standing above them.

“Do you like rabbits?” she asked Nicky, and he looked at her curiously. Then, slowly, he nodded.

“Do you want to go look at one? He lives upstairs,” she said, holding out her hand. Nicky nodded again, excitement eclipsing his earlier tantrum, and put his hand in hers.

Agatha didn’t know whether to feel grateful for the save or annoyed that her plan was thrown even farther off course. She watched them walk to the stairs, her elbows still resting on her knees.

Rio glanced back at her. “Give me fifteen,” she muttered.

“You don’t have to—” Agatha started, and Rio just rolled her eyes, disappearing from view with Nicky in tow.



 

After Agatha had collected herself and cleaned up the various toys Nicky had left in disarray, she climbed the stairs to the sound of Rio’s voice. The giggles she'd heard from downstairs had faded, and now it was just a low murmur of sound. She peeked into a room to the left, and pushed the door open a crack.

She was unprepared for the sight that hit her. It felt like when she'd fallen off of the monkey bars as a kid, straight onto her back, the wind knocked out of her for the first time as she lay there and thought she was dying. 

Nicky was on the bed, curled on his side, his hand stroking a fluffy rabbit who seemed perfectly content to sit still. Nicky’s eyes were open, but just barely. And Rio was reading to him. 

It shouldn’t have been so shell-shocking. Agatha had seen her read almost every day, and knew the lilt of her voice, low and musical. She knew the way her thumbs brushed the pages and held her place before she flipped. But this wasn’t the classroom of kids around the blue armchair under the paper tree—this was her son, curled in a bed, next to Rio.

Rio thumbed through a jacket-less book, her voice already lulling Agatha into the calm of the room, and she paused when she heard the door creak. Nicky yawned, and spotted her. 

“Mama, come read with us.” His eyes were unfocused with near-sleep. 

Agatha thought about telling him it was time to go, and then she swallowed her words. He looked so peaceful. She supposed carrying him to the car asleep would be easier than dragging him kicking and screaming. And then she supposed that had been Rio’s plan along. Smartass know-it-all.

Agatha sat down on the other side of Nicky, propping a pillow behind her.

“Who’s your friend?” she murmured, nodding at the bunny.

“I named him Señor Scratchy,” he said. “Ms. V said he likes when people give him new names.”

Agatha looked at Rio, laughing at the obvious lie. Rio hid her grin behind the pages of the book.

“So, I know you guys are just reading,” Agatha said casually, and leaned her head back against the pillow. “But if anyone did happen to fall asleep here, I would be downstairs if you needed anything.”

Nicky looked at her, and then swung his head to Rio, as if she were the one being addressed. She smiled.

“Good to know,” she said, nodding seriously.

“Don’t let me interrupt." Agatha gestured to the book and pulled the blanket over Nicky.

Agatha listened to the sound of Rio's voice coating the surfaces of the room like honey, low and smooth and warm. She watched Rio’s lips form the words. She noticed the way her chest rose and fell, how she crossed one leg over the other. She noticed the edge of a bruise on her forearm, and her mismatched socks, and the gentle turn of the page. Her hard edge was gone, and left in its wake was this. Agatha watched the crease between her eyebrows disappear, and she supposed that even the most feral animals curled up in something soft at dusk or dawn.

Agatha hadn’t been this still in weeks. Her mind had been either occupied unintentionally with the needs of a small human, or intentionally with anything else to distract herself from the Rio of it all. And now, here they all were, half-asleep in a stranger’s bed, Nicky sandwiched between the two of them. He hadn’t been sandwiched at bedtime for a while now, she realized. 

In some ways, he belonged to both of them, at least during the day. And now it was night, and Rio was still here. And Agatha didn’t want to make Nicky leave this bed full of people that loved him.

She sank lower and listened to the sound of the wind against the side of the house. She thought about tomorrow’s planned lesson about storms, and how the eye of a storm was caused by warm air, sinking in the middle. That’s how she felt here, in this house, on this bed. Warm and quiet, the swirling debris somewhere far out of view. 

Rio’s thumb brushed against the page, and she yawned through a word. Her free hand stroked the rabbit’s ear. Agatha suddenly pictured them from above—a teacher, a dancer, a seven year-old, and a rabbit. They made as much sense together as the mismatched furniture in this house. 

Then the wind rattled the window pane, and Agatha remembered reading beyond the second-grade science. She'd learned that the cause of the eye-in-the-storm phenomenon was not fully known. It was a mysterious combination of momentum and force—heat, pressure, and air in the right place at the right time. 

Maybe that’s what all of this was—some unexplainable pressure system, pushing her closer and pulling her apart. She wanted to reach over Nicky and pet that damn bunny, too, and touch the skin on the back of Rio’s hand. To see what she would do—if she would stutter over her words, or look at her, or maybe even do nothing, because it felt so inexplicably normal, the three of them reading on a Sunday night. 

“She was perfect,” Rio read. “I knew this the moment she emerged from my body, white and wet and wailing. Beyond the requisite ten fingers and ten toes, the beating heart, the lungs inhaling and exhaling oxygen, my daughter knew how to scream.”

Agatha knew this book— the Language of Flowers. Josh had gifted it to her one anniversary with a yellow rose, and she had loved the gift until she read the part where a yellow rose signified infidelity. After a shouting match, it became clear that he had picked up the book on his way home because he thought it had nice cover art.

She’d never finished the book, but Rio was reading somewhere near the end of it. And this one didn’t have any cover art, or a cover at all. She thought about yellow roses and Christmas trees, and Rio continued.

“She knew how to make herself heard. She knew how to reach out and latch on. She knew what she needed to do to survive. I didn’t know how it was possible that such perfection could have developed within a body as flawed as my own, but when I looked into her face, I saw that it clearly was.”

Agatha’s hand tangled in Nicky’s hair at Rio’s words, and she felt the tears push past her throat this time, and prick at her eyes. She loved him so fiercely sometimes, it overtook her—the love and the anger and the grief, and then the love again. Especially when they'd spent the day solidly at odds.

She'd gotten pregnant at thirty-five. She'd long thought there was something wrong with her, something irreparable—that she'd spent so long ironing out the creases inside her that it had actually worked. By the time she was in her twenties, she had a house and a husband and job. She was all smooth, clean, quiet lines. She didn't steal books from garage sales anymore, and she worried that her body didn't remember how to grow something wild. 

She had tried, and hoped, and cried. She hated the women on the internet and the in the grocery store and on the sidewalk of her cul-de-sac that said it would happen when she least expected it. That it would bless her like an angel once she'd all but given up. 

Agatha didn’t know how to give up. And so Nicky was born not in a gentle blessing of surprise, but a bitter haze of determination and relief. He was small and screaming and entirely uncontainable.

Now, on the bed, his eyes were closed, his breathing even. His hand lay next to the sleeping rabbit. Agatha felt the air on her face, and it was cool and wet, and she knew there were exactly two tears on her cheek, because she’d counted them spitefully as they fell.

Rio shut the book. She didn’t say anything, but Agatha felt her gaze like high beams, aimed directly at her crumbling resolve. She willed the tears to go backwards, back into her eye sockets, down her throat. Away. 

She didn’t look at Rio. She kissed Nicky on the cheek, wiped her own, and stood. She didn’t stop until she made it all the way down the stairs, and past the beaded curtain that had surely belonged to Lilia. 

 

 

Agatha heard Rio’s slow footsteps behind her, and then felt her phone vibrate in her pocket. Saved, for the third time that day. She held up the phone and jerked her head toward the living room. 

Rio nodded. She was still watching her, concern etched across her face. Agatha realized she’d only ever seen Rio look at the kids that way. Now, that gaze was turned on her, wide-eyed and furrow-browed.

It felt… something. Not bad, exactly. People were concerned about her all the time—she did concerning things. But Rio’s concern was different. Agatha felt her guts rearrange themselves under her inquisitive brown eyes. She ignored the flutter in her stomach, and ducked through the doorway.

 

 

Agatha sat on the gold couch as Josh stalled in her ear. This was definitely not Lilia’s, she thought, fingers trailing over the silky fabric of an elegant pillow as Josh mentioned Nicky’s gymnastics class for the third time.

“Josh,” she interrupted. “You told me this already. If you wanted to talk to him, you should have just called earlier. He’s asleep.”

“No, that’s not why—” he paused. Agatha felt suddenly uneasy, her knees and shoulders locking.

“Has he said anything to you about Disneyland?” he asked.

“Well, yeah, every waking minute of the fucking day.” She rolled her neck, willing it to loosen. “Should I be listening for anything in particular?”

“Yes… No. I just, uh, wanted to give you a heads up,” he said. He cleared his throat, and sniffed.

“Spit it out."

“I started seeing someone.”

Agatha blinked. She didn’t know what she’d expected, but it wasn’t that. 

“Great. Good for you,” she said finally. “No offense, but I don’t really need to know who you’re fucking around with.”

“No—look, it’s serious, Agatha.” He sighed. “She’s meeting Nicky next weekend. And I’m bringing her to Disney.”

Agatha’s fist tightened automatically, and the tassel from the pillow snapped off in her hand.

“You’re… what?”

“It’s been a few months, and she’s really great, and I think it would be a good chance for her to get to know him, you know?”

“You’re not even asking,” she hissed. “You’re giving me a heads up? You weren’t even fucking invited, Josh.”

“You offered.”

“I offered because—” Agatha stopped, her voice dropping to a heated whisper. “You know why I offered. I didn’t offer so that you can have a sunny getaway with your rebound.”

“Okay. Calling was a mistake,” Josh said. “Let’s talk about this in person.”

“Or, how about let’s not,” Agatha said, and jabbed the red button.

She slammed the phone face down on the couch beside her, and dropped her head into her hands. He already had another something serious. She was meeting Nicky. Nicky would have something whole, and two people in his bed at night.

She didn’t want to be stranded in Disneyland, watching her family slip away, unable to thrash and scream, like a mountain lion on a chain leash. And that was it, really. She wanted back what she'd lost: the biting and the scratching and the altogether wildness of seven.

Rio leaned in the doorway, taking her in. Her arms were crossed, the crease back between her eyebrows.

“I don’t want to talk about it,” Agatha said preemptively, and she felt the threadbare quality to her own voice. 

“Okay,” Rio said. The single word was low and smooth, and it made Agatha’s shoulders drop an inch. 

“What do you want?” she asked simply.

Agatha looked up at her messy hair and Goonies shirt and dark eyelashes. She wanted to stay here, in this dreamy, ambiguous place that wasn’t home school. She wanted to touch Rio’s face again, to know what it felt like under her fingertips when she yawned—if her jaw clicked, if it vibrated with noise. She wanted to remember what it was like before she’d learned to keep her hands to herself and her flailing limbs a secret. 

“I want,” Agatha started, her voice hoarse, and it felt like a sound escaping her before she stopped. Maybe that was the end of the sentence. She wanted.

Rio stepped forward, until she was standing in front of Agatha, and Agatha tilted her head up to look at her. 

She swallowed, and cleared her throat. 

“I want the good weed.”

Rio didn’t respond—just raised her eyebrows, and then she was gone. It was so fast, Agatha wondered if she'd hallucinated the last minute altogether, but then she was back, a decapitated plastic squirrel in her hand. A battered-looking dime bag dangled from her index and middle finger.

“Fucking knew it,” Agatha muttered, and followed Rio out the back door.

 

 

They were shoulder to shoulder on the cold dirt ground, backs against the side of the house, laughing silently. The door was cracked open in case Nicky woke up, but Agatha knew he wouldn’t. He slept the deepest on the days he fought the hardest. 

They sat draped under their big jackets, the air just above freezing but enough to sting, and Agatha felt her nerve endings calm. Shadows of white pine towered above her, needles blue with frost, and she could see stars peeking through the branches. It felt like high school, slumped in a park next to a nervous, skinny boy whose hands shook when he rolled a joint. Except that Rio was anything but nervous, holding it between her index and middle like it was second nature.

Agatha watched Rio inhale, steady and slow. The orange glowed in time with her breath, her eyes fluttered shut, and the smoke spilled from her lips. 

She hummed low, and Agatha felt the sound in her every bone. She’d heard that sound before, she realized, with Rio’s tongue vibrating against the inside of her mouth. 

Agatha shut her eyes. “This isn’t nineties dirt pot, is it.”

“No,” Rio laughed. “No, these ladies keep current.” She looked at Agatha. “Wait, don’t tell me the last time you smoked weed was in the nineties.”

Agatha’s laughter spilled out of her, and she opened her eyes, rolling her head to look at Rio. 

“Of course not." She took the joint from Rio’s hands. Their fingers brushed over the paper, and Agatha tried to focus on the simple act of pinching her fingers together. 

She held back a cough. “2006.”

“Shit. What was the occasion?”

Agatha snorted. “E. Coli outbreak. Remember that? Or, fuck, I don’t know, you probably don’t? I was, like, terrified that I was going to die of e. Coli because I had just eaten a salad. And then I turned on the news and it was this huge thing. So Josh and I decided to get high and watch a movie to take my mind off of things. But the guy at Blockbuster was depressed because they'd just gotten, like, fifty copies of Cars, and no one was renting them because no one fucking went to Blockbuster anymore. So we felt bad and rented Cars and got shit-faced. Pro tip: do not watch a movie about talking Cars when you’re high and terrified of dying.”

Rio was already laughing, and Agatha joined her, shoulders shaking. The kind of laughter that was full and meaningless and all-consuming.

“You know the first movie I watched while I was high?” Rio said, still laughing. “Girl, Interrupted.”

“Jesus. Why?” 

“My parents said it was satanic, but I’m pretty sure they just wanted to keep me away from Winona. So I stole the DVD from the library, and faked being sick to get out of church, so I could get high out of my attic window and just fucking look at Winona Ryder.”

Agatha hummed, feeling oddly endeared by the image of a young Rio hiding a DVD in her jacket, the way Agatha had stolen the book from the garage sale. She understood, though—Winona was hot.

Rio looked at her with a grin, raised eyebrows, and Agatha realized she had said that last part out loud. She considered that it was possible she'd gotten too high. She started laughing again, and Rio did, too.

“Shhh,” Agatha gasped, putting her hand over Rio’s mouth, and Rio laughed even harder. Their breath made clouds in the January air, and Agatha watched them, suddenly hypnotized by the contrast, white wisps against the dark sky.

Her hand fell to her side, back under the warmth of the jackets, and her fingers brushed against Rio’s thigh in the dirt. Cold against warm, mud against fleece.

“Ri-o,” she said, drawing out the word, as if she was testing the syllables for the first time.

Rio widened her eyes eerily, and grinned at her. She did look a little devilish sometimes, Agatha had to admit. 

“A-ga-tha,” Rio replied.

Agatha watched her, her long fingers around the joint, lips parted, smoke filtering in, then out, above their heads. The flick of her thumb against the joint. The bow of her upper lip. 

Agatha blinked slow, and her smile faded with the smoke, and her hand climbed all the way up from the dirt until it was resting on Rio’s thigh, just above her knee—the knee that had just decided one day it’d had enough. 

She thought about the picture on the fridge, the one Rio had taken down, crutches and bandages in the sunset. She wanted to see the picture again—to know it. To know what Rio was thinking when it was taken, and why she still couldn’t look at it. She wanted to know how often she came here, if it was every Sunday or just the ones after Christmas. 

When she was done at the club, women staring up at her like deer in the headlights in the middle of a dark highway—when she was done showing them the universe and taking it away, like she’d done to Agatha without a second thought—did she come here, where someone had her picture on the fridge, to lay down and pet a soft rabbit?

Agatha wanted to know. She wanted. 

Her fingers tightened over Rio’s leg. 

“The bunny,” she said, her voice feeling slow and sticky to her own ears. “What’s his real name?”

"Robert Redford.” 

Rio seemed unfazed that Agatha’s hand was gripping her thigh, and through her weed-addled mind, Agatha felt annoyed. She wanted her to be a little fazed. 

“People don’t know how much old lesbians love the Sundance Kid,” Rio said, and then she was laughing again, head tipping upwards.

Agatha laughed, too, because that was funny, and because her mother had liked Butch Cassidy and hated Sundance, so that apparently meant she wasn’t a lesbian, and if she’d had a rabbit, maybe it would have been named Paul Newman instead. It made perfect sense. She wished things made sense like this more often.

Agatha watched Rio laugh, her throat exposed, and Agatha opened her mouth to tell her all of it—her mother, Butch Cassidy, the rabbit she’d never had. 

And instead, still half smiling, she heard herself say: “Why did you kiss me?”

Rio’s laugh faded in slow motion. She looked at Agatha, eyes dark and wide. Agatha’s gaze flitted between Rio’s eyes and her throat. Wanda once told her she looked like she was scanning people’s identity when she did that, but she couldn’t help it—there was too much to see at once. She watched Rio swallow, and then turn away again, setting her lips to the joint. 

Agatha felt something out of reach, something akin to disappointment, having won the game of chicken she started in the first place. She moved her hand higher up Rio’s thigh, not really on purpose, but definitely not by accident, either. She felt the mountain lion strain against the chain leash, the links loosening.

Her fingernails caught on the fleece, and she scratched slowly, back and forth, fixated on the feel of the soft fabric and bits of leaf and grainy dirt under her fingertips. Maybe she’d imagined the words. Why did you kiss me? Maybe she hadn’t even asked it out loud.

She heard a soft thump and looked up. Rio's head was leaned back against the wall, and she was looking up at the trees above them in the dark night. She looked like Rio—sharp jawline, expressive nostrils, chaos brewing behind the clear brown of her irises and clean angles of her cheekbones. She looked like a movie poster. Agatha wondered if she looked like that too, if they looked like that together. Like Thelma and Louise, side by side in their car in the desert. Or Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid, mid-run, four guns drawn between them. 

That was too many guns for two people, she thought.  

She scratched her fingernails against Rio’s thigh, harder this time, and Rio’s eyes fluttered shut. The small, persistent clouds in the air came quicker, and Agatha thought: it’s cold and your breath is giving you away. 

Agatha felt that she could watch the rise and fall of the draped jacket for another few hours, tracing slow patterns on top of black fleece, and then underneath it, watching the patterns escape in white, through Rio's lips and into the air.

“Agatha,” Rio said, and she said it half like a warning, half like a plea. Her lips were dry, and her voice was hoarse from the smoke, or maybe from all of it. And Agatha had never heard a better sound in her life than the way the syllables of Agatha rolled off of Rio’s tongue, like her name was water in the desert of the wild, wild west. 

Butch Cassidy and Sundance died at the end, Agatha remembered suddenly. In a hail of bullets. Just like Thelma and Louise in their convertible, driving right off the edge of the cliff. Maybe it wasn’t enough, after all—four guns and a Ford Thunderbird.

“You kissed me,” Agatha said. This time she knew she didn’t imagine it, and this time it wasn’t a question. Rio looked at her, finally. 

“I kissed you,” she said. 

And then she looked at Agatha’s mouth, and said: “I’m sorry.” 

Agatha’s hand stilled, and Rio’s thigh tensed, just briefly. It was like her body was primed for rhythm, for movement, and now it missed the repetition—the slow, lazy pressure, back and forth.

Agatha didn’t want to ask again. She didn’t want to hear why. She didn’t want Rio to say sorry to her mouth like she regretted what she’d done to it. Agatha just wanted to stay here, in the mind-numbing cold, with the mind-numbing weed, and her hand on Rio’s thigh, with no one feeling sorry at all. 

An owl hooted somewhere over their heads. A pine needle landed on Agatha’s ankle.

“You bought new mugs,” she murmured, the gears in her mind drifting and slotting together in that satisfying way she'd forgotten in the years since 2006. “Uranium-free.”

Rio shook her head, and let out a half-laugh. “You’re so fucking high.”

Agatha looked at the small parts of her she'd come to know. The soft, dark hair at her temples, just in front of her ears. The tiny dimples that formed above the corners of her lips when she smiled, instead of in her cheeks. The mismatched socks on her feet. 

“The devil wouldn’t do that,” Agatha said. “Replace the Fiestaware.”

Rio didn’t say anything. She watched the smoke curl into the sky, and Agatha watched her watch the smoke. Rio’s leg was still warm beneath her palm, and Agatha kept it there. She reached over Rio’s lap with her other hand, and pinched the joint from her fingers. 

“God,” she said. “I’m so fucking high.” 

She set her lips to the end.



 

She didn’t remember when exactly it was that they moved inside, except that her fingers had gone numb. It couldn’t have been that late, but every time she tried to look at the chicken-shaped clock, its beak started opening and closing and she had to look away for fear that it was going to jump off the wall and come for her eyes.

She'd thrown her own plan out the door with the ashes on the patio. Nicky was asleep upstairs and she wouldn’t be able to drive until morning. Monday morning, as in, school.

She grabbed Rio’s arm in the living room, the warmth spreading back into her fingertips, and laughed uncontrollably until she managed to breathe enough to gasp: “We have school tomorrow.”

And then Rio covered her face with her hands, shoulders shaking, and whined, “Whyyyy would you say that right now?”

At some point, either five or fifty minutes later, Agatha realized Goonies was playing on the TV. She was sitting on the carpet with a stuffed animal in her hand. She looked into the pig’s face, its cartoonish eyes and snout, and wondered where it had come from. She smushed her face into the side of it, because it looked soft and pillowy, and felt a tug at her hair.

“Ow.” 

“Stop moving,” Rio said, and then Agatha remembered that Rio was sitting on the couch behind her, braiding her hair. 

She tried not to close her eyes, but the feeling of Rio’s fingers against her scalp was too therapeutic to resist. She felt her neck muscles loosening, until finally, she tilted her head all the way back, resting it against the couch between Rio’s knees, trapping her hands underneath her tangled hair. Rio stared at her, upside down, grinning slow. 

“I only did one side,” she said, and Agatha focused on the shape of her upside down mouth when it moved.

“Do you like Disney?” Agatha said suddenly, her head feeling heavy.

“Like, the movies?”

“No,” Agatha murmured, still looking up at her. “The land.”

“Never been.” Rio dragged her hands through Agatha’s hair, freeing them, and Agatha felt a quiet moan escape her own lips at the feel of it. Her eyes fluttered shut.

“Come with us,” Agatha mumbled. “Next month.”

“I’m never letting you smoke weed again.”

Agatha laughed, eyes still closed, and the train of thought left her as Rio moved to the floor  and settled herself between Agatha’s outstretched legs. She shook her hair back.

“Do mine,” she said.

Agatha smiled, pulling her fingers through the dark hair. “I don’t want to brag,” she said, parting it gently. “But I’m really good at this.” 

Rio slapped her thigh, her gaze focused on the TV. “Shut up. They’re about to go into the Inferno.”

Agatha smiled again, her hands twisting and pulling, and her focus narrowed, and then slipped.

 

 

She didn’t remember finishing the braids, or the kids going into the Inferno, or falling asleep in the living room. 

When she woke, it was still dark, and her mouth felt like it was stuffed with cotton. Her first thought was Nicky, before remembering where they were. Her second thought was that her back hadn’t hurt this badly since Nicky was a baby and his doctor said she was “carrying him too much.” As if she was supposed to believe that was a real problem, even though she could barely stand up straight enough in the doctor’s office to properly tell him off. 

And the third thing she thought was that her arm was asleep, because Rio was laying on top of it. Agatha’s back pressed along the bottom of the couch, and Rio lay curved into her. Her hair was in two perfect braids, and Agatha suddenly remembered the day before in soft flashes of color and sound. The trees on the chopping block, the hot chocolate and beer, the bedtime story. The patio and the white pine, and the Goonies and the hair, and the weed. God, that new-age weed. 

The guilt hit her hard, the thought of Nicky upstairs in a strangers bed while she fucked around and got high on a school night at the inexcusable age of forty-two.

She watched Rio’s side rise and fall, her free arm wedged between them on the carpet. Neither of them had moved onto the couch. 

Her nose was almost touching the back of Rio’s neck, and she inhaled, softly. Her hand twitched. She wanted it to be last night again. If this were last night, maybe she would have reached out, and traced a slow line from Rio’s shoulder to her waist, and then lower, fingers wrapping tight around her hips and pulling them flush against her own. But she was sober, and her neck ached, and it was Monday morning.

She touched Rio’s hair instead, tracing one of the braids. The knots were even and tight, and Agatha may not have remembered doing them, but she recognized her own work. It filled her with a strange sense of sharp satisfaction, seeing the evidence left behind of her hands.

She thought of what Rio had asked her, if she remembered what it felt like when everything was sparky and desperate. But that was about Rio getting fingered by a cheerleader over a math textbook, not lying on the carpet next to someone who was far too old to be sleeping on the floor.

Still, she thought, her fingers lingering over the shell of Rio’s ear. She was starting to get the idea.

Her eyes drifted past Rio, past the doorway, to the kitchen window. The dark was turning slowly purple, and it was a Monday.

She needed to drive Nicky home, and get them both in a shower that would be mostly her blessed four-in-one (“Shampoo and conditioner for both kids and adults!” ) and some quick, unpleasant scrubbing. And maybe she’d have time to throw out the marinara-soaked paper towels she knew were still on the floor.

This time, when she looked at the clock, her hand resting on the ridges of Rio’s left braid, the chicken stayed mercifully still. 

 

 

She carried Nicky down the stairs and took one last look at Rio. Agatha had wanted to put a blanket over her, but there were none in sight. So she left her lying there, curled up on the carpet, one arm under her head and mouth slightly open. 

Nicky stirred in Agatha’s arms, and then clung to her tighter, burrowing his head into her neck. Agatha squeezed him back a little. She’d never say it out loud, but sometimes, it was so much easier to love him like this, heavy and dreaming.

She turned to the door, and caught a glimpse of the salt and pepper shakers in their place by the coffee maker. Road Runner was leaned up against Wile E., so the pepper wouldn’t spill out of his head when he inevitably toppled over. Wile E. stood solidly with his useless stick of dynamite, his dinner grown tired of the chase, and nestled tight against him.

 

 

Agatha walked up the winding path in the near-dark, stars still twinkling in the pre-dawn. Nicky was dead weight. Her thighs burned and her back still ached, seven years later. She said silent goodbyes to the dead trees in piles on her right, and felt the rustle of condolences from the live ones on her left.

Nicky’s thumb found his mouth as she put him in his booster, his eyes still shut. He was too old for that, but when he was asleep, he forgot everything that seven was supposed to be. And anyway, it wasn’t his job to know what he was supposed to be. Maybe it wasn’t really anyone’s job.

She drove out of Salem, the sky growing lighter over the horizon as trees thinned and the shapes of homes started to repeat themselves. Something bumped under her tires, and caught her eye in the rearview, in the middle of the otherwise empty road. She didn’t slow down, but she craned her neck to the mirror to see. It was brown and red and wet with recent death, small and unmoving. 

With a jolt, she realized she hadn’t seen the damn bunny when she scooped Nicky from the bed. She hadn’t even remembered him at all, until this unidentified carcass on the pavement. She knew it probably wasn’t Robert Redford. And then, the further she drove, the knowing turned uncertain, until it evaporated to desperate hope. She said a little prayer just in case, that she hadn’t killed the soft thing. Not really to God, but to whoever looked out for the sister witches on a seventies farm. Maybe their god was more of a Joni Mitchell type. 

And if I did kill him, Joni, please let a seagull find him first, instead of anyone who’d recognize him. 

She signed her chest with a cross because she didn’t know what this god accepted in prayer. She hoped it worked for her, too—the god of good weed and french braids and The Goonies, who tended the life cycle of the Christmas trees, and rabbits, and skunks.

 

 

It was seven forty-six a.m. before Agatha started to worry Rio hadn’t yet woken up. The teachers trickled out of the lounge, grumbling and slow to start after the beginning of the semester staff meeting. Agatha shuffled her papers, shaking her still-wet hair off of her shoulders. 

Alice handed Agatha a steaming mug of coffee, and Agatha took it, surprised.

“You look like shit,” Alice shrugged.

Agatha scoffed, but it was half-hearted—she believed it. She glanced down at the dark liquid, and grimaced, holding it back out. 

“This was a generous gesture,” she said, “but I don’t take my coffee in acid form.”

Alice eyed her. She didn’t touch the mug. “Cream and sugar make the hangover worse.”

“I’m not—”

“Heads up,” Alice mumbled. 

She walked swiftly away, and when Agatha turned, Jen was standing in front of her with her arms crossed. 

“Where is she?” 

“Where’s who?”

“Agatha, stop it.”

Agatha raised her eyebrows. “Ooh, my full first name? Relax. It's the first day back. I’m sure she’ll be here any minute.”  

Jen looked at Agatha’s hair draped over her wet collar. “That’s not what I asked.”

Agatha stilled, meeting her eye. "Then why don't you try being a little more specific, Jennifer?"

As soon as she’d said the words, Rio appeared through the door in her winter jacket, cheeks flushed, breathless as if she had run from the parking lot. She had two to-go cups in her hands, faint splatters dotting lids and her sleeves.

Agatha almost dropped her mug of acid-coffee on the floor. Her stomach did a strange, swooping thing, and she felt a sudden, obscene urge to smile. Rio was still wearing the braids.

“I’m so sorry I’m late, Jen; I–” Rio slowed, taking in the near-empty room. She caught her breath, scanning Wanda, Jen, and Alice. Then, her eyes landed on Agatha. 

“Hey,” she said, her shift in focus abrupt and undisguised. 

Her voice was still rough with morning disuse, or maybe with the weed. Or, fuck, Agatha realized with a stab of guilt—maybe it was her asthma. She felt the full force of her own pure idiocy, showing up unannounced on Sunday night with Rio’s inhaler, and begging her to smoke. Agatha looked her over, scanning for damage she might’ve caused.

Her eyes fell to the two coffee cups again. She recognized the logo of a dog from the shop she had passed on her way home this morning. Her brain felt disjointed as she tried to put those simple facts together, and the realization crept in, embarrassingly slow: the second cup was for her.

Maybe Agatha had made a mistake, blindly assuming it went without saying that neither of them would mention last night. Not the impromptu trip, not the weed or half-confessions, not the sleeping together. Or rather—the not sleeping together, which felt even more incriminating. Because now here was Rio, bringing her coffee, late to the worst possible room at the worst possible time.

Agatha buried her face in the papers in her hand, trying to make herself as small as possible despite the fact that there were only four other people in the room. She glanced up once in polite greeting, like she didn’t want to interrupt Rio’s apology, which was absurd because Rio had already interrupted herself in the most conspicuous, ridiculous, insane way possible.

Rio blinked, her eyes rising above the obvious fog for a second, and looked around again. “Uh, everyone. Hey—everyone.” She waved once in awkward, broad greeting. 

Agatha suppressed the urge to climb under the desk.

“I had a pet situation,” Rio said, keeping her eyes firmly on Jen and Wanda this time. “My rabbit got out, but I found him. Little bastard.”

“Thank fucking god,” Agatha muttered, forgetting herself for a moment.

“Nice braids,” Jen said. “You could give Agatha a run for her money.”

Rio’s cheeks turned a distinct shade of pink, and Jen eyed the coffee in Rio’s hand.

“Is that a peace offering?”

To her credit, Agatha noted, Rio only hesitated for a second. 

“It won’t happen again,” she said, handing over the cup. “I swear.”

“Don’t sweat it. Everyone gets one." Jen took a sip of the coffee and blinked, her eyes widening. "Hell no, take this back. I think I just got diabetes."

Rio took the coffee and ducked her head, making her way to Agatha. She set both cups wordlessly on the desk.

“I see you kept the hair,” Agatha said under her breath, handing her the stack of papers. 

Rio grabbed it, and narrowed her eyes. “I see you didn’t.” 

“You only did one side.”

“You didn’t let me finish.” Rio said, indignant, and Agatha bit back her smile again. Indignant Rio was hard not to smile at.

“Well you–” 

Agatha caught a glimpse of Jen out of the corner of her eye, watching them. The sudden buoyancy in her chest sank to her stomach. 

“You have toothpaste on your face,” she muttered, and left the coffee on the desk. 

 

 

On rainy days, the cafeteria felt too full. Teachers flanked the sides of the room, pretending not to notice most of the things they did. And Agatha leaned heavily against a cement pillar, the ache in her back and poor night's sleep compounded by the chaos. Her hair caught on a plastic poster taped to the pillar, and she untangled it, glancing at the rules written in comic sans. God bless Sharon.

  1. Walk, don’t run.
  2. Clean up after yourself.
  3. Say your “pleases” and “Thank you’s.”

Since her second day of school at twenty-one, student-teaching with Vinegar and the picture of the dead skunk, she'd always thought teaching itself fairly simple. Carved within in structure and routine, she’d felt free to create her own rules, though she’d never named them in comic sans, or laminated them against a wall.

  1. Tell kids the truth; they can handle it.
  2. To make them laugh, just start laughing. 
  3. Sitting next to a kid in their wild and moody darkness is much more effective than turning on a light.
  4. Tell girls to stop saying sorry so much.
  5. Sometimes, say you’re sorry.

The Rules had served her for twenty years with no major hiccups. But motherhood was a different story altogether, and personhood, even worse. If she had to pick one problem, it would be that the authors of Your Seven Year-Old: Life in a Minor Key stopped writing books after sixteen.

She was still herself at sixteen, unapologetic and deliciously inappropriate. But seventeen was when her life changed, when she watched Good Will Hunting in Brandon’s basement and stole the book from the garage sale. Seventeen, too, was when Rio fucked a cheerleader, and got kicked out of her house, and fell into a family of ex-dancers, farmers, and soothsayers. Seventeen was when Things started to Happen, to everyone, apparently. And yet, there was no book for seventeen.

There was no book for forty-two, either. She looked at Rio, three tables over, opening a carton of milk for a first-grader. She handed it to him, and another one asked her to cut his hot dog into pieces. She obliged him, too. 

Agatha remembered last night suddenly, Rio snuggled next to Nicky and Robert Redford the rabbit, her voice soft and fingers stroking fur. Soft edges, willing and easy. She’d let Agatha take the beer bottle from her hand, and replace it with a knife, no questions asked. No caution. All give. 

Rio stood there now, in the crowded lunchroom, again with knife in hand—plastic, this time. She made it halfway through the hot dog, and her eye caught Agatha’s. Agatha wasn’t sure what her own face was doing, but she figured it was doing something, because Rio’s expression turned from amusement to intensity, the knife frozen in her hand.

Of course, Agatha had no rules for this—for the way Rio looked at her, with a knife in her hand. The way her feet rubbed together automatically when she read aloud in bed. The way she curled up fetal in her sleep, against Agatha’s chest, like a burrowed animal on the living room floor. 

Before yesterday, or maybe before today, right now, she would have argued that Rio was right—that one night in June, Rio had destroyed her with just a look. But now, it was more than half a year later, and she was locking eyes with Rio across a lunchroom.

 

She'd known, when she was twenty-one, about the life cycles of rodents, scavengers, and common reptiles. On the day she drew the roadkill, she knew that there were two reasons Lucille’s pet gecko had stopped eating the frozen, dead crickets she was feeding him. 

The first was that geckos stopped eating when they shed their skin. This was a temporary discomfort, and if this was the cause, he would be just fine.

The second, more unfortunate option, was that the hands of evolution hadn’t meant for him to live in a glass tank with a lid and a heat lamp. He didn’t recognize the frozen things as prey at all.
