He'd be looking for something warm and alive, and only remember something he'd never really known—the instinctual urge to scramble and run, to chase the signs of life across the desert rocks, under the wide open sky.

And now, Agatha knew about the life cycle of a Christmas tree, too. She knew that on the other side of a tree trunk, split by an axe, lay the soft mulch that made way for saplings and blooming azaleas. 

Now they were back at school, where Agatha had lived since she was twenty-one, and where all of a sudden, her beloved routine felt like four walls with a lid. And looking at Rio across the heads of her seven year-olds, she understood that Rio hadn’t destroyed her at all. She'd broken Agatha open completely, cracked the hard ground of her and molded the clay underneath—heat, pressure, and air in a mystifying combination.

Rio was still looking at her over the tables, braids dangling too close to the open milk carton, and the kid was starting to get upset that she hadn’t finished cutting up the hot dog. Agatha swallowed, and once more felt it in her stomach—the twisting and twirling and light-spotting that meant that this was something important. 

 

 

When Sunday night turned to Monday morning and the forest thinned to fences, the rabbit, Robert Redford, returned to his cage. He knew he should be grateful, not lying on the side of the road. But every night from now on, as his beloveds placed him gently into the hutch, he'd rustle back and forth: two feet left, two feet right.

He'd think about escaping up the winding path, the one that he had seen from the window, and he'd know that he could choose one of only two. One: escape, and risk his guts splayed open on the side of the road. Or two: resign to be restless for the rest of his life, after knowing the softness of a new name, and a bedtime story, and the slow, gentle stroke of the devil’s loving hand.

Happiest Fucking Place on Earth

Chapter Summary

PLEASE READ: this chapter centers on the themes of age, bodies, doctors, and the medical/biological lens of the female reproductive system, including the menstrual cycle and bleeding. If all that’s not your cup of tea, please feel free to abstain! I also feel compelled to mention that folks who have a uterus (across all genders) differ in their relationships to their bodies, and this chapter explores a representation of ONE such relationship. Yours, if different, is just as valid. 🖤

- your local enby mother

“Just because you are soft doesn't mean you are not a force. Honey and wildfire are both the colour gold.”

― Victoria Erickson

“I’m not big on substitutes.” She eyed the woman’s immaculate ponytail. “I would have just rescheduled with Dr. Foster.”

The typing grew louder. Agatha’s shoulders tightened with each keystroke.

She nodded at the stethoscope draped around Pepper’s shoulders. “Why do you need that?” she muttered. “To check if my vagina’s still alive?” 

Pepper was still smiling, still typing, and still unruffled. Agatha assumed she’d dealt with her share of snarky assholes. Agatha tried to remind herself this was a perfectly normal woman who was just doing her job.

By the time she was thirty-three, she’d seen five different fertility specialists. All of them had x-rayed and imaged and probed, and then sat her down to tell her it wasn’t her fault. It had to do with the coincidental shape of something inside her—a tube or cavity she refused to acknowledge—that made it sound like it was very much her fucking fault.

The last doctor, a balding man with stale coffee-breath, had leaned in after Agatha’s fifth desperate question, and said definitively: “At your age and with your history, it is likely impossible that you will ever naturally get pregnant.”

Three for three, she’d thrown up in the wastebasket by the door. By then, she’d stopped bothering to feel bad about it.

“No,” Agatha almost laughed. Not even by her own miracle hand.

“It’s perfectly okay if you have—”

Pepper’s voice was softer now. “Is that something you’d like to discuss further?”

“No,” Agatha repeated.

“Okay.” Pepper shifted seamlessly. “Have you noticed any other symptoms? Sometimes there are more clues to what might be going on.”

“No symptoms." Agatha crossed her arms.

“Cute.”

Agatha balked. “I teach second grade. So, yes. Constantly.”

“It’s been an eventful year.”

“But it could be?” Agatha said again, thumbing the smooth paper in thought. “Hormones.”

“It could be.” Pepper’s voice was gentle. Her hand loosened on the door. “Do you want me to walk you through the information?”

“No,” she said quickly. “I’m familiar with homework.”

Pepper smiled. “Of course. Call if you need anything, or have any questions at all. You’re in excellent health, okay, Agatha?”

Changes in libido, check.

Irritability, check.

“I have a question for you,” she said, turning back to Nicky. She glanced at him, and then back to the plate. The syrup pooled on the side. She moved it around uselessly with her fork.

“You like Ms. V, right?”

“Uh-huh,” Nicky nodded, distracted by a bright orange car out the window.

“Why? What do you like about her?”

Nicky looked at her and shrugged. “She’s nice. She taught me and Luca how to do a dance. And we talk about Mario Kart sometimes. She’s, like, really good—she knows all the shortcuts.”

“What?” Agatha said, baffled. “When did you play Mario Kart with her?”

“On rainy day lunch in the library,” he said. “Luca had his switch and we took turns, and Ms. V showed us this crazy path with the dinosaurs that we never even saw before.”

Agatha smiled, the image of Rio with her head bent over a video game between two little boys. She knew Rio’s brow would’ve been furrowed in concentration, tongue between her teeth. 

“So you like her because she plays with you?”

“Yeah,” he said, like it was obvious. And really, she didn’t know what she’d been expecting. Eight year-olds weren’t exactly known for their sentimentality. Kids never wielded any reasons why they liked adults, they just did or didn’t. 

“Do you like Ms. V?” Nicky asked her back, and she raised her eyebrows. 

“Yeah,” she said after a beat. “I really do.”

He considered, and tilted his head.

“Did you guys ever play Mario Kart?” he asked. 

Agatha laughed, her chest bubbling with sudden warmth. 

“No,” she said finally. “I don’t think I’m there yet. But if we do, I’m going to need you to drive for me—you know how I am with those games.”

“You’re so bad at it,” Nicky giggled, and she dropped her mouth open. 

“Hey!”

“You always go backwards.”

His sides shook with laughter. She laughed with him, and for a second, she forgot. She forgot about all of it, except her best little friend, and mouse waffles, and big-kid sounds of happiness.

 



Wanda was waiting for them in the parking lot of the diner. She leaned against the rental van, arms crossed, and Agatha froze as they stepped onto the pavement.

“Aunt Wanda!” Nicky called, and ran to her. "We had Micky Waffles!"

Agatha watched them hug, Wanda’s hair backlit a dazzling red. Agatha had called her Little Red, that first year, when she’d still thought she could make Wanda an enemy if she just tried hard enough.

Agatha walked slowly toward her, shivering. Her thin pajama pants did nothing to block the morning chill.

“Mama, I have to go to the bathroom.”

“Okay,” she said, relieved. “Let’s go.”

“Can I go by myself?”

Agatha blinked. “Oh. Yeah. You can.” She tried to sound normal about the fact that this was the first time he’d ever asked that in a public place. “If you’re not out in five minutes, I’m coming in swinging.”

Nicky grinned and ran back inside. She watched the diner door jangle shut behind him. Maybe eight felt different than seven, the same way forty-three felt different than forty-two.

The tears came out of nowhere. She pulled the neckline of her t-shirt over her face and let a few tears fall, her throat tight with the effort of staying silent.

“Agatha.” Wanda’s soft voice cut through the quiet. Agatha dropped her t-shirt, the salt and air stinging her cheeks.

“You can’t fire her.” The words tumbled out, rough and too loud. It was still early enough that she could see her breath escape with them.

“Agatha—”

“She’s a good teacher. You know she is. Or you wouldn’t have gotten her that interview. She’s already lost a career once, and no one should have to do that twice.”

“That’s not fair.” Wanda's voice wavered, and Agatha’s chest clenched at the sound of it. “They want to know she’s someone I stand behind. I have to sign off on it! She lied to me. You lied to me.”

Agatha flinched. She watched the traffic as Wanda continued.

“And what about you? Your classroom? How am I supposed to trust that you know how to separate…” She stopped herself. “Whatever this is.”

“Wanda, it’s—”

“Agatha, if you say ‘complicated’ one more fucking time—”

Agatha fell silent. She looked at Wanda, her fire-lit flyaways and the crease between her eyebrows. She noticed the faint lines on her face that had appeared sometime in the last six years. No doubt from the last eight months, cleaning up Agatha’s trail of debris. 

Agatha felt the guilt squeeze her. She could say it—I fucked Rio. But somehow, thinking it so plainly felt wrong and incomplete. How could she explain the feeling of Rio’s hands on her in the dark, her hooded eyes fluttering shut? She didn’t have the words. She wasn’t sure the words existed.

“Okay,” she said instead, and felt something harden in her chest. “Let’s make a deal, then.” 

The crease between Wanda’s eyebrows grew deeper. Agatha took a deep, steadying breath, and felt the air still. She was too many miles past the first sign of crisis.

“She’ll quit the club,” Agatha said. “Give me one week. And you sign off, or call them, or do wherever it is you need to do to make sure the offer sticks. Before you leave for the airport.”

Wanda let out a defeated sound. “You’re in no position to bargain. You should’ve stopped this the second you knew. You should have told me the second—”

“I know.”

They were silent then. Agatha watched the cars rolling by, people on their way to work on a Monday morning while she was here, in the parking lot of a diner, in shambles. It felt odd, that the world would be spinning, business as usual. 

“She flew home early, you know,” Wanda said finally, glancing at her. “Last night.” 

Agatha’s stomach dropped, and she tried to keep her face even. Wanda reached out and touched her arm. Agatha wanted with her whole body to melt into it. She stood with her back straight instead, and kept the warmth from Wanda’s hand confined to the cold air of the parking lot.

“There’s a question I’m not asking,” Wanda said quietly. “And it’s killing me not to ask.”

“Don’t.” Agatha shook her head. She pulled away, and jostled her legs to keep warm. She hugged her arms to her chest so that Wanda wouldn’t try to touch her again. To try and soften her.

“Please, don’t. I can’t—not right now.”

She heard the jangle of the diner door, and Nicky was pushing the door open with great effort. Agatha felt her eyes drop in an automatic check for his wet hands.

“There are things more important than my job,” Wanda said. “You’re my best friend. And I’m just…” She swallowed, and shook her head again. “I’m just trying to be your best friend.”

Agatha forced the words to roll off of her, like water on wax. She looked at Wanda, staring back at her—a mirror image, with arms and legs and eye sockets, floating ribs and all. She wondered what pieces of Wanda’s life were notched into her spine. If Agatha’s initials were carved there, the way best friends were supposed to be, or if it was just a mess of scratch marks and tallies, marking the days until she was freed from all of this. From Agatha.

“Do we have a deal?” Agatha asked, eyes meeting Wanda’s.

Wanda’s eyebrows twitched, face settling into clear disappointment. She stepped back. Something soft and whole pulled back with her. Agatha felt cold again.

“Yeah, okay,” Wanda said. “Fine. One week.”

“One week,” Agatha repeated. 

She turned towards her car, and ignored the twinge of soreness in her back and legs. She pushed away the memory of Rio’s hands on her stomach, and cradling her face, and coated in her own blood that had surely, willingly exposed the truth of her.

She ignored the desperate promise she’d just made, the solution that had presented itself with seeming clarity, and was now starting to make her stomach ache. She ignored the mimicked snake rearing its head once more. With one more shallow breath, she crossed the asphalt, her baby’s hand in hers.
