“Harper: In your experience of the world. How do people change?

Mormon Mother: Well it has something to do with God so it's not very nice.

God splits the skin with a jagged thumbnail from throat to belly and then plunges a huge filthy hand in, he grabs hold of your bloody tubes and they slip to evade his grasp but he squeezes hard, he insists, he pulls and pulls till all your innards are yanked out and the pain! We can't even talk about that. And then he stuffs them back, dirty, tangled and torn. It's up to you to do the stitching.

Harper: And then up you get. And walk around.

Mormon Mother: Just mangled guts pretending.

Harper: That's how people change.”

― Tony Kushner, Angels In America

 

 




 

I. SATURDAY

 

When Josh moved out that July afternoon, the first thing Agatha thought was: I’m ordering Thai food for dinner. She knew that wasn’t what she was supposed to consider. She was supposed to cry in her driveway and think of Nicky, or holiday plans, or even the broken-up set of antique lamps. But coping, as always, took the path of least resistance: Josh hated Thai food, and now Josh was gone. 

Truthfully, that day in her driveway, she’d thought they’d lived separate lives long enough that the separating itself would come easy. She figured the details would work themselves out eventually, with all the other Serious Things To Be Considered. So when he finally pulled out of the driveway, she scratched the mosquito bite on her arm and thought about Pad See Ew.

It was embarrassing how little she’d anticipated the logistics of divorce. Who’d take the Netflix account? (Josh.) Who’d get the free sandwich from the punch cards at Mo’s? (Agatha.) How should she adjust the timing of the shampoo-and-conditioner subscription now that it would run out half as fast? (N/A. She’d forget to cancel.) She forgot to cancel the next three deliveries in a row, then realized she might actually hate the smell of cherry blossoms. The pink bottles piled up under the sink, then in the laundry room, slotting in with the rest of the excess. 

On the first Saturday morning in March, Agatha realized that aside from the shampoo, there was one more thing she’d forgotten to consider: the likelihood of running into her own family in the grocery store. She realized this, of course, because on the first Saturday morning in March, the likelihood was one hundred percent. She stood behind the self-serve grains, bag of uncooked rice in hand, sweating in her winter jacket. Josh stared from across the cereal aisle.

“Mama?” Nicky bounded into view.

She waggled her eyebrows. “Fancy seeing you here."

“Fancy?” He eyed her clothes, like he’d misunderstood the meaning of the word all this time.

“Different kind of fancy.”

“Oh. Me and Daddy are making pancakes! Are you coming, too?”

Her heart jolted. Josh rolled closer, and her eyes caught on a bag of frozen crab legs in his cart. He’d never liked crab—he’d always maintained that cracking open the shell hurt his teeth. She wondered why he was buying them, if that was just something he did now.

“I can’t, baby. I’m sorry.” 

“Hey,” Josh tapped him. “Can you go get the chocolate chips?”

He ran out of the aisle without bothering to answer.

Josh opened his mouth, and she laughed, as mean as she could manage. “Oh, I would really rather not.”

“I’m sorry,” he said. “I’ve been calling—I should have said it earlier, and I didn’t. I’m sorry.”

Agatha picked a box of Honeycomb off the shelf and read the label. “I’m not going to pretend to get along with that—”

“I know, and I’m—”

“Let’s just agree that we’re never going to be able to do joint family vacations. Okay?”

She looked at him, and felt a horrifying lump in her throat. By far, the most irritating logistic of divorce was the prickly little bramble of hope, the way it tumbled around and snagged on the idea of normalcy. Until she said the words out loud, she hadn’t even known she’d entertained the idea—civil holidays, easy grocery store run-ins, maybe even the occasional joint pancake breakfast. She felt cold with the loss of these hopes, shivering and sweating like a stray in the cereal aisle.

“I broke up with her.” Josh eyed the cereal. “And Nicky doesn’t like Honeycomb anymore, by the way.”

Agatha stared at him, nails digging into the cardboard. “Am I supposed to congratulate you? Maybe fall on my knees in gratitude? And fuck you; maybe I like Honeycomb.”

“I just wanted you to hear it from me.”

She studied Josh’s face, searched for the mask of passive indifference, and found only exhaustion. 

“I know you don’t like Honeycomb,” he said. 

“And you don’t eat crab legs.” 

Agatha threw the cereal box into her cart, too hard.

“Mama, Daddy, look—I got fancy toppings.” Nicky wandered proudly into the aisle with four bags of chocolate chips and a jar of something red.

Agatha kissed him swiftly goodbye, and left Josh to deal with the excess.



 

II. SUNDAY

 

Agatha stood in her empty kitchen, chewed her lip, and tried to think of anything but Rio. It felt like a bruise, the memory still fresh and tender in technicolor blue. She couldn't help but press on it a little, to see exactly how it hurt. When she dug her fingers in, she felt Rio’s arms around her waist, lips brushing hers. The slow, demanding rock of her body, the quiet shudder of her shoulders, like even with her own hand buried inside Agatha, Agatha was the one who’d taken her by surprise.

Somewhere before the end of the memory, she realized her phone was pressed to her ear. Rio picked up on the first ring. Nobody picked up on the first ring.

“Hey,” Rio said.

Just Hey, And Agatha’s stomach lurched. She was full to the brim, suddenly, with nerves. The realization was so shocking, she considered hanging up—Agatha Harkness did not get nervous.

“Agatha?”

“Sorry, I’m here.” Agatha shifted in her seat. “Hey.”

“Hey,” Rio said again. She didn’t ask why Agatha called.

“We need to talk.” Agatha winced. “Um, not about— I mean, about—”

“Yeah, we do.”

Agatha swallowed a handful of dry Honeycomb with some effort. She really did hate it. It was too sweet, and went soft too fast under the milk, collapsing into a sort of gruel. She rubbed her tongue against the roof of her mouth to rid the grit, and wondered how long they would stay on the line despite already having run out of things to say.

“So,” Rio said. She stretched the single word. “Um, how are you?”

Agatha paused with a plate in hand. She almost laughed. It was such a normal question, and so undeniably strange.

“I’m making a turkey club. Trying to figure out how Mo gets it so good.”

Rio hummed. “It’s the bread. Freshly baked.”

“Okay, well, fuck me, then.”

“You’re a regular Martha Stewart,” Rio laughed. “Let’s not forget what happened last time I ate something you made.”

“That doesn’t count—you had an undisclosed pre-existing condition. I, on the other hand, never get sick.” 

“That’s not possible.”

Agatha was still smiling as she reached for the lettuce. She was smiling as she opened the bag. Staring up at her from between the leaves was a tiny, green frog. She stopped smiling.

“Fuck, Jesus.”

She flung the bag across the room. The heads of lettuce spun through the air, her phone clattering onto some unknown surface. The frog was no bigger than her thumb, black eyes and bright green skin. She tossed a spoon at it, which did not work.

“Get out of my house, you little fucker.” 

That didn’t work, either.

She lost track of how long she’d been stalking around the living room, caught in a standoff with a frog, when her front door opened behind her. She turned, tupperware in one hand and mustard in the other, and realized too late she’d squeezed the bottle. The tupperware followed half a second later, hitting Rio in the face.

“What—ow.” Rio rubbed her nose. She was standing in Agatha’s doorway, shirt covered in mustard.

“What the fuck are you doing?” Agatha shouted.

“What am I doing? What are you doing?” Rio stared at her. “You screamed ‘Fuck Jesus’ and ‘Get out of my house,’ and then your phone went dead—I thought you were getting murdered!”

Before Agatha could respond, she felt a soft thump against her chest. The frog perched comfortably on her lapel.

“Woah,” Rio said. “Cool.”

“Get it off me.” 

“This is what you were yelling at? It’s just a tree frog.”

“I don’t care if it’s a fucking endangered species, Rio. If you don’t get it off of me, I’m shoving it in the garbage disposal.”

“Okay, okay. Stop moving.”

Agatha watched Rio cradle the frog and go about making him comfortable. She huffed and rolled her eyes, and somehow managed to keep her mouth shut until Rio sprinkled in water from the sink. 

“He doesn’t need a five star spa experience. Just toss him outside.”

“You can’t release an American Green Tree Frog in Massachusetts,” Rio said, as if it were obvious.

“Why the fuck not?”

“They’re not native. He’ll die, or infect the local ecosystem. Or worse—overpopulate. You could disrupt the whole natural order of things.”

“Wouldn’t we need two frogs for that?” Agatha grumbled, and Rio squinted at her.

“You keep a bird skeleton in your desk drawer, and you’re afraid of frogs?”

“Stop going through my desk.” Agatha pointed an accusatory finger. “That was a gift. From an old student.”

“Right.” Rio said it almost affectionately, like Agatha was missing some kind of point. Agatha didn’t care for it. She turned to survey the mess.

“Nice place, by the way,” Rio offered, straight-faced.

“It’s better without all the—” Agatha gestured to the lettuce on the ground, then paused, turning to look at her. “How did you know where I live?”

Rio drummed her fingers on the counter, and then she blushed. That was interesting.

“Should I be worried?” Agatha tapped her chin and circled Rio slowly. “Let’s see—you can ID animal skeletons on sight. You like to hang out in dark places. I saw you laugh at Sharon last week when she walked into a pillar. Lack of empathy. They always say there are signs.”

“You’re the one who needs intervention,” Rio said, emphatic. “You bring your mail to school.”

“I grab it from the mailbox on my way out.”

“Well you also dump it all over your desk. The desk that I'm forced to share. Then I have to organize it before some kid puts stickers all over your 401K summary.”

Agatha wandered closer, the smirk finally spreading across her face.

“Rio, did you memorize my address?”

“I didn’t memorize it. You just get way too much mail. Sign up for paperless billing, Agatha, I’m begging you.” Rio was rambling now, her cheeks still flushed. “And our little klepto Oliver has been eyeing Nicky’s Highlights magazines, which I know are actually for you. I’ve seen you doing the puzzles.”

“I like the ‘spot the difference’ ones,” Agatha said absently.

She felt a curious sort of warmth in her gut, like an unexpected hug from Nicky in the grocery store, or the first glimpse of her name on a piece of mail. It hit her, then, on the heels of Rio’s ridiculous rant: Rio was standing in her kitchen for the first time. She thought of the last time they had been standing across from each other, and her mouth felt suddenly dry. She reached for the dish towel to distract herself, running it under the tap. She wrung it out, and paused—she’d meant to hand it over, but she found herself stepping closer instead, until she was dabbing at the yellow spot on Rio’s chest. 

“You know, if you wanted to see me,” Rio lilted, “you didn’t have to lure me to your house and dump mustard all over me.”

“I was fresh out of garlic fries.”

She laughed, and Agatha scrubbed harder.

“Sorry I didn’t call,” Rio said softly, and placed her hand on top of Agatha's. “I was thinking about… everything.”

Agatha moved her hands under Rio’s, trying to resume the familiar, satisfying motion of scraping something clean.

“Agatha, can you just—just stop for a second.” 

Agatha struggled under grip, and finally, Rio stripped off her shirt entirely. She dropped it on the floor.

“There,” she said. “Problem solved.”

Agatha stared at her torso, bare under the edge of a sports bra. The tree tattoo wrapped around her ribcage. She remembered, suddenly, seeing it for the first time under the pink light of the back room, wishing she could trace the lines.

“Miracle tree,” she muttered. 

Rio looked at her in surprise. “How did you know that?”

“Fuck you. Maybe I’m worldly.”

Rio narrowed her eyes. “Lilia.”

She eyed Agatha, and then wandered into the living room, hands clasped behind her back. It reminded Agatha of the first day of school, how Rio had wandered around her classroom, inspecting the decorations. She’d definitely been wearing a shirt then.

Spot the difference, Agatha thought.

“When I first moved in with Lilia, I wasn't exactly good company.” 

Rio said it offhand, picking at objects on the shelf by the TV, but Agatha stilled behind the counter. 

“They always taught us in church that everything happens for a reason. Part of the Plan, and all that. So for a while, I was just angry. My Plan seemed pretty shitty.”

Rio brushed her fingers along the bookshelf, tipping out the spines and slotting them back.

“I really put Lilia through it. One time I set fire to her shawl in the bathtub.”

Agatha let out a startled laugh. “What did she do?”

“She just opened a window to let the smoke out.”

Agatha snorted, and her eyes fell on the frog in the tupperware. He was looking at her. She shuddered, and turned the container to face the wall.

“She made two cups of Moringa tea every morning, and every morning for two years, I’d toss mine out the window.”

“I would too,” Agatha said. “That tea is repulsive.”

Rio grinned, and then her face softened. She looked at Agatha. “That’s something family does, you know? Makes you tea in the morning. And I didn’t want Lilia to feel like family.”

“Why not?” Agatha asked. “I don’t exactly… get her, but you two seem to have some strange understanding.”

“Well,” Rio said. Her mouth was an odd twist of a smile. “The last time I’d had a family, it didn’t go great.”

“But Lilia wouldn’t…” Agatha paused. “She would never do that.”

“No,” Rio said. “But that’s the thing—when it happens once, it’s on the table forever. You’ll always know there’s the possibility, no matter how unlikely.”

Agatha thought of chasing her mother down a crowded walkway, and swallowed.

“But then I fucked up my knee," Rio continued. “My first night home, I felt like I was in someone else’s body. Like someone took all the important parts of me and ran them through a shredder. I wasn’t me, you know?”

Rio looked at Agatha again, her face serious, and Agatha couldn’t do anything but nod. If she did something else, it would be something insane, like walking across her house to wrap Rio tightly in her arms in the middle of this story. She didn’t know why Rio was even telling her this, in a voice that was so matter-of-fact, the way she explained the moral of a picture book to a second-grader. 

Agatha reached for the bread on the counter. It was no doubt contaminated by frog feet and in need of disposal. She stacked the slices uselessly on the plate, one on top of the other. 

“That night, Lilia told me that churches are institutions, and institutions are in the business of opportunistic bullshit. She said: ‘Darling, anyone with real clairvoyance knows that sometimes, things just happen.’”

“Shockingly lucid observation,” Agatha muttered, but for the first time, felt a sudden wave of affection for Lilia.

Rio crossed back to the kitchen island and picked up the tupperware, turning it in her hands. She examined the frog’s sticky feet.

“She said that making a life out of devastation was like making tea from the dead leaves of a tree.”

Agatha didn’t speak for a long moment. Then, she touched Rio’s hand. Her fingers landed on the space between her forefinger and thumb, stretched against the plastic. Rio put down the frog, and grabbed hold of Agatha’s fingers.

“Is there a point to this story?” Agatha cleared her throat.

Rio smiled, and rounded the kitchen island without letting go.

“It wasn’t some sort of divine intervention that you walked into the club that night, or that I ended up in your classroom. It just kind of happened. So maybe I’m supposed to be focusing on what comes next.” 

She paused in front of Agatha, and for the first time, her gaze faltered. 

“What does this have to do with tea?” Agatha's voice came out too weak. She was overcome with the immediate, overwhelming urge to turn and run to her room. She wanted to climb under the covers, the way she used to wait out a thunderstorm, or the sound of her mother yelling at someone in the living room.

“I drank the tea that next morning.” Rio said simply. “And now I can’t go one day without it.”

Agatha snorted. “You’re saying you believe in miracles.”

Rio rolled her eyes and stepped closer.

“I’m saying I’m glad I chose to start drinking it, instead of waiting around for things to get better.”

She paused. She still hadn’t let go of Agatha’s hand. 

“And I’m saying, once I get fired, I think I know what I’d choose to do next.”

“What,” Agatha said dumbly, and why was she asking, why the fuck was she asking? Because Rio wasn’t getting fired, and Agatha needed to tell her that. She definitely didn’t need to hear whatever it was Rio was doing a very poor job explaining. But not that poor, because Agatha knew the exact, terrible, insane thing she was trying to say.

“Tell me, Agatha. Have you thought about it?” 

Rio brushed a piece of hair behind Agatha’s ear, and Agatha’s felt her eyes flutter shut.

“Thought about what?”

Rio hummed. “Try again.”

Agatha hummed in response. She meant it to be a little cheeky, but it came out entirely too breathy to pass. 

“Maybe once or twice.” 

Rio set her lips to Agatha’s jaw, just under her ear. “Good. You’re getting closer,” she whispered, and Agatha’s breath hitched.

“Yes,” she breathed finally. 

The single word felt more damning than begging Rio to touch her in a storage closet—the admission that she’d been thinking of all the ways in which they’d happened to each other. She opened her eyes, suddenly unsure what she would find. Rio was smirking.

“And what exactly have you been thinking about?” she said, thumb flexing at Agatha’s hip. “The roller coasters?”

Agatha kissed her then, if only to wipe the stupid smirk off her face. Because—and it was utterly maddening—Rio was right. For six days straight, Agatha had thought of nothing else but this. She wrapped an arm around Rio, fingers bumping over the bare ridges of her spine, and felt her knees weaken at the bend. There was a hesitance to the way Rio was kissing her, moving only her lips. It was almost sweet, the slightest tilt of her head, fingertips resting on Agatha’s face like this was some kind of quiet Sunday Miracle. But her kitchen was no place for miracles. She didn’t want it to be sweet, or hesitant, or any of the things that came after a thing that happened. So she dug her nails in, pushed Rio against the counter, and swallowed her gasp.

She hummed too needily, pressed too hard, as Rio’s tongue slid against hers. She licked at the inside of Rio’s mouth, the back of her teeth, the spongy space under her tongue. It was soft and wet like milk-mush cereal, like biting down on a crab leg and hollowing her cheeks to suck out the meat. Rio made a noise like a half-whimper against her mouth, and the sound made Agatha soften for a second against her. It made her want to slow, and cradle her. She wanted to stroke her fingers through Rio's dark hair and kiss the places she hadn’t yet, starting with the top of her forehead and tip of her nose, and ending at the soft skin behind her knee. And certainly not skipping any of the more notable middle parts. 

She thought about the promise of her mouth on those middle parts. Then, for some reason, she remembered that Rio had memorized her address. It was an odd combination of thoughts, and her mouth felt strange, until she realized she was smiling. She bit down, and Rio made a noise of surprise, because it was her lip Agatha was really biting.

Agatha knew she would need to say something sooner or later. She wanted, desperately, for it to be later, and not sooner. So she shoved her hands higher, under the band of Rio’s bra, and dug into the meat of her back.

“Just so you know—” Rio mumbled, and then she gasped as Agatha moved a hand to the front. Agatha meant to palm her roughly, to catch her nails on the curve of her breast, but her hand stilled there instead. She bit back a moan at the impossible softness of Rio’s skin, at the feel of the single breast in her hand. She tested the weight of it, the give, her lips on autopilot as she mapped the shape so different than her own. Vaguely, she thought it might be a little strange, that she was just letting it rest on top of her palm, like the bag of uncooked rice from the bulk bin. But Rio didn’t seem to mind. She pushed her chest further into Agatha’s hand, and Agatha had to stop kissing her altogether at the sensation, the rub of fine hairs and goosebumps under her finger pads, the pebbled peak tightening under her thumb. 

“Fuck, Agatha,” Rio was already panting against her mouth. “This really isn’t why I came over.” 

“I don’t care.” Agatha knew it sounded mean, and she didn’t care, she didn't. She slotted a leg between Rio’s, but just on the off chance that it had been too mean—which she didn’t care about—she said: “I don’t see you trying to stop me.”

Rio’s eyes were shut, her hips rocking slow into Agatha’s thigh, and Agatha paused. Her hand lay motionless against Rio’s chest. “You’re not, right? Stopping me?” 

Rio made a strangled noise and shook her head vehemently, clutching Agatha’s arms. It was childlike—just plain silly—the way she shook her head. Agatha felt a sudden jolt of affection, and kissed her hard to banish it. She nipped and pulled and sucked, biting and bruising, until she tasted copper.

“Fuck, sorry,” Agatha mumbled, swiping a thumb against the bleeding spot.

“It’s fine, I’m fine, it’s—” Rio didn’t bother to finish the sentence, pulling Agatha’s mouth back to hers. Agatha obliged, licking the sore spot with the tip of her tongue, and laid a hand over the Miracle Tree. She dragged her nails as she finally, finally, traced the lines. Rio twitched and moaned, her hips rutting harder, and the reality of it was staggering, all of a sudden—Rio, who turned people on for money, was coming apart against her, again.

Agatha broke her lips away to breathe, because apparently that was still needed despite the urgency of the situation. Rio’s breath smelled like those ridiculous white tic-tacs she carried around like a rattling pill box. It struck Agatha—she knew the difference between the smell of toothpaste and tic-tacs on Rio’s breath. 

Catastrophe, she thought. She finally looked at Rio's face, at her bright eyes and flushed cheeks and swollen lip. She looked like someone who’d been thoroughly kissed. She looked so goddamn pretty. This was certainly not one of the Serious Things To Be Considered.

“Rio.” Agatha's voice was firm, albeit a little breathless, but she didn’t remove the hands from the soft line of Rio’s jaw. 

“Sorry,” Rio said finally. “I know, we have a lot to figure out, I just—” her cheeks turned pink again. “I just really wanted to do that.”

“No, it’s not that; I—” Agatha stumbled backwards, out of Rio’s grasp. Her back caught the edge of the oven door handle, and she pressed her fingers shakily into the sore spot. She still didn’t know what made people want to do that—dig hard into a hurting spot. She dug hard, too, until she only felt a hot, dull ache. Her eyes fell to the container on the counter, and the frog stared back at her, small and unmoving. She wondered if frogs sat up straight when they died. 

She didn’t look at Rio when she said it. She pressed her fingers into the bruise, looked at the frog, and said: “Wanda agreed not to fire you.”

In the silence that followed, Agatha kept her eyes on the red plastic lid. She flicked it, and the frog startled. Alive, after all.

“What do you mean, ‘agreed?’” Rio said finally.

Agatha steeled herself. 

“I mean that you have until Friday to quit the club. Your teaching job and your California offer will still be good.”

She heard a burst of air, like Rio was scoffing at her. She didn’t like being scoffed at. She sulked and tapped the lid with all of her fingernails. 

“Look at me.”

Agatha didn’t look up right away. She looked at the water jug, then the browning bananas by the fridge, and then finally at Rio, like it had been her own idea all along. Rio’s face had gone pale, and Agatha knew now that the sound hadn’t been a scoff. It had been the release of all the air out of her lungs, like Agatha had kicked her clean in the stomach. 

“You want to rephrase that as a question?” Rio said.

“No, Rio, I do not,” she replied automatically. Calling Rio just to talk, and kissing her in the middle of her kitchen—that was uncharted territory. This part, she knew. 

“It’s not a question,” she continued, louder now. “A kindergartner could make this decision with their fucking eyes closed. You quit the club—the job that is literally killing you, by the way, according to your doctor—and keep doing the job you told me you loved. Where’s the huge, complicated dilemma here?”

“My doctor?” Rio’s eyes blazed. “You talked to my doctor?”

“I didn’t go out of my way. Carol seemed obliged to elaborate on the risk factors of asthma, and I just happened to be there, because you were walking around with no inhaler, no epipen, and a fucking death wish, apparently! And don’t think I don’t know you’ve been upping your refills. Turns out—working in a smoky club is bad for one’s lungs. Who knew!”

Rio snatched her shirt and her keys off the counter, her teeth bared in a way Agatha had never seen. 

“You are fucking unbelievable, Agatha, you know that?”

“You’re unbelievable,” Agatha yelled, following her to the door. “If it were up to you, you would have just gone home and never shown up to school again. That’s what you were telling me just now, wasn’t it? That you’re ready to just roll over on a career before you’ve even gotten your goddamn degree. That’s unbelievable, Rio.”

Rio turned slowly to face her. 

“You didn’t even think to ask me, did you?”

Agatha scoffed in response, ignoring the ugly twist in her chest.

“Fuck you, Agatha.”

Agatha stopped short at the sudden frailty in Rio’s voice and looked at her standing on the porch. Rio opened her mouth like she might say something, and her lower lip trembled. Agatha had five excruciating seconds to memorize the flaring nostrils and white-knuckled grip on the t-shirt before Rio turned.

Agatha slammed the door behind her, not bothering to watch her leave. At least, not until she made it all the way down the path, and into her car, and down the block. Agatha could see that much from the window, and she wasn’t going to do something ridiculous, like cover her eyes in her own house, or turn the other way.

Once her street was empty, she turned sharply and slipped a little on the forgotten mustard. The frog paced in the plastic container, suddenly restless. She wondered if he knew the only person alive who cared about his well-being had just left. She sank to the hardwood, back against the door, and tucked her head between her knees. The tang of mustard burned her nose and made her dizzy. She decided it was altogether much worse than a roller coaster.

 

 

III. MONDAY

 

“There was a frog in one of your lettuce bags.”

Agatha savored the next sip of her coffee as a reward for her superior patience. She’d waited on hold for three minutes and forty seconds.

“Oh, okay, yeah—it’s actually really common.” 

Agatha couldn’t tell if the customer service representative was stoned or just spoke that way to irritate her. Either way, it didn’t bode well for her chances of a solution.

“The produce is distributed from Florida, and sometimes the little guys make it all the way onto the trucks.”

“Maybe I wasn’t clear.” Agatha set down her coffee, and held the phone to her mouth. “I don’t care how common it is. There is a frog in my kitchen. What’s stopping me from suing you for emotional and physical damages?”

“Actually, tree frogs aren’t a food safety hazard, which is why they end up in the produce so often. You can just throw the frog away and wash the lettuce.”

“I’m not—” Agatha looked at the frog, still unmoving except for its bobbing throat. “Throwing away a live frog seems… messy.”

“It’s alive? Shit, usually they’re dead. Maybe get it a tank or something? My girlfriend has a pet turtle, if you want me ask what—”

Agatha hung up and tossed the phone in defeat. The frog stared through the plastic.

“Lucky bastard,” she muttered, and carried him to the car.

 

 

IV. TUESDAY

 

By recess, Agatha realized she’d been counting the hours since Rio had looked her in the eye. Almost forty-eight since she’d stood there and stared, as if Agatha had yanked out her guts and strung them over the porch railing for good measure. Agatha considered Rio’s behavior a blatant overreaction. Then she stopped considering, because she didn’t want to stumble across anything inconvenient. 

She paced by the slide, and kicked idly at the cracks in the crumb rubber. For once, she didn’t want to be out here. She wanted to be at her picnic table, which had become Rio’s picnic table. She didn’t know exactly it had happened, but she realized one day that it was always where she went looking first, under the maple tree. 

Agatha found, to her surprise, she didn’t mind giving up her spot. Rio looked good there—dark hair falling over her face, legs stretched across the opposite bench. Agatha had spent six months trying to understand this simple logic, and the last seven days wondering if she might try explaining it to Rio. But when she imagined saying it out loud—I’ve gotten used to you—it sounded like comparing her to an old shoe. And then came the terrifying thought: maybe she’d never grow tired of Rio at all. Maybe she’d just grow more used to her, year after year, until she was old and unstable and couldn’t remember the picnic table had ever been hers in the first place. She shook the thought away, and the resulting crawl up her spine, as Grace and Kara sidled up to her.

“Ms. Harkness?” they said together, and Agatha raised her eyebrows. The unison always set her teeth on edge. She tried not to think of the twins from The Shining.

“Ye-es?” She matched their tone.

“Do frogs like to eat grass? We can get some for him.” Grace looked up at her earnestly, and Agatha wanted to groan. For some reason, she was still unable to make good on her threat of shoving it down the garbage disposal. Even more disturbing, she also couldn’t look at the back door without thinking of how upset Rio would be if she—how had she put it? Disrupted the natural order of things. 

She’d taken a spare tank from Peggy’s classroom in the name of education, leftover from a prior class pet situation. (If any kids asked, she was supposed to say that Knuckles the Lizard died of old age) (Knuckles hadn’t died of old age, though; he’d died from eating crayons) (at the scene of the crime, the victim’s tail was blue and red, and the frozen crickets meant to be his dinner had thawed, inching across the classroom floor). Agatha had left Peggy’s with a sincere hope that the crayon curse came with the tank.

“I think he eats lettuce,” Agatha said to Grace. And then, because she knew she should, added: “We can check the library and see if there are any books about frogs.”

“What’s his name?” Kara asked, swinging from foot to foot. “And where’d you get him?”

“I was making a frog sandwich,” Agatha muttered.

Kara looked uncertain, like she wasn't quite sure if it had been a joke, and Grace looked up. 

“His name is Sandwich?”

Agatha watched them spread the news across the playground and knew that she would never, as long as she lived, call a frog by name. She smiled all the same.

 

 

When they came in from recess, Rio’s arm was dangling into the tank. She held what looked like deformed worms, and Nicky was crowded next to her, watching with wide eyes.

“Sandwich eats worms?” Grace asked.

Rio hummed, completely unfazed by the new name. “And other insects.”

“Ms. Harkness said he eats lettuce.”

Fucking narcs. Agatha tried not to glare at a seven year-old, and Rio turned her head ever so slightly. She didn’t look at Agatha, not all the way. Forty-eight hours and counting.

“Ms. Harkness was wrong,” Rio said. “Tree frogs are carnivores. Do you know what that means?”

“I know!” Nicky waved excitedly. “They only eat other animals.”

Rio smiled at him, and handed him a worm. Agatha was proud despite herself as Nicky placed it gently into the tank.

“Thank you all for your help,” Rio said. “Sandwich is very lucky. Let’s get ready for art, and we can learn more about him tomorrow.”

“Go wash your hands if you touched the frog,” Agatha added, eyeing their wandering fingers.

Nicky opened his mouth, and Agatha shot him a look.

“Or if you touched a worm.”

He shut his mouth and strolled casually out the door.

“Hey,” Agatha muttered in Rio’s direction. 

“Touched the worms,” Rio called over her shoulder, and held up her hands. She didn’t look at Agatha.

 

 

Peter Parker was not an art teacher. He was sixteen, volunteering as some sort of detention, which Agatha could not for the life of her figure out how he’d gotten. She was familiar with delinquency in all forms, and he seemed at most a harmless science whiz. Which, at the moment, was not coming in handy for his internship teaching art to second-graders. Agatha watched him draw a shaky outline of a dog mixed with an alligator, which he was describing as a dinosaur. 

“I’m supposed to sign off on his hours for that?” Agatha mumbled to Rio. Rio didn’t answer. She sat in a kid’s desk, pen tapping her thigh. 

“Rio,” Agatha whispered again, unable to keep the desperation out of her tone. The tapping sound stopped, and Agatha held her breath.

Finally, Rio stood, her movements jerky and rigid, and gestured to the back door.

 

 

Rio left the door propped open at Peter’s look of utter alarm, and Agatha followed her to the maple tree. She wondered when the buds of green would arrive—if, by then, the tree and the picnic table would still belong to Rio, or if Rio would be gone. Agatha found she’d rather never come out here again, if those were the terms and conditions. Rio shuffled her feet, nose already red from the chill, and looked at the trunk of the tree.

“Are you just not going to look at me for the rest of the—” Agatha stopped herself. Week? Year? 

Rio traced the damp tree bark with her index finger. It slotted into a smooth groove, and Agatha knew from the easy way her fingers fit that she’d traced this section of the tree before. Agatha felt, for the first time, jealous of a maple tree. Then, she felt the hair raise on the back of her neck. She wondered if she were a wild dog, if her hackles would be up. She thought probably they would be, most of the time.

“There’s something I don’t understand,” she said, biting down on the words. “You’re so angry at me, but you’re here. Go ahead, tell me you quit.”

Rio was silent.

“No one is forcing you to stay.” Agatha took a step closer. She could see the bags under Rio’s eyes, the red rims of her eyelids that she’d missed until now. If she were smarter, or nicer, or a different third thing she didn’t know because she’d been raised by Evanora—she’d see those things and decide to stop. She took another step.

“No one forced you to teach Ellie how to double dutch, or engage in some weird philosophical discussion with Oliver about what happens after you die, and take it so goddamn serious, even though he was talking about blood-eating lizards, or whatever.”

She stepped even closer, until she was standing too close to be acceptable, right outside the back windows of the classroom. 

“No one forced you to shove your arm into a box of worms for a stray frog, and yet. Here you are, Rio.”

It had started as a prosecution, but she felt the utter betrayal of softness creeping into her tone. Here you are. She balled her fists, and reminded herself that Rio had already tried to leave once before. Rio was a Leaver. Things only got worse, the longer a Leaver stayed.

Rio’s eyes were trained on the picnic table. Agatha couldn’t help herself. She reached for Rio’s face in broad daylight—reckless, stupid—and curled her fingers around her jaw. Rio’s eyes snapped to hers in surprise and then heat, and Agatha drank in the color with a shocking kind of thirst. She was relieved there wouldn’t be a forty-ninth hour.

“Let go.” 

Rio’s voice rasped. Agatha couldn’t look away now, after being denied for so long, so she caught it—the pleading in her eyes where she’d expected fury.

Leaver, Agatha thought. Leaver, Leaver, Leaver.

“If you want to choose the club over this classroom,” Agatha said, tightening her grip, “do it now. Don’t waste your time. Believe it or not, I managed on my own for nineteen years before you got here.”

Rio grabbed the fingers digging into her jaw. For a short, awful moment, Agatha couldn’t tell if Rio was trying to hurt her back, or trying to keep her there. Rio hesitated, like she wasn’t sure either. Then she stepped back, and She left Agatha standing under the maple tree.

 

 

V. WEDNESDAY

 

“I’m not going.” Agatha shrugged. “Makes no fucking sense.”

“I agree.” Rio fiddled with Wanda’s windowsill herbs, tilting the stalks this way and that. 

The three of them twitched in tandem at the faint beep of the school bus, six eyes finding the clock.

“Scott is expecting two teachers on the bus.” Wanda sounded tired. “You’ve got ten minutes—someone explain.”

“You asked me to cover you!” Agatha looked at her, incredulous. “Why can’t you just go on the damn field trip; they’re your kids!”

“I have a lunch meeting with Hank.” 

Agatha scoffed. “Hank Pym? You’d rather have lunch with the tightest asshole in the fifth grade than chaperone a field trip?”

“Agatha.” 

“He doesn’t believe in teachers' unions. How do you teach U.S. history and not believe in unions?”

“It was a standing meeting,” Wanda said. “How did you two not talk about this?”

“I’d already signed up,” Rio said, exasperated, "which she would know if she ever checked her email.” 

“You emailed me?” Agatha squinted at Rio in disbelief. For some unassailable reason, she had the whole planter in her hands now. “We work eight inches apart all day, and you fucking emailed me that you were going on a field trip.”

“I do have a job to do, I’m not just hanging around waiting for you to—”

“Stop it,” Wanda cut in. “Rio, who signed off?”

“Heyyy, guys,” Jen poked her head through the doorway, then slipped in entirely. “I couldn’t help but overhear this very normal-sounding conversation.” She looked at Wanda. “You told me to find her more fourth grade opportunities, and this seemed like an easy one.”

“Why does she need fourth grade—”

Agatha stopped herself short. The office was suddenly silent.  She stumbled to the details, two seconds too late: California.

“So you signed up for this…”

“Before the break, yes,” Rio snapped. “We’re all caught up now.”

“Oop.” Jen slunk backwards, hands raised in front of her. “Didn’t mean to get in the middle of this little lovers’ tiff.”

“It’s not a tiff,” Agatha snapped, just as Rio said, “We’re not lovers.”

Jen and Wanda exchanged looks, and then turned slowly to Agatha. Agatha felt her cheeks blaze.

“And, obviously,” she added, gesturing vaguely, “we’re not…”

“Oh my god,” Jen muttered, hand to her forehead. “Please stop talking.”

Agatha stared pointedly at the bulletin board. Someone had removed the red smiley face of thumbtacks since the fall, and they were scattered normally across the edges.

“J, just take my place.”

“You want me to spend the day at, quote, ‘New England’s most historically accurate colonial village?’ Hard pass.”

Agatha blinked, and scratched at her nose. “Okay,” she said finally. “That’s fair. I’ll go. Enjoy subbing second grade.”

Jen blinked. “Wait—what?”

Rio followed Agatha out, careful to stay a few paces behind.

“I rotated the plant,” she called over her shoulder. “The one on the end wasn’t getting enough light. And don’t forget to check on Sandwich.”

“What?”

“They have a frog together,” Agatha heard Wanda mumble. She wasn’t far enough down the hallway to miss their conspiratorial laughter. Fucking idiots.

“I didn’t know you were going,” she emphasized. Rio had already sped past her.

“I don’t care,” came her offhand reply.

 

 

Agatha dragged her hand along the wooden fences of Old Sturbridge Village. She usually led the front of the pack and played snakebite, tapping anyone that passed her and sending them sprinting to the back of the line. But these weren’t her kids, and she had the unexpected luxury of ambling with the slow-walkers.

The air was thick with an early warm front and recent rain, and the grass already grew lush between the cottages. It smelled good here, too, like freshly baked bread and hay, and something else. She inhaled deep, over and over, trying to identify the unfamiliar smells (she recognized candle wax by scent, and boiling wool-dye by sight), until she was light-headed. She only stopped when Billy squinted up at her and said loudly, “why are you breathing like that?”

For the most part, she kept her distance from Rio. By mid-morning, she’d remembered she loved doing this, and decided it was a good thing she’d come after all. It wasn’t until she was sweating in a barn, fighting Rio for slippery purchase on the handle of a butter churn, that she decided she’d been terribly wrong.

 

 

The East Barn towered over a meadow, and Agatha was impressed despite herself—she’d never seen a barn so tall. With the following wave of disgust, she wondered when this had become the kind of thing that impressed her—a green ground, and a big, red barn. She remembered a farmhouse at night—Nicky in bed with a rabbit and Rio curled on the carpet. Smoke rising above their heads under towering trees, the quiet hoot of an owl, and her hand over the soft ridges of Rio’s braid.

The feeling hit her with a kind of sweeping intensity, and she put a hand out on the nearest fence. She didn’t know what it was, or why it was happening, but she stood there and breathed in the smell of pine until it passed. She had a nagging thought that she might be turning into someone who liked peace and quiet, now that peace and quiet had a certain kind of shape.

 

 

“Does anyone know where butter comes from?”

The tour guide—who may have actually lived here, judging by his smell and his tunic—spoke too animatedly for this time of afternoon in a hot barn.

“Milk," came a chorus of replies.

“That’s exactly right. And where does milk come from?”

No one answered, and Agatha stifled a laugh. The gangly teen with dark, curly hair, had underestimated the power of a too-obvious answer. With the massive rust-colored cow standing next to him, the kids were instead looking at him like he was stupid.

“Raise your hand,” Agatha muttered to Billy, who hadn’t left her side at the back of the group. “You know the answer.”

“What if it’s not right?” he whispered.

“It’s right.”

“But what if it’s not?” He stared at the cow, as if there might be some kind of trick.

Agatha hummed. “Wouldn’t you rather find out than wonder forever?”

“No. I’ll just wonder forever.” 

She swallowed her laugh again, and her eyes fell automatically to Rio across the heads of children. It was a habit—a kid said something particularly good, and they’d look for each other. It was a quick glance-and-hold to bookmark the story for later, where they could laugh and laugh, alone and unencumbered by Best Practice. Agatha didn’t want to go back to Best Practice. 

The light from the open loft made a sun spot on the floor, and dappled part of Rio’s arm. Agatha wondered what the light did to her eyes. Suddenly, she wished they were alone in this barn. She wished she was close enough to smell the spice of Rio’s cologne and the intoxicating base of her plain, boring soap, to inhale slow and full against her neck. She would press her nose just there, she knew, at the spot underneath her ear. She wished Rio wouldn’t push her away if she did. She wished and wished, and breathed in hay and cow shit.

It was somewhere in that fantasy about Rio’s clean-smelling neck that she raised her arms behind her to stretch. And it was there, with her hands raised, that her day took its somewhat unexpected—but really, mostly expected—turn for the worse.

“We have a volunteer!”

The kids spun their heads to look at her, and she lowered her hands.

“Oh—no.” 

“Come right up!” The boy looked at her with clear desperation, held out a bonnet, and Agatha sighed.

 

 

She churned butter to the teen’s instruction of: “It’s easy—just like you’re plunging a toilet.” After fifteen minutes, the bucket’s contents were still mostly cream, and Agatha was starting to sweat under her bonnet. Apparently, historically accurate meant incredibly inefficient. She’d barely noticed that the kids had been dismissed to their own churning stations until the teen came back to relieve her.

“Thanks for that,” he said. 

“Oh, anytime.” Agatha wondered if this pilgrim boy understood sarcasm. She tugged on the handle. 

“Oh, sometimes it helps if you—”

“I got it.”

She put all her weight on it, wiggling from side to side. Still nothing.

“Can you believe they used to do this every day?” the teen said finally. “Make their own butter?” 

“They didn’t. They had to skim enough cream first.” Agatha paused to catch her breath, and gestured to the door. “It’s on the sign.”

“Oh.” His face reddened. “Yeah. Of course.”

“Maybe you should listen to him.” Rio spoke casually, but there was a coolness underneath that made Agatha look up. “It’s his job—maybe he’s actually the expert on this very specific topic.”

“Clearly.”

“William,” Rio said, and Agatha stared. Since when did the teen have a name? 

“Isn’t it true that the amount of cream would depend on the number of cows?”

“Well, yeah,” he said, rubbing his neck. “Some families had up to a hundred cows.”

“Wow, a hundred.” Rio tilted her head. “And isn’t it true that in summer they’d churn more frequently so it wouldn’t spoil?”

“That’s right,” William said, as if he’d just remembered. “They mostly had red cows, like Luci over there. They produced more milk.”

“So it’s entirely possible that some families had to churn every day, isn’t it?”

“Yes, definitely." William's eyes flitted between them.

Rio shot Agatha a look. “I can read signs, too.”

Mean was rare on Rio. Agatha didn’t prefer it. 

“This village doesn’t have a hundred cows, does it?” Agatha said finally. “I’m trying to keep a fourteen year-old from making a fool of himself in front of the children.”

“Um, I’m sixteen,” William said.

Agatha blinked. “Well, you should... eat more protein. Or drink a glass of that milk you love so much.”

“This isn’t about him,” Rio said. “God forbid you can’t control a situation for thirty seconds, Agatha. We’re in a barn. On a kids’ field trip.”

The hot stink of butter and manure was starting to make Agatha nauseous. Rio still being angry with her was unexpectedly adding to the effect.

“Sorry—” William stepped forward, gesturing weakly at the churn. “Can you not break that? I don’t want to get in trouble.”

“I’m not breaking it, Teen. I’m fixing it.”

“Jesus Christ.” Rio strode towards her, and grabbed hold of the handle, her hands slotting between Agatha’s. “Let go.”

“You let go.” Agatha felt the faithful prickle at the back of her neck, hackles spreading slow to the base of her spine. She tried to ignore the fact that hackles only made a wild dog look bigger—a fairly useless trick to use on Rio, who already seemed to know the exact size and shape of her.

They stood there, hands clasped over the butter churn, like they were just another historical exhibit. Agatha wondered what the sign would call them—colonial sisters, or wives, or probably sister-wives if she was really committed to being historically accurate. She corrected her earlier assumption—sister-wives were surely one point for efficiency. But inside this real barn, inside a pretend village, they weren’t on the same team at all, by blood, marriage, or otherwise. Agatha pulled with all of her strength, and she could see the hard line of Rio’s jaw that gave her away. 

“It’s really okay,” William said, uncertain. “We’ll fix it later, you can—”

“Stop it,” Agatha hissed at Rio. She could feel the sweat on her brow.

“For the love of god, Agatha, LET GO!” 

Rio’s hand slipped a little, and her thumb pressed into the soft of Agatha’s hand, the bit between her forefinger and thumb. Agatha dropped the handle before she even realized she had. The force snapped the churn free, and Rio, still gripping the handle, stumbled backward. A rain of milk semi-solids flew through the air, the churn overturned at their feet. 

Rio stood in a puddle of cream, her hand braced against the cow’s flank. Luci the Red Cow mooed, and turned her head to see who’d undone her hard, lonely work.  

“Sorry,” Rio muttered.

Agatha was surprised it would be over so easily, then realized Rio had apologized to the cow.

“There’s a sink out back,” William said, defeated. 

Agatha didn’t bother answering before she turned.

 

 

The “sink” was a small metal trough that hugged the outer wall. Agatha stood there with the tap running and no inclination to scrub herself clean with the frigid water. She gripped the edge of the steel and shut her eyes. She only opened them when she heard movement beside her. She watched Rio toe off her milk-soaked shoes and socks with jerky movements. When she couldn’t look anymore, she turned to the back field. 

The yellow-green was laced with early white wildflowers, just starting to poke above the grass. Swallows swooped, dragonflies hovered, and gophers poked their heads out to watch. Agatha blinked against the aliveness of it, the intricate dance of movement and color and sound. On a different day, or even one hour ago, it would have been breathtaking in its beauty.

When she turned back, Rio was wringing out her socks in the sink. She finally looked up, socks balled in her fists, and the hard lines of her face made Agatha’s mouth run dry. She gestured to Rio’s bare feet.

“I didn’t mean to—”

“I’m done with this.” Rio spoke at the same time, and they both fell silent.

“You’re going to have to be a little more specific,” Agatha said after a beat, although she didn’t really want that at all. This was the other side of the bramble of hope, when the blackberries had been picked and sold before they could be eaten at all, leaving a thicket of thorns. Agatha felt them all scratch at once. 

Rio’s hands tightened around her socks, and Agatha watched the water drip from her hands.

“This,” she said again, gesturing between them. A drop of water flicked onto Agatha’s cheek, and she tried not to flinch.

“It’s been six months, Agatha. We’ve spent every day together for six months, and all of it has been so fucking hard. This was supposed to be one semester, okay? And I just—” her voice broke. “I don’t want to do it anymore.”

Agatha crossed her arms, fingers digging deep into her own sides until her ribs ached.

“You’ve changed, since we met,” Rio said. “In a good way. I liked who you were becoming, I—” she stopped, and started again. “You want things. You feel things. For me, I think. Or, I thought.” She paused, her gaze faltering. “But you have no respect for me. You say I'm good at this, and then you treat me like I will never be good enough. That I’m always doing something wrong, that I can’t make the right decisions about my own life.” 

“Rio—” Agatha’s voice trembled.

“I’m sure your other student teachers didn’t mind it,” Rio continued, her tone bitter now. “Your hand up their ass, controlling them like brainless little puppets. I get it, I do—when you’re in your element, the way you command a room, it’s fucking magnificent, it’s—” she swallowed, blinking like she’d gotten carried away. Her face hardened again.

“I never had this much fucking trouble before you,” Agatha said finally. The words hung between them, and Rio took a step closer.

“And why do you think that is?” 

Agatha knew how to identify a leading question—it was the nature of her job. 

Don’t you think it’s better to share? 

Or Wouldn’t you rather know for sure that milk comes from a cow, instead of wondering forever?  

Or Why is it, Agatha, that out of everyone, you treat me so differently, so much worse, so much better, and just so much? 

But as far as leading questions had gotten her up to this point, she’d never yet learned how to answer one.

She looked at Rio, and circled the differences in front of her: 

Difference 1: Someone who’d once been a stranger and now was not.

Difference 2: Cheeks flushed with anger instead of the soft press of Agatha’s lips. 

Difference 3: Socks in her hands, not on on her feet. 

She still looked pretty, though, even covered in spoilt milk. Not a difference. 

“I never asked for a student teacher.” Agatha yanked off her soiled sweater, and hurled it into the sink. The tap was still running. She breathed through her nose, and squared her shoulders.

“You are the single biggest inconvenience of my entire fucking life.” 

She said it in grief, not anger, though her teeth were bared. But what was grief, if not injustice? It was the first kind she’d ever known: being handed to the world already assigned to Evanora. And now, here it was again—the smoking gun, in her own clammy hands. It was the case she’d been making to herself since the day Rio had stepped into her classroom; the same needling thought that made her say, that first afternoon: “You don’t remember me?” 

Six months later, and the final verdict was the same: Agatha Harkness was just one more woman that Rio had happened to.

She looked at the soft face she’d gotten used to. The hands and lips she’d felt in a pink room in June, and everything she’d suddenly, plainly known. And she understood now, standing in front of a barn with a bonnet at her feet, that she would never go back to not knowing. She thought that might have been the worst thing anyone had ever done to her. For that, she thought she might hate Rio more than she hated her own mother. So she looked at the face she might hate the most, and said: “I never even asked for that lap dance.”

She saw it break Rio’s exterior like a crack in the earth. Rio stepped forward, and poked a finger into Agatha’s chest so hard she stumbled a step.

“You kissed me in that closet, Agatha.”

Agatha wrenched her hand away from her chest. She squeezed too hard. 

“You kissed me first.”

Rio went completely still. And then, to Agatha’s utter, gut-wrenching dismay, her brown eyes welled. She took a step back.

“Yeah, well.” Her voice wavered. “You kissed me second.”

 

 

Agatha didn’t hear Rio’s bare footsteps in the dirt, and she didn’t turn. Some moments later, she knew she was alone. Her eyes blurred against the back field, her wet sweater cradled in her arms. She supposed it was always going to happen this way. Like the cherry blossom shampoo that ate through the plastic and oozed, until she stepped into the great big puddle on the laundry room floor. They had a way of doing that—the Serious Things to Be Considered that never did get considered. 

She watched a swallow dive, beak snapping shut on an unsuspecting dragonfly. She felt stupid to have thought it was any sort of dance at all, instead of what it was—missed collisions and gnashing bone. It was over one in one crunch, and the swallow didn’t even land.

She didn’t know when exactly Rio had left, just that she had. She left Agatha shivering in the shadow of the very tall barn. She left the bonnet on the ground, and her socks in the sink.

 

VI. THURSDAY

 

On the heels of the most eventful day of her week—but not even in the top five of this year—Agatha took her first sick day in half a decade. Maybe it’d been caused by some sort of emotional whiplash, the speed and force at which she’d gone from peace and quiet to no-so-peace-and-quiet. More likely, Billy’s sniffling hadn’t been a result of the chill in the air.

She'd woken up with her head heavy, nose stuffed, and eyes watering. She’d made it all the way to the front door, until she’d sat down on the bench to put on her shoes. She’d quickly given up on the idea of getting back up. 

On the one hand, she’d been spared the torture of sharing a room with Rio. But on the other hand, she’d been denied the opportunity to share a room with Rio. Instead, she laid in bed with pieces of tissue stuffed up her nose, and finally relented to texting Wanda on the hour:

 

Agatha : Hey. Did she decide yet?

Wanda : Still no. 

 

Agatha : How about now? She usually takes her break now. 

Wanda : She’s eating a granola bar. 

 

Agatha : It’s almost recess, did you hear anything?

Wanda : If you need soup, I can bring it to you. Otherwise, please shut up.

 

“Selfish bitch,” Agatha muttered, and dropped her phone onto the nightstand. She stared at the ceiling for a minute, and then reached for it again. For the fourth time, she read her last text to Rio.

 

Agatha : Lesson plan’s in your inbox. Taking a dick day.

Agatha : SICK day.

 

Rio : of course you are. very classy move.

Agatha : Fuck you, I’m really sick. 

Then:

Agatha : I hope that frog chokes on a worm and dies in front of everyone.

And then, five minutes later:

Agatha : Tell Nicky. I don’t want him to worry. And tell him that Josh is picking him up. 

 

She dropped her phone on her nightstand again, and rubbed her hands over her face. She decided that maybe she wasn't sick, after all. Maybe she would sleep it off, and go in after lunch. She pinched her eyebrows to check if they hurt—the usual quick-test that would tell her if her stuffed-up nose and aching head were, in fact, due to illness. It was the same way she checked the weather even as she saw the rain from her window, to be absolutely certain she'd need to inconvenience herself with umbrella.

She pinched both sides at once. They did hurt, but maybe that was just because she was pinching them. She was never sure, because it was the only time she ever pinched herself in the face. She yawned, and her fingers were still resting against her forehead as she drifted into a fitful sleep.

 

 

The next thing she registered was the doorbell pulling her from some strange fever dream—something with elements of colonial history and strip clubs and Disneyland mascots. She blinked awake, and groaned against the rush of blood to her head as she pulled herself out of bed.

She opened her door to Josh.

“The fuck are you doing here?” she grumbled.

“You asked me to come get Nicky’s stuff.”

“Oh.” Agatha blinked. “Shit, it’s almost three? Give me a second.”

Agatha brought out Nicky’s bag, stuffing his shoes and jacket inside, and heard Josh shifting next to her.

“Hey, I do still want to apologize,” he said. “Properly.”

Agatha groaned again, and didn’t care that she sounded like a petulant child. The sound vibrated through her sore cheekbones.

“Do we have to do this now? Here? Ever?” 

“I would really like to,” he said.

“Fine.” Agatha turned on the tea kettle. “You have until the water’s boiling.”

“I shouldn’t have let McKenna say those things,” he said earnestly. “It was the worst thing I’ve ever done to you. I shouldn’t have brought her to Disney. I shouldn’t have even dated her, I mean Jesus.” He shook his head, and looked at the pictures of Nicky on the fridge. Agatha had taken down the ones with him in frame.

“I lost things, too,” he said. “You just handled it better than I did—you always do. And I’m sorry I couldn’t. I don’t want to be that kind of example for Nicky.”

“Where is this coming from?” Agatha said finally, unsure what else to say.

He smiled weakly. “Quit weed, started therapy. Trying to cook more. You know, all the stuff you’re supposed to do after…" He trailed off and gestured around them.

“Crab legs,” she muttered, and he had the wherewithal to blush.

“Crab legs,” he echoed. “Still don’t like ‘em.”

Agatha looked at him. She waited for the wave of annoyance and righteous indignation, but it didn’t come. 

“I will never like Honeycomb,” she said finally. He smiled. 

They stood there, looking at each other in the living room that used to be theirs. It felt strange—less like pressing hard on a bruise, and more like touching the ridge of an old scar, or slipping her fingers into a familiar groove of a tree trunk.

“As you’ve mentioned not a small amount of times,” he said after a beat, “I’m an insecure man who can’t help but offer his opinion on things that aren’t my business.”

Agatha pursed her lips. “And?”

“You know, for the first seven years we were together, you really put me through the ringer.”

“This again?”

“I got you a giant balloon on your birthday, and you popped it with your fingernails. I wrote you cards for every occasion, and you threw them away without reading them.”

Agatha blinked. With her transgressions listed out in this way, they felt remarkably more cruel than she’d remembered. She watched the steam trickle from the kettle, willing it to sound, and conceded she was not an exception to the rule of watched pots.

“Okay,” she said finally. “Maybe, possibly, that was a bit much.”

Josh hummed. “But you let Rio buy you cotton candy.”

Agatha turned to look at him in disbelief. 

“There is no fucking way you’re about to say what I think you’re about to say.” 

Josh held up his hands. “I could never get you to let down your guard in twenty years, and she did it in two fucking seconds! Someone has to say it, even if that someone is me, which is… ” He hesitated. “You don’t realize you’re doing it, do you?” 

“Doing what?” she mumbled. 

Josh laughed, rubbing his mouth. “This is so fucking weird. Look, when you’re around her, you’re just so you. Like… Agatha with a capital A.” 

The kettle whistled, and she jumped. Her heart pounded. 

“So, you woke up today and decided to wingman your ex-wife?” 

“No, definitely not.” He exhaled, puffing out his cheeks. “It’s more like… remember when Nicky would tell you when your sunscreen wasn’t rubbed in, and you’d rub the wrong spot and say ‘where?’ And he’d laugh so hard, until you did it one too many times?” 

“Oh, god, that’s right.” Agatha smiled a little. “He was so annoyed, he did that scrunchy-mad thing with his face.”

“Yeah,” Josh said. “It’s always a little annoying, when the bit goes on too long.” 

He handed her the top worksheet from Nicky’s pile. She looked at the the art lesson from earlier that week.

When I grow up, I want to be a ________.

Agatha squinted at the drawing of the strange creature, and the scrawl above it. 

“So? Our son wants to be a dinosaur,” she said, annoyed again. “I wouldn’t get hung up on it; his art teacher is basically in pull-ups. And my hot water is ready, which means you need to get to school.”

Josh left the paper on the countertop.

“I’ll drop him off when you’re feeling better,” he said. “And I don't know if you remember, but he still does his C’s as S’s.”

She wanted to yell, “I fucking know that,” after him out of habit, but the door was shut, and she was staring at the paper.

D-A-N-I-S-A-R

She laughed, incredulous, and looked at the creature with its claw-hands and crudely drawn smile. She felt the tears spring behind her sore eyes. He wanted to be a Dancer.

 

 

VII. FRIDAY

 

When Agatha’s doorbell rang that evening at eight on the dot, she figured it was Josh coming back to collect Nicky’s worksheet. She rolled off the couch, fever freshly broken, and braced a hand on the walls. She was still breathing a little hard at the laborious four-foot hike when she opened the door to Rio.

“Mo makes soup, too,” Rio said, and held up the bag in her hand.

Agatha was overwhelmed with the urge to walk right into her arms. She started to blame the sick, and the weakness in her limbs, but she gave up halfway. There was nothing left to blame for the way she wanted to kiss Rio on her front porch, except for the exact thing it was.

She thought about the feasibility of picking up the tissues on the floor, brushing her teeth and hair, and coming back to find Rio waiting patiently. And then Rio asked: “Can I come in?” and Agatha gave up on this plan, too, and let her inside.

 

 

With Rio sitting uncomfortably on the armchair in her living room, still wearing her coat, Agatha wondered if she herself looked as worn out—red-rimmed eyes, pale face, and something resolute in the line of her mouth. 

“Do you—” Agatha paused, and gestured to the stove. “I can make some tea, if you…”

“No, thanks.” Rio hesitated. “Josh told me you were really sick, I— Sorry. I thought you were just avoiding me.”

“Nope. Sick,” Agatha said, and on cue, broke into a violent coughing fit.

“I thought you ‘never got sick,’” Rio said, but she sounded more concerned than anything.

“Hard legacy to uphold in this line of work.”

Rio opened her mouth, and her fingers played with a button on her coat. 

“I have to say something,” she said finally. “And I want you to just listen until I’m done. Can you do that, please?”

Agatha balked at the word please, and felt her achy legs comply. She sat on the couch opposite and curled her feet underneath her. Rio took a deep breath.

“I never told you how I became a stripper,” she said. “And I’d like to, if you want to know.”

Agatha’s eyes widened. Then, with great effort, she shut her mouth, and nodded.

 

 

“After I got hurt, I was in recovery for two years,” Rio said. “I had two separate surgeries, which meant I was in the hospital in between. No insurance. Lilia paid for all of it.”

She looked at a spot on the rug, fingers still toying with her coat.

“On my last week of PT, I was leaving the building, and I saw an ad: ‘Dancers needed. $500 a night. Brunettes. 5’9 or taller.’ I needed a job to pay Lilia back, and I had no high school degree or actual skills. So I bought a pair of heels and went in the next day. I was twenty-one and so fucking terrified. I didn’t think I could be sexy—I barely even knew what sex was. I was literally an altar boy.”

“You were an altar boy?” The words burst out of Agatha’s mouth before she could stop them, and then she held up a hand. “Sorry.”

Rio smiled, just barely. “None of the boys in my church could hold the wine without spilling, so the pastor changed the rules. No more interrupting.”

Agatha bit back her smile, and filed away several follow up questions for later.

“It was so different than actual dance—I wasn’t prepared,” Rio continued. “I’d only ever had sex with two people—the cheerleader that got me kicked out of my house, and some random widow who came to get her fortune read from Lilia and had a weird thing for my bandaged knee.”

Agatha felt her chest swell at the innocence of it.

“In those early years, the guy in charge didn’t give a fuck about anything but profit. Security turned a blind eye to anything bad, and if someone paid enough, they could do whatever they wanted. It was—” She shuddered, and Agatha's grip tightened on the armrest. “It was terrible, for a little while. But those women saved me. Besides Lilia and her sisters, they were all I had. They taught me how to fight for myself. They became my family. So I need you to understand—you counted on me giving up my family. And I can’t do that again.”

Agatha nodded, the pieces of Rio clicking firmly into place. 

“But—” Rio’s voice wavered, her face fighting for control. “You were also right. Teaching is what I love to do. And you made me want to be the best.”

Agatha swallowed. Made. Past tense.

“I’m sorry for what I said.” Rio looked up tentatively. “When you told me I wasn’t getting fired, it meant that I had to decide this impossible thing, and I panicked. It felt like I was seventeen again, or nineteen and in a hospital bed, with everything I love about to be taken away, and having no choice about any of it.”

Agatha nodded. She didn’t know what else to do without speaking.

“I don’t blame you for this,” Rio continued. “Not at all. But I also need you to trust that I know what’s best for me.”

She stood.

No, Agatha wanted to say. Not yet.

“You can speak now,” Rio said quietly, fidgeting with her button again.

“I’m sorry,” Agatha said. She stood too quickly, her head aching from the sudden movement, and put a hand on the couch to steady herself. “I should have just let you take care of it, and I’m sorry. You were right. I just…” She trailed off, her nose starting to run, and she reached for a tissue. 

“I need you,” she said through her tissue, and Rio’s eyes widened.

“To stay in the classroom,” Agatha added. “I know that’s not fair, and I’m sorry. But Nicky—”

She thought of the drawing of Dancer, and her eyes stung.

“Nicky loves you, Rio. I don’t want him to lose someone that he loves, either. I don’t—” She stopped, the lump in her throat threatening to spill.

Rio’s face softened. “I worked something out with Wanda,” she said. “I can finish out the year.”

Agatha’s knees nearly buckled in relief, and then she paused.

“Does that mean you’re—”

“I’m not quitting the club, either.” 

Agatha felt the confusion on her face, and Rio looked at her, gaze steady as Agatha had ever seen it.

“I’m going to help the dancers unionize. They taught me how to fight for myself, and I’d like to return the favor.”

A smile pulled at the corner of her mouth.

“You actually gave me the idea,” she added. “When you were shit-talking Hank.”

“Rio,” Agatha breathed. “That’s amazing. That’s an amazing idea. You’re amazing, you’re… amazing.”

She couldn’t think of another word except amazing. She couldn’t think of anything, just stepped forward and cupped Rio’s face. “I—” She faltered, and one hand slipped down Rio’s sleeve to her fingers. 

“Look, about the things you said.” Agatha's voice felt shaky with the unfamiliarity of sincerity. “On Wednesday. About me, and how I…how I feel.” 

“You don’t have to—”

“I want to,” Agatha said, pulling Rio closer by the lapels of her coat. She could smell it again, that boring, plain soap. 

“I’m—I’m trying, okay? I’m terrible at this, at all of this, and you’re not—” 

She leaned closer, and shut her eyes again, because saying this was too hard, but leaning into Rio never seemed to be. She brought her hand to Rio’s cheek, thumb tracing the familiar curve of her cheekbone, and brushed their noses together. She liked that this was familiar. She breathed against the bow of Rio’s upper lip, in and out. She felt the slow, gentle sway of their bodies. She didn’t kiss her. It was enough to just breathe and sway, and know that her neck smelled like hotel soap.

“You’re not an inconvenience, Rio.” 

She whispered it with her eyes closed, faces tilted together, and tightened her fingers on Rio’s jaw. “You’re not, you’re whatever’s the opposite of that. You make me—” 

She let out a breath, and then another, and it felt like the start of a laugh or a sob. 

“You make me capital-A-Agatha.”

She felt Rio exhale, the breath warm against her lips. 

Rio stepped back so suddenly, Agatha’s hands were still outstretched.

She could still feel her own body swaying, a little.

“I’m—” Rio paused, and wrapped her arms around herself, like she was cold. Agatha wondered why she’d stepped back, then. Why she wanted her own hands instead of Agatha’s.

“I’m taking the California job,” Rio said. “It starts in June.”

Agatha’s knees locked. She stopped swaying.

“June?” she heard herself say.

“They want me to run this great summer program, and meet the community before the school year starts, you know? ” 

She paused, tugging hard on her button. It was coming loose from the coat now, thread hanging from the side.

“It’s a great program,” she repeated. The way she said it, Agatha thought she might be reminding herself.

She felt her head rush, her knees buckling again, and this time she almost sat.

“Agatha?” Rio was saying, a hand on her shoulder. “Are you okay?”

Agatha nodded, head spinning, and forced a smile. It felt grotesque, the shape of it stretching across her face. “I’m happy for you,” she said. 

Rio was looking at Agatha the way she had on Sunday morning, like she wanted to hold her, and Agatha couldn’t have that. Not now, not anymore.

“I didn’t get a lot of sleep last night,” Agatha said mechanically, and swept her hair over her shoulder. “Can we talk more about it on Monday?”

Rio just looked at her for a second, hand still on her shoulder. Agatha shrugged it off as casually as she could manage. Rio looked gutted at the motion.

Good, Agatha thought. It was childish and she knew it, and she still thought it: See how it feels to be me.

“Sorry, yeah. Of course.” Rio saw herself to the door. “Get some rest, Agatha. And I’m… I’m sorry.”

“Don’t be.” Agatha waved her hand. “Can you do me a favor?” 

“Anything.”

“Take the frog home. I don’t want him, and he’s going to die an untimely death in our classroom.”

“I took him home yesterday," Rio said with a small smile. "I’m headed up to the farm tonight.”

Agatha tried not to think about the colorful house, and hot chocolate, and pine trees. 

“I’m glad I’m staying the rest of the year,” Rio said quietly. “I love Nicky, too. You know that, right?”

Agatha pressed her teeth together and nodded, once. She looked down at the soup container in its plastic bag, still waiting on the entryway bench. Agatha felt the familiar twist in her stomach at the sight of something meant just for her: the pink on Rio’s cheeks in her kitchen, or an exciting piece of mail, or an offering of small animal bones. And she only recognized now, under the scrutiny of Rio’s dark, searching eyes, that the feeling was wonder.

It was a wonder—that this extraordinary, inconvenient creature had almost been hers. 

 

 

This time, when Rio left, Agatha didn’t watch from the window. Her legs gave out and the sobs came quickly, pulling strange noises from her chest. This was a different kind of grief—the end of the picture book and the moral of the story, that people would sooner leave her than love her. She cried until her eyes were bleary and her head pounded. Curled on the carpet over a bed of tissues, back against the sofa, she thought only of Rio on the farmhouse rug.

Her neck was slick under her hair, and she thought maybe her hackles were gone altogether, like the smooth skin of a tree frog. She turned on her back, looked at the ceiling, and thought about Sandwich. She wondered, wet-faced and aching, which posed more danger to the natural order of things: a wild dog that looked bigger than he was, or a carnivorous runaway frog.

 

 

One hour after Rio left, Agatha stood up from her floor. She breathed harshly through her mouth, her nose red and rubbed raw. She wiped her sleeve across her face, and stuffed her feet in her slippers. At nine-thirty on the dot, she got into her car, the bag of soup buckled into the passenger seat.

 

 

She didn’t bang on the door. She almost couldn’t manage to lift her arm at all, so the knocks were quiet and few. This time, when the door opened, Agatha was overcome with an unfamiliar sensation of anguish tangled with relief. For some reason unknown to her, her eyes filled with tears once more.

Lilia stood in her doorway, and took in Agatha’s appearance.

“She’s not here,” she said. “But once again, I think you know that already.”

“Yes,” Agatha said, and swiped at her eyes. “I’m here for you. Can I come in?”

“Oh, that’s it!” Lilia clasped her hands together. “This must be why I brewed a full pot this evening.”

Agatha sagged against the door frame and sniffled.

“Tea sounds great." Her voice was hoarse. She’d left her glasses in her car, and the hallway fluorescence swam in front of her eyes. Lilia raised her eyebrows.

“Oh, my. You really are here for me, aren’t you?” 

She took Agatha’s hand, and tugged softly. “Come, darling. I have a fresh deck I’ve been saving for you.”

And Agatha let herself be pulled into the apartment of a clairvoyant witch. A madwoman with miracle tea, who opened her door to misfits at midnight, and invited them in.
