Quarter Four (and Three Quarters)

Chapter Summary

tw: mention of abuse

Chapter Notes

ok this is mostly not how i had planned this chapter originally, but oh boy do i love where it went! @burtletass on twt if you're into that, a certain link of interest in my bio if you're into that, and don't forget to hydrate whether or not you're into that 😙

enjoy!

See the end of the chapter for more notes

 

 

 

“She is the witch you burned / by daylight and crept from your home

to consult & bribe at night. The love / that tortured you you blamed on her.” 

- Margaret Atwood



 

Of all the names Agatha had been called before her tenth birthday, the one she liked the most was goddamn motherfuckin’ liar. There was something about the rhythm of it—much more percussive than good for nothin’, but more thrilling than ungrateful, like fireworks exploding out of her mother’s mouth. It was, of course, Evanora who called her a liar, when Agatha’s face was still freckled and her hair still lay in bright red tangles down her back. She hadn’t yet learned how to brush it properly, or to care whether it was brushed, and every so often Evanora would say, “Your head looks like a goddamn rat’s nest on fire. No child of mine’ll be seen walking around like that.” Agatha would put up a half-hearted struggle, squirming under the rake of the brush, and feel secretly thrilled that her mother had called her mine.

In the first week of July, when she was nine, Agatha would wake up and walk straight to the harbor. She’d sneak past the fence when it was just starting to get light. Sometimes she’d pause to look at the line of mothers down the street, waiting at the mailboxes for the welfare checks. Evanora would have a bony hand on their mailbox, too, hair in a wrap and gaze pinched toward the other women. When Agatha had asked why she couldn’t just get the mail later, she’d said, "That government mail’s got my fuckin’ name on it. That money is mine.” 

Agatha would amble past the new apartment complexes where she’d see men in suits and women in heels. She’d walk barefoot along the harbor beach, heeding Val’s warnings about needles in the sand, though she’d never seen one. She kept her eyes open for dead things, too, because Val had gifted her a dead bird, and she wanted to find an even more grotesque thing to present in return.

Agatha would walk all the way to Carson Beach, running to the water and back, until she fell, exhausted, onto the park lawn. She’d stay for the summer movie specials, bug bites and sunburn and salt water blending together into one big itch. She’d scratch at her legs in the grass, watching mothers and fathers holding their children, and think how strange it was that they were all there together. Who was in their driveways, keeping a hand on the mailbox?

One particular Friday, she’d just finished the Karate Kid double feature, and the excitement of it had followed her all the way home. She made-believe that she was Johnny Lawrence for close to an hour, until she chopped the side-table tiffany lamp, imagining it was Daniel LaRusso’s head. Broken out of her fantasy by the sound of it shattering, she looked at the kaleidoscope pieces on the floor. Johnny Lawrence had broken the lamp, but Johnny Lawrence was no more. Agatha was Agatha again, and she felt the triphammer of her little-girl heart.

When Evanora came home, her eyes unfocused and hands twitchy, she’d bellowed: “Agatha Harkness, did you break my motherfuckin’ good lamp?”

Agatha, already hiding in the bottom section of the hall closet, yelled back: “Of course I fuckin’ didn’t! You probably did and forgot about it!”

Evanora yanked open the closet door and crouched to her level. Agatha wrinkled her nose at the sour, burnt smell of her breath. 

“You are a goddamn motherfuckin’ liar,” her mother said. 

Agatha felt the thrill at the familiar lilting phrase. By age nine, she knew what would happen next. For example, when Evanora called her ungrateful, Agatha knew that the back of her hand would follow. But when she was a goddamn motherfuckin’ liar, she’d be set free to the tune of “Get outta my sight.” 

She held her breath and waited, staring at her mother from the bottom of the closet.

“You are a shit liar,” Evanora slurred. “But if you’re smart enough to lie, you’re smart enough to go find me a new lamp before the streetlights come on. Get outta my sight.”

So Agatha scrambled out the door, resolved to be a better goddamn motherfuckin’ liar next time, and wandered the streets until she was sure her mother had fallen asleep. Then she crawled up the fire escape, and dug out her World’s Strongest Glue. She’d been saving it, because it was another gift from Val, and gifts were supposed to be saved for someday. But she didn’t want to turn from liar to ungrateful, so she sat cross-legged on the living room floor, uncapped her gift, and put the colorful lamp back together. 

 



Agatha blinked up at a ceiling that wasn’t hers, and wondered if she’d always been a liar, or if one day, she’d just woken up as one. As she’d grown past nine, her hair had darkened to rust, then chestnut, and her mother had stopped calling her a liar. She’d stopped waiting by the mailbox, or brushing Agatha’s hair, or calling her mine. By the time Agatha was forty-three and lying in a bed that wasn’t hers, she was disappointed to realize that now, she was mostly just called Agatha.

She grabbed blindly for her underwear, and tried to remember why she was still wearing her shirt. She groaned against the pounding in her skull, and closed her eyes to flashes of a mahogany bar. She remembered Wanda laughing with her body tilted sideways out of her chair and Alice on a stool. She definitely remembered Jen ordering a round of shots “to education!”

Agatha turned to her side and looked at the lump under the sheet. She hesitated, and then pulled it down. Rio was asleep, her mouth open and brow furrowed. Agatha smiled, then caught herself, hands flying to her face.

“Fuck me,” she muttered against her fingers. 

“Mmmyeah,” Rio mumbled, and the mattress dipped as she rolled toward Agatha.

“If you were trying to murder me, you wouldn’t have eaten me out then bought me a burger.” Rio looked at the trees towering above them.

“What if that was my thing? Like, my serial killer calling card?”

“Then I would want more for you, professionally.”

Agatha smiled, and nudged her shoulder after a beat. “You’re really not going to ask why I brought you here?” 

“You usually tell me anyway if I’m quiet long enough.”

Agatha scoffed. “You’re secretly a little mean, aren’t you?”

“And you’re secretly sentimental.”

“Take that back!”

Rio gestured to the headstone in front of them.

“Fine,” Agatha said. “But I am not sentimental about her.”

Rio’s face softened, and she took Agatha’s hand, prying her fingers open so she could lace hers through.

“That much, I know.” She looked at the headstone again, and Agatha smirked at the familiar engraving.

 

EVANORA HARKNESS

 ____ - ____ 

INSCRIPTION HERE

 

“I never emailed them back,” Agatha shrugged.

“I like it.” Rio considered the text. “A permanent ‘Fuck you.’”

“It wasn’t on purpose,” Agatha said. “Josh was freaked out because I didn’t have any sort of reaction when she died. He thought I was on the verge of a breakdown, so he did all the things you’re supposed to do: a funeral that no one came to, a will reading for a will with nothing in it, and a headstone in a cemetery in Saugus, where she was born.”

“That was nice of him.” Rio squeezed her hand.

“It was,” Agatha said. “But I never did have that mental breakdown.”

“It’s not mandatory, you know.” 

Agatha rocked on her tiptoes, then kicked the bottom of the headstone with one foot. 

“I lied,” she said, still looking at the stone. “Last week. I do hate talking about my mother. I do feel like I’m going to crumble at even the slightest goddamn inkling of Evanora Harkness, even thirteen years later. And since I met you, I’ve been thinking about her a lot.”

Rio eyed her, and Agatha saw the crinkle in her nose that meant she was confused. But she didn't say anything, so Agatha closed her eyes, and told her anyway. She said what she’d never spoken aloud to anyone—not Jen, not Wanda, not even Josh. She stood over Evanora’s grave, took a deep breath, and said: “My mother told me every day that I was the worst thing that ever happened to her.”

“Agatha—” Rio squeezed her hand again, and Agatha shook her head. 

“Let me finish, okay? Just let me get it out.”

Rio nodded. Agatha took another breath, steadier this time, and looked at the name, EVANORA HARKNESS, in those loud, unforgiving capital letters.

“She told me that I ruined her life. That’s what she’d say, when she was drunk or high, or just mad for no reason. She’d pull me close, like she was going to tell me a story, and say: ‘Agatha, did you know that when you were born, you ruined my fuckin’ life?’ She’d say, ‘I was just about to be somebody, and you took it all away. Poof!’ And then she hated me for it until the day she died.”

Agatha paused, staring at the inscription, feeling the burn of tears in her throat. She turned to Rio, and found her eyes already wet.

“I don’t want you to think that about me,” Agatha said, her voice wavering. “I can’t ask you to stay here for me, Rio. Because I just don't think—I can't risk it. Ruining your life, too.”

Rio didn’t say anything, and Agatha squeezed her hand tightly, the tears coming fast again, until finally, Rio pulled her into her arms. 

She let out a half-sigh and half-sob into Rio’s hair, and they swayed there together, standing at Evanora’s grave in the dark. Agatha didn’t know how long they stayed like that. 

“I’m so sorry, Rio,” Agatha said finally. “I really am.”

Rio wiped her own cheeks and gave a watery smile. “If your mother was alive, she’d have fewer teeth, and my handprint across her face.”

Agatha snorted, and held her tighter.

“And I want to tell you that you could never ruin my life,” Rio added softly. “But I guess neither of us really know that for sure. Maybe one day, it won’t be so scary for you.”

“God, but what if I’m miserable and scared forever?”

“Close your eyes,” Rio said, and Agatha did. “It’ll go like this. One day, you’ll wake up, and you’ll make your coffee. You’ll put on your purple robe, and bring in the mail, and wake up Nicky, and you’ll realize that you’ve gone all morning without thinking about all the worst things that could happen. Or all the things you’re scared of. And it’ll happen more and more, until it starts to feel a little exciting—all the things you don’t know, that are going to happen next.”

Agatha kissed her with her eyes still closed. She didn’t say the obvious—that she couldn’t yet say what Rio needed to hear, and Rio couldn’t stay for less. It was simple and inevitable, like two vines of Bittersweet nightshade that twisted together along a fence before growing their separate ways.

Agatha just kissed her, lips pulling softly until Rio hummed, and Agatha brushed her tongue against hers in their familiar way. She felt Rio’s breath against her upper lip, and felt overcome with a sharp, needy pull to do this more, and again, and all the time. She kissed her harder, nails digging into the back of her neck.

“Okay, um,” Rio said, breathing hard against her mouth. “Do you have a thing for this? Like, cemetery stuff? Not that I have an issue with it; you just seem a little… wound up.”

“Not all of us had the pleasure of an orgasm served up on a silver platter this evening,” Agatha said, biting at Rio’s lip, and Rio laughed. 

“But,” Agatha added, “I do like the fact that my mother would hate me desecrating her grave with a gay makeout.”

“You’re gay?” Rio said, deadpan, and Agatha shoved her. Rio dissolved into laughter, and Agatha couldn’t help but laugh with her, pushing her in the direction of the car.

“Oh, wait, hold on.” Rio jogged around the tree, and Agatha frowned as she returned with a sprig of small, white flowers. “Saw these on our way up.”

Rio laid the flowers down on Evanora’s grave, and Agatha shot her a look.

“Relax,” Rio said, taking her hand. “It’s poison hemlock.”

Agatha’s mouth dropped in surprise, and then she couldn’t do anything but kiss Rio again. 

“You are so hot right now,” Agatha said, grabbing Rio’s ass. “Now, be a good boy and finger me in the parking lot of this very creepy graveyard.”

“Yeah, yes. Let’s do that. Now.” Rio tugged her forward. They stumbled to the car, Rio tripping and laughing against Agatha’s mouth. They lost the gravel path and backtracked, bumping their foreheads together in the dark like children in a playground collision. Rio kissed the sore spot and Agatha laughed, and as Rio pressed her into the backseat, Agatha thought that maybe all of this would be a little less scary tomorrow.

 

 

Agatha woke to the sound of pounding on her front door in between the ringing doorbell. She’d been dreaming, the kind where the sounds crept in. She and Rio had been playing in a band, the pounding taking the shape of a bass drum. Rio’s arms were wrapped tightly around her midsection, and Agatha, half-asleep and wanting nothing more than to get back to the band’s performance, buried her head in Rio’s neck to muffle the noise.

When the doorbell rang for the sixth time, she remembered all the things she was supposed to think of when she was unexpectedly awakened—was Nicky okay, and where was her phone, and was she late for school? She pulled on her robe and walked quickly to the door. Standing on her porch, face pale and eyes wide, was Wanda.

“Thank fucking god, open the door,” she hissed, looking around.

“What? What is it, what happened?” Agatha’s heart raced as she undid the chain. “Are the twins okay?”

Wanda squeezed past Agatha and shut the door behind them, holding a hand to her forehead. Agatha felt awareness settle into her limbs as she registered the quiet panic on her face. 

“Hey—” Agatha grabbed her shoulder to keep her in place. “Is everyone okay?” 

“Yes, I’m sorry. Everyone’s fine. Agatha, Hank called me this morning.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes. “Okay, what’s the deal with Hank? Why have you two been ‘discussing administrative matters?’ Is this about my job?”

“No, not exactly,” Wanda said, shutting her eyes for a second. She rested a hand on her head, taking a deep breath. “I’ve been trying to convince him to retire. I’ve been meeting with him since March, and I’ve thrown him every offer I can think of. I thought if he was gone, then Scott could move up to fifth grade like he’s wanted, and then fourth grade would be open if—”

Wanda looked over Agatha’s shoulder, and Agatha remembered too late that she’d forgotten to shut the bedroom door. Rio stood in the entrance to the kitchen, and Agatha’s breath caught. She wore Agatha’s flannel, the fabric hanging over her bare thighs, hair egregiously messy. She looked like she’d been thoroughly and completely fucked—because she had—but Agatha didn’t remember being quite so reckless. In fact, she realized as she looked at her that there would be no way around the hickeys smattered across her neck and bruises on her thighs. 

“Uh… hey,” Rio said finally, her voice hoarse. 

“Hi.” Wanda took in her appearance, and looked pointedly at Agatha. She looked a little like she was about to laugh, or cry—Agatha wasn’t sure which. “Sorry to interrupt.”

“Apology not accepted,” Agatha grumbled, her cheeks feeling warm. 

“Okay, well, you should hear this part, too, Rio.” Wanda took another deep breath, and looked between the both of them. “Hank called me this morning to tell me that he saw you two idiots. Together.”

“What? Where, in the classroom?” Agatha demanded, and felt Rio sidle up next to her. She smelled like Rio, warm and sleepy, and Agatha wanted to bury her face in her neck again despite the urgency of the situation.

“No, at the happy hour in April.” Wanda paused. “Wait, what happened in the classroom?”

“Nothing,” Agatha and Rio replied at once.

“Great. Fantastic. You guys couldn’t keep it in your pants for, like, thirty more seconds? Who makes out behind an Applebee's?”

“What’s he going to do?” Rio asked.

Agatha half-laughed. “He wants me to quit, so his waspy wife can take my job.”

“That’s not happening,” Wanda said firmly.

“Wanda, I think we’re officially out of options here.”

“No, we are not.” Wanda ran her hand through her hair, and Agatha realized she was still in her pajamas. Her heart swelled with sudden affection for her friend. Wanda had been trying to keep Rio here. 

“Look, if he called me, that means he hasn’t called Ralph yet,” Wanda muttered, pacing. “He probably got tired of waiting for you to take the bait, and decided to escalate, now that we only have a week left.”

“The teacher feature,” Rio exclaimed, clutching Agatha’s arm. 

Agatha and Wanda looked at her blankly, and Rio rolled her eyes. 

“Oh my god, have you two ever read a single email? Here.” She held her hand out for Wanda’s phone, and Wanda complied, peering over her shoulder. 

“Hank is the awardee this year.” Rio showed them the teaser. “It’s going to detail out his career accomplishments and the district is going to present him with the award.”

“That’s why he can’t tell Ralph,” Wanda said, realization dawning. “It would overshadow his pathetic five minutes of fame.”

“The article comes out on the last day of school,” Rio read, and rubbed the bridge of her nose in thought.

Agatha resisted the urge to sit down on the floor and curl into a ball. “You know I want to keep my job, but how are we going to pull this off in one week?”

Wanda rounded the kitchen, brows still knitted together. “I might have a plan,” she said, and reached for the coffee grounds. Agatha squeezed her eyes shut as Wanda paced by the coffee maker. 

“Fuck,” she said, lowering her voice so only Rio could hear. “FuckFuckFuck.”

She gripped the edge of the barstool and willed it to be ten minutes ago, curled into Rio and dreaming about their make-believe band. This was much less enjoyable.

“Yeah,” Rio said.

“You can go, you know.” Agatha looked at her and shook her head. “You could just go home right now. You’re so close to getting out of here. You’ll be fine.”

They glanced at Wanda puttering around the kitchen, her fingers drumming against the counter, eyes focused at a spot on the wall.

“I know that look,” Agatha whispered. “She’s like a shark on the scent of blood. A non-threatening shark, maybe, like a baby one, but still. Whatever she’s got, it’s going to be fucking insane.”

Rio’s mouth twitched, and she stepped closer. She reached up and rubbed the corner of Agatha’s left eye.

“You have sleep crusties,” she murmured, and Agatha smiled. 

“I’m in,” Rio added. “Let’s take care of this thing for good. I don’t want any loose ends lying around on my account.”

“Great,” Wanda called, three cups of coffee in front of her. “Now that that’s settled, sit down. You two need to tell me everything, and then we’ll figure out how to put a stupid old man in his place and save both of your careers.”

“Oh, is that it?” Agatha said, but she sat anyway. She held Rio’s hand under the counter and squeezed. Then she took her first sip of coffee, looked reluctantly at Wanda, and began.

 

 

 

 

Chapter End Notes

😀

Last Day of School II

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes



 

“There are some who can live without wild things, and some who cannot.”

― Delia Owens, Where the Crawdads Sing




 

With the evening arrival of June beetles and bloomings of false indigo, the nightmares came crawling, too. They crept into the households of those who’d known warm-weather torments, and overstayed their welcome. Agatha felt them return each summer and imagined them like nighttime moths in migration, following her scent on the first harbor breeze to her bedroom window.

She would dream of running. Down the hallway of her childhood house, or through the corridors of some school or other. She hid under her bed, under desks, and in bathroom stalls with her arms over her face. She’d call out for her mother, or sometimes even Val. No one would come, and eventually, having twisted the sheets into a sort of straightjacket, she’d struggle enough to wake herself.

She’d seen a June beetle last night. She’d seen false indigo that morning, bright purple against her neighbor’s fence. This is how she knew, in the middle of the night when Nicky woke her, that she must have been dreaming of running.

 

 

"What was it about?" Agatha mumbled, her eyes closing again.

"There were these giant monsters," Nicky said, "and they were chasing us, and they looked like dogs, but meaner and scarier. And we were on the playground, but it was night."

"Mmm." 

Her hand loosened around his ankle. She’d finally found somewhere dark and small to hide. 

"I can't go back to sleep, Mama."

"You can sleep here. Close your eyes really tight, until the monsters are gone, and then… "

She wasn’t sure she finished the thought. Just as sleep curled its arms around her, she felt her shoulder shake under pinching hands. 

"Mama. I need a nighttime drink."

The warm, thrilling dark slipped away, fading to the familiar walls of her bedroom. She’d learned long ago that some arms were more demanding than sleep’s.

"I'm up."

"You sound funny," Nicky said, bounding to the kitchen.

Agatha pulled on a sweatshirt and paused, breathing deep. She looked down at the lichen green and wondered when Rio had left it. She wondered, too, if she should take it off, because the thought of slipping it back over her head felt like an insurmountable loss. The sleeves wrapped around her, and she felt she might cry about it, right here in the doorway. The sweatshirt, and Rio. Always, somehow, about Rio.  She supposed that things were usually a little scarier in the middle of the night that way.

 



"Who's Rio?" Nicky asked, rubbing away his chocolate milk mustache with the back of his hand. Agatha stared, suddenly wide awake. 

"Rio is Ms. V.'s first name."

“Oh, yeah.”

Nicky swung his legs in his chair. Agatha had set them at the dining table, afraid they'd both fall sideways off the kitchen stools in their current state. 

"Was she in your dream?" Agatha asked finally, curiosity winning out. 

Nicky put down his glass with both hands. "No, not today.” 

“Then why’d you ask about her?” 

“You said 'Rio.’ When you were asleep. Was she in your dream?"

Agatha’s neck grew hot under the sweatshirt collar. She couldn’t quite remember, but knew the answer all the same. She cleared her throat.

"Sometimes we dream about people or things that we see a lot. And we see Ms. V every day.”

"But I don't see any monster dogs. Why did I dream about them?"

Agatha wondered, not for the first time, if she’d passed down some sort of rare disease that caused early-June nightmares. 

"I don’t know.”

"Do you have bad dreams, too?"

She yawned, and rested her head in her hand. “When I was a kid, I used to get in a lot of fights with other girls at school, and I’d dream that they were chasing after me."

“Did they yell at you?” Nicky asked, wide-eyed.

Agatha remembered the slam of her back into a locker and the wet, metallic taste on her tongue.

“Yes,” she said. 

Nicky stared at her, lost in thought, the way he got whenever he considered small Agatha.

"And then you would go to your mama’s bed, and she would make you a nighttime drink?"

Agatha took a deep breath through her nose and beckoned him into her lap.

"No, baby. My mama didn't do that.”

“Why?"

She kissed the top of his head, and breathed him in. As he grew heavy against her, she explained that some Mamas rocked their babies, and some didn’t. Maybe it was because her mama never knew her mama, or maybe she just didn’t know what to do with herself when Agatha cried out with her arms outstretched, mumbling of ghosts and mean girls who came for her in the night. Or maybe, when the sun set over their rickety apartment and the muffled shouts faded, the nightmares came for Evanora worst of all.

Agatha rocked Nicky, and felt his breath even out. She whispered, "You tell those monster dogs that your mama is the biggest, scariest one of all. And she will protect you. Okay?"

He hummed, his head limp against her shoulder, and she carried her baby back to bed.

 

 

Agatha knew, as she got into her car the next morning, that she had more to blame for her nightmare than the changing of the season. If she had to guess, it would be the phone call she’d received from the Boston police last night, informing her that a man of the name “Wilburt Moore” had been murdered in the apartment she grew up in. When she’d explained she hadn’t lived there in decades, and knew no one by that name, the cop on the phone had said: “Can you explain why he had a hat box with your report card in it?”

Besides the fact that Agatha didn’t remember ever bringing home a report card, she’d only ever seen one hat box, in the top of her mother’s hall closet. 

“What was his mother’s name?” Agatha had asked. She’d heard the rustle of papers, and thought, Of course the Boston police department still uses papers. The cop had replied: “Norma Moore. You knew her?”

Agatha thought about saying, “My mother stole her cable.” But instead said, “Not really.”

Not really was apparently reason enough to make her come down in person, and murder was reason enough that she needed to miss a day of school (she’d asked the cop, just to be sure, and he hadn’t seemed to appreciate the question). That Willie’s murder—and what would follow—happened around the changing of the season was simply a coincidence. On the Tuesday before the last day of school, Agatha drove to Boston in a lichen-green sweatshirt, and felt there was no harm in placing a little blame on the flowers and bugs. 






Next-door Norma’s son, Willie, had been killed in a drug deal gone wrong. The policeman explained this to Agatha at the kitchen table in dead-Willie’s apartment, which used to be hers. He explained it as if it were a horrible thing that would either reduce Agatha to a stuttering mess, or mark her as a suspect—witch-trial logic, her mother had called it. Agatha hadn’t killed Willie. And of course, his death was a bad thing. But even though Agatha was scared of many bad, horrible things, murder was not one of them. 

She vaguely heard the cop explaining how Willie had expanded into the unit after her mother had died, and rented it out. Somewhere in the back of her mind, she wondered if it was normal for drug dealers to host on Airbnb, or if his business hadn’t been going particularly well. She didn’t ask. She was too caught up staring at a dark spot on the hallway floor, and wondering if that’s where Willie had died, or if had been leftover from her mother.

She’d seen dead people before—when she was young, it wasn’t uncommon to come across someone who’d overdosed on the street, or to catch a flash of a black bag being deposited into an ambulance. Sometimes the neighborhood boys would shove each other for a peek at a foot, but Agatha always made it to the front. She’d see the same strange bruises each time, and the cousins and mothers of those bruised feet would tearfully announce to the onlookers that their grown-up children had died of chicken pox or heart attacks. Agatha had thought purple spots were signs of a heart attack until she was fourteen, when the boy at the corner store (the one she’d kissed for a free pack of cigarettes) called her stupid and told her about AIDS.

She’d walk to school the same way until she forgot anyone had ever died there, and it became just another stoop or curb. She’d thought nothing of it. But now, staring at the single dark stain on the floor, her stomach rolled. She suddenly wished she’d told Rio where she was going.

“How did he die?” Agatha said, still staring at the floor. She’d cut the policeman off as he was saying something, she wasn’t sure what.

“He– He was murdered, Ma’am.”

“Yes, I heard,” Agatha said dryly. “I mean—how?”

The cop—his name was Doyle, she was almost sure—blinked at her. “Four gunshots to the chest, then stabbed.”

Agatha stood up. “Seems redundant. Can I see the box now?”

Doyle cleared his throat and nodded toward the hallway. “I’ll give you a minute. It’s in the room at the end, last on the left. Can’t miss it.”

Agatha laughed. She couldn’t help it.

She’d felt relieved, walking in the front door, that almost everything about the place was different. It smelled like fresh paint and instant coffee. She could still recognize the shape of it, the bones of the living room where she’d broken the lamp, or hidden behind the couch. But the lamp was gone, and the faded, floral couch had been replaced with dark gray.

Her bedroom, like the rest of the apartment, was unrecognizable. Willie had converted it into a guest room, with light pink walls and a sign that said “Home is where the dog is!”—an odd choice, as she hadn’t seen any evidence of a dog. She edged around the bed to peek out the window, and where she expected to see the familiar brick pattern of the next apartment, she saw instead the green ‘FOO’ of a Whole Foods. 

She sat back on the bed, next to the hat box, and looked at it. For the first time, she wondered why Willie hadn’t thrown it away with the rest of the rotted things. On the front of it, in faded, cursive letters, she read: Property of Evanora Harkness.

Doyle hadn’t told her the box had her mother’s name on it, and she understood, as she’d already guessed, that she was a suspect in the murder of this man she barely knew. It didn’t bother her much, because she was familiar with the operating principles of the cops in this area, the main ones being see and do, with no room for think in between. She’d seen the police make themselves welcome in this apartment building since before she was old enough to run away from them. They wouldn’t look twice at a white woman from the suburbs in a clean, green sweatshirt. 

For a minute, she wished they did suspect her—she almost wished she had murdered Willie. She tried to think about how it would feel to shoot him four times in the chest and then stab him, but didn’t uncover anything exciting at the thought of it. If this were a podcast, she’d have already skipped past this part.

“You don’t seem all that shaken up,” Doyle said, and Agatha looked up to see him leaning against the doorway. 

“Someone used to die here once a month,” Agatha said, pulling the hat box onto her lap. “The only reason you’re here at all is because Willie was whiter than the plates in his cupboard. But even for a white guy, he’s Southie trash. Late fifties, single with no relatives. The suits gave you their sloppy seconds, didn’t they?”

The cop shifted uneasily. “No, that’s not—we are taking Mr. Moore’s murder very seriously, as we do every murder.”

“Of course.” Agatha looked at him plainly. “I’m guessing you want to watch me open this?”

Doyle still seemed put off at her blunt assessment, so she took the lid off the box unceremoniously. 

She felt him lean closer, and looked down at the contents herself, keeping her face even.

She picked out the yellow report card first. She’d written her own name at the top in shaky letters, and she let her finger brush the peeling sticker in the category marked “Participation.” None of the other categories had stickers. Red pen overflowed into the margins, and she read the careful documentation of her unwillingness to stay seated, habit of interruption, and general dislike of her peers. She didn’t remember the report card, but holding it now, she knew she’d brought it home to show her mother proudly, red pen and all.

For the first time since entering the building, she felt her throat tighten. She stuffed it back in the box, picking at various other odds and ends—parking tickets, the address of a hair salon, and finally, a small black notebook. She thumbed through it, taking in the strange names and phone numbers. 

“Address book?” Doyle said casually, and Agatha knew immediately that he’d already been through it.

“Looks like it,” she said flatly.

“Recognize any of the names?”

“Maybe some.” Agatha flipped through again, and then dropped it on the bed beside her. She put on her best grieving daughter voice, and said, “My mother was a troubled woman, who suffered from early onset dementia. We were estranged.”

The words felt egregiously false as she said them, even though the dementia could have been real, for all Agatha knew—pulmonary embolisms didn’t reveal much in the way of mental health—but there was nothing more unlikely than Evanora Harkness keeping an elementary report card in a bout of sentimentality.

“My grandmother had dementia,” Doyle said, suddenly wistful. “Terrible disease. I’m sorry for your loss.”

Agatha waved at him, wiping a nonexistent tear. She looked at the clock, and knew it would be lunchtime at school. Rio would be on yard duty to cover her shift. Agatha wondered how much longer she’d have to sit here. 

“Is there any reason you can think of that Mr. Moore would keep this box?” Doyle said, and Agatha could tell he felt bad for making her come here, now. “Were they close? Did they ever speak?”

Agatha remembered a handful of times Evanora had threatened to bash his head in with a baseball bat, and thought it would make a much more interesting podcast episode if Willie had been murdered by the ghost of her mother.

“You know,” Agatha said, with faux interest, “I always suspected he had a crush on her. The way he would look at her, well, you know—some people have a thing for older women.”

She said it just so she could tell Rio about it later, and almost grinned imagining it. Then her stomach twisted as she remembered, like she did every few hours now, that Rio was leaving in a week. She wondered what Doyle would do if she lay down on the double bed and hugged her knees to her chest. Suddenly, this whole trip felt utterly meaningless, like an errant stabbing after she’d already been shot and left for dead.

Doyle jotted her lie in his notebook and looked just as deflated—a decades-old crush was surely less interesting than some kind of secret vendetta.

“Can I go now?” Agatha let the exhaustion surface in her voice. 

“Of course. I appreciate you coming down. Sorry to drag you through all the trouble, you know—due diligence.”

Agatha nodded, and brushed past him in the hallway. She didn’t bother with last looks.

“Wait, Ma’am—”

Agatha turned and plastered on as polite a look as she could manage.

“You’re her only next of kin. Did you want to keep her personal effects?”

Her immediate urge was to scream at him, “No I fucking do not!”, but she figured that would not help her case. She reluctantly took the report card and the address book, and walked quickly to her car before he could change his mind about her innocence.

She sat in her car for almost an hour, looking at the address book. She hadn’t been lying about the names—some did sound familiar. She tried to match them with the shifty memories of people that had come through the building with briefcases or wads of cash over the years. Willie may have hidden his drug habit, but he’d always been openly economical. Agatha knew he’d kept the book to poach Evanora’s contacts—for buyers or houseguests, she wasn’t sure which. 

Agatha was startled by an old woman with unfocused eyes who came up quick by her window, pausing her mumbling about government conspiracies to say, quite clearly, “That green color looks fuckin’ ugly on you.” 

Agatha told the woman her hat looked even uglier, then rolled up her window and threw aside the notebook again. She drove home, and tried to ignore the hollowness of her mother’s life, reduced to a box of junk and an uninscribed headstone. She thought it odd—and then, suddenly, devastating—that the place she’d grown up now had pink paint on the walls, and someone else’s blood on the floor. 

 

 

At six o’clock that evening, having returned directly from a murder investigation, Agatha found herself standing awkwardly outside Wanda’s door with a box of cheez-its in one hand and a bottle of wine in the other. She’d never before felt awkward on Wanda’s porch, but there was something uniquely humiliating about showing up to an event she’d already declined, just because she’d seen a bit of blood and felt a little lonely about it.

She toyed with the wind chimes by the railing until they tangled, and thought about what she’d say: “Nicky forgot his favorite jacket,” or “They let me go early,” neither of which seemed like good options, given that she didn’t bring Nicky’s jacket, and she was already late. She was halfway through untangling the chimes and sorting out her story when the door opened to Rio, mid-laugh. Agatha blinked and righted herself.

“I didn’t know you—”

“How was jury duty?”

They spoke at the same time, and mumbled stiff apologies. Rio gestured to Agatha. 

“It was good. Open and shut. Easy.”

Rio looked at her, and Agatha had the distinct impression she’d already long sniffed out the lie.

“I didn’t know you were coming,” Rio said. “I thought you’d rather ‘swallow a sea urchin’ than attend a PTA potluck.’”

“I would,” Agatha said automatically, and flicked the handle of the wind chimes with her finger. “But I… ”

The half-excuses stuck at the back of her tongue. She almost said: “I saw the spot where Willie died.” But then she would have to explain who Willie was, and why it mattered when she didn’t really know him, and then about the hat box and star sticker and little black book. She thought maybe Rio was a person she was no longer allowed to tell these things.

“I didn’t know you’d be here, either,” she said instead, and Rio half-smiled. Her eyes flitted down.

“Green looks good on you.”

Agatha felt the heat flood her face, and considered tearing off the sweatshirt right then and there.

“Keep it,” Rio said. “I have plenty.”

Agatha looked at her clenched jaw, and the line between her eyebrows. She thought about reaching out and smoothing it with her index finger, but if she did that, she’d have to continue down the line of Rio’s nose, and then her thumb would press against her lips, and then her own lips would follow. And anyway, her hands were full.

“Is Wanda here?” she said. “Or have you been promoted?”

“I never thought I’d see the day.” Wanda’s voice came over Rio’s shoulder, and her head followed. She reached for the bottle in Agatha’s hand. “Lovers in the Mist?”

“It’s an old anniversary gift—sorry. Look, what do you want me to say? I didn’t have time to go to the store, and I wasn’t sure—” 

“Good enough for me,” Wanda cut her off gently, and Agatha fell silent, embarrassed at her quick defense. 

“Can we go in now?” she said, shifting her weight. 

“In a minute.” Wanda caught Rio’s eye and lowered her voice. “We were going over the plan again, and I still feel like there’s something I’m missing. Have you thought of anything?”

Agatha took back the wine, more roughly than she intended. “This isn’t Ocean’s Three—I’m having one conversation with Hank.”

Rio bit back her smile, and Wanda crossed her arms. “Are you going to start taking this seriously at any point? ”

Agatha sighed, and pushed past them. “Open the wine, and I’ll consider it.”

 

 

Agatha’s job the next afternoon would be to record Hank blackmailing her in earnest, and catch something self-incriminating. Agatha had nearly told Wanda, half a dozen times, that this was a plan only an elementary principal could come up with—specifically, the kind who thought putting paper in the wrong bin qualified as criminal activity. But then she would see the stubbornness in Wanda’s eye, and remember that she was doing all of this for her, and she bit her tongue with some effort.

Years later, Wanda would begin retellings of the story by explaining that the original plan, as she’d derived it in Agatha’s kitchen on a Sunday morning, was simple. As she would also note, it was so simple that only Agatha could find a way to complicate it beyond all recognition.

 

 

“Are you sure you want to be the one to do it?” Rio said, cornering her in front of Wanda’s sink. 

“I think I can handle that pathetic little twig,” Agatha scoffed. She stared Rio down, and finished the rest of her second glass in one swallow. 

“Fine. Are you sure you remember the plan? And how to hit record on your phone?” 

“Fuck you.” Agatha reached for the bottle, and Rio stopped her with a hand on hers.

Rio studied her, and Agatha felt her summer bug bites itch all at once under her gaze.

“Are you okay?” Rio asked, and Agatha shrugged her hand off, but didn’t reach for the bottle again. 

“I’m spectacular.” Agatha tried and failed to keep the meanness out. “How was school?”

She eyed Rio, and wanted desperately to hate her. She steeled her gaze, and thought, You are awful, for what you’ve done to me. Then her eyes fell to the space open at Rio’s collar, and the hint of red on her cheeks, and hate turned into something sharper and hotter. Rio’s eyes darkened, and Agatha knew the days were long gone where either of them had known how to hide their intentions.

“School was good,” Rio said after a beat. “They missed you.”

Agatha swallowed, setting down the glass. “Mmm, did they?”

“Yeah,” Rio said, her voice low now, and took half a step closer. Agatha let her eyes fall to Rio’s lips, red from the wine with a spot on the left side that Rio liked to worry with her teeth. Agatha suspected herself as the cause of this, too. She found it strange how she could so easily stomach the idea of shooting Willie in the chest, while the thought of Rio splitting her lip in anguish made Agatha want to turn herself in. 

She looked at the sore spot, and wanted to soothe it with her tongue. Or maybe licking a wound kept it from healing properly—she couldn’t quite remember which. She wet her own lip, looking at Rio’s, and thought about what and what not she’d like to do to it. Rio’s chest rose and fell, the flush spreading under her collar.

“Hey, hi.” Wanda shouldered between them with a dirty dish. “Can we do all the slow-blink, batting-your-eyelashes stuff a little later? Maybe when the entire PTA isn’t four feet away, and your job isn’t at stake?”

Agatha stepped back and clenched her jaw. Truthfully, even besides the tackiness of the plan, she was starting to wonder if the results were all that important. After she’d sat in dead Willie’s kitchen and looked at the blood-stained wood, her job was starting to feel like a trivial thing to try and salvage.

“Hey, it’s a party in here!” Josh sauntered past with an empty cup. He reached between Agatha and Rio, eying the wine label.

“Lovers in the Mist? I love this stuff!” 

“I’m going to—” Rio gestured vaguely, turning on her heel, and Agatha pinched the bridge of her nose.

“Read the room, Joshua.” Wanda pointed after Rio, and Josh slinked back out the way he came.

Agatha felt Wanda’s hand close around her wrist. 

“I know the plan,” Agatha said. Her voice sounded weary to her own ears.

Wanda’s face shifted, and the softness of it made Agatha feel a little sick. 

“Are you torturing yourself about Rio, or your mother?”

Agatha turned then, to get the boxcutter she’d come for in the first place. It was in the first drawer she opened, and she wished it’d been harder to find so that she could have made a real show of searching. She started to wash the dishes in the sink instead, and considered saying, ‘I am in no way thinking about Rio, or the fact that she loves me, or that she is leaving forever and I will likely never be the same. I am feeling incredible. It’s almost the last day of school, after all!”

The words, and the cheek, didn’t come as easily as she’d hoped. 

“I’m just thinking,” she said finally, scrubbing an empty glass, “that I grew up in a giant pile of horse shit. And I managed to get out of it, but now I’m standing off to the side, still covered in shit. And I can’t mix with all those clean, shitless people, but I can’t go back into the pile, either. I just miss it sometimes. Fuck was it was easier, being buried in shit.”

Wanda was looking at her with her eyebrows furrowed in concentration, like she was trying very hard to get on board with the metaphor. Agatha sighed, gritted her teeth, and added, “Yes, it’s about Rio. And my mother.”

She felt the soft pull of the water draining around her finger in the sink, and it reminded her, like it always did, of a newborn’s suckle on her pinkie. She felt suddenly like crying. She thought unwillingly of the black book that was in her back pocket, and wondered if any of the names had known her as a baby—if they remembered whether she’d come out a coward, which she was starting to suspect was just another name for Leaver.

She scrubbed at the glass in the sink, even as Wanda wrapped her arms around her and the water turned clear.

 

 

Agatha found that despite all promises of knowing the plan, the only part of the plan she seemed to be able to focus on was Rio. She’d never been one for drawn-out goodbyes, but all she could think about was how many hours she had left to focus on Rio at all. Rio high-fiving Nicky, Rio talking to parents and spilling zucchini bread crumbs all over her lap, Rio rolling up her shirt sleeves and shoving her hands in her pockets. 

Agatha hadn't really spoken to Rio since Sunday morning in her kitchen. After Wanda had left, they’d reluctantly agreed it was best to leave things as well as they could, and then, immediately following, they'd ended up naked on the couch. Unwilling to separate, they’d lain there for hours, kissing lazily to the low, droning sounds of Physics in Motion on the TV.

“Did you put this on because of Brynn?” Rio had asked finally, pulling her mouth away.

“It’s stupid that a fifth grader watches more PBS than I do. But physics is fucking boring. I’m more partial to anatomy.” Agatha dug her fingers into the backs of Rio’s thighs for emphasis.

“Oh, are you?” Rio kissed her again, her fingertips resting on Agatha’s jaw.

“This is physics. Not anatomy,” she mumbled against her lips. Agatha pressed her hips up.

“I’m pretty sure you’re wrong.”

“No, it’s like—” Rio sat up, and Agatha let her eyes wander appreciatively. “It’s like what they said in the show—when you throw two bouncy balls at each other. An elastic collision. That’s what this year has been like. We came at each other so fast, so intensely. It makes sense, that it’s time to go opposite directions.”

“You were listening to Physics in Motion while I was sitting on your face?”

“I’m a good multitasker.” 

Agatha hummed in agreement, and reached up to stroke lightly at Rio’s belly. Rio flinched at the ticklish spots, and Agatha passed over them again until Rio trapped her hands and lay down. She burrowed her face into the space above Agatha’s shoulder, and Agatha felt the mismatched press of their chests out of sync. She held her breath to make it even. 

“I really hate this part,” Rio mumbled against her skin. “Endings.”

Agatha felt the lump in her throat, and wished she weren’t afraid of frogs or monsters or someone who loved her, the way she wasn’t afraid of murder. She closed her eyes and inhaled against Rio’s neck, one last time.

 

 

On the Tuesday night before the last day of school, Agatha watched Rio play with her son in her best friend’s living room and thought about bouncy balls. She wondered if Rio’s neck still smelled like the plain lotion from her bedside table, and four-in-one shampoo.

 

 

Agatha rifled through the address book again, studying EVANORA HARKNESS inside the front cover. Underneath it, read: DAUGHTER: Agatha Harkness.

She leaned back against the auditorium door, and tried, still, to remember any of the people in it. She’d stayed up through the night, looking up all the names and numbers, hoping for just one normal, sane, good person that might remember the real, original Agatha, and tell her something miraculous and redeeming about her mother—what that would be, Agatha couldn’t say. 

Of course, they were all landlines, and she encountered dial tones and dead ends, one after the other. Once the sun came in, she’d given up and gone to wake Nicky. By five o’clock that evening, the book had found its way back into her pocket, her eyes bleary from lack of sleep.

"What the fuck are you doing?" Rio whispered. Agatha hadn’t even realized Rio was coming towards her until she was crowding her into the dark corner outside the door.

"I'm watching Hank. What the fuck are you doing?" Agatha crossed her arms, trying not to think about how close Rio was standing or how good she looked in business formal. 

"Oh, are you?" 

Of course, Agatha hadn’t been watching Hank at all. She’d been watching Rio, and thinking about her lips tinged red with wine, and musing that maybe they were in Oceans Three. The way Rio was greeting parents and setting up auction items while keeping Jen away from Wanda was notably impressive.

“Of course I am,” Agatha said, as convincingly as she could manage.

"Great! Then where is he?"

"He's–" Agatha glanced at the now-vacant spot. “Fuck.”

She looked around wildly, and spotted Wanda coming towards them. 

“I cannot believe you,” Rio hissed, and Wanda glared, pulling them further into the hallway.

"Are you two incapable of looking like you’re having a normal conversation?" 

"She lost Hank,” Rio said tightly.

Agatha was about to throw a spirited Fuck You Rio’s way, and realized suddenly that she looked more concerned than angry. Agatha wished everyone would stop looking so concerned about her. She opened her mouth to tell her as much. 

Wanda held up a hand to shush her, and they followed her gaze to the end of the hallway. Across from Hank, red-faced and slack-jawed, stood the unmistakable slouching silhouette of Ralph Bohner.

"Fuck me," Agatha muttered, already moving. She felt Wanda at her heels until they gave up the pretense and broke into a flat-out run. 

"Ralph, hi," Wanda said, guiding him around the corner.

"You, stay." Agatha pulled Hank back by the elbow. She glanced at the doorway, then dragged him exactly two feet out of sight, and pushed him up against the wall. She barred an arm at his throat and wondered if Willie’s murder might have started a little bit like this, had she never moved away.

"Did you tell him?" she asked lightly.

"Get off me," Hank rasped. Agatha pressed harder, and thought that maybe the cop from her mother’s house was a little stupid for letting her go.

"You can take the girl out of Southie, and so on,” she said, grinding her teeth. “Did. You. Tell. Him?"

Hank sputtered and nodded, his face turning red. Agatha pressed harder for the count of five-mississippi—which was really more like seven-or-eight-mississippi. She was choking a man in broad daylight, which she’d never done before, but remembered suddenly that her mother had—she saw Evanora with her arm pressed against a man’s throat in the kitchen, and heard the shattering of a plate. 

Agatha’s breath went shallow, as if she were the one with a hand at her throat, and she let go quickly. She put a hand automatically to the book in her pocket, and wondered if this could be considered demonic possession. 

“You did this to yourself,” Hank rasped. “And now you’re on the school cameras trying to strangle me. I hope your final moments in these halls were worth the trouble.”

To her horror, Agatha felt the hair raise on the back of her neck at the word “final,” even as Hank slumped against the wall. She hated endings, too.

She leaned, and jerked her head at the small bulb over the doorway. "Dome cameras can’t see through pillars.”

Agatha patted the cement to her left and widened her eyes in mock surprise. “Kind of like this one, that you happen to be standing right behind!”

“You—”

“Quiet,” Agatha said, stepping closer. “If you don’t stay out of our way, next time, we’ll have this chat in Wanda’s office, where there are no cameras at all.”

She jerked her fingers half-toward him, and he startled with a choked noise of terror. She left him sagging against the wall.

 

 

By the time she shut the library door behind her and leaned heavily against it, she was sweating and breathing so hard, the janitor had asked with considerable alarm if she was alright.

"Agatha," Ralph hissed, advancing on her. "An affair with your student teacher? Christ, have you gone completely insane? You have destroyed the reputation of this entire district!”

Agatha’s stomach turned, and she bit the inside of her cheek. She willed herself to feel as disaffected by this plan as she had twenty-four hours ago, but all the running and threatening Hank’s life had shifted something in her, and she couldn’t quite catch her breath. She pressed her palms behind her and felt like a squirrel, flattening herself against the door.

"Has he been this dramatic the whole time?" she called to Wanda, keeping her voice even.

"Worse." Wanda leaned her head back against a book rack. “He keeps going on about calling the board, and ‘unveiling the truth.’”

Ralph jiggled the door handle.

"Give me your keys."

“No," Wanda said sweetly. Agatha joined her under Nonfiction (Science), and looked at the phone clutched in her hand.

“Is that his?”

"Change of plans,” Wanda muttered. “Any other ideas?”

"I have one,” came a voice to their left. Agatha and Wanda jumped violently, and Alice emerged from the shadows under Fiction (Fantasy). 

“I like to nap on the tables in the back,” she offered, after a second of stunned silence.

“What—” Agatha cleared her throat. “How much did you hear?”

“Everything.” Alice held up her hand for a high five. “Nice.”

Wanda pulled her hand down. “No, very not nice, and very not allowed. What’s your idea?”

“We need a golf cart. And a distraction for the front of the school.” 

“Okay,” Wanda said, starting to pace. “What’s distracting?”

Alice looked pointedly at Agatha. 

“Kids,” Agatha said finally. “Kids are distracting.” She pressed her fingers into her aching temples.

“Fine. If we’re doing this, give me ten minutes. I’ll take the back—do not let him out.”

 

 

Happy drove them across the blacktop under the guise of escorting Ralph to the parking lot, Agatha stony-faced at his side. Ralph waxed on about the danger of her indiscretions and she listened with half an ear, focusing instead on the growing noise from the front of the school.

The distraction was—to Nicky and Tommy’s delight, and Billy’s trepidation—a volley of curses so filthy, even Agatha winced a little. She’d taught them a few more words on the spot, for fear that they wouldn’t be able to sell it, but she shouldn’t have worried. 

Wanda stared, speechless in the front seat. Parents paused their chatter to face the boys, until all Agatha could see were the backs of fifty heads, save one. Through the blonde and brunette mass, a single pair of dark eyes stared back at her. 

Rio was watching them, mouth open, and Agatha thought of how somber she must look—straight-backed in a golf cart with Ralph, as if in a procession to her own trial. She wanted to tell Rio: no, they were tricking him, they were winning. But there wasn’t time. And anyway, she’d never been convinced they were winning at all. 

 

 

Wanda stepped gingerly over a pile of hula-hoops, following Ralph into the P.E. shed—Alice’s recommendation, for its out-of-the-way nature and lack of cell service.

“Where are we?” Ralph demanded, and Agatha rolled her eyes at the sheer stupidity of walking first into a dark shed.

“You want me to tie him up?” Happy asked, head poking in the door.

“No.” Wanda pointed outside. 

“You all are—you’re crazy!” Ralph said. 

“Alice,” Agatha said slowly, still looking at Ralph. “Give us a minute.”

Wanda looked at her curiously as Alice brushed past them.

“You were right, Wanda. Something isn’t clicking.” Agatha paced across the small space. “Tell me again, Bohner. What exactly do you plan to do?”

“I’m telling the board and getting you fired,” he exploded. “And then I’m calling the police—this is illegal!”

“I’ll give you the number for one,” Agatha said. “He’s a crack shot at murder. I think playground kidnapping would be right up his alley.”

“Sit down,” Wanda said firmly, and Ralph blinked at them. He sank slowly onto a dusty beanbag.

“You’re telling the board,” Agatha said. “But not the PTA, or half the town that’s walking into the auditorium right now. You want this handled quietly.”

“Of course,” Wanda said. “This would ruin you, too. Let me guess—you stack the vote, get rid of Agatha, and then leak her firing to the press.”

Agatha scoffed. “What are you going to tell the press about me, Ralph? That I couldn’t handle a little divorce? That I refuse to adhere to your shitfaced policies?”

“That you’re unfit to teach,” Ralph said. “That your compulsive behavior is a danger to young students. I wouldn’t have to lift a fucking finger to sell it, you know—what does that tell you?”

Agatha swallowed. She felt quietly for the wall behind her. She thought about how it would happen—first she’d be fired, then the teachers would stop talking to her. Nicky’s friends would stop coming over, and he’d be made fun of at school, and she’d be made a ruiner, once again. She hadn’t considered until now that there could be something worse than a scandal. 

“You know the people in this town,” Ralph said, desperation creeping into his tone. “When they decide to hate you, they make your life a living hell. Better you than me.”

Agatha pressed her palms flat against the wood to keep them from shaking. The thought of a squirrel returned, oddly specific, and she blinked hard. This time, the image grew sharper, and she realized it wasn’t an odd, stray thought—it was a memory. 

Agatha looked at Wanda, and the fear rose sour in her throat. She knew with sudden clarity how this story ended. She knew the pattern: wind-up, strike, fall, run. Ralph thought himself a bully, like the cop in her mother’s bedroom, and her mother to Willie, and Willie to the neighborhood boys. Then one of those boys to Willie, in the end.

Agatha knew the only thing scarier than a bully was a bully that was scared. And she was so sure of it, so well-versed in bullies, because—of course—she’d always been one. 

 

 

In the June of eighth grade, she’d found the squirrel trapped behind the door of the girls’ locker room. It was a small, scrawny thing, its eyes black and tail not yet bushy. She didn’t like the look of its heaving chest—she thought it was weak, and stupid for coming into a locker room—but she didn’t want to go to class. So she stood a furious kind of guard by the door, pushing girls out of the way. She pinched and kicked and hissed at them like an angry cat, most of them jumping when they realized someone was standing there, then jumping further when they saw who it was.

One girl, Janet, got a particularly nasty pinch and a shove, and shoved back. Agatha felt her head hit the wall, and blinked through the spots in front of her eyes. The hair prickled at the nape of her neck. 

“Don’t you fuckin’ touch me,” Agatha yelled, and Janet came all the way up close. Agatha remembered her from the pool party last summer. 

“You fuckin’ touched me first, Agnes,” Janet snarled. “And for what? Doin’ a seance with that fuckin’ thing, you witch? Or trying to catch him for dinner, for you and your skank mutha—” 

Agatha punched her then, and felt the crack of it in her bones. She knew, usually, to use an open palm and avoid the cameras. But she was thirteen, and she wasn’t always thinking then. So this day in June, she stood right under an old camera in the doorway, and split her knuckles open over Janet’s crooked teeth. 

Janet spat and ran away, crying about her teeth, which Agatha thought ungrateful, because now she’d at least have the chance to get straight ones. She turned to the squirrel, and watched it quiver. 

“All that to save your life, fuckin’ runt.” 

She didn’t think it needed to know that she’d wanted to skip class anyway, or that she’d always wanted to hit Janet in the teeth. She watched it some more, then knelt slowly, and put out her hand. 

“Well, come on, then, if you’re going to be a fuckin’ baby about a door.” 

She heard a small cough behind her, and turned her head quickly. A mousy, brown-haired girl stood alone. 

“I’m Mandy,” she said, and Agatha scowled. 

“What’s it to me?” 

Mandy’s pale cheeks reddened. “I’m sorry for what Janet said. She says things like that all the time. I don’t think your mother’s a skank. We can eat lunch together, if you want.” 

Agatha stared at her splotchy cheeks and big eyes, and opened her mouth to say something—what, she wasn’t sure. To this day, she didn’t know whether she'd been planning to say yes or no to sorry little Mandy. She never got a chance to know, because the squirrel bit her on the hand. 

Mandy gasped, and Agatha yelped, staring at the creature in shock. It dashed between her legs, climbing the nearest tree and staring down at her. Agatha shook her hand out again, and felt the sting of tears. 

“Why’d you make me do all that if you could fuckin’ run the whole time?” she shouted. 

Mandy shrank at the menacing tone, and Agatha snapped her attention back. She walked to Mandy and looked her in the eyes, which were hazel and wet. Agatha felt the smart of her knuckles, and the blood starting to run from the bite on the back of her hand. 

“You’re wrong,” Agatha said, laying the bloodied hand on Mandy’s shoulder. “My mother is the biggest skank in all of Boston. And I don’t eat lunch with sissy wimps.” 

Then she wound back. This time, she was careful to keep an open palm.

 

 

“Agatha,” Wanda was saying. Agatha’s head went faint. She was overcome with sudden remorse for the mousy girl she’d hit, and remembered how it felt to be called Agnes—thrilling and lonely, all at the same time.

“They would believe you,” she said finally, looking at Ralph. Her heart hammered, something slotting together slowly in her head. “If you told them that I was unfit to teach—they’d believe you. They don’t even know me.”

Agatha reached suddenly for her pocket. She flipped frantically through the pages of the address book, her stomach rolling.

EVE PEABODY, married, plays bridge.

LAUREL O’DOWD, nurse, single.

She flipped to the front cover again:

EVANORA HARKNESS. 

Daughter: Agatha Harkness

Agatha’s hands shook, and she looked at Wanda.

“They’re all her.”

Agatha stood frozen, the room silent except for her ragged breathing. She moved, then, pushing her way outside. The sun was blinding, and she braced her hands on her knees. It was a sudden, ugly kind of loneliness that took her breath away—a notebook full of whole lives that had never been all the way real. Even under Evanora’s real name, she’d written Agatha down, as if she were a grocery item, or proof of life.

Agatha stood bent over and near-heaving for an indeterminable amount of time, until she felt Wanda’s hand on her shoulder. Agatha choked on the air. The ground was blurry, and the words tumbled out of her.

“I don’t want no one to know me.” 

A sob escaped her, and she dug her fingers tighter into her thighs.

“I don’t want the board to vote me out, and the town believing that I’m a danger to children, and I don’t want to be a miserable coward that never told Rio how I— that I—” She was crying, then, and didn't bother trying to finish the sentence or stay standing. She didn't know what to try, anymore. 

Wanda held her upright by the shoulders, gripping her hard.

“You’re not a coward,” she said firmly. “And I’m going to be there for you. But first, I’m going to save your job. And I think I just figured out what we’ve been missing.”

 

 

When Agatha had rubbed her eyes raw and gathered herself enough to re-enter the shed, she stopped in the doorway and stared. Wanda had Ralph by the collar.

“The vote,” Wanda said, shaking him a little. “Last summer, when the board voted to revoke Agatha’s tenure. What was the split?”

“They already voted?” Agatha said faintly.

“It failed. They needed an eighty percent majority. And you had enough to deal with last summer without the details. Ralph.”

Ralph held his hands up against the shaking. “Three-three, Jesus. Monica, Tony, and Bruce against revoking. Me, Sam, and Steve for.”

Wanda let go of Ralph and paused, a hand to her forehead, then let out a half laugh. When she turned, her eyes had a crazed sort of look about them.

Agatha let out a hopeless laugh. “Fifty-fifty? Jesus. Even if I could flip Sam and Steve, in—fuck, twenty minutes—there’s an entire town banging down my door either way once Ralph leaks the story.”

“Agatha, it’s Steve,” Wanda said, her voice vibrating. “Sam votes however Steve votes. This entire town likes whoever Steve likes. And of course Steve voted against you—he hates impropriety.”

“Right.” Agatha wondered if Wanda had hit her head on her way into the shed. “And this is the definition of improper.”

“He hates impropriety,” Wanda repeated. “But the only reason he isn’t board president is because he wanted to spend more time with his wife!”

“So?” 

Wanda came closer, lowering her voice. Her eyes glimmered in the dark. 

“He and Peggy are high school sweethearts. Steve Rogers loves a love story.”

Agatha’s stomach dropped, and she put a hand on an orange cone to steady herself.

“No fucking way. Not over my dead body, rotting into the fucking ground.”

“To make this go away, you need both bases covered—the board vote, and the public on your side. You can be in charge of how this ends, and you have twenty minutes to do it.”

Agatha laughed weakly. Her head ached. “I’m not using Rio for political pandering. I’m better than that. And I'm just so tired of it all, okay? I’d rather quit.”

“Would you?” Wanda said, exasperated. “Being better doesn’t mean changing everything about yourself. You’ll never be a PTA mom who makes cookies and lemonade and waits her turn to speak. You are loud, and loyal, and a little mean, but you are not your mother, Agatha. You’re Agatha Harkness. You protect yourself by protecting the people you love. Stop giving a dead bitch the satisfaction of ruining your life, and show them exactly who you are.”

Agatha looked at her, blinking back tears, and felt tingling replace the numbness in her limbs. The blood rushed to her face, and her ears, and she looked at Ralph. She looked at every inch of his pathetic face.

“I won’t know what to say,” she said, her voice shaking a little.

“Yes, you will.” Wanda took her hand. “I’ll be right next to you.”

Agatha blew air out of her cheeks, her heart thundering in her chest. She felt the terror and fury in her throat that had never really dissipated since last summer, and maybe from her entire childhood before that. She grabbed hold of it the best she could.

“Fuck it,” she said finally. She shook her hair back. “What are they going to do, fire me?” 

She shot Wanda a watery smile, thought about one person she loved, in particular, and pushed her way into the harsh sunlight.

 



“I don’t want to rush you,” Wanda whispered, “but she’s the only one left.”

Agatha glared in her direction. “I’m waiting for the right moment. There’s a lot riding on this, just to remind you of the extremely high personal stakes.”

“Sorry, you’re right. Take your time.”

“She’s taken quite enough time,” came a voice to Agatha’s right, and she jumped in her seat. 

She stared at Lilia across the empty row. 

“You see her, too, right?” she muttered.

But Rio would catch her eye every time, and smile, and Agatha would remember that Rio had woken up in her bed, and driven with her to school, and leaned over and kissed her from the passenger seat in the middle of the parking lot. Agatha rolled her eyes at the pout on her face, and pushed her head back down. “Stop being so easy to distract.”

“Who is it?” She yelled from the couch, and Rio bit down on her inner thigh, harder this time.

“Ow,” Agatha said, but it came out too pitchy to be convincing.

“It’s me,” came the muffled voice through the door.

Agatha and Rio froze and looked at each other. Wanda.

“And me!”

Nicky.

Agatha flew off the couch, almost knocking Rio over in the process.

 “I thought you said they wouldn’t be here until six!” Rio whispered.

“They weren’t supposed to!” Agatha hissed back, buttoning her flannel and yanking on her shorts. “Will you put a damn shirt on?” Then, louder, “Be there in a minute!”

“We’re just playing charades!” Wanda’s voice came through the wood, muffled. “Take your time!”

“Jesus, it is six.”

She grabbed her phone and scrolled past the polite questions from Wanda and the “open the fucking door” from Jen, and one to a group chat with her and Rio that said: ‘WHERE R. U IM 🚓🌳😵’

“It means she’s here.” 

“I told her a million times to just use her words. It takes me five fucking hours to read her texts.”

“There was a reason I never bought her a phone,” Rio said, kissing her cheek. “But for the record, I love that you did.”

Agatha shook her hair back. “That woman leaves way too many candles burning to be unsupervised for an entire summer.”

Rio laughed, and Agatha opened the door, finally, to the gaggle of guests on her doorstep. Wanda and Vision stood in a casual embrace, watching Nicky and the twins act out some indistinguishable animal. Lilia, Jen, and Alice—engrossed in a conversation about skin care—turned together at the sound of the door. 

Nicky barreled into her, and she picked him up and swung him. She couldn’t get him very high anymore, but she managed to lift him a few inches, at least. 

“How was camp today?” 

“Good,” Nicky said. “Is Ms. V here?”

“She’s—” 

The boys pushed past her without waiting for her answer, and Vision followed jovially, waving the wine bottle.

Lilia held up her phone. “It’s rude not to text people back, you know. My new friend, Jen here, has informed me that it’s called ghosting. Which is actually very funny. Ghosts do do that.”

Agatha opened her mouth to give her an earful as Alice led Lilia inside, but Wanda took her hand before she could follow.

“You two were busy, what, folding laundry?” she said, her voice low and teasing.

“Yes, actually.”

“Huh, it’s weird.” Jen gestured to Agatha’s face. “You have this sort of… glow. From the laundry.”

Agatha tried to roll her eyes, but couldn’t quite manage it. She smiled instead, pulling them inside, and looked over just in time to see Rio nudge something under the sofa with her foot. They locked eyes, and turned away to stifle their laughs.

“I want tacos,” Tommy announced, and the crowd gave a general chorus of agreement. 

“Ms. V,” Nicky said, jumping in front of Rio. “Mom got me Zelda, the one you told me about, and did you know you can ride a bear?”

“You can?” Rio said. “I knew you could ride a horse, but not a bear!”

Agatha smiled, joining them on the carpet. “Hey, baby, remember what we talked about?” she said, putting a head on Nicky’s hair. He looked up, confused.

“Rio,” she mouthed.

“Oh yeah,” Nicky said. “Rio. Did you get to go on a plane today? We missed you, Ms.--- I mean, Rio. Mama was like, “I miss Rio,” all the time. You were gone for so many days.”

Rio grinned and squeezed his shoulder. “I missed you guys so much, too. I’m not going back until next summer, and we have plenty of time to play Zelda before school starts next week, if you want.”

Nicky nodded, bouncing a little, then paused. “Are you still Mama’s girlfriend?”

Agatha snorted, and Rio smiled. 

“I am,” she said. “Is that okay with you?”

“Yeah. Are you guys going to get married?”

“Okay,” Agatha said, turning him by the shoulders to the kitchen. “Go look at the menu and tell me what you want.”

Agatha turned back to Rio, her face hot.

“Are you freaking out?” Rio asked, lips twitching.

“Nope.”

Rio wrapped her arms around her waist. “You’re cute when you’re lying. And when you’re freaking out.”

“I know we haven’t had time to really talk yet,” Agatha said slowly. “But, you’re okay with taking this one day at a time, right? It’s been twenty years since I entered a relationship, so I’m going to need a little time to remember, like, how all of this—”

Enough to read an article about frogs, and buy a video game.” She made her mouth into an ‘O’ shape, widening her eyes.

Agatha smiled, and nosed her cheek. “Did you know you can ride a bear?” she said, in perfect impression of Nicky. “It was the headline in my house for five fucking weeks.”

“I did know that, actually. It only took me two weeks to figure that out. Just so you know. That I’m very smart.”

“A good boy and a nerd,” Agatha hummed. “How did you get a girlfriend?”

Rio laughed through her nose, and swayed a little. She cleared her throat.

“Just so we’re clear,” she said after a beat, “you’re at least eighty percent sure you’re not going to wake up one day and decide this was a terrible idea, right?” 

She said it lightly, but Agatha heard the rare note of uncertainty in her voice. She leaned back and took Rio's face in both hands.

“I chose this.”

She moved a hand to Rio’s chest, and poked a finger gently into her sternum. 

“I kissed you second.”

Rio smiled. She curved a hand around Agatha’s neck. 

“I kissed you first.”

Agatha felt Rio’s lips on hers, and let it wash over her, calming the nerves in her belly. The voices were still there, like the colorful street signs on Rio’s bedroom door: “Look out! Beware!” But now, she could close her eyes and tell the voices, I think I am kissing the love of my life. Please keep it down.

Her head went quiet, and she smiled.

“Hey, you two are sickening,” Jen called, “and you need to put your orders in, or you’re not eating.”

Agatha grumbled a mocking, ‘or you’re not eating!’ against Rio’s cheek. Rio laughed, and pulled her to the kitchen. 

Agatha accepted a wine glass with a healthy pour from Wanda. She danced to This Will Be (an Everlasting Love) and watched Rio try to teach the boys to snap on the right beats. They waited for their taco delivery and slid around the tile floor in socks, and she felt the pieces of herself fit together like the sections of a broken lamp.

The air around her widened into something larger and lighter, like the smell of warm, wet earth, like blooming thistle and peppermint leaves. The kitchen window was open to the late summer storm, and she could hear the wind chimes sing. 

She watched the biggest and littlest loves of her life dance in her kitchen, and she danced with them. In that moment, with Rio swaying under the warm yellow light, Agatha didn’t worry or wonder much at all. She spun, her hands in theirs, and joined them in a simple four-step, which—once you’d found your footing—turned out to be fairly simple after all.

 

One.

How fun it was to shake her hair around, and wave her arms, and make them laugh.

Two.

How lucky she was to have wandered, sad and alone, into a pink room in June. 

Three.

How special it was to be protected in this life, by those that loved her and those she chose to love. 

Four.

How glad she was to be Agatha Harkness, because Agatha Harkness deserved this: to have found something good, and from scratch, made something better. 

 

Five.
