First Day of School II (Epilogue)

Chapter Summary

I made it! You made it! They made it! We all made it!

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

 

 

“Love. It was the beginning and end of everything, the foundation and the ceiling and the air in between.” 

- Kristin Hannah




 

6 YEARS LATER




 

The sun slanted through the open window, and Agatha shielded her eyes, still unused to morning sun in her bedroom. The light peeked over the hill, dotting the scattered white pines and gravel path. She caught the reflection of the fresh orange paint on the sign that now read: Joni’s Tree Farm.

Agatha smiled. Rio had changed it back last year, once Lilia’s second-to-last sister had moved to Italy, and the last one had died. Rio and Lilia had inherited the farmhouse in the will, and Lilia had promptly refused with some nonsense about feudal energy. Rio had told Agatha with her eyes shining in excitement, and Agatha felt like she could breathe better just at the thought of all the wide open space. She also thought she’d breathe better if she never let Rio give up another thing for her. So they’d asked Nicky, and reminded him of all the space he’d have to himself, as was most important to a fourteen year-old, and he’d answered: “Does that mean I get a dog?” 

With a pit stop at the shelter, six years after Agatha and Rio met, they moved to the farmhouse in Salem where they’d spent each Christmas since. Agatha saw it differently than she did at Christmas, now. It was the place she and Rio had once laid in bed with Nicky—where they’d smoked new age weed, and braided each other’s hair, and gone back to school permanently undone by the softness of it all. It became that place of softness again, now that it was theirs.

Agatha lay down on the bed on top of the unfolded pajamas. Her hair was still wet, and she could feel her dress wrinkling with all the lying on the laundry. She took a deep breath. It had been a particularly long summer without Rio, and no chance to visit—she’d drowned in moving, school prep, and Lilia disappearing to god-knows-where, reliably raising everyone’s blood pressure by ignoring their calls. 

Agatha could hear something sizzling in the pan downstairs, and took another deep breath, this time to smell the bacon and coffee. She’d forgotten by the end of this long summer, what it was like to wake up to her wife making her breakfast. She inhaled deep, and pictured Rio swaying to flamenco with her spatula counting the beats, until Agatha felt that she couldn’t lay in bed for another minute without actually seeing it. 

She tripped over the box in the upstairs hallway for the third time and cursed under her breath, throwing Nicky’s door open to wake him with an earful about unpacking. His bed was empty, and Robert Redford the rabbit paused mid-chew. He stared at her from his cage, and Agatha stared back. She couldn’t recall a time in the last few years that Nicky had woken up before her.

She started curiously down the stairs until she heard voices, and peeked her head around the corner. Nicky had his head bent over the counter, Rio flipping a waffle in a frying pan. Agatha leaned against the wall and let her eyes wander around the familiar kitchen, at the half-opened boxes and new pictures on the fridge. She’d kept up the ones of Rio as a teen, and Rio had let her now, less bothered these days by the twinges in her knee or old pictures of crutches. 

Agatha watched Nicky again, his hair freshly trimmed to shoulder-length, and his frame broader than it'd been the previous morning, she was sure. Her heart flipped in her chest the way it still did whenever she saw him with Rio. She almost wanted to stand like this on the stairs for the rest of the morning like a spy in her own home, so that she wouldn’t ruin the peace and quiet.

Then the dog pushed past her legs, and the cat streaked after him, and she cursed, flailing to keep her balance.

“Hey, did you feed the crazies?” she called, making her way to the kitchen.

Nicky hummed, his voice cracking on the low rumble, and Agatha paused at the counter. She looked at the book in front of him.

“And you’re doing your summer reading the morning of your first day of high school why?”

“The book had parts in Spanish,” Nicky said plainly, “and Rio wasn’t here to help.”

Agatha scoffed. “As if you two didn’t facetime every week? As if you don’t spend half your time googling god knows what on that phone?” 

“Can I get you some breakfast, love?” Rio smiled, spatula in hand—the red one, just as Agatha had pictured it. She leaned to peck Rio on the lips, wincing as she neared. 

“Good morning, bacon breath,” she muttered, and then Rio kissed her twice more, and breathed out a little extra. Agatha snorted and pushed her back, settling next to Nicky with a hand on the back of his neck. 

“My baby boy is starting high school,” she said dramatically. She was making a show of it, she knew, but Rio had caught her crying in earnest and clutching a baby picture in the bathroom last night. Agatha had been embarrassed at the unexpected flood of tears, but Rio was used to the menopausal mood swings by now—she’d just hugged her and kissed the top of her head until it passed.

“Mom.”

“Listen, I know you’ve had a lot of first days of school,” she said seriously, “but I just want to remind you that you probably shouldn’t pull your pants down in front of your class.”

“Mom. You’re the only one that even remembers that.”

“Mmm, sorry, kid,” Rio said, grinning at him. “Kind of an unforgettable first impression.”

Agatha smiled, remembering Rio’s arguably much more unforgettable first impression. Eventually, the unopened boxes stole her attention once more as Rio and Nicky continued with the homework, and feeling useless, she set out for the mail. 

The walk to and from the mailbox was almost ten minutes now, but Rio still objected to taking mail to school, so today Agatha decided to be generous. She stuffed her fuzzy socks into her untied boots, draped her jacket over her shoulders, and walked down the gravel path. She watched the farm rabbits follow her at a distance, and made a mental note to tell both Nicky and Rio to stop feeding them, because if they obtained one more animal in their household in addition to the dog, cat, frog, and existing rabbit, she was going to start sleeping outside.

She thumbed through the envelopes on her way back, and checked on a couple saplings in the field that had been looking a little sickly. Upon her return, she threw the mail on the counter and read the first line of Nicky’s book report over his shoulder. She was impressed, despite herself. 

“Anything for me?” Rio said, eyeing the pile. Her mouth was full, a half-eaten piece of bacon between her pinched fingers.

“Postcard from Lilia,” Agatha said, raising her eyebrows.

“Japan?” Rio squinted at it. “I thought she was in Egypt.”

“Apparently,” Agatha said, scanning the looping script, “Beatrice wanted her ashes scattered in five different countries, and Lilia doesn’t want to be haunted by her spirit.”

“Of course.” Rio smiled, reaching for the postcard, and glanced at the rest of the pile.

“Oh, also,” Agatha said lightly, catching her eye. “There was some extra special mail for you.”

Rio hummed, following her nod to the envelope addressed with red ink.

“I know about the hate mail,” Nicky said, rolling his eyes. “I’m not a kid, you don’t have to speak in code.”

“Okay,” Agatha said after a beat. “Sorry. Just surprised they got our new address so fast, but it’s nothing dangerous, baby. Total bullshit.”

“I know,” Nicky said. “It’s whatever, people are stupid. Oh—the twins invited me to this thing after school at their house. Can I go?”

“A thing?” Agatha said, crossing her arms. “What kind of thing?”

“It's just, like, a back to school thing.”

Agatha scoffed. “Wanda is letting the twins throw a back to school thing on the first day of school?”

“It’s just some people hanging out.”

“Some juniors.” Agatha crossed her arms.

“Mom, come on. It’s the twins. Plus, don’t you want to spend more time with Rio? She just got back.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes, and looked at the back of Rio’s head. She knew Rio was grinning by the sudden twitch of her ears.

“Fine,” she said finally. “Text me when you get there, and I’ll pick you up on my way home.”

“I don't need a ride, I’m biking today,” Nicky said, hopping off the stool. “Luca’s here, I gotta go.”

Agatha stared. “I thought I was driving you!”

“Nope,” Nicky said, kissing her on the cheek, and then jogging to Rio and dealing her one, too. “Bye Mom, bye Rio, love you both. Remember, don’t read the hate mail!”

He grabbed his bag and jogged out the door, and Agatha followed, yelling after him.

“Nicholas Scratch, do not forget to text me tonight! And be home before dark!” She stared after his retreating lanky form and uneven gait. Her heart swelled. “And I love you too!” she added hastily, her voice echoing across the field.

Agatha walked slowly back to the kitchen and rolled up her shirt sleeves, glaring at Rio.

“Did you two plan that?”

Rio shrugged innocently, sidling to the counter. “It was his idea, but we had overlapping interests. Don’t worry—you can drive me to school.” She leaned over and nudged Agatha’s nose with her own. 

“I guess,” Agatha teased, capturing her lips. She pulled back, looking at Nicky’s place. “Seriously, does he think we’re stupid? Wanda will be at school until at least six. Since when do high schoolers party on Monday afternoons? And did he even eat anything?”

“It was very big of you to let him get away with that awful lie,” Rio smiled. “And yes, he ate four waffles. He doesn’t complain about how I make them.”

“Waffles aren’t for flipping.”

“They get crispy in the bacon fat, and I know you like it. Plus, check this out—” She flipped the waffle behind her back, and stretched the spatula to catch it. She missed, and it fell onto the floor where it disappeared quickly under two blurs of black fur.

“Really good,” Agatha said, blinking up at her. “I’m so turned on. You should work at Benihana.”

Rio grinned, and opened the side door so that the dog and cat could fight over it in earnest. Tom was a scrawny stray, Jerry a seventy-pound Lab, but Agatha and Rio always put their money on Tom. 

“We have a whole thirty minutes,” Rio said, gesturing to the chicken clock she’d insisted on keeping, “if you want to see what else I can do.”

“No sex until you unpack that stupid suitcase and at least two boxes,” Agatha said, rounding the counter and biting into the waffle on Rio’s plate. 

“Then what was last night?”

Agatha paused mid-bite, just like Robert Redford. “Fine, no sex besides last night.”

“Evil witch!” Rio proclaimed, and pressed Agatha back into the counter. She nipped her neck, and Agatha laughed, her hands gripping tighter at the back of Rio’s sleep shirt. 

“I missed you,” Rio groaned, burying her face tighter against Agatha’s neck. “I’m sorry I wasn’t here to help move.”

“It’s fine, you’re here now,” Agatha said sweetly, “and I’m not lifting another finger.”

Rio smiled and leaned back, taking in the wall of photos by the entrance. Agatha had kept the one in the middle—the kick line of Lilia and her four sisters—and the rest had been replaced by photos of them and theirs. Agatha followed Rio’s gaze to the close up shot of their hands, Agatha’s with a silver wedding band and Rio’s with a tattoo of a black heart.

“Any twenty year-old summer camp lesbians try to hit on you this year?” Agatha said, taking her hand and examining the tattoo.

“Not a single one,” Rio said solemnly.

Agatha snorted. “Please. You’re a thirty-six year-old former professional dancer, and you’re their boss. That’s like injecting stray cats with catnip.”

“You had at least three marriage proposals last year!”

“From seven-year-olds, Rio.”

Rio smirked, leaning in close. “Well, lucky for you, this catnip is only interested in one very specific cat.”

Agatha groaned, and Rio laughed against her neck until she switched surreptitiously to kisses, and Agatha sighed. She forgot all of her recent threats with the threading of her hands in Rio’s hair. She breathed in and smelled maple syrup and white pine, which she’d thought was a part of the house, but had seemed to have seeped into Rio’s skin. Or maybe it was the other way around, and the house had always smelled like Rio. Agatha kissed her temple and hummed at the taste of spice and sap.

Rio managed to distract Agatha for twenty of the thirty remaining minutes with her mouth on any available skin and her fingers slipping under the hem of her dress, until Agatha smelled something burning and grasped wildly at the stove dial to keep their new home from burning down. No such fire occurred, so Agatha held up the dress herself and pushed Rio to her knees, with the breathless instruction: “You have three more minutes.” 

On the first day of school, they walked out of the house, as usual, ten minutes late.

 

 

 

 

“You’re not worried about the mail?” Agatha said, gravel crunching under their boots. 

Rio eyed her and adjusted her bag over her shoulder. “Are you worried?”

“Not worried,” Agatha said. “I just don’t love it.”

Rio took her hand and squeezed it, sniffling in the cool morning. “I seem to remember you saying it’s written by hateful, jobless fuckers who have nothing better to do.”

“That’s when the mail was for me.” Agatha paused in the middle of the path, and tugged on Rio’s hand. “I haven’t gotten any in two years, and you’re starting to get more than I ever did.”

“Jealous?” Rio smirked, coming slowly back towards her.

“Rio. More people are starting to see your name in the paper. Wanda can only do so much, and I can’t go to every idiot asshole’s house and choke them out, no matter how badly I would like to. At some point, you’re going to need to make a choice again. You know that, right?”

Agatha crossed her arms, and Rio’s face softened. She pried Agatha’s arms loose and held her hand again.

“We talked about this, love,” she said softly. “Unionizing was great, but it was a bandaid solution. I can’t advocate for more institutional change without drawing attention to the dancers who could get arrested for the sex work they’re doing on the side.”

“So naturally, you have to try and decriminalize sex work in the entire state of Massachusetts?”

Rio scratched her nose. “Well, yes.”

Agatha let out a hopeless laugh, and took Rio’s face in her hand. “I love you, Rio. I love what you’re doing. I think you’re fucking incredible for doing it. I’m just—”

“Thinking about our family, I know.” Rio nodded. “I’ve been thinking about it, too.”

“Okay,” Agatha said finally. “As long as we’re both thinking about it, maybe it goes on the back burner. Until we get settled.”

“Deal.” Rio kissed her once on the lips, then the cheek, then the forehead, until Agatha was smiling again. They walked up the rest of the path, with young trees to their left and right. Rio checked a couple that Agatha had missed in her earlier scan, and Agatha made menacing eyes at the rabbits following them to no avail. 

Rio went back to explaining how crisping a waffle in a pan was a superior culinary technique, and Agatha nodded in the right places, thinking about all the Things to be Considered. She wondered how long it would take to print her worksheets, and whether the internet company had switched the billing, and when she’d get a chance to call Jen and ask about her new job as principal of a residential school in New York. She chewed at her thumbnail until they jostled over the first parking lot speed bump, and Rio flicked her on the shoulder.

“Ow!”

“You’re not listening.”

“I’m sorry, baby,” Agatha smiled, reaching out to stroke her hair. “You’re right, your waffles are delicious.”

“That was twenty minutes ago,” Rio said, rolling her eyes. “I was talking about what we could do to pass the time this afternoon without Nicky, but I guess you don’t want to hear about that.”

“We live too far away now for the answer to be anything interesting.”

“Ye of little faith,” Rio said, arching a brow and trailing her fingers along Agatha’s thigh. 

Agatha hummed, rolling her head sideways on the seat to smile at her. Rio patted her thigh and kept her hand there comfortably, yawning and leaning back again. The sun streamed in through the window as they sat stuck in a long line of cars, and Agatha watched the light dance across Rio’s face in flashes of gold. As the car inched forward, she looked at the mural of the otters. Now when she saw it, she thought there really hadn’t been anything wrong with it in the first place. River otters didn’t eat oysters, after all, and who was to say they’d never held one? 

Their coffee cups steamed in the cupholders—green for Agatha and purple for Rio, so that they could hold a piece of each other through the day, which Agatha had told Rio was a disgusting idea, but had secretly always loved. Her back seat long empty of a booster seat and her first true love, and her front seat now occupied with the second, Agatha felt that being stuck in the parking lot on the first day of school was one of her favorite things to be. 

She leaned over suddenly and kissed Rio on the mouth, and Rio hummed in surprise. Agatha knew, now, that they fit together, in a world where otters were pink and orange, and her home was filled with strange animals and Christmas trees. 

Agatha rolled the windows down once the kids spotted them and tugged on their parents’ hands. They skipped to the car, then grew suddenly shy. Agatha shooed them lovingly away for fear of running over their feet, and made it to their parking spot with a small gaggle in tow. Rio laughed at the sight in the rearview, and they shut their doors at once, holding their arms open for the various combinations of high fives and hugs and tales of summer adventure. Agatha caught Rio’s eye over the top of the car, and mouthed, “Happy Doomsday.” Rio grinned, her eyes shining, and Agatha felt suddenly giddy with the excitement of it all—the first day of school, and all the unknowable things that would happen next. They took the kids in tow to where Wanda was waving with both hands, and traded hugs. Then they made their way up the front steps, and through the green double doors, together.

 

 

Chapter End Notes

This has been an 8-month labor of love, in the sweetest, realest way. It was, at some points, a total pure joy, and at others, a necessary escape. When I first had the idea for this story, I didn’t know so many people would be invested or delighted or touched by it. It feels humbling to receive that, every time. In what I’d consider one of my toughest years, this (and you all) has served as a reminder that when you do the things you love, people will come.

All that to say—thank you! Thank you for reading, for leaving kudos, for commenting, for pulling me onto twitter, for telling your friends about this story, for engaging with me about it, and for thinking about it in your day to day life. I’ve met new people, and learned from them, and discovered other writing I aspire to. What a gift!

If this leaves you with anything, remember that 1) it’s never too late to have a coming of age, 2) Agatha and Rio are hot, and 3) guys… please don’t fuck your student teacher.

Anyway, rest assured, I have more gas in the tank and ideas tumbling around the brain. Stay tuned! The fifth beat is the same as the first, after all.
